#Act I
## Chapter 1: The Warehouse

### 1.1.1
The warehouse sat along Southwell Road, a dead rectangle of brick and corrugated roof, the windows on the upper floor sprayed over with layers of graffiti and old council notices. The loading bay doors were tagged and dented, one hanging crooked on its runner as if some giant had tried to prise it open and thought better of it. To most people walking past in the early February rain, it was another piece of neglected industrial estate: ugly, invisible, someone else’s concern.
To Miles, however, it read like a list of risk assessments: guttering clogged along the roofline; standing water collecting in the sag between two panels; a rusting fire escape on the side that might barely hold a human’s weight; no visible fire extinguishers through the cracks in the loading doors; no obvious escape route that didn’t involve either a six‑foot drop or squeezing under rusted metal. It was normal for him, part due to his German Shepherd nature, part due to his engineering and store managing training.
He slowed as he rounded the corner, the plastic carrier bags rustling in his hand, his breath visible in the chill air. After his shift, his feet should already have been facing the Mandela Street flat he was sharing with his girlfriend Lillian – well, actually, the Mandela Street flat she was sharing with him. That day, however, he’d diverted, taking a side road because a colleague had mentioned that place with the kids in an offhand grumble about shoplifting.
He hadn’t liked the way the colleague said kids. Like they were a category of problem on a spreadsheet, not people.
Now, standing opposite the warehouse, he could hear them. Just scraps of words and conversation: a high laugh, the scrape of something being dragged, the faint metal clatter of a shopping trolley. Then another voice, young, sharp, commandeering: “Leave it, I said,” and the smaller answer that followed, muffled and tired.
Miles shifted the bags in his hand. His tail flicked once behind him, a short, controlled movement. He checked the pavement: no one close enough to pretend he hadn’t seen. He crossed the road.
Up close, the place smelled of damp, old oil, and the sour tang of too many bodies in not enough space. His barefoot claws clicked lightly on the concrete as he approached the loading bay. Someone had jammed a wooden pallet against the half‑open door to stop it slipping shut. He could see shoes beyond it, and bare paws, and the curve of a small back under an oversized hoodie. “Alright,” he said, pitching his voice low and steady. “Anyone home?”
The movement inside stopped.
His canine hearing let him listen to the only breathing in that moment: his own, slow and deliberate; theirs, quick and shallow. Then the pallet shifted a fraction. A narrow golden face appeared in the gap, one ear nicked at the tip, fur flattened under dirt. Brown eyes, too large for the sharpness of the muzzle, flicked over his chest, his carrier bags, the tips of his claws. Calculating. “We’re not supposed to talk to people,” she said.
Miles’ eyes also scanned the young anthro he was facing. Thin, sharp, tangled cream fur jutting from oversized and worn clothes. Younger than he’d expected from the voice, maybe fifteen or sixteen. Golden Retriever, though the usual softness of the breed’s markings was sharpened by hunger and whatever she’d had to learn to keep this place running. “That’s sound advice,” he answered. “I’m Miles. I work at the grocery store on Coldharbour Lane. Some of the staff said there were… kids out here. Thought I’d have a look.”
One of her ears twitched at the word staff. Her gaze dipped to the bags. “We’re fine,” she said, the tremor in her tone turning her words into a lie.
He pretended not to notice. “Alright.” He shifted his weight, letting the plastic handles bite a little deeper into his palm. “I’ve got a problem, see: end of the week, we’ve got stock that can’t go back on the shelves. Fresh date’s still good, but the boss would rather bin it than mess up his rotation. I hate wasting food. Makes my fur stand on end, that.” He smiled a little as he said it, enough to show he was trying, not enough to show teeth. “So I thought, maybe there’s someone who could use it, right that?”
Her eyes narrowed. She slid out a little further from behind the pallet, revealing a hoodie with the logo long since cracked, jeans rolled up above bare ankles. Her tail, tucked tight, betrayed more than her face. “We need no charities,” she said.
“Didn’t say you need.” Miles let the words rest in the air. “But food’s food. Once I’ve carried it all the way here, it’ll be rude if no one takes it off my hands, yeah?”
Another voice sounded behind her, younger and rough with sleep. “Sarah? Who is it?”
The girl didn’t look back. He noted her name on his personal mind list: Sarah. “No one. Go back inside.” There was a small shuffling noise and then silence. “You can leave it outside,” Sarah said. “If you want. We’ll… we’ll make sure foxes don’t get it.”
“Foxes are crafty,” Miles replied. “Maybe you should let someone who knows food storage handle it. I’m qualified, you know.” He tapped his name badge, still clipped to his jacket from habit: MILES FERGUSON, ASSISTANT MANAGER.
Her eyes flicked to the badge. Something loosened, minutely, around her mouth. “If you come in,” she said slowly, “then the others will see you and then they’ll think it’s okay, and then it won’t be safe. So you leave it outside, and then you go. That’s the deal.” Miles could hear in her cadence she’d already negotiated this a hundred times, with imaginary adults who never showed up. She built rules because no one had given them any.
He nodded, stepping back from the pallet. “Deal,” he said. “I’ll set them here. You can decide what to do after I’m gone.” He crouched, the concrete cold seeping through his jeans at the knees, and laid the bags down one by one just inside the shadow of the loading bay roof, out of the worst of the weather. Bread, pre‑packed salads, yoghurts, bananas freckled but still firm, a tray of chicken that needed cooking today. He’d chosen things that could be eaten cold or cooked in a pan over a camping stove, if they had one. He didn’t look inside the warehouse, though his eyes wanted to catalogue everything: bedrolls, damp patches, improvised curtains. He could feel Sarah watching every movement. “There’s enough here for a few days, if you’re careful with it,” he said as he stood. “More where that came from if the boss keeps being an idiot. I’ll put it on the waste sheet.”
“Why?” she asked.
“Because it’s easier to fiddle the waste numbers than it is to explain to my conscience why I binned ten loaves of bread when there are kids sleeping in an empty warehouse,” he said. That got him a tiny, unwilling twitch at the corner of her mouth. Not quite a smile, but an acknowledgement that the joke existed. “If anyone gives you grief,” he added, “tell them Miles from the grocery store said they can come talk to him. I’m there most days.” He paused, ears perked forward. “But don’t send the little ones on their own, right that? I’ll keep coming here instead.”
“We don’t need anyone to fight our battles,” she said, involuntarily clenching her fists.
“Didn’t say you did,” Miles replied, keeping his tone even. “But sometimes it helps to know who’s on your side. That’s all.” He then stepped back from the pallet properly, giving her the space she’d demanded. The drizzle beaded on his fur, soaking into the undercoat. “I’ll come by in a couple of days,” he said. “If you don’t want more, you can tell me then.”
“We might not be here,” she said quickly.
“Alright.” He tipped his head, as if that settled everything. “Then if I don’t see you, I’ll know you’re somewhere better.” He turned and walked away. He could feel the eyes in his back all the way to the corner. Years of managing shoplifters and anxious customers had given him a sixth sense for when someone watched. 
At the end of the street, he paused as if to check his phone, angling his body so he could see the warehouse reflected in the cracked glass of a bus shelter. The pallet stayed in place for three breaths, four, five. Then it shifted. Sarah slid out, quick and low, a small pack of even smaller figures closed behind her, and they fell on the bags in coordinated intensity. She was already rationing, sorting, sending one kid back inside with the chicken, another with the fruit. They were hungry, but they were organised.

### 1.1.2
Lillian stood at the stove in socked feet, hips braced against the counter as she stirred a pan with unhurried authority, the wooden spoon moving in lazy figure eights. Lillian sighed, thinking about the onions she'd started chopping before her stomach rebelled.
Miles came in quietly, habit rather than stealth. The television in the background was murmuring something about the Royal Baby again, breathless speculation delivered with the same gravity as a weather warning. “Miles? Is that you?” Lillian called from the kitchen. Before he could answer, she added: “Don’t shake and drip all over the entrance! I’ve just finished mopping the floor. Use the towel to dry your fur, honey.”
He brushed his fur absent-mindedly with the towel he found hanging on his left. His elbow hit a framed picture on the wall, but his reflexes allowed him to catch it on time; his gaze moves slowly to the photograph: a picture taken ten years before, him and Lillian holding the prize for their victory at the band challenge in 2003. Under his tender amber eyes, he noted how they both changed over years, his fur less scruffy and his shoulders broader, Lillian's body softened overall from cheeks to thighs, her thick badger frame carrying the accumulated weight of a decade spent comfortable rather than careful - yet both keeping the same light in their eyes.
He reached her in the small kitchen, hanging his jacket on the back of the chair. He washed his hands at the sink without being asked, sleeves pushed up.
“You’re late,” she said, not looking around.
“Day went sideways,” he replied. He leaned in to kiss the top of her head, nose brushing her hairline, breathing in her complex scent of cardamom and iodine. Home. “Smells good.”
“It will,” she said. “If you don’t distract me and make me forget the garlic.”
He moved to the table, smiling, noticing a pot full of onions. " Have those onions been found guilty of something?" he commented.
"High treason. My stomach is staging a protest against them."
"Right. Onions are inherently English, the blood of clans running in your veins must be boiling." She smiles, moving a bang of her long black hair from her snout, while he pulled out the battered chopping board and starting on the bread without ceremony, slicing thick, uneven pieces the way she liked them, a choreography perfected over ten years of cohabitation. She dished up when the timer went, steam fogging her glasses as she carried the bowls over. They ate leaning against the counter, shoulders touching, tails occasionally knocking in unconscious synchrony. Outside, a bus roared past, tyres hissing on wet tarmac. Inside, there was only the scrape of spoons and the TV’s low insistence. “So,” she said eventually, when the edge had come off her own hunger. “What sideways looks like today?”
Miles wiped his mouth with the back of his hand, thinking. He could feel the words queueing up behind his teeth, each one wanting to come out in the right order. “I took a detour,” he said. “The warehouse off the estate near Southwell Road.”
“Uh-uh,” she noted. “I heard about that.”
“There are kids living in.”
Lillian’s spoon paused halfway to her mouth, held stiff, like a musician suspending a note. “Kids,” she repeated.
“Yeah. Anthros.” He nodded once. “Proper set up. Organised. There’s a girl running it.”
He watched her badger snout carefully as he spoke, the way he always did when the subject was something that might bruise if handled wrong. She didn’t interrupt. She rarely did. Her attention settled on him fully, warm and weighty as a blanket. “How old?” she asked.
“Sixteen, maybe. Old enough to have learned not to trust anyone. Sarah, it’s her name. Golden Retriever.” He took a breath. “I dropped off food. Didn’t go in. She negotiated terms like she’d been doing it her whole life.”
Lillian set her bowl down and turned properly, leaning her hip against the counter to face him. “And you’re telling me because?”
“Because I keep thinking about it,” he admitted. “Because it’s not just a bad feeling I can shake off. And because if I don’t say it out loud, I’ll start pretending it’s only my problem.”
She studied him for a long second, then reached out and wiped a smear of sauce from the corner of his snout with her thumb. “You don’t have only your problems,” she said. “You know that.”
“I know.” He exhaled, relaxing his shoulders. “I thought maybe… I’d go back. In a couple of days. Just to check.”
She nodded slowly. “I’d expect nothing less from you.” Then, after a beat, “You’re not going back alone.”
He blinked. “Lils—”
She raised a paw, stopping him gently. “I’m not saying to barge in. I’m saying if this is going to sit in our lives, it sits in both of them. I want to see the place. I want to see her.”
He looked at her, really looked, and felt that familiar mix of pride and fear coil in his chest. “It’s not safe.”
She smiled, soft and infuriating. “Neither is the world, yet we manage.” Lillian reached for their bowls and stacked them in the sink. “Eat up,” she added. “Then tell me everything you remember. Especially the boring bits.”
 
## Chapter 2 — Math and Nausea

### 1.2.1
“You’re doing it again,” Faroukh said.
They were sitting at a makeshift table, made using an upturned pallet with a sheet of thin plywood laid across it, hunching under the weak cone of light from a camping lantern. The rest of the warehouse lived in a coughing dim around them: sleeping bags draped over scavenged sofas; a curtain made of ripped bin-liners. Somewhere near the far wall, a kid snored with a thin whistle in their nose. Rain ticked on the corrugated roof like fingertips.
 “I’m doing what?” Sarah didn’t look up. She kept her pencil moving, as if speed could turn wrong answers into right ones. Faroukh’s handwriting on the recycled paper sheet was small, slanted, and confident in a way that made Sarah want to tear the paper in half on principle.
“You’re guessing.”
“I’m not guessing.”
“You are. You can’t multiply by intuition.”
Sarah huffed. She could feel herself bristling, her shoulders inching up toward her ears. Faroukh sat opposite her in his faded school blazer, too clean for the warehouse. His cream-gold long Afghan Hound fur was brushed, his claws neat. He smelled like laundry detergent and that metallic tang of bus rails. The phone lay on the table beside his exercise book, screen dark, a sleek, smug rectangle of outside life. She tapped her pencil against the paper. “It doesn’t matter,” she commented, passing a paw through the golden locks of hair and fur mixing on the top of her head.
“It does,” Faroukh said. “If you want the council to stop treating you like you’re stupid, you need to be able to do basic numbers when someone shoves a form at you.”
Sarah scoffed. “The council will treat us like we’re stupid even if I can solve your little equations, yeah?”
Faroukh’s mouth twitched. Not quite a smile. “Well, sure. But if you can count properly it will be much more difficult for people to trick you and steal from you. And you can teach it to the pups, too, so they’ll learn earlier.”
That landed. Sarah’s pencil stilled. She stared at the long multiplication problem he’d written out—three digits, two digits, neat columns—like it was a lock she’d been trying to pick. “I already know how to count and make operations,” she muttered.
“Look,” he said, softening, “you don’t have to like it. Just do it. Start with the six.”
Sarah swallowed her pride and did as she was told.
He didn’t touch her pencil. He kept his paws on his own side of the table, fingers laced, and talked her through it like it was a song: rhythm, pattern, repeat. Sometimes he moved a bang of long fur from his eyes. Sarah hated that it worked. She hated the small, surprising relief of seeing the columns line up. “You carry the one,” Faroukh said. “There. Good. Now the next.”
A scuffle of movement by the far curtain, a whispered argument, then quiet again. Sarah’s attention tugged toward it automatically. Warehouse noise always meant potential problem. Faroukh’s attention stayed fixed on the numbers. “How many tins left?” he asked abruptly.
“Why?”
“Just answer.”
Sarah frowned, inventory list unspooling in her head: beans, tomatoes, soup, a few peaches if no one was greedy. “Forty-seven,” she said. “Not counting the dented ones.”
Faroukh nodded once, as if updating an invisible ledger. “And if you’ve got twelve mouths and you want it to last—”
“Don’t,” Sarah snapped. “Don’t do that. Don’t make it sound like it’s a maths problem. It’s people. It’s food.”
Faroukh’s gaze lifted to her face. In the lantern light, his eyes looked older than fifteen. “It’s both,” he said quietly. “That’s the point.” The phone vibrated, interrupting him. Faroukh’s back straightened, eyes widening. His paw moved to the phone too fast for it to be casual. Sarah watched his thumb hover over the screen. No contact photo, no friendly nickname, just a number.
Faroukh glanced toward the sleeping area. Toward the curtain. Toward the dark corners where little eyes might be open even when bodies weren’t moving. Then he stood.
“Stay here,” Sarah said.
He didn’t answer. He walked toward the door that led to the side alley, moving in that careful, tidy way he moved when he was trying not to look like he belonged here. The phone was pressed tight to his ear.
Sarah pushed up from the pallet table, quiet on her bare hind paws, and followed just enough to keep him in sight. “…yeah,” Faroukh was muttering into the receiver, voice pitched low. “I’m here. I told you… I’m busy.” A pause. He listened, face blanking out as if he’d turned it into a wall. “No,” he said. “I can’t do tonight. I’ve got… I’ve got coursework.” Sarah stopped behind a stack of old tyres and a broken trolley, just far enough that she couldn’t hear the other voice. She could see Faroukh’s shoulders, though, rise on a slow inhale. Another pause. His free paw lifted, two fingers rubbing at the bridge of his muzzle. “Don’t,” Faroukh whispered. “Don’t start that. You said—you said it’d just be…” He swallowed. “I said I can’t.” She heard the phone crackling. Faroukh’s fur along the back of his neck prickled: Sarah could see it. “Alright,” he said, too fast. “Alright. Fine. I’ll do it. I’ll come.” He ended the call with a sharp press of his thumb.
Sarah stepped out from behind the tyres before she could talk herself out of it. “Who was that?”
Faroukh yelped, eyes flashing to her like he’d forgotten she existed. For a second his face was pure anger, then he put it away again. “No one,” he said.
“Funny,” Sarah replied. “Because it sounded like someone.”
Faroukh’s nostrils flared. He looked past her, toward the sleeping bags, toward the kids. “None of your business.”
“It is if it’s my warehouse,” Sarah said.
Faroukh’s mouth tightened. “It’s not your warehouse.”
Sarah’s hackles rose. “What did you just say?”
He shut his eyes briefly, like a person stepping back from a ledge. When he opened them, his voice was flatter. “I’m sorry. I didn’t mean it like that. I just—”
“You just what?” Sarah demanded. “You just come in here with your clean blazer and your phone and your maths lessons and you think you can tell me what is and isn’t mine?”
Faroukh’s gaze flicked to her feet, bare on the concrete, then back up. “I’m trying to help.”
Sarah laughed, sharp and humourless. “Help doesn’t come with secret calls.”
Faroukh emitted a low yelp. “It’s an errand,” he said at last, as if naming it made it harmless.
“An errand for who?”
He didn’t answer. Sarah stared at him, looking for the line where his life split down the middle. She found it in the way he held the phone: like a leash. “How long you gone?” she asked, tone clipped.
“A couple of hours.”
“And if you don’t come back?”
Faroukh’s eyes hardened. “I’ll come back.”
Sarah didn’t believe him. But she also didn’t have the right words to say what she believed instead. They got back into the warehouse, keeping the silence between them like a wall. Faroukh shoved the phone into his pocket, grabbed his school bag from the corner, and slung it over his shoulder. He paused by the side door, hand on the latch, and for a moment he looked again like the kid he was, after all: fifteen, scared, trying to be brave. Then he slipped out into the alley and pulled the door shut behind him with a careful, quiet click.
The warehouse settled back into its night noises.
Sarah stood where he’d left her for a long moment, listening to the rain and the distant cough of traffic. Her fingers flexed, empty without the pencil. On the pallet table, his neat numbers waited, half-finished.
Sarah went back and sat down.
She picked up the pencil.
She carried the one.

### 1.2.2
The room smelled of brass, wet fur, and bleach.
Lillian stood at the front of the small practice hall, palms flat against the edge of the piano, watching sixteen second‑years teenagers wrestle their way through a piece that was, in theory, well within their ability. In practice, half of them were looking at the clock, three were trying not to laugh, and the trumpeter in the back row had decided dynamics were optional.
“Again from bar thirty‑two,” she said. “And this time, Jamie, if you play fortissimo where I’ve marked mezzo, I will revoke your biscuit privileges for the rest of term.” There was a ripple of muffled laughter. The trumpeter, a lanky human boy with a permanent cowlick, raised his instrument in mock salute.
She lifted her hand. The first notes came in slightly more together, the clumsy unison beginning to find its shape. She felt it in her chest before she really heard it by the way sound made the floorboards hum beneath her held.
And then the room tilted.
Not much. Just a small, slow slide, as if the building had shifted a fraction on its foundations. The black notes on the page swam for a moment, smearing into one another. A wave of heat rolled through her, starting low in her belly and blooming up into her chest. Her mouth filled with saliva. She swallowed hard and kept her face neutral. “Watch your entries,” she called. Her voice sounded normal to her own ears, if a little tight. “Trombone, you’re early there, love.” The trombonist blushed and adjusted. The bar passed. The room settled back into place.
Perhaps she’d not eaten enough. Breakfast had been rushed: a too‑hot mouthful of tea, a slice of toast she’d eaten standing at the counter while the kettle boiled again. She’d been running late, and Miles had been leaving at the same time, tall body ducking to kiss the top of her head as he squeezed past her in the narrow kitchen. “You alright for lunch?” he’d asked, already half in his coat.
“I’ll grab something between classes,” she’d said. “Go, you’ll miss your bus.”
He’d gone, and she hadn’t grabbed anything. First period had started, then admin, then an anxious parent at her office door, and then the second‑years, and now here she was, feeling as if her body had been very gently put down in the wrong place.
The nausea rolled again during the repeat. Not sharp, not like the stomach bugs that occasionally swept through the building every winter. It was a deep, sour wave that seemed to rise from somewhere behind her navel, meet the back of her throat, and hover there, waiting. She tightened her grip on the piano edge, taking care not to press her claws into the polished wood. Her belly, layered and soft, felt suddenly heavy, as if it had acquired its own separate gravity. “Right,” she said briskly as they crashed into the final chord. “Five minutes. Get water, stretch, do not tackle each other in the corridor or I’ll know. Then we’ll try that again with fewer murders of the key signature, aye?” They laughed and scattered, chairs scraping, cases snapping.
The room emptied of sound with a speed that always surprised her. Teenagers could make a church seem loud and an auditorium seem small.
When the last of them had gone, she let herself sink down on the piano stool. It creaked in polite protest under her weight. She closed her eyes and breathed through her mouth, counting.
One, two, three, four. In.
One, two, three, four. Out.
The nausea ebbed slightly, leaving behind a strange metallic taste, as if she’d bitten her own tongue. She opened her eyes. The room was the same: pale walls, scuffed skirting boards, the faint outline of an old notice board that had once hung on the far wall. Nothing had moved, but she felt misaligned inside her own body, like a song played in the wrong key.
“Och, get a grip,” she muttered under her breath. “It’s just low blood sugar.”
She reached for the battered satchel she kept beside the piano and dug until her claws brushed the familiar crinkle of a biscuit wrapper. Two rich tea biscuits, slightly crushed, wrapped in a folded napkin. She’d meant them for a student, a small bribe for a reluctant clarinettist, but needs must.
The first bite was dry and sweet. She chewed slowly, letting the crumbs sit on her tongue for a moment before swallowing. The biscuit landed in her stomach like a stone. The biscuit stayed. “There we are,” she said aloud, trying to convince her own body. “Nothing dramatic.”
The knock at the door was soft, hesitant. She straightened automatically, her expression smoothing into the professional warmth her students trusted. “Come in.”
The door opened a crack and Juno Radcliffe slid in sideways, clutching her flute case to her chest like a shield. She was a badger, just like her, and looked like Lillian in reverse: where Lillian was short and soft, Juno was tall and broad; where Lillian wore her body fat like a high-couture dress, Juno’s was thick and layered like an armour. “Sorry,” Juno whispered. “I know it’s not time yet. I just… I was early.” She passed a paw over her straight, green-tipped bob cut, trying to further straightening her hair.
Lillian forced her mouth into a smile. “You’re not in trouble for being early,” she said. “Close the door, love, or we’ll lose what little heat this glorified cupboard has.”
Juno obeyed, the door clicking shut behind her. She hovered by the chairs, eyes flicking to Lillian’s face, then away, then back again. “You look a bit… are you alright?” Juno asked finally. Her accent softened the edges of the words, the faint Irish lilt making alright sound like a question about the whole world.
Lillian considered lying. It would have been easy to say she was fine, just tired. But she’d drilled into her students that bodies, hands, breathing, posture mattered, and that ignoring pain was a sure way to end up injured. “Bit off,” she admitted. “Stomach’s having opinions. Nothing serious. I’ll live, I swear.”
“Should I get someone?” Juno’s brow furrowed.
“No.” Lillian shook her head, gently, in case the room thought about following. “No need. Sit yourself down, since you’re here. How’s your embouchure today?”
Juno smiled, tension easing a fraction. “Better. I think. I’ve been doing the exercises you showed me.”
“Good girl,” Lillian said. “We’ll check it in a minute. Talk me through what you’ve practised. Take my mind off my ill‑advised choice to skip lunch.”
Juno hesitated, then began to speak, detailing scales and arpeggios and a new piece she’d found online. As she talked, Lillian let the words wash over her, a thread of sound she could follow out of her own body for a moment. The nausea settled again, not gone, but demoted to background hum. She listened with half an ear, nodding in encouragement, asking the occasional question. The rest of her attention was on the strange, off‑centre pull in her abdomen, the way her breasts felt suddenly heavier against the fabric of her bra, the faint ache low in her back.
It felt, absurdly, like the week before her period, except her period had already come and gone. Right on time, as always. But bodies are not clocks, she told herself. Stress did odd things. She’d been running more, trying to keep up with the demands of the academy, the band. She’d been sleeping badly. 
She watched Juno’s fingers move, the silver keys flashing. Her own paws felt heavy in her lap, claws dull. The nausea curled in on itself like a question mark. “That’s better,” she noted when Juno finished, her voice only slightly rough. “Your tone’s much more even through the register. We’ll work the middle section next week.”
Juno beamed. Praise sat on her shoulders like sunlight. “Thanks, Miss,” she said. “I’ll… I’ll go. Let you have your break.”
When the door closed behind her, the room was suddenly too quiet. The clock on the wall ticked, each second landing somewhere between Lillian’s ribs.
She pressed the heel of her paw lightly against her belly. The flesh yielded, soft, familiar. 
The bell rang, shrill and insistent, dragging her back to the timetable. She rose carefully from the stool, waiting to see if the room would spin. It didn’t. “Right,” she said, smoothing her blouse over her belly. “Next lot.”
She walked to the door, her steps measured. As she opened it, the corridor noise rushed in: chatter, footsteps, the clatter of instrument cases. Life, carrying on at its usual volume. She smiled at the students waiting outside, ushered them in.


 
## Chapter 3: Intimacy

### 1.3.1
Morning came by degrees, the way it always did in their little flat: not as a clean line between night and day, but as a slow un-knotting of darkness. The radiator clicked once, tired. The upstairs neighbour’s shower coughed to life. Somewhere outside, a bus exhaled at the stop, the sound of its doors folding in on themselves like hands closing.
Lillian lay on her side with her muzzle to the window, cocooned in the warmth of the duvet. Miles’s body was a steady heat behind her, long and solid, his arm draped over her waist with a weight that felt both possessive and ordinary. His breathing was a slow, deep rhythm; every now and then his tail tapped the mattress once, as if he’d found a dream he approved of.
She could smell him in the sheets: fur shampoo, lavender and turpentine. The pillowcase smelled like her: cardamom, iodine and freshly-dug soil. Lillian blinked awake and lay still for a moment, cataloguing herself the way she always did before she got up.
Head: fine.
Shoulders: the usual mild ache from conducting teenagers who thought tempo was optional.
Belly: heavy, warm, tender.
Her mouth tasted faintly metallic, as if she’d been sucking a coin.
She frowned into the pillow. The nausea from yesterday wasn’t full force now, but it was there, a low, sour suggestion. Her breasts felt tight and sore beneath the cotton of her sleep shirt. She pressed her thighs together and felt a dull throb in her lower back.
Miles shifted behind her, half waking. His nose nudged her shoulder, a gentle, absent-minded nuzzle that was more instinct than choice. “Happy Valentines’s day,” he murmured.
“Aye,” she said quietly, voice still rough with sleep.
His hand slid, warm and broad, from her waist to the curve of her belly. It was not sexual, not at first—just that habitual touch he used to check she was there, that she was real, that the day hadn’t moved him somewhere else. His palm rested on her softness, fingers splayed. Miles made a small sound against the back of her neck, half hum, half sigh. “You’re warm.”
“That’s what quilts are for,” she said, though the words came out more tender than teasing.
He kissed her shoulder. Then again, higher, closer to the base of her neck. His whiskers brushed her fur. His body pressed in behind her with slow, careful intent.
Lillian turned, shifting her weight with the practiced, unhurried adjustments of someone who knew her own body and the space it occupied. The duvet slid down to her waist, cool air kissing her skin. Miles’s eyes were half-lidded, amber soft in the grey morning. He looked, as he always did up close, like someone built to be reliable: broad muzzle, steady gaze, the faintest scar above his brow from some childhood mishap he never talked about.
“You’re staring,” she whispered.
“Just looking,” he replied.
It was a familiar line between them, one that had lasted through a decade of cohabitation and all the ways the world tried to make anthro bodies into a debate. His hand moved to her cheek, thumb stroking the fur along the edge of her glasses where she’d fallen asleep in them again. “You didn’t take these off,” he noted.
“I was tired,” she said.
“Aye.” He smiled. He kissed her then, slow and unhurried. His mouth was warm, his breath smelling faintly of last night’s tea and toothpaste. Lillian’s body answered with its own quiet language: a softening, an opening, the small shiver of being touched by someone who already knew where the edges were.
They moved together the way they always did—competent, affectionate, un-showy. There was nothing frantic in it. Miles paused to pull the duvet aside so it didn’t tangle around her legs; Lillian adjusted the pillow under his shoulder so his neck didn’t kink. Their intimacy was full of small design choices, made without comment, like people assembling a space where tenderness could happen without strain.
After, Miles lay on his back, one arm thrown over his eyes, chest rising and falling with the deeper breaths of satisfaction. Lillian curled against his side, cheek resting on the warm stretch of his ribs. She could hear the faint thud of his heart. It sounded like a metronome set to the tempo of home.
Miles’s hand found her again, tracing idle patterns on her shoulder. “You alright?” he asked.
Lillian went still. It wasn’t the question itself. Miles asked that every day, sometimes three or four times, like an engineer checking a system he cared about. It was the way he’d said it now: quiet, almost cautious, as if he’d felt something shift under his palm.
“I’m fine,” she said automatically.
He didn’t argue, but she could feel his attention sharpen. “You were quiet,” he said.
“I’m always quiet in the morning,” she replied.
Miles’s mouth twitched. “No, you’re usually opinionated in the morning. You wake up and immediately start complaining about the BBC.”
“That’s because they deserve it,” she muttered. Then, before she could stop herself: “Miles.” He hummed in response. Lillian stared at the ceiling, at the hairline crack above the window where the landlord had promised—three times—to repaint. She could feel her own heart speeding up, as if she’d started running without moving. “Do you ever…” She swallowed. The words felt thick. “Dae ye ever think aboot bein’ a faither?”
Miles’s arm came down from his eyes. He turned his head, looking at her properly. For a second, his face was blank with surprise. Then his ears shifted forward, attentive.
Lillian’s stomach clenched. “I mean,” she rushed, “not like—I’m not—I’m just asking. You’ve been… you’ve been in a mood lately. With the warehouse. With the way you talk about Sarah. Like you’re already building plans in your head.”
Miles was quiet. In the pause, Lillian heard all the things she wasn’t saying: the clinic folders in the drawer; the words different species; the way they’d both agreed to stop grieving the thing they couldn’t have. Miles reached out and took her hand, turning it palm-up as if inspecting it for a splinter. He pressed his thumb into the soft pad beneath her fingers, grounding her. “I think about it,” he said finally, each word spoken after careful choice and consideration.
Lillian felt a dryness building in her throat. “Aye?”
He nodded once. “Not in a… fantasy way. I’m not at work daydreaming about nappies. But… yeah. I do.” He glanced toward the kitchen, as if he could see the kettle from here. “I think about what it’d be like to have someone at the table who isn’t just us. Someone who needs you. Someone you can—”
“Fix,” Lillian suggested.
Miles huffed a laugh. “Give over. I know you can’t fix people. But you can build something around them so they’re not falling through holes all the time.”
Lillian traced the line of his forearm with one claw, slow and absent. “And you want that?” she asked.
Miles met her gaze. His eyes were steady, but there was fear there too—fear of saying the wrong thing, fear of wanting something that would hurt. “I want whatever makes sense for us,” he said. “If it never happens, I’m not leaving. I’m not… I’m not missing anything that makes me resent you. I love you, I love being with you, I love our life together as it is right now. But if it does happen—if a kid comes into our life, or if we choose it… then yeah. And I’d want to be good at it.” Lillian blinked hard. Be good at it. That was so Miles. Miles’s hand tightened around hers. “Why are you asking?” he said softly.
Lillian’s mouth opened. She had no safe answer. Instead, she leaned forward and pressed her forehead to his shoulder, hiding her face in his fur. “Because you’d be good,” she said into him, voice muffled. “That’s all.”
Miles kissed the top of her head. His paw rested between her shoulder blades, heavy, steady. “You would too,” he murmured.
Lillian closed her eyes and let herself pretend, just for a minute, that the world was simple.

### 1.3.2
The February rain slid in sheets down the glass doors and made the trolley bay smell like wet metal and old leaves. Miles shook his jacket out in the staff corridor, water flicking from his sleeves, and hung it on his hook with the neatness of someone who couldn’t relax unless objects were where they belonged.
The break room was already loud with kettles and complaints.
“Morning, Miles,” Linda called, tapping ash into a mug on the windowsill even though the sign said NO SMOKING in large red letters. The sign had been there for two years. The ash was still there.
“Morning,” he replied, voice steady.
On the wall-mounted television above the vending machine, a presenter smiled too brightly beside a graphic of a crown.
ROYAL BABY WATCH: DUE DATE SPECULATION.
Someone had turned the volume up. The presenter’s voice filled the room with cheerful importance, as if the womb of a stranger was national infrastructure.
“Another one, then,” Colin said, rolling his eyes. “Like that’s gonna fix the economy.”
Linda snorted. “At least they’re not cutting that benefit.”
A couple of people laughed. Miles didn’t.
He poured himself tea, black, too hot, and leaned against the counter. The words from the screen slid into the back of his mind where all the other headline noise lived. He watched his colleagues talk around it—banter, boredom, the small relief of shared cynicism.
He thought about Sarah’s face in the warehouse doorway.
The way the younger kid’s head had appeared at her elbow like a question mark.
On the television, the presenter cut to a “human interest” segment: a woman in a tiny pram shop, cooing over designer baby clothes, smiling like the world had never been cruel to anyone.
Miles felt his stomach tighten. He finished his tea and went out onto the shop floor.
The store smelled of disinfectant, bread, and damp coats. Fluorescent lights made everything look flatter than it was. The produce section had been re-stacked overnight, apples turned so their shiny sides faced out like they were posing. Miles walked the aisle with a clipboard, checking displays, making adjustments with his hands more than his eyes.
“Morning,” he called to Jamal at the bakery.
“Alright, boss,” Jamal replied, grinning.
“Don’t call me that,” Miles said.
“Can’t help it,” Jamal said. “You’ve got the vibe.”
Miles almost smiled and shook his head. He moved on.
By ten, the first delivery arrived.
A lorry backed up to the loading bay with a complaint of brakes. The driver jumped down, collar up against the weather, and handed Miles the manifest. Miles checked it against the order, pen scratching, mind already organising the day into compartments.
Frozen first.
Chilled.
Dry.
Waste numbers.
Staff rotas.
In the middle of it, his phone buzzed in his pocket.
A text from Lillian.
Home safe. You? x
Miles stared at the screen for a second too long.
He typed back with his thumb.
Yes. All good.
Then, after a pause, because he couldn’t help himself:
Still thinking about those kids.
He put the phone away before it could vibrate with her reply and went back to the manifest.
At half ten, a woman stopped him near the nappies aisle.
“Excuse me,” she said, voice tight. She was human, wearing a glossy raincoat and carrying a carrier bag from a baby boutique. “Do you have any of the organic cotton ones? The ones without… you know.”
“Without what?” Miles asked, eyebrows raised in concern.
She glanced at him, then away, as if meeting his eyes was a misstep. “Chemicals,” she said, as if the word tasted bad.
Miles relaxed his shoulder and pointed her to the endcap display without moving closer than necessary. He watched her walk away, her bag swinging, her phone in her hand. She looked safe in a way that had nothing to do with caution.
A child in a pram squealed, delighted by their own noise.
Miles’s chest tightened again. He thought about the warehouse—about damp concrete and rationing and the way Sarah had made hunger into a system because systems were the only thing that kept you alive.
He went into the stock room and started counting crates. Care, he reminded himself, was mostly numbers.

### 1.3.3
Lillian was in the kitchen, hair pinned up, cardigan sleeves pushed to her elbows, humming under her breath as she moved between kettle and cupboard with that calm domestic competence that could carve a loft from their shared cramped space. She greeted him with a kiss and a hand on his damp shoulder, then turned back to the stove as if she’d always known he’d arrive exactly then.
They ate at the living room table. They talked about the warehouse. Lillian asked questions the way she asked questions of a difficult score: precise, practical, unafraid of the ugly bits. Miles answered as best he could. Later, they sat on the sofa with the television on low, the Royal Baby speculation reduced to background chatter.
When Lillian finally went to bed—early, because she’d been fighting that nausea again and refusing to admit it—Miles stayed up.
He told himself it was for tidying.
It was for thinking.
He washed the dishes, wiped the counter, checked the lock twice. Then he sat at the small table with his laptop open and the sound muted, scrolling through property listings with the methodical patience of someone selecting parts for a machine.
The flat they had was fine. It was home. But fine wasn’t always sustainable, and the warehouse had shoved a new variable into his equations.
He filtered by price. Then by postcode. Then by “garden,” because Lillian liked to have something green she could touch.
The photos blurred together: narrow hallways, magnolia walls, kitchens that pretended to be bigger through clever angles. Miles zoomed in on radiators, on window seals, on the state of the roofline visible in one corner of a badly framed estate agent shot.
He made a list because he always made lists.
A ping from his phone broke his concentration.
Felix.
Mate u still awake
Miles stared at the message.
Felix Murano, bassist, rat, cynical in a way that functioned as armour. The friend who could be relied on to tell you you were being an idiot—then show up with a toolbox anyway.
Miles typed back.
Aye. Can’t sleep.
Then he added, because there was no point pretending.
Need to talk.
Felix’s reply came quickly.
Oi dont tell me u murdered sum1 cos you seem like
Remember that its murder only if they find the body
Othrwise its just missing prson
Miles exhaled through his nose, half laugh, half sigh.
Not that. Just… a situation.
Always is with you innit. u and Lils okay?
Miles’s fingers hovered over the keyboard.
He could hear Lillian in the bedroom, the quiet shift of her body in sleep, the soft exhale. He pictured her face this morning when she’d asked him about fatherhood, the way her voice had gone too careful.
We’re fine
Just thinking about… kids.
Felix didn’t reply immediately.
Miles scrolled another listing. Two bedrooms in Islington, “character features,” which usually meant damp and a landlord who liked the smell of their own paint.
Then Felix’s message came through.
Whose kids
Miles stared at the screen until his eyes began to ache.
Strays. Warehouse near Coldharbour. Teen girl running it. Sarah.
A pause.
Bloody hell
Miles’s shoulders slumped, the weight of that simple exhale landing in his chest like something solid.
Yeah.
u been there
Twice. Dropped food. Didn’t go inside.
He rubbed a hand over his face, palm catching on the short fur along his muzzle.
What d’you want from me?
Miles read the question and realised, with a small jolt, that he did want something. Not advice, exactly. Not permission. He wanted… another adult witness. Someone to confirm that he wasn’t losing his mind.
He typed slowly.
I don’t know yet. But if I end up doing something stupid, I’d rather you heard it from me first.
That’s not how u do stupid
I’m serious.
I know
U cant be stupid
Another pause.
Alright Tell me
Miles looked at the open laptop. At the list of houses. At the rain tracking down the window.
He thought about Lillian saying: especially the boring bits.
He typed.
The boring bits are the worst.
Then he started from the beginning. 
# Act II
## Chapter 4:
### 1.2.1
When Miles got back to the warehouse, the subsequent week, he brought paper.
Food was obvious: he had three more carrier bags of end‑of‑day stock looped over his fingers. But as Miles walked the now‑familiar route in the thin grey light of late afternoon, his mind kept circling back to one detail from the first visit: the way Sarah had scanned the contents of the bags, not just with hunger, but with a sort of practical inventorying. Who would get what. What would last. What could be traded.
He’d seen that look before on Lillian’s face over sheet music, or on his own when he was rearranging a storeroom: the look of someone organising a world. He remembered the cheap sketchpads they kept near the tills for parents to shove at bored children, the covers bent and ugly, but the paper decent. He’d bought two after his shift, throwing in a pack of pencils and a white rubber. He hadn’t quite known why until now, standing once more in front of the warehouse with the weight of them in his backpack.
He called out before he got close. “Alright, it’s Miles again,” he said. “Got more stuff for the foxes.”
The pallet had been pushed further open since last time, a sign of either carelessness or trust. He feared the first, hoped for the latter. Sarah appeared soon after: she must have been closer to the door. She had a smear of something dark, grease, maybe, along her forearm, and her hoodie sleeves were shoved up past her elbows. Her fur lay flatter, though, brushed through. She looked less like someone who might bolt and more like someone who had decided, provisionally, to stay. “You’re early,” she said.
“Boss did the waste sheet himself today,” Miles replied. “Didn’t like the look of his numbers, so I rescued what I could. You want it?”
She hesitated for half a heartbeat, then nodded and shifted fully into the doorway to make room. “Same deal,” she said. “You leave it. You go.”
“Same deal,” he agreed.
He knelt again, setting the bags down where the concrete floor rose to meet the loading bay. Sandwiches, rice salads, tinned soup, a bag of carrots, a sack of potatoes nearly at their date but still sound. He added a smaller carrier with tea and sugar bought on his own money. “Thought you might appreciate hot drinks,” he said.
Her gaze flicked to the tea bags, then to his face. Surprise, cautious and quick, crossed her eyes. “We’ve got a kettle,” she said, as if to make clear they weren’t completely feral. “And a camping stove. We’re not… we’re not stupid.”
“I knew,” he replied. “Takes brains to keep a place like this running.” He heard shifting and whispers behind her. A small face appeared at her elbow, a fox kit with one folded ear and enormous green-grey eyes. Sarah nudged them back without looking, the movement protective and automatic. “Go on, get the others,” she murmured. Then, to Miles: “You’re in the way.”
It was not an insult; it was logistics. He respected that. “Right,” he said. “One more thing and I’ll clear off.”
Her shoulders tensed at the word thing. He took care to bring the backpack around slowly, as if every motion had its own health and safety protocol, and unzipped it to reveal the sketchpads. He could see Sarah’s nostrils flaring, catching the smell of new paper and cheap glue. “These aren’t from the store,” he said quickly. “Bought them myself. Figured you might… I dunno. You’ve got kids, small ones, someone might use them, right that?” He kept his eyes on her face, not on the quick intake of breath from the kit behind her.
She opened and closed her mouth once before replying. “You think we’ve got time for colouring in?” The anger in her voice was bigger than the question. It had edges, as if she’d heard too many people talk about keeping them occupied and something fun while ignoring leaks in the roof.
“I think,” Miles said carefully, “that if you spend all your time keeping everyone alive, your head might explode. And sometimes it helps to put things on paper so they stop bouncing ‘round your skull. Or maybe some kid might need to stay busy for a while.” He shrugged. “But you’d know that better than me.”
Her eyes flicked down to the pads again. She twitched one of her paws, her blunt claws worn out due to her continuous work, towards it, then jerked it back. “We don’t need handouts,” she said.
“They’re not handouts,” he replied. “They’re… supplies. For a different kind of stocktaking.”
That got him a real almost‑smile, there and gone like a shadow when clouds move. “We’ll see,” she said. “You done?”
“I’m done.” He straightened. “Same as before. If you don’t want more, tell me next time.”
“You said that,” she reminded him.
“And you’re still here,” he said and turned back. The urge to look back was even stronger today. He made it to the end of the street before he let himself glance in the bus shelter glass.
They descended on the bags faster this time. A fox, a rat, a tiny rabbit, a pair of mutt pups with their fur in tangles. Sarah barked an order and the kids sorted cans from perishables, long‑life from urgent. The sketchpads lay untouched by the door, two white rectangles on the grey floor.
One of the mutts nudged a pad with a toe. Sarah snapped something at him and he flinched, then slunk inside. She stood where she was a moment longer, arms folded, shoulders high, staring down at the paper.
Miles watched as her resistance and her curiosity fought in the slope of her back. Curiosity won: she bent, snatched up one sketchpad and the pencils, and vanished into the dark with the others. Miles let his shoulders drop.
He had a staff meeting in an hour. He would stand in a well‑lit, clean and dry room while Mr. Bahadur went over shrinkage figures and new promotional endcaps, and he would nod in all the right places. He would think about the warehouse while they talked about seasonal displays, and he would not mention any of it because there was a fine line between helping and violating safeguarding protocols and he did not yet know how to walk it.
But he knew one thing: there was a girl in a warehouse, somewhere behind rusted metal and damp concrete, holding a pencil like a weapon and a lifeline at once.
He could work with that.

### 1.3.1
Lillian stood at the front of the small practice hall, palms flat against the edge of the piano, watching sixteen second‑years teenagers wrestle their way through a piece that was, in theory, well within their ability. In practice, half of them were looking at the clock, three were trying not to laugh, and the trumpeter in the back row had decided dynamics were optional.
“Again from bar thirty‑two,” she said. “And this time, if you play fortissimo where I’ve marked mezzo, I will revoke your biscuit privileges for the rest of term, Jamie.” There was a ripple of muffled laughter. The trumpeter, a lanky human boy with a permanent cowlick, raised his instrument in mock salute.
She lifted her hand. The first notes came in slightly more together, the clumsy unison beginning to find its shape. She felt it in her chest before she really heard it by the way sound made the floorboards hum beneath her held.
And then the room tilted.
Not much. Just a small, slow slide, as if the building had shifted a fraction on its foundations. The black notes on the page swam for a moment, smearing into one another. A wave of heat rolled through her, starting low in her belly and blooming up into her chest. Her mouth filled with saliva. She swallowed hard and kept her face neutral. “Watch your entries,” she called. Her voice sounded normal to her own ears, if a little tight. “Trombone, you’re early there, love.” The trombonist blushed and adjusted. The bar passed. The room settled back into place.
Perhaps she’d not eaten enough. Breakfast had been rushed: a too‑hot mouthful of tea, a slice of toast she’d eaten standing at the counter while the kettle boiled again. She’d been running late, and Miles had been leaving at the same time, tall body ducking to kiss the top of her head as he squeezed past her in the narrow kitchen. “You alright for lunch?” he’d asked, already half in his coat.
“I’ll grab something between classes,” she’d said. “Go, you’ll miss your bus.”
He’d gone, and she hadn’t grabbed anything. First period had started, then admin, then an anxious parent at her office door, and then the second‑years, and now here she was, feeling as if her body had been very gently put down in the wrong place.
The nausea rolled again during the repeat. Not sharp, not like the stomach bugs that occasionally swept through the building every winter. It was a deep, sour wave that seemed to rise from somewhere behind her navel, meet the back of her throat, and hover there, waiting. She tightened her grip on the piano edge, claws pressing into the polished wood. Her belly, layered and soft, felt suddenly heavy, as if it had acquired its own separate gravity. Sweat prickled along the back of her neck under her blouse.
“Right,” she said briskly as they crashed into the final chord. “Five minutes. Get water, stretch, do not tackle each other in the corridor or I’ll know. Then we’ll try that again with fewer murders of the key signature, aye?” They laughed and scattered, chairs scraping, cases snapping. The room emptied of sound with a speed that always surprised her. Teenagers could make a church seem loud and an auditorium seem small. When the last of them had gone, she let herself sink down on the piano stool. It creaked in polite protest under her weight. She closed her eyes and breathed through her mouth, counting.
One, two, three, four. In.
One, two, three, four. Out.
The nausea ebbed slightly, leaving behind a strange metallic taste, as if she’d bitten her own tongue. She opened her eyes. The room was the same: pale walls, scuffed skirting boards, the faint outline of an old notice board that had once hung on the far wall. Nothing had moved, but she felt misaligned inside her own body, like a song played in the wrong key.
“Och, get a grip,” she muttered under her breath. “It’s just low blood sugar.”
She reached for the battered satchel she kept beside the piano and dug until her claws brushed the familiar crinkle of a biscuit wrapper. Two rich tea biscuits, slightly crushed, wrapped in a folded napkin. She’d meant them for a student, a small bribe for a reluctant clarinettist, but needs must.
The first bite was dry and sweet. She chewed slowly, letting the crumbs sit on her tongue for a moment before swallowing. The biscuit landed in her stomach like a stone. The biscuit stayed. “There we are,” she said aloud, trying to convince her own body. “Nothing dramatic.”
The knock at the door was soft, hesitant. She straightened automatically, shoulders squaring, her expression smoothing into the professional warmth her students trusted. “Come in.”
The door opened a crack and Juno Radcliffe slid in sideways, clutching her flute case to her chest like a shield. She looked like Lillian in reverse: where Lillian was short and soft, Juno was tall and broad; where Lillian wore her body fat like a high-couture dress, Juno’s was thick and layered like an armour. “Sorry,” Juno whispered. “I know it’s not time yet. I just… I was early.”
Lillian forced her mouth into a smile. “You’re not in trouble for being early,” she said. “Close the door, love, or we’ll lose what little heat this glorified cupboard has.”
Juno obeyed, the door clicking shut behind her. She hovered by the chairs, eyes flicking to Lillian’s face, then away, then back again. “You look a bit… are you alright?” Juno asked finally. Her accent softened the edges of the words, the faint Irish lilt making alright sound like a question about the whole world.
Lillian considered lying. It would have been easy to say she was fine, just tired. But she’d drilled into her students that bodies, hands, breathing, posture mattered, and that ignoring pain was a sure way to end up injured. “Bit off,” she admitted. “Stomach’s having opinions. Nothing serious. I’ll live.”
“Should I get someone?” Juno’s brow furrowed.
“No.” Lillian shook her head, gently, in case the room thought about following. “No need. Sit yourself down, since you’re here. How’s your embouchure today?”
Juno smiled, tension easing a fraction. “Better. I think. I’ve been doing the exercises you showed me.”
“Good girl,” Lillian said. “We’ll check it in a minute. Talk me through what you’ve practised. Take my mind off my ill‑advised choice to skip lunch.”
Juno hesitated, then began to speak, detailing scales and arpeggios and a new piece she’d found online. As she talked, Lillian let the words wash over her, a thread of sound she could follow out of her own body for a moment. The nausea settled again, not gone, but demoted to background hum. She listened with half an ear, nodding in encouragement, asking the occasional question. The rest of her attention was on the strange, off‑centre pull in her abdomen, the way her breasts felt suddenly heavier against the fabric of her bra, the faint ache low in her back.
It felt, absurdly, like the week before her period, except her period had already come and gone. Right on time, as always. But bodies are not clocks, she told herself. Stress did odd things. She’d been running more, trying to keep up with the demands of the academy, the band, the council meetings Miles had started dragging her to about Strays and cuts to youth services. She’d been sleeping badly. It was no wonder her stomach was staging a protest.
Still.
When Juno lifted her flute to play, Lillian watched the set of the girl’s shoulders, the rounding of her large belly as she took breath, and the thought slid in, quiet and unwanted.
What if.
She watched Juno’s fingers move, the silver keys flashing. Her own paws felt heavy in her lap, claws dull. The nausea curled in on itself like a question mark.
“That’s better,” she noted when Juno finished, her voice only slightly rough. “Your tone’s much more even through the register. We’ll work the middle section next week.”
Juno beamed. Praise sat on her shoulders like borrowed sunlight. “Thanks, Miss,” she said. “I’ll… I’ll go. Let you have your break.”
When the door closed behind her, the room was suddenly too quiet. The clock on the wall ticked, each second landing somewhere between Lillian’s ribs.
She pressed the heel of her paw lightly against her belly. The flesh yielded, soft, familiar. For a moment she imagined something else beneath: not just organs and muscle and fat, but the idea of a shape, small and stubborn. “Don’t be ridiculous,” she told herself, aloud this time. Her voice echoed faintly in the empty room. “You cannae even…”
The sentence trailed off. They’d always assumed it was impossible. Different species, different genetics, the virus playing wildcards with reproductive odds. Lillian had never once let herself daydream beyond what the doctors had told her: that if they wanted children, they’d have to look outward, not inward. Adoption, fostering, or the kids they taught. Different species couldn’t breed anything viable.
She’d accepted it the way she accepted London weather and Royal Academy rejection letters. Some doors weren’t built for her. She found other ones.
The bell rang, shrill and insistent, dragging her back to the timetable. She rose carefully from the stool, waiting to see if the room would spin. It didn’t. “Right,” she said, smoothing her blouse over her belly. “Next lot.”
She walked to the door, her steps measured. As she opened it, the corridor noise rushed in: chatter, footsteps, the clatter of instrument cases. Life, carrying on at its usual volume. She smiled at the students waiting outside, ushered them in, and kept the question tucked behind her teeth like a seed under the tongue.
Later, she told herself. When the day’s done. When you’re home.

 
# Act II
## Chapter 4: The Impossible Test

### 2.4.1
Juno arrived early again. Not by five minutes, not by the polite little margin that let you pretend it was coincidence, but by a full half hour, long enough to sit outside the practice hall door with her flute case hugged to her chest and to listen to the building settle into itself, to the distant scrape of chairs in other rooms.
Lillian found her in the corridor, shoulders rounded as if she were trying to become a shape no one could bump into. “You ken you’re not being graded on punctuality,” Lillian said.
Juno startled, then straightened with an effort that made her look even bigger. She was a tall badger, almost as tall as Miles was, the top of her head just a palm beneath the lintel, and her body carried weight the way a ship carried ballast: purposeful, heavy, hard to move. She was wearing an hoodie that should have been oversized but looked like it was wrapping her torso in a cotton burrito, sleeves pulled over her paws, and the hood sat up like a protective ridge around her head.
“Sorry,” Juno whispered.
“For what?” Lillian asked. She unlocked the practice room door and nudged it open with her hip.
“For… taking up space,” Juno said, her voice a whisper so subtle that Lillian almost missed it.
Lillian paused. The words were familiar. “Och, love,” she said, with a half-laughter, “if you want to be a musician, you’ll have to do a lot more space-taking than this. Come in.”
Inside, the practice hall smelled of brass polish and old carpet and the faint bleach tang of the cleaners. Lillian crossed to the piano and set her satchel down. The nausea that had been nagging at her since yesterday was present again today as a low, sour line under everything, a wrong note held too long.
Juno placed her case on a chair with great care, as if she were laying down something alive.
“What did you practise?” Lillian asked her, mostly to keep her own attention anchored.
Juno’s shoulders tensed under her black armor. “The long tones. The… breathing. And scales.”
“Good,” Lillian said. “And did you eat breakfast?” Juno blinked, then shook her head. “Right. Of course you didn’t.” Lillian reached into her satchel and pulled out the emergency snacks she kept for students who arrived pale and shaking from too much anxiety and too little food. She offered a wrapped cereal bar. Juno stared at it as if it were a trick. “It’s not poison,” Lillian said. “It’s a biscuit with a very nice wrapping. Take it. Sugar can play nasty tricks on your body.” Juno took it, fingers trembling slightly as she unwrapped it, and ate it in quick but small morsels. “Now,” Lillian said, sitting at the piano. “Show me what you can do.”
Juno opened her case. The flute inside was not new, but it was clean—polished until it caught the overhead light in small, bright flashes. She assembled it with a competence that didn’t match her shyness, her long fingers sure on the joints, like a soldier putting together pieces of a rifle.
When she lifted it to her mouth, something in her posture changed. Not confidence, exactly, but a settling: her shoulders lowered. her chest expanded. The breath she took was quiet and deep, controlled in a way that belonged to someone who knew how to make air into sound.
The first note was a long, steady line that cut through the room like a thread of silver.
Lillian felt her own body respond the way it always did to good music: a soft loosening behind the ribs, the sense of something aligning. “That’s it,” Lillian murmured. “Aye. Hold it. Don’t chase it. Let it sit.”
Juno held the note, then let it bloom into a small phrase. The tone was remarkably even through the register. “You’ve got an ear like a tuning fork,” Lillian said when Juno stopped for breath.
Juno’s face flushed under her fur. “It’s just… practise.”
“It’s talent,” Lillian corrected. “Practise is what you do with talent so it doesn’t go to waste.”
They worked through exercises: long tones, articulation, then a passage that required nimble shifts between notes that would have turned most beginners into a breathless mess. Juno made a mistake once—an ugly squeak that made her flinch—and she froze, as if expecting punishment.
Lillian didn’t comment. She simply played the passage on the piano, softly, giving her a base to re-enter.
Juno swallowed and tried again. That time, it rang clean. “Better,” Lillian said.
Juno exhaled a shaky laugh. “You always say that.”
“Because it’s always true,” Lillian replied.
When they paused, Juno wiped condensation from the mouthpiece with the edge of her sleeve. Lillian gently took her paw and analysed it. “I’ve noticed something… off. And I see what might be wrong.”
“I knew that—”
“Shush, Juno. It’s not about you. It’s the flute.” She spread Juno’s fingers across the flute. “See? We both had wide paws, we are badgers, aye? But your finger are larger than mine, yet you’re using a flute that would fit for a human kid.”
“It’s all I could find at home,” Juno replied, almost shaking in terror.
“And it’s perfectly okay,” Lillian objected, a paw on her large forearm. “You’ve adapted to something not fitting, and you did a very good job at it, too. Imagine what you’d do with an instrument fit for your fingers.”
Juno's eyes went distant for a moment, as if she were trying to picture it and couldn't quite make the image hold. Her eyes flicked up, hesitant. “I’ve been listening to… to things I… I would like to play,” she said.
“Oh aye?”
“I found this Irish group,” Juno said, and her voice warmed on the words, the Cork in her accent becoming more pronounced. “The Corrs. My mum used to play them when she was in a good mood. There’s this song—” She faltered, realising she’d offered a piece of herself to someone else and deciding it was too much.
Lillian kept her expression easy. “Play me a bit.”
Juno hesitated, then lifted the flute again and played a line that wasn’t classical at all—something with a folk lilt, the melody shaped differently, less obedient to the grid of the staff. And it was beautiful. It was like listening to the kind of song a waterfall would sing if it had a mouth.
Lillian felt the nausea roll up her throat again, and she had to swallow hard.
“Miss?” Juno’s brow furrowed. “You alright?”
“I’m fine,” Lillian lied, then, after a beat, decided not to. “Bit off. Stomach’s still being a drama queen.”
Juno lowered the flute. “Should you… should you go home?”
“No,” Lillian said, too quickly. The room tilted slightly at the edges of her vision, then steadied. She pressed her fingertips into the piano bench, grounding herself in the wood. “I can teach through nausea. I’ve taught through worse. I just need—”
She stopped.
Because the nausea didn’t feel like illness.
It felt like a message.
After Juno left—praised, given a clear plan for the week, sent away with a second cereal bar “for later”—Lillian sat alone in the empty room for a long time.
She watched dust drift in the thin winter light.
She listened to the radiator click.
She put one paw, absently, on her stomach, the softness familiar beneath her palm.
Her period had already come and gone.
Right on time.
On the way home, she stopped at the chemist. She told herself it was for paracetamol.
She didn’t buy paracetamol.

### 2.4.1
Sarah was on the roof when she heard the change in the noise below.
Up there, the city sounded different. The warehouse roof was a patchwork of corrugated sheets, warped and rusted, patched with tar in places where the rain had found a way through. She was scribbling sketches with a pencil on the homework papers Faroukh prepared for her. From that height, she could hear the buses on the main road, the occasional siren, the distant wail of a fox. The murmur of voices, the clatter of cups, the thud of footsteps that were the warehouse’s heartbeat had become a familiar undertone.
But then, that heartbeat stuttered.
Voices rose, sharper. A chair scraped hard across concrete. Someone shouted something she couldn’t make out. Then silence fell in a way silence shouldn’t in a building full of children. Sarah’s whole body went still. The pencil froze in her paw, the unfinished line hanging just short of the roofline she’d been tracing.
“Stay,” she told Rafi, the fox kit, quietly.
He blinked up at her from his nest of blankets by the hatch. She brought him up here sometimes when the warehouse felt too full. Tonight he’d fallen asleep almost as soon as she’d spread the sleeping bag. His fur was ruffled, a smear of beans still visible near his mouth.
“What’s wrong?” he whispered, instantly more awake.
“Nothing yet.” She set the sketchpad down, closed it, and slid it under the edge of the nearest metal sheet, out of sight. “If anyone comes up, you do what we practised. Quiet as a mouse, yeah?”
He nodded, eyes huge.
She crossed to the hatch, dropped through it, and slid down the ladder in three swift movements, the metal cold under her paws. The warehouse air closed around her, thicker and denser than the air above, full of heat and the tang of too many bodies.
At first, all she saw were faces turned towards the loading bay. The littlest ones huddled near the back, Vee clinging to Mia’s sleeve, the twin mutts pressed shoulder‑to‑shoulder. The older teens stood a loose line in front of them, casual on the surface, but she could see the tension in their shoulders, the way their ears angled.
Faroukh stood slightly ahead of the others, a low growl building up through his throat.
In the doorway, framed by the sagging pallet that served as their gate, were three figures Sarah knew too well. Connor, a long‑limbed thin greyhound, leaning against the doorframe as if he owned it. His fur was patchy in places, one eye narrowed permanently from an old injury. He smelled of stale smoke, the scent cutting through the warehouse’s own odours like an infection.  Beside him, Jo and Mikey, a tabby cat with too‑bright eyes and a deer with both antlers broken at the base. They were all in their twenties, but the lines around their mouths had settled into something older and meaner. “There she is,” Connor drawled as she approached. “The little princess herself.”
Sarah stopped a safe distance away, just out of arm’s reach, hands loose at her sides. Her tail hung neutral behind her; not tucked, not flared. Her heart thudded against her ribs, but she kept her breathing slow, even. It took her a great conscious effort not to let her heckles rise up. “We’re closed,” she said. “You know that.”
Connor’s grin showed too many teeth. “Didn’t realise you were running a shop now. I thought this was public property.”
“It’s ours,” one of the twins muttered behind her. Nate, by the pitch. Sarah flicked her ear and he subsided.
“What do you want?” she asked.
Connor’s gaze drifted past her, taking in the crates by the wall where they kept the food. He’d seen the extra loaves, the stacked tins, the bag of potatoes. “Heard you’ve had a windfall,” he said. “Whole lot of nice, clean food dropping out of the sky. Thought that was interesting.”
“We’ve always found food,” Sarah said. “We just got better at it.”
“Uh‑huh.” Connor tapped his claw against the doorframe, a little rhythmic tic that had made younger kids jump before. “Funny, though. Same week people at the grocery store start talking about ‘losses’ going up, they also mention some big Shepherd guy carting bags off somewhere. And would you look at that.” He gestured lazily with his chin. “Kids with full bellies. Miracle of the age.”
Murmurs ran through the older kids behind her. Faroukh shifted his weight.
“You’ve been listening at doors again,” Sarah said. “Dangerous habit.”
“Occupational hazard.” Connor shrugged. “Point is, you’ve got more than you used to. And we’ve got less.”
There it was. The core.
Sarah felt the familiar, miserable flicker of guilt. They weren’t the only Strays in that part of London. Connor’s crowd had staked out spots nearer the main road, closer to the shops and the alleyways where tourists occasionally dropped coins. They’d been older, bigger, when she’d first run with them as a terrified six‑year‑old. They’d taught her how to spot security cameras, how to tell when a drunk man’s friendly hand was about to turn, how to sleep in doorways without getting kicked. They’d also taught her that being smaller meant being fair game. “We’re feeding kids,” she said. “You know that.”
“We’re feeding kids too,” Connor shot back. “You remember it, right, princess? You were with us, in the beginning. Before finding this. Difference is, yours are cute.” His eyes flicked to Vee, whose rabbit ears flattened. “Big Shepherd looks at you and sees little lost lambs. Looks at us and sees trouble. So he brings you the goods and we get to hear about it from random humans.”
Sarah swallowed the retort that rose. He wasn’t wrong about perception. Miles’s kindness wasn’t blind; it was aimed. She’d seen his eyes soften when he clocked the younger ones. She doubted he’d have the same patience for Connor’s lot, all sharp bones and edges. “What are you asking for?” she said. “Be clear.”
“Half,” Connor said immediately. “Anything comes in from now on, you cut it in half. We take our share. Call it tax for using our patch.”
“It’s not your patch,” Faroukh muttered.
Connor’s gaze slid to him. “Is that so, pup?”
Faroukh’s hackles rose. Sarah stepped half a pace to the side, blocking the line between them without making a show of it. “You didn’t want this place when I found it, six years ago,” she said, keeping her tone level. “You said it was too far from the road. Too cold. Too drafty. You said only an idiot would try and live here long‑term.”
“Yeah, well.” Connor’s grin tightened. “Idiots seem to be doing alright out here now.”
Sarah let the insult slide past. “We’ve got twenty mouths to feed,” she said. “Half of what we get isn’t enough. We can stretch it because the kids help. They sort, cook, clean. They’re not dead weight.”
“Neither are ours,” Jo put in, voice sharp. “We’ve got little ones too, you know. Not that you care, obviously.”
Sarah pressed her tongue briefly against the back of her teeth to stop the immediate, hot reply—about whose bright idea it had been to drag toddlers through the tube stations at midnight, whose temper had made a seven‑year‑old pee herself in fear during a harsh winter. That road led nowhere useful. “How many?” she asked instead. “Little ones.”
“Five,” Jo said. “Under ten.”
Sarah pictured them: faces she’d glimpsed on the periphery, kids who hovered behind older, more dangerous bodies, eyes already learning to dart. Guilt and anger knotted in her chest. It wasn’t fair. None of it was fair. But fairness didn’t feed anyone. She did the math. “A quarter,” she decided. “Of what he brings. You get first pick on stuff we can’t store. Milk, fresh meat. Things that’ll go off quick. We keep the dry goods.”
Connor laughed, a sharp bark. “You bargaining with me now, princess?”
“I’m making sure my lot don’t starve,” she said. “And yours don’t either. If we give you half, we’ll both run out faster. He’ll notice something’s off. He’ll stop. Then none of us get anything.”
He tilted his head, considering. His good eye narrowed; the damaged one stayed a milky slit.
“Why would he stop?” Mikey asked, bewildered. “He likes playing hero.”
Sarah seized the opening. “Because people like him like to feel in control,” she said. “If he thinks we’re taking advantage, he’ll stop bringing the bags or he’ll tell someone. You know how that goes.”
The corners of Connor’s lips twitched. He knew. “You trust him that much?” he asked.
“I don’t trust anybody that much,” she said. “That’s why I’m not letting you blow this for us.”
Jo stepped forward, shoulders tense. “You think you’re better than us now, just ’cause some big dog with a name badge looks at you with puppy eyes?”
“No,” Sarah uttered. “I think I’ve got fingers pointing at me every time someone’s hungry. I think if I make the wrong call, I’ve got to look at them while they go to sleep with cramps. That’s all.” She turned slightly, enough that she could see the kids over her shoulder. Rafi’s face was pinched. Vee chewed her thumb, eyes huge. “You hurt any of them,” she said quietly, “and I don’t care how big your pack is, I will make sure you don’t sleep easy again.”
Connor’s grin flickered. For a heartbeat he looked almost her age, not some mythic terror of the underpasses. Then he laughed again, shaking his head. “Listen to you,” he said. “Little queen of the warehouse.”
He stepped into the room, just one pace over the threshold. The younger kids shrank back; Faroukh bristled. Sarah held her ground. Her body screamed at her to move, to do something, but she knew that giving ground now would set a pattern she couldn’t break. “Quarter,” she repeated. “You take a quarter of what he brings. No coming in when the kids are sleeping. You send one person at a time. No shouting, no fights. You don’t touch anyone. We’ll put it in the rota.”
Connor barked another laugh. “The rota.” He looked at Jo and Mikey. “She’s got a rota.”
“At least someone does,” Faroukh muttered, too low for anyone but Sarah to hear.
Connor’s laughter faded into a thoughtful noise. He scratched at his chin, then flicked his gaze back to her. “Alright,” he said finally. “Quarter. For now. But if that Shepherd starts bringing more, we’ll talk again.”
“We’ll talk,” she agreed. “Not fight. Talk.”
“We’ll see,” he said.
He took a step back. The tension eased a fraction. Jo and Mikey relaxed their shoulders, just a little. Behind Sarah, she could feel the collective exhale of the kids, the way they shifted as if someone had loosened a strap around their chests.
Connor tapped the doorframe one more time. “You’re playing a dangerous game, princess,” he said. “Getting used to someone like that showing up.”
“I don’t get used to anything,” she said. “I use it while it lasts.”
He smiled, briefly, as if she’d passed some test he’d set without telling her. Then he turned and slipped out into the dusk, his two shadows following. The pallet scraped as he shoved it back into place from outside. For a long moment, no one moved.
Then Vee let out a small, hiccupping sob. The sound broke the spell.
“It’s alright,” Sarah said, turning back to them. Her voice came out steadier than she felt. “We’re alright.” She wanted to sit down. Her knees felt weirdly hollow. Instead she clapped her paws together once, hard. “Okay,” she said. “Show’s over. Faroukh, write Connor’s name on the rota for collection days. Tuesdays and Fridays, after dusk. Nobody goes near the door then except whoever I say. Got it?”
There were nods, half‑murmured agreements.
Rafi edged closer. “He’s not allowed to come in at night, right?” he asked, voice very small in the silence of the huge warehouse around them.
