“He’s not allowed to do anything we don’t let him,” Sarah said. “And we don’t sleep all at once, remember?”
Rafi nodded. They’d worked that out ages ago: staggered naps, someone always half awake. It wasn’t foolproof, but it made her feel less as if she were balancing them all on the edge of a knife.
“You sure about this?” Faroukh asked later, when the younger ones had drifted back to their games and the older group had dispersed into smaller knots. He leaned against one of the pillars, arms folded, eyes fixed on the door. “Cutting them in.”
“We can’t pretend they don’t exist,” she said. “And we can’t feed them all. This is the least bad option.”
“Least bad for now,” he said.
“For now’s all we ever get,” she replied. “We’ll deal with later when it gets here.”
He grunted, but he didn’t push.
When the few lamps they’d rigged up on stolen extension cords had been turned down and the warehouse had settled into its night‑time murmur, Sarah climbed back up to the roof.
Rafi had fallen asleep properly this time, curled in on himself, his chest rising and falling in a quick, steady rhythm. She tucked the blanket more firmly around his shoulders and then retrieved the sketchpad from under the corrugated overlap. She flipped it open to a fresh page. The pencil felt heavier than earlier. Without quite deciding to, she drew Connor first: the tilt of his head, the way his eyes never seemed to settle. Then she drew Jo and Mikey, hovering behind him. Then she drew the loading bay, the pallet, the line where light from outside met shadow inside. Last, she drew herself, small and straight‑backed in the centre, shoulders squared, tail neutral.
She shaded carefully, making the shadows dense around her sketch‑self, leaving a thin halo of lighter paper where she stood. It wasn’t accurate. No one had ever stood in any sort of light down there. But it made the composition work, and it made something inside her unknot, just a little.
When she was done, she wrote, in tiny letters in the corner, a single word she didn’t intend anyone else to see.
Mine.
Then she closed the pad, slid it back under the corrugated edge, and lay down beside Rafi, her back against the cold metal, her eyes on the slice of sky she could see between the roofs.
Somewhere out there, she imagined, the Shepherd was doing inventory or arguing with a manager or whatever big dogs did when they weren’t hauling bags to broken warehouses.
Down there, in the in‑between, Sarah kept watch.

## Chapter 5: Tenderness

### 2.5.1
28th of February, Thursday. Asim woke to the sound of Toby swearing at a kettle.
Not a loud swear, because Toby never seemed able to be consistently angry and loud about anything, but a series of soft, escalating mutters as the appliance refused to behave. The flat was warm in that uneven London way: one radiator blasting as if trying to compensate for the rest, the window edges cold enough to make the air taste faintly of damp.
Asim lay on his back for a moment, listening: to the kettle’s disgruntled click, to the faint hum of Toby’s computer in the other room to the soft, familiar breathing of home.
He rolled onto his side and found Toby in the kitchen wearing one of Asim’s shirts and pants. Toby was a Riesenschnauzer—solid, broad, grey, fluffy, with eyebrows that made his expressions look perpetually earnest. Even in the morning, hair sticking up in chaotic tufts, he had a steadiness to him that made the room feel calmer by association.
Toby was also holding the kettle plug in his paw at a very specific angle, as if coaxing it into cooperation.
“It’s sulking,” Toby announced.
“It’s a kettle,” Asim said, voice rough with sleep.
“It has vibes.” Toby glanced over, eyes bright. “Morning, habibi,” he added, his tail wagging. 
“Don’t call me that when you’re wearing those pants,” Asim said. Then he smiled despite himself and crossed the small space to kiss Toby’s cheek. Toby smelled of citrus, pine resin and whatever washing-up liquid he’d bought because it had a nice label.
Asim started the ritual without thinking: tea bags, mugs, a spoon for each. He didn’t need the mugs labelled, but Toby had bought them anyway: one with a little graphic design joke on it, one plain black, a chipped one that Toby insisted had “character.”
“Your mum texted again,” Toby said casually.
Asim’s fingers paused on the sugar jar. There were certain sentences that made the air change shape. “What did she say?” he asked, trying for neutral.
Toby leaned against the counter, watching him. “Just… Tea on a Sunday, if you can. It’s progress,” he added gently.
Asim laughed once, sharp and disbelieving. “It’s a text. It’s not repentance.”
Toby’s eyebrows did that thing they did—lifting in a way that made him look both fond and quietly stubborn. “It’s an invitation.”
“It’s a trap,” Asim said.
“Or it’s your mum trying,” Toby countered. “Trying badly, but trying.”
Asim filled the kettle from the tap, water cold and hard against his paw pads. He tried to let the sound of it running drown out the swirl in his chest.
Birmingham.
His mother’s kitchen.
The smell of cardamom and fried onions.
His sister’s laugh—sharp and bright.
His brother’s silence.
His father’s eyes, the way they’d looked through him after the first fight, as if he’d become a shame-shaped hole in the room.
“You don’t have to go,” Toby said softly, reading his face in the way he always did.
“I know,” Asim replied. Then, because truth was not a single statement: “But if I don’t go, I’m the one who stays estranged. I’m the one who has to carry it.”
Toby nodded, mouth tightening with empathy. “I just… I think it could be good. For you.”
“Good,” Asim echoed, sarcasm trying to climb into his voice and failing. “Yeah. Nothing like a nice cup of tea with the woman who spent years praying my gay would fall off.”
Toby winced. “Asim.”
“I’m joking,” Asim lied.
Toby reached out and took Asim’s hand. His paw was warm, rougher at the pads from the way he fiddled with design tools and keyboards all day. “You’re allowed to be scared,” he said.
Asim’s throat tightened. “I’m not scared.”
Toby’s gaze never left Asim’s eyes. “You are. Your ears speak for your mouth, and they’re screaming right now. But it’s ok.”
Asim breathed out. Sometimes Toby’s kindness was infuriating because it left nowhere to hide. “I’m not going back to be fixed,” Asim said, voice lower. “I’m not going back to apologise for existing.”
“Then don’t,” Toby said. “Go back as yourself. The version of you who prays and sings.”
The kettle finally clicked on.
Asim squeezed Toby’s paw once, then let go. He moved on autopilot: mugs, tea, milk for Toby, none for him. 
“A Sunday,” Asim said, tasting the word.
Toby’s ears perked. “A Sunday. Any Sunday.”
Asim took his mug and sat at the small table. He stared into the tea as if it might arrange itself into an answer.
“Do you want to go to the mosque later?” Toby asked, casual but careful.
Asim looked up. There was love in Toby’s face, and respect, and no demand for explanation. “Aye,” Asim said. “Yeah. I do.”

### 2.5.2
1st of March.
Miles had never liked walking Lillian past places that smelled like that. “You sure about this?” he asked as they turned off the main road. The drizzle became a fine mist that beaded on their fur and made his shirt cling under his jacket. He shifted the tote bag on his shoulder; the paper inside thudded softly.
“You’ve asked me that three times,” Lillian said. “If you ask again, I’ll assume you dinnae want my company and go home, aye?” Her tone was light, but he didn’t miss the way she adjusted her scarf, tucking it more firmly around her throat. She walked a half‑step ahead of him, hips swaying with the kind of unconscious authority she carried into classrooms and onto stages. The damp didn’t flatter her; droplets clung to the fur at her cheeks and along her jaw, catching in her glasses.
“Just…” he searched for the word, tail flicking once. “It’s not pretty down here.”
“Miles,” she said. “I’ve seen derelict. I’ll cope.”
He gave up arguing. The bags in his hands were heavier than last time: bread, tins, carrots, a tray of yoghurts he’d smuggled out on the flimsy pretext of customer damage. Lillian’s tote held sketchpads, cheap watercolours, a handful of brushes wrapped carefully in an old tea towel, detergents, combs. “They might not want those,” he warned, nodding at the tote.
“They might not,” she agreed. “But I would’ve killed for someone to bring me paper when I was stuck in the flat after graduation, and for a comb to fix my fur when I had to molt. You said that Sarah was a dog, right? She’d need it, then. And if they dinnae, we’ll find someone else who does. No harm done.”
He thought of Sarah’s face when she’d taken the first pad, the way her paw had hovered, then snatched. He suspected harm had already been done, just not by them.
As the warehouse came into view, he felt Lillian’s pace slow a fraction beside him. The building loomed out of the damp: blank brick, steel doors, the crooked loading bay. He watched her eyes take it in, the way her pupils narrowed, the way her nostrils flared at the smell. “This is where they live?” she asked.
In the evening darkness, the warehouse was the embodiment of the concept of “looming.” A form more than a shape. A cage that was a house for kids who weren’t meant to be anybody’s kids.
“Best I can tell,” he said. “For now.”
She hummed under her breath, a sound he recognised as the badger equivalent of a swear: a low, displeased noise that came from her chest. “Right,” she said. “Let’s not stand about gawping.”
He called out as they approached the pallet. “Alright, it’s Miles again. I’ve brought someone with me.”
There was a flurry of movement inside. The pallet shifted, then halted. A narrow gap opened. Sarah’s face appeared, eyes wary, moving quickly from his muzzle to Lillian’s, nose flaring to smell her scent from a distance.
If she’d looked like a stray dog when Miles first saw her, that day she looked more like a sentry. Her blonde hair was scraped back into a stubby ponytail; the hoodie she wore had been washed recently, the logo still cracked but less grey with dirt. Her eyes were rimmed in red, as if she hadn’t slept properly in a while. “Who’s she?” Sarah demanded.
“This is Lillian,” Miles said. “She’s my mate. She works with kids. And adults. And music.” He ducked his head towards Lillian. “Lils, this is Sarah.”
“We dinnae know each other,” Lillian said smoothly, her Scots showing a little with nerves. “You dinnae have to tell me anything you don’t want to, love.”
Sarah’s gaze flicked over Lillian’s body, taking in the long coat straining slightly over broad shoulders, the curve of her belly. Her eyes caught on the tote bag. “You brought more food?” she asked Miles.
“Food, aye,” he said. “And… supplies.” He nodded at Lillian’s bag.
“Not charity,” Lillian added. “Just things. If you want them. If you don’t, I’ll take them away.”
Sarah hesitated, then shifted the pallet back enough that they could see inside.
The warehouse smelled stronger today: sweat, wet clothes, the metallic tang of dried blood from a skinned knee, the faint burnt edge of toast. To their right, the metallic staircase leading to the mezzanine and the now unused offices, with a second ramp leading to a door for the roof; pillars larger than Lillian’s hips holding the leaking rooftop together, small mattresses and folded covers leaning against them; a row of windows running across the upmost section of the walls, broken ones repaired with cardboard and pallets. The makeshift kitchen on their left was tidier than Miles had seen it last: mugs stacked, pots washed. A whiteboard propped against some pallets bore equations in shaky marker. Forty‑odd pairs of eyes stared at them from various corners. Some small, wide; some older, narrowed and assessing. Faroukh lounged against a pillar, arms folded, attempting to look indifferent and badly failing.
“We’ll leave the bags here,” Miles said, setting his load just inside the threshold. “Same as before.”
Lillian set her tote down beside them. “Hullo,” she said. “You lot get visitors often?”
Silence. A small rabbit girl half‑hid behind an older weasel’s arm. “We don’t like visitors,” Sarah said. “Visitors tell other people where we are.”
“Fair,” Lillian replied. “I’m not here to take roll or anything. Miles has been fretting about you.” She shot him a quick look that held affection and mild exasperation. “I thought I’d come see whether he was exaggerating.”
“I don’t exaggerate,” he muttered.
“He does,” she told Sarah, conspiratorial. “He’ll tell you he only needs five minutes to fix something and three hours later he’s still got the screwdriver in his hand.” A couple of the younger ones giggled at that image. Miles rolled his eyes but didn’t contradict her. If letting her take the piss got a laugh out of them, he’d happily be the punchline.
Sarah’s mouth twitched, not quite a smile. “What’s in the other bag?” she asked, nudging Lillian’s tote with a toe.
“Paper,” Lillian said. “Pencils. Some paint. Brushes, but they’re old. I washed them.” She opened the tote, letting Sarah see the sketchpads, the cheap watercolour sets, the jars she’d brought for water. “We had some lying about. Figured they’d do more good here than sitting in my cupboard. Plus there are—”
“We’re not at school,” Faroukh said. “We don’t need homework.”
“Och, if I were giving you homework, you’d ken it,” Lillian said. “This is for mucking about. Drawing. Painting. Whatever you fancy. Or not. Maybe you can use the paper for something else. Origami bombs. Shopping lists. Dramatic ransom notes. Homework.”
The corner of Sarah’s mouth curved, just a fraction. “We’ve got other things to do,” she said, trying on a scowl that didn’t quite fit. “We don’t have time for colouring.”
“Aye,” Lillian said. “I’ve heard that before.” She reached into the tote and took out one of the sketchpads. The cheap cardboard cover bowed slightly in her paw. “Here.”
She didn’t push it towards Sarah; she set it on the cracked concrete just over the threshold and slid it in with the back of her claws, as if offering food to a nervous animal. Her movements were slow, transparent. “If you don’t want it,” she said, “leave it by the door and I’ll take it back.”
Sarah looked down at the pad as if it were an unexploded device. “We’re not a project,” she said quietly, looking back up. “For you to feel good about.”
Lillian’s face softened. Miles felt something in his chest loosen at the sight; this was the version of her he saw with anxious students, with the kids who wandered into her office pretending they had questions about scales when really they had questions about whether they wanted to be alive. “I know,” she said. “You’re people. That’s enough.”
Sarah’s ears flicked, as if she were testing them for traps. After a moment, she said, “You’re… different.”
“That’s one way of putting it,” Lillian replied dryly.
Behind Sarah, one of the smaller kids, a hamster, tugged at her sleeve. “Is that your mum?” the girl whispered, not very quietly.
Heat flashed up the back of Sarah’s neck. “No,” she snapped. “She’s not—”
Lillian didn’t flinch. “I’m someone’s daughter,” she said. “And someone’s teacher. And someone’s… nuisance.” She shot Miles a look. “But I’m not anybody’s mum yet.” Yet, Miles thought, and the word lodged somewhere tender. He shoved the thought aside. Wrong moment. “Anyway,” Lillian went on. “I’m with a bunch of people who look like they’ve got as much to say as any of my students. Seems a shame if none of that ever ends up on paper. That’s all.”
Sarah’s gaze drifted to the pad again. Her paw twitched. “We’ve got one,” she muttered. “Already.”
“Then you know how it works,” Lillian said. “You can always do with more.”
Miles cleared his throat. “We’ll leave you to it,” he said. “No hovering. You know where I work if you need anything.” 
“We don’t need anything,” Sarah said automatically.
“Course you don’t,” he said. “But if you want anything, that’s different.”
Faroukh snorted. “Sounds like the same thing.”
“Trust me,” Lillian said. “It isn’t.”
She pushed herself to her feet with a small huff. Miles picked up the now‑empty carrier bags and stepped back. “We’ll be around,” he said. “Not every day. But we’re not just dropping in for one show.”
Sarah studied him for a long moment. Her eyes looked older than they had any right to be. “We’ll see,” she said finally. Not really a permission, yet not a slammed door.

### 2.5.3
As they walked away, Miles kept his pace even until they turned the corner. Only then did he let out the breath he’d been holding in a long exhale. He looked at her, but she wasn’t returning the stare: her eyes were fixed somewhere ahead of her, large tears rolling down her cheeks. “Oi, Lils,” he said, his paw immediately raised to wipe her fur.
She dismissed it with an angry movement. “It’s bloody kids,” she hissed. “Hou mony o’ thaim?”
“I counted twenty,” he replied. “Plus Sarah and the other boy, name starts with an F.”
“Two were cross-breeds. Dogs.” She looked at him, straight into his amber eyes. The fury he could see behind her round glasses was something to be afraid of. “It means baith parents were anthros, Miles. Like us. They knew how it feels, how could they—”
“We can’t tell, Lils.” He slowly dragged her into a hug; she didn’t deny his warmth. “We can’t tell. We can only do something for them.”
“Do you ever think at… how many are out there, like them? Alone. Hopeless. Forgotten. Angry.”
“I do, yes,” he replied. “And I hope I can do something more. But I also know that… I’m not able to save all of them. I’m not a saviour. We’re not superheroes, Lils. But we can do a lot for those in that warehouse, though. I… I don’t know what, yet, but I know we will.”
They walked in silence for a while, holding paws. A bus roared past; a group of teenagers on the corner laughed too loudly. “D’you feel better now?” he asked eventually.
“No,” she said. “But I will be. I dinnae ken how they do it. How she does it.”
“Same way you do,” he said. “One list at a time.”
“I’m paid to care,” she said. “She’s just… doing it.”
“Leaders happen,” he replied. “Whether they ask for it or not.”
Lillian hummed. “You’ve decided, then,” she said. “You’re in this.”
“I was in it the moment I saw them,” he replied. “You know that.”
She nodded. “Aye,” she said. “I ken.”
He glanced at her. Her free hand had drifted unconsciously to her middle, resting there as they walked. He filed the gesture away without examining it too closely. “We’ll figure something out,” he said. “For them.”
“We will,” she said. “And Miles?”
“Yeah?”
“Thanks for bringing me,” she said. 
They walked on, two silhouettes in the mist, carrying the warehouse with them like an extra weight across their shoulders.

## Chapter 6: Assessments

### 2.6.1
Faroukh found her at the edge of the warehouse, where the light from the one working lamp drew a tired circle on the concrete.
Sarah was checking the supplies list again—because if she didn’t check it, she felt like the whole place might slide sideways when she blinked. Her pencil moved in short, controlled strokes. The names on the list weren’t real names, mostly, but labels she’d given them when she’d had to start making categories.
Rice.
Tins.
Tea.
First aid.
Faroukh hovered, hands in his blazer pockets, the clean lines of him looking wrong against the warehouse’s makeshift mess.
“You’re doing it again,” he said.
Sarah didn’t look up. “I’m doing what?”
“Counting like numbers are going to grow if you stare at them hard enough.”
Sarah’s pencil paused. “They might,” she said.
Faroukh huffed a laugh, then went quiet. He leaned his shoulder against a pillar, eyes on the loading bay door as if he expected Connor to appear out of the gloom on cue.
“You trust them?” he asked.
Sarah’s ears flicked. “Who?”
“Adults,” Faroukh said, like the word was a dare.
“No.”
“Not even the Shepherd?”
Sarah looked up then. “His name’s Miles.”
Faroukh’s mouth twitched. “Yeah. The one with the name badge and the saviour complex.”
“He’s not—” Sarah stopped. She could hear herself getting defensive. That annoyed her. “He brings food. That’s all.”
“That’s not all,” Faroukh said. “He brings attention. He brings a trail.”
Sarah stared at him. “And you bring what?” she snapped. “A phone and homework and secret calls in the middle of the night?”
Faroukh’s eyes flashed. For a moment he looked like he might bite back. Instead, he looked away. “My dad used to lock the fridge,” Faroukh said, voice flat.
Sarah’s anger stalled.
Faroukh’s gaze stayed on the door, not on her. “Like I was a dog. Like food was something you earned by being human enough.” He swallowed. “Sometimes he’d eat in front of me. Just… to make a point. My mum would sit there and say nothing. Silence was safety.”
Sarah felt the warehouse air shift. The hum of the kids in the background seemed farther away.
Faroukh exhaled, a rough sound. “When I left, I promised myself I’d never ask anyone for anything again. But then you get hungry enough and your pride becomes optional.”
Sarah stared at the list on her paper. The lines blurred slightly. She hated feelings. They made decisions messy.
“I don’t trust adults,” Faroukh said again. “But I trust systems. Paperwork. Rules. Things that can be written down and checked.”
Sarah’s laugh was short and sharp. “Rules? Like the ones that got us here?”
Faroukh shrugged, one shoulder lifting. “Not those rules. Our rules.” He nodded toward her list. “You’ve got rotas. Supplies. Names. You’re building a system so you don’t have to rely on anyone’s mercy.”
Sarah’s pencil tightened in her paw until it almost snapped. “Mercy is a joke,” she said.
Faroukh’s gaze flicked to her face, then away again. “So why let them in?” he asked softly.
“Miles?”
“And the badger woman,” he added.
“Lillian,” Sarah corrected automatically. She hated that she’d learned the name.
Faroukh’s eyes narrowed. “Yeah. Her. Why?”
Sarah pressed her tongue to the back of her teeth.
Because the little ones had laughed when Lillian made a joke.
Because the sketchpad had felt like something that didn’t belong to survival.
Because she’d looked at Lillian’s face and seen no disgust.
Because there was a piece of her that was tired of being the only wall.
“Because if I don’t take what’s offered,” Sarah said finally, “we die faster.”
Faroukh nodded once, as if that was the only answer that made sense. “And if it comes with strings?” he asked.
Sarah’s mouth went dry. She looked at his blazer. His phone. The careful neatness of him.
“Everything comes with strings, and you know it well enough,” she said. “It’s just a matter of… choosing which ones.”
Faroukh flinched, like she’d poked a bruise. He let out a breath, then, unexpectedly, asked, “What about your parents?”
Sarah’s body went cold. It wasn’t a dramatic reaction. It was physiological. Like someone had opened a door to winter inside her. Faroukh watched her carefully. “You never talk about them,” he said.
“I don’t have parents,” Sarah replied.
“You had something,” Faroukh pressed. “Before Connor. Before all this.”
Sarah’s gaze dropped to her hands.
She remembered a smell—soap, maybe. Or milk. She wasn’t sure.
She remembered a door closing.
She remembered the sound of her own crying getting smaller as she got older.
“I don’t know,” she said, and the words were the closest thing to truth she could tolerate. “I was six. I don’t even know my birthday.” Faroukh’s eyes softened, just for a second. Sarah hated that softness. It made her feel like something breakable. “Don’t,” she said.
“Don’t what?”
“Don’t look at me like that,” Sarah snapped.
Faroukh looked away immediately, as if obeying. In the pause, a child laughed somewhere behind them. Someone argued over a deck of battered cards. Life, insisting. He cleared his throat. “Alright,” he said. “No parents. No birthdays. Fine. But you’ve still got history. And you don’t have to carry all of it alone.”
Sarah’s shoulders tensed. “I do,” she said.
Faroukh’s voice went quiet. “You don’t have to.”
Sarah returned to her list with a violence that was almost relief. “Go do your homework, then,” she said, hard.
Faroukh’s mouth twisted. “I did. Twice.”
Sarah’s ears flicked despite herself.
He pushed off the pillar, lingered a moment as if about to say something else, then seemed to think better of it.
As he walked away, Sarah stared at the paper. Next to Connor’s collection days, she wrote, in smaller handwriting than usual, a new line.
Miles / Lillian — ???
She underlined the question marks so hard the pencil dented the paper.

### 2.6.2
Miles bought the ring on his lunch break.
He told himself it was the only practical time, between a delivery that had arrived late and a staff meeting that would drag on because Mr. Bahadur liked the sound of his own voice. He told himself that if he didn’t do it now, he’d keep putting it off, waiting for a perfect moment that didn’t exist.
He also told himself it was not romantic.
That was a lie.
The jeweller’s shop smelled of polish and old velvet. The display cases were too clean, too bright. Everything in there had the kind of shine that suggested it had never been touched by mortal hands. A young woman approached, human, smiling in a way that tried to be warm but landed as rehearsed. “Can I help you?”
Miles kept his voice steady. “I’m looking for an engagement ring.”
Her smile widened. “Congratulations.”
Miles nodded once. His ears flicked backwards for a second. He had done his research: he knew which metals Lillian’s skin tolerated best, he knew her hands, the way her fingers moved over piano keys, strong and precise, claws kept filed but always present, and he knew she would hate anything fussy. He needed something simple, solid, something that would not snag on a scarf or tear a glove. A ring built to be lived in.
He chose one with a plain silver band and a small stone that caught the light without shouting about it. It was thin, yet present, visible, like the person you loved that you could easily spot even in a crowd. The woman slid it onto a velvet pad and pushed it toward him.
Miles stared. How much meaning could be crammed into a piece of metal.
“You can try it on?” the woman suggested.
Miles shook his head. “No. It’s not for me.”
He didn’t smile. It wasn’t her fault. But his body was tight with the knowledge that he was making a decision that would change the shape of his life.
He paid.
He left with the small box in his pocket, his thumb pressed against it through the fabric as if it might vanish if he didn’t keep contact.
Outside, the air of the first week of March hit him with damp cold. Miles leaned against the wall beside the shop for a moment and breathed. Then he pulled out his phone.
He didn’t call Lillian.
The line rang twice.
“Alright, love,” his mum answered, voice already in motion. “You eaten? You better not be living off them sandwich deals again, I swear—”
“Mum—”
“And don’t ‘Mum’ me, I’m talkin’. How’s work? You still doing them daft hours?”
Miles huffed a laugh despite himself. “I’m fine. Honest. Can I speak to Dad?”
A pause, then her tone shifted—still brisk, but attentive. “What’s up? Somethin’s up. I can hear it.”
“I just… need to talk,” Miles said.
“Will!” his mum shouted, muffled now as she moved the phone. “WILL! It’s Miles. He wants you.”
His dad’s voice came in like a warm door opening. “Alright, lad?”
“Alright, Dad.” Miles swallowed. He looked down at the ring box in his pocket like it might give him instructions.
“What’s this then?” Will asked. “You sound… serious.”
Miles exhaled. “I’m thinking about getting married.”
Silence.
Then, faintly in the background, his mum inhaled like she’d been waiting a decade for that sentence. “Married,” his dad repeated, voice careful now, as if the word might break if he handled it wrong.
“Aye,” Miles said. “Me and Lillian. We’ve been together a long time. It… it makes sense.”
His dad’s breath came through the line. “Does it for you?”
“Yes, Dad.” Miles’s ears warmed. 
Will’s voice returned, calmer. “Lad,” he said, “you love her?”
Miles looked at the grey London street, at the wet pavement and the people hurrying past like none of this existed, and all he could think of was Lillian’s hearty laugh, Lillian’s soft fur, Lillian’s warm gaze. “Aye,” he said. “I do.”
“And she loves you?”
Miles exhaled. “Yeah.”
“Then you do it,” Will said. “You do it proper. You look after her. And you don’t let anyone make you feel daft for wanting a family.”
Miles swallowed hard. “Right. Speaking of which…”
“…Yes?” his father asked, the word dragging out slowly from his mouth.
“We’ve met someone,” he added.
“Someone?”
“A kid,” Miles said. “An anthro kid. She’s… she’s living in a warehouse with other… abandoned kids. She’s sixteen.”
His dad went silent again. Miles could hear his mum in the background, suddenly quiet too. “Bloody hell,” Will said at last.
“Yeah,” Miles replied. “We’ve been bringing food. Lillian’s met them. I… I’m not trying to rush out, but… they need help and—”
“Lad,” Will said, “you’ve always been like this. Once you see a problem, you can’t unsee it.”
Miles chuckled. “Maybe that’s why I chose engineering over other courses at the uni.”
Will laughed. “That’s true, that’d be you. Let me guess: somethin’ in you head tells you that you need to take those kids out of that place.”
“Yes.”
“And you’re thinking of adoption, right that?”
“Right that. But it’s just me, and I don’t…” His throat tightened. “But I don’t know if I’m doing the right thing.”
“Lad, I can’t tell it for you, right that?” his father said. “But I know for sure that, as long as it rings true to you, then it’s the right thing. It’s your instincts talking.” 
“It’s easier if… And if we want to adopt, or foster, or even just… speak to anyone official without them treating us like idiots. It’s all paperwork. And rules.”
“Aye,” Will said, voice low. “The state loves a form.”
Miles made a humourless sound. “I’m not doing it for the state. But I’m not letting the state be the reason a kid stays in a warehouse.”
“Good lad,” Will said.
There was a shuffle and his mum’s voice cut in, sharper. “You getting married and you didn’t tell me first?”
“Mum—”
“When were you planning on telling us?” she cut him.
“I’m telling you now,” Miles said, trying not to laugh.
His dad’s chuckle rumbled through the line. “Grace, let him breathe.”
“No,” his mum snapped. “Let him answer. And let me say this: I’m proud of you. Both of you. And if you think we’re not coming down to meet this girl and Lillian and whatever warehouse nonsense you’ve attached yourself to, you’ve lost your mind.”
Miles blinked, warmth and panic colliding. “Mum, it’s not— it’s complicated.”
“Life is complicated,” she said. “We’ll manage.”

## Chapter 7: Tests and Secrets

### 2.7.1
The bathroom on Mandela Street was barely big enough for her and her thoughts.
Lillian shut the door with a soft click and turned the bolt, more for the ritual than the security. The lock had a habit of sticking; Miles had promised, more than once, to sand the frame, but life kept providing more urgent repairs.
The mirror caught her as she turned. A broad‑shouldered badger woman with damp fur at her temples, blouse hanging open at the collar, a nice pair of round glasses framing smart albeit tired eyes. Her face looked paler than usual beneath the black and white markings, the fur along her jawline fluffed where she’d run her paws through it on the walk home.
She glanced away quickly, as if the mirror might ask questions she wasn’t ready to answer, and set her handbag down on the closed toilet lid with a small thud.
The chemist’s bag rustled inside: thin paper, glossy logo crinkling like leaves. She pulled it out, fingers suddenly clumsy, and tipped the contents onto the lid. A rectangular box slid free, white and blue, the words PREGNANCY TEST printed across it in cheerful, neutral font. Nothing about the packaging suggested this was anything more than another household product, filed in mental cupboards somewhere between plasters and paracetamol.
Her heart thudded unevenly. The sound felt louder than the traffic outside, louder than the bus lumbering past on the street below, louder than the muffled bassline leaking from the downstairs neighbour’s stereo.
She picked up the box. The cardboard edges dug into her paw pads. “Och, you’re being daft,” she told herself. The empty bathroom swallowed her words and gave them back in softer echo. “You’re tired. That’s all.”
The nausea that had ambushed her all afternoon had subsided into a sullen ache now, but her breasts still felt swollen and sore under her bra, and a faint tug low in her abdomen pulsed in time with her heartbeat. Her period had been and gone. She turned the box over to the back, scanning the instructions. They were simple, insultingly so.
Remove cap.
Hold in stream of urine for five seconds.
Wait three minutes.
Compare line to diagram.
Three minutes. The kind of time it took to make tea. The kind of time in which it was perfectly possible for a life to tilt on its axis.
Lillian exhaled slowly, the breath shivering as it left her chest. “Right,” she said. “We’re juist… gathering data, aye? That’s all.”
She tore the cardboard along the perforated edge with more force than necessary. Inside, the plastic test lay in a foil wrapper, glossy and anonymous. She opened that too, the thin metal crackling in her paws, and stared down at the small, smooth stick in her hand. It looked like nothing. A bit of plastic, a strip of hidden chemistry. Not a verdict, not a promise, not a betrayal. Just a tool.
Her claws tapped once against the white body of it.
“If this is negative,” she murmured, “you’re going to feel very silly.” She tried to make it a joke. It landed flat.
She set the test on the edge of the sink. Her belly pressed softly against the waistband as she lowered the zip of her trousers, familiar weight folding over the elastic of her knickers. She did as the instructions said. Five seconds, she counted silently. Maybe six. Better too long than too short. The test’s absorbent tip darkened. She replaced the cap with hands that shook more than she would have liked and set the stick down carefully on a folded towel on the edge of the sink, as if it were something fragile that might shatter.
She stood, pulled up her knickers and trousers, zipped and buttoned, each movement feeling twice as slow as usual. She washed her paws at the small sink, watching the fur darken under the stream, white soap suds sliding down to the drain. The test sat beside the tap, face turned away from her. A small, mute thing.
She dried her paws on the hanging towel and forced herself to look up.
The woman in the mirror hadn’t changed. Same round glasses, same tired eyes, same hair pinned back in a quick twist. But there was a tightness around her mouth that hadn’t been there this morning, a fear trying very hard to wear the clothes of composure.
Three minutes.
She checked her watch. The second hand ticked with infuriating calm.
She could leave the bathroom, walk into the main room, distract herself with washing up or marking or any of the thousand tasks that filled their small flat. She could also sit here, perched on the closed lid, staring at the towel, letting panic build with each passing tick.
Instead, she reached for the string hanging from the bare bulb overhead and turned the light off.
The bathroom plunged into a softer darkness, lit only by the strip of evening through the frosted glass window. The street lamps hadn’t come on yet; the city outside was a muted, grey‑blue hum. In the half‑light, her reflection blurred into suggestion rather than detail. 
She leaned against the cool, tiled wall and slid down until she was sitting on the floor, knees up, arms wrapped loosely around them. The tiles were hard against her backside, but the coolness seeped pleasantly into her overheated fur.
She closed her eyes and listened.
Downstairs, Mrs Gainsbury’s television murmured a BBC documentary. Somewhere in the distance, a siren rose and fell. A bus drew up at the stop outside, brakes exhaling with a hiss. She could picture it without looking: passengers shuffling to the doors, damp coats and umbrellas, the faint halo of advertisement lights on the side.
Her own heartbeat was the loudest thing in the room.
Dinna get ahint o yersel', she told it. Dinna write tales afore ye hae the facts.
Her mind ignored her. It travelled, unhelpfully, to the drawer where they kept the folders from the clinic, pages of information about anthro fertility and interspecies pregnancies. Charts and statistics and phrases like negligible probability and no recorded cases and little chances for viability in cross-species pregnancies. A doctor’s voice, kind and tired, explaining that the virus had made some combinations simply non‑viable. “You’re not broken,” the doctor had said. “You’re just… you.”
She and Miles had gone home that night and made tea and sat at the table without speaking for a long time. The conversation had always drifted back to the same place: their lives were full already. Students, bandmates, community kids. They were needed in so many ways that didn’t involve nappies. She’d believed that. Still believed it.
Mostly.
But there had always been a small, secret corner of her that had wondered. Not dared, exactly, but wondered.
A tap sounded somewhere in the plumbing. Someone upstairs flushing. The pipes rattled and settled. Lillian opened her eyes and checked her watch again.
Three minutes.
Her paw hovered over the test. She breathed in through her nose, out through her mouth, as if she were about to step on stage. “Whatever it says,” she murmured, “you’re still you. That’s all.”
She picked it up and turned it over.
One blue line for no, the box had said. Two for yes.
The plastic window showed two lines.
The first was bold, the control line, asserting that the test itself worked. The second was fainter, but still unquestionably there: a pale blue stroke, delicate as a vein beneath skin.
Lillian stared at it.
For a moment she didn’t feel anything at all. There was a strange, weightless pause, as if the world had been holding its own breath and had forgotten to exhale. The traffic noise receded; the tick of the watch vanished. There was only the tiny window, the ghost of a line.
Then the floor seemed to come back up under her in a rush.
Her stomach swooped. Her chest tightened and expanded at the same time, as if she’d inhaled too sharply. Heat flooded her face. She realised belatedly that her paw was shaking. “No,” she whined. The word came out without meaning. Not no to the idea, but no to the possibility that the universe might have changed its rules without telling her.
The second word arrived on a different breath.
“Oh.”
It wasn’t eloquent. It didn’t need to be. It held surprise and terror and something else that burst through all of it like light through a crack in heavy curtains.
Joy.
Small, disbelieving, wild joy.
She pressed her free paw flat to her belly. Nothing felt different there. The folds of fat, the firm muscle beneath, the familiar softness. Her body didn’t hum with magic. There was no sudden clairvoyance, no music swelling out of nowhere. There was only her, on the bathroom floor, holding a bit of stained plastic.
“Oh,” she said again, a fraction louder. “Och.”
Her eyes stung. She blinked hard. Tears slid into the fur at the corners of her eyes, disappearing quickly. She let herself imagine, for one reckless moment, Miles’s face if she told him. The way his ears would shoot up, the way his tail would beat once against his leg before he caught himself. His big hands coming up, uncertain, to touch her belly as if it might break.
The image hit her so sharply that she had to curl forward, folding over her knees, the test still clutched in one paw.
“We cannae,” she murmured into the hollow between her thighs. “We cannae get used tae this. It might be wrong. Tests can be wrong.”
But the box had promised ninety‑nine per cent accuracy if used correctly. She’d followed the instructions. But plastic was plastic and hormones were hormones. Her mind began to race ahead, down corridors lined with what‑ifs. What if this was real. What if it wasn’t. What if the clinic had been wrong. What if there was some risk they’d never told her about, some hidden clause in the virus’s contract with her DNA that turned hope into cruelty.
What if telling Miles lit his eyes up in a way she couldn’t bear to see dim.
The thought snagged. Held.
She pictured him standing in their tiny kitchen, reading the stock rota, humming under his breath. She imagined walking in, holding the test up between two claws. Imagined his face, whatever it did—shock, delight, fear. Imagined that same face, weeks or months later, if it all went wrong.
Her heart squeezed so hard it hurt.
He had been the one to steady her when the Royal Academy turned them away. The one to bring curry when she came home from work smelling of fryer oil and humiliation. The one who, when she’d confessed in a choked, miserable voice that she felt like a failure, had said quietly, “You’re not a failed anything. You’re just playing in a room they don’t deserve to hear you in.” He was the person she loved the most, he was the person she would never hurt in any way.
She could not bear the idea of being the person who handed him a fragile thing and then, through no choice of her own, took it away again. “No,” she decided, more firmly now. “Not yet.”
The bathroom felt very small. She stood up slowly, her knees creaking. She rinsed the test under the tap from sheer habit, even though there was no need, and wrapped it in a square of toilet paper before setting it on top of the medicine cabinet, out of immediate sight. Later, she would find somewhere to hide it properly. For now, she needed the kitchen, the kettle, the illusion of normality.
She washed her face, letting the cool water run over her muzzle, through the fur, dripping onto the already‑damp front of her blouse. She patted herself dry with the corner of the towel, avoiding the mirror’s gaze.
“Aye,” she whispered to herself. “Whatever happens, you’ll handle it. You always have.”
She unbolted the door and stepped out into the hallway. The flat smelled of the day—piano polish, the faint ghost of this morning’s toast, the damp of her coat hanging on the peg. She could hear someone moving on the stairs outside, keys jangling.
For a fraction of a second, panic flared. If Miles walked in now, it would be written all over her face. She was rubbish at lying to him. Years of friendship and the kind of intimacy that came from shared survival, combined with his German Shepherd instincts, had made him an expert in understanding her micro‑expressions.
But it wasn’t his tread on the stairs. It was the upstairs neighbour’s. Their door opened and closed with its usual slam. Lillian let out a breath. She crossed to the kitchen and put the kettle on, the familiar click and hum grounding her. As the water began to heat, she rested both paws on the counter and bowed her head.

### 2.7.2
Milla’s name flashed on Felix’s phone. 
He had the television on mute. The news anchor’s mouth moved silently, teeth too white, headlines scrolling beneath. His mother was sitting on the sofa, dozing quietly after her afternoon exercises. He could see her lithe frame from the bedroom mirror. He stopped folding the laundry and picked up the phone.
Milla’s voice came through the line thin and careful. “Hi.”
“Alright,” Felix said, because that was what you said when you didn’t want to ask the question directly. It hurt him, but he knew he had to do it. At least according to Milla’s therapist. There was a pause in which he could hear breathing and, faintly, cutlery. “Were you eating?” Felix asked.
“Yes,” Milla replied too quickly.
Felix’s own chest loosened like a knot coming undone. “That’s… that’s brilliant,” he said, and he meant it. “That’s belting news.”
“It is,” Milla agreed. Her voice had a strange brightness, like a lamp turned up too high.
Felix rubbed a hand over his rat snout, a steady wave of relief washing over him. “How do you feel right now?”
On the other end, there was a pause. Felix could hear the faint rustle of fabric, like Milla shifting in her chair. “Fine,” she said.
“Just that? Fine? I thought I deserved more than just a bloody four-letter word,” he commented feigning to be upset.
Milla snorted on the other hand, a stifled laughter he always found so endearing. “I had a salad,” she specified. “With tuna, three slices of bread, oil and a tomato.”
Felix’s voice softened. “Come round tomorrow,” he said. “I’ll make pasta. Proper, with sauce, not that sad jar rubbish. You can sit in my kitchen and tell me what you’re not telling me.”
A pause.
Milla’s voice came back, smaller. “Okay.”
Felix’s throat tightened again. “Okay.”
He heard her inhale, then, almost as an afterthought, she said, “Felix?”
“Yeah?”
“Thank you.”
Felix scoffed because tenderness made him itchy. “Give over.”
But his voice was warm.
When he hung up, the room felt different. Relief sat in his chest like something heavy and holy.
Worry sat right beside it.
He got up, went to the kitchen, and put a pan on—caring not to wake his mother up.
 
# Act III
## Chapter 8: Drawings and Mothers  
 
### 3.8.1
Sarah liked the roof because from up here, everyone looked small.
The warehouse was squat and ugly, a concrete box with a patchwork roof of corrugated metal and tarpaulin. One section, near the fire escape, had a shallow lip where she could sit with her back against a chimney stack and her legs stretched out, toes dangling over the gutter. From there she could see the whole little universe: the railway arches, the canal glinting in the gap between buildings, the backs of terraced houses with their tiny gardens, the supermarket loading bay where lorries reversed in and out.
She could also see, if she craned her neck just right, the road that led to the warehouse door. She’d long ago memorised the shapes of the regulars’ cars and vans. The social worker’s little blue hatchback, when she still bothered to come. The white van of the charity that dropped off blankets twice last winter. The boxy red one that belonged to the bloke at the corner shop. And now, the grey delivery van that meant food and paint and the strange, patient adults who brought them.
She sat with her sketchpad on her knees, stub of charcoal pinched tight between her fingers, tail curled around her ankle to keep it from flicking too much. The March wind tugged at the edges of the tarpaulin nearby; somewhere below, a kid yelled, another laughed.
Her stomach growled, but she ignored it. She’d eaten already—half a banana, a heel of bread. Enough to keep her head clear. The rest of the food had gone to the younger ones. That was how it worked. That was how she made it work.
Movement at the edge of her vision caught her eye.
The van.
From this angle it looked like a toy, trundling along the road, turning with careful, practised precision into the narrow lane that led to the warehouse. She watched it pull up, watched the big figure in the driver’s seat twist to grab something from the passenger side.
Miles. The German Shepherd. The one with the warm eyes and the bad jokes and the uncomfortable awareness of where every exit was.
The passenger door opened. Lillian climbed out, coat flapping in the wind, scarf tugged close around her throat. She squinted up at the building as if she could see through layers of brick and grime to where Sarah sat.
Sarah’s heart jumped, then thudded, annoyed at itself. She hunched down a little, making sure she was out of sight. The adults talked briefly by the van, gesturing at boxes. Miles pointed towards the door, then upwards, tracing routes in the air like he always did when he was explaining a plan. Lillian nodded, her paw brushing his arm once, quick, then dropping. They moved in sync, she noticed. Not in a sloppy, romantic way, all hand‑holding and kissing. More like bandmates. Familiar with each other’s rhythms. When Miles bent to lift a crate, Lillian was already moving to take one side. When Lillian shifted her grip, Miles adjusted without looking.
Sarah’s pad stayed blank on her knees, charcoal hovering above the page.
She had drawn plenty of adults before: social workers with tight mouths, police officers with bored eyes, charity volunteers with their pity packaged along with their donated clothes. She knew how to catch the slump of a shoulder that said “I’m just here for the paycheck”, the tilt of a chin that said “I think I’m better than you”.
These two were different, and that difference made her restless.
They carried the crates inside, the door swallowing them up. She knew the route they’d take: along the ground floor, past the broken forklift, up the stairs to the main hall where most of the kids were. One of the younger ones would have spotted the van already, would be bouncing by the door, tail a blur.
Sarah had told them not to mob the visitors. “We don’t scare them off,” she’d said. “We get more if they’re no’ freaked out.” She’d turned it into a game: count to twenty before you run, whoever waits the longest gets first pick of the biscuits—but the rule underneath was real.
She should go down. She should be there, supervise, make sure no one grabbed too much, that the older teens didn’t push the little ones aside. That was her job. Her role. The thing that held the whole precarious arrangement together.
Instead she stayed where she was, on the roof, watching the empty patch of concrete where the van had been. Her paws itched. She put the charcoal to the page before she could think better of it.
Two figures, side by side.
She worked quickly, letting lines find themselves. Height first: Miles taller, shoulders broad, ears pricked. Lillian shorter but solid, weight carried like she owned it. She sketched the line of Miles’s tail, the way it curved down and then up again at the tip when he laughed. The slouch of Lillian’s shoulders that somehow didn’t read as defeat, more like she refused to make herself smaller for anyone.
Faces next. She had to slow down for that. Adults were tricky; if you weren’t careful, you ended up drawing your own fear instead of their features. She started with Lillian: the rounded cheeks, the glasses, the way her mouth tilted slightly to one side when she listened. Charcoal smudged under her fingers, blurring into something that looked, if you squinted, like warmth. Miles’s snout was easier; dogs were familiar shapes. But she added the small details that made it him: the nick in one ear, the faint lines at the corners of his eyes from squinting in warehouse glare. She drew their paws last. Not holding hands—she wasn’t ready to draw that—but close enough that the space between them meant something. A gap that could be closed. Or not.
When she stopped, breathing a little harder than the effort warranted, she realised she’d done something she hadn’t meant to. She’d drawn them walking away from the viewer. Towards something she hadn’t sketched yet. On impulse, heart thudding, she added smaller shapes around them. Not defined, not detailed, just suggestions: a kid with ears too big for their head, one with a tail that dragged, one with a backpack nearly as big as their body.
She didn’t draw herself. Didn’t know where she would put her if she did.
She scowled at the page, annoyed at the softness that had crept in. It would be easy to rip it out, scrunch it up, throw it off the roof and watch it flutter down into the gutter.
Instead she turned the pad, looked again.
There was something in it that made her chest ache. Not because it was good—she knew, objectively, that the proportions were off, that Lillian’s head was too big, that she’d smudged Miles’s ear. But because it was honest. It was, apparently, what some part of her believed when she wasn’t on guard. She shut the pad with a snap, pressing her paw hard against the cover as if that could keep the image from escaping.
Below, a cheer went up from inside the warehouse: the sound of kids spotting biscuits, crisps, the rare holy grail of fresh fruit. She could almost see them in her mind’s eye, swarming the crates, eyes bright, hands grabbing, the careful rules she’d set half‑remembered in the excitement.
She should go down.
She didn’t move.
Eventually, reluctantly, she slid the pad into her battered rucksack, tucking the charcoal into the elastic loop she’d sewn in herself. She got to her feet, flexed her numb toes, and made her way back to the hatch that led to the stairwell.
Inside, the air was warmer, thicker with the smell of bodies and food. The main hall buzzed. Kids perched on broken pallets, on the stripped‑down old sofas, on the floor. A few of the older ones hung back, leaning against the walls, arms folded, pretending they didn’t care. Miles stood by the crates, paws on his hips, supervising with a practised eye. Lillian was kneeling on the floor next to Rafi, helping her peel a banana, her fingers moving slowly so the girl could copy. “There you go,” Lillian said. “You do the last bit, aye? I believe in your banana skills.”
The girl giggled, tongue sticking out in concentration as she pulled the strip down.
Sarah’s chest did that annoying ache thing again.
She cleared her throat deliberately as she approached. It was important to arrive as if she’d been somewhere purposeful, not just sitting on the roof drawing fantasies.
“You’re late,” Faroukh called. “We had to start without you.”
“Good,” Sarah said. “Means you don’t need me to hold your paws every time you see a biscuit.”
Some of the kids laughed. Faroukh pulled a face, but he scooted over, making space for her near the crates.
Miles looked up, ears flicking. “Hey,” he said. “We saved you an apple. And some biscuits that have miraculously not vanished yet.”
“I told them,” Lillian added, “that if anyone nicked the last of the chocolate ones before you got here, they’d have me to answer to.”
Sarah snorted, but took the apple, fingers brushing Miles’s paw for a fraction of a second. Her fur prickled at the contact. “Cheers,” she muttered. She checked quickly: the little ones all had something in their paws, faces intent. The older teens had either grabbed food already or were trying to look like they weren’t bothered about it. The crates still held a decent amount; Miles must have rationed carefully. She relaxed a fraction, tension in her shoulders easing.
Lillian shifted to sit beside one of the younger boys, legs folded underneath her. It was an awkward position for her size; Sarah could see the strain in her knees. But she didn’t complain. She took a piece of bread from the crate, tore it into smaller pieces, handed them out, laughing quietly when crumbs stuck to whiskers.
“You no hungry?” Faroukh asked, nudging Sarah with his elbow.
“Had something earlier,” she lied. “You lot need it more.”
“Stop playing martyr,” he muttered, but his eyes were grateful.
Later, when things had settled, Sarah slipped away to the little office up to the mezzanine. It wasn’t really an office anymore. The old desk was covered in boxes and blankets, the chair’s wheels jammed with dust. But the light worked, and there was still a pinboard on the wall where someone long gone had once tacked shift rotas.
She shut the door behind her and pulled her pad out again.
The drawing stared back up at her.
Two adults, walking into a future that didn’t exist. Not yet. Maybe not ever. She could have kept it. Hidden it under her mattress. Torn it into strips and used the back for something more practical.
Instead, she unclipped it carefully and smoothed it out. Her paws left faint smudges of charcoal on the paper.
She pinned it to the board.
Not in the middle. Not too obvious. Off to one side, where you’d only really see it if you were paying attention.
It felt like a dare.
At some point, she told herself, they’d see it. Miles would wander in here to check stock lists. Lillian would follow him, mug of tea in paw, curious. One of them would notice the new piece of paper amid the old notices about “electrical safety” and “no smoking”. They might think it was someone else’s work. They might never mention it. That would be safest. Or they might look at it and understand it was her hand that had made it, her mind that had imagined that specific arrangement of bodies and movement.
And then… what?
They’d probably say something nice. Adults did that. “Oh, that’s lovely.” “You’re very talented.” Words that slid off the surface of her skin without getting in. They might ask a question that made her want to crawl out of her own fur. “Is that us?” “Are these meant to be parents?” Questions that would require answers she didn’t have.
She stared at the drawing until the lines blurred. The ache in her chest deepened, turned into something like anger. Stupid, she told herself. Stupid to want things. Stupid to put them on paper where anyone could see.
But she didn’t take it down.
When she emerged from the office, wiping her charcoal‑stained fingers on her jeans, Lillian glanced over. “Y’alright?” she asked.
“Fine,” Sarah said. “Just checking something.”
Miles was at the far end of the hall, talking quietly to one of the older boys about the best way to stack pallets so they didn’t fall. His ears twitched as if he was listening to everything at once. Sarah watched them both for a second, then forced herself to move, to re‑enter the flow of the room. There were disputes to settle, bruises to check, an inventory to update.
Life went on. Kids shouted, laughed, cried. The roof would need patching again before the next rain.
In the little office, pinned to the board between a faded fire safety poster and a notice about long‑expired training, two adults walked into a future they didn’t yet know they were part of.

### 3.8.2
Lillian waited until the flat had settled into its evening shapes: Miles in the shower, water hammering through the pipes, television murmuring in the other room, sound turned low, subtitles on. The kettle clicked off with a tired little sigh.
She stood in the kitchen with her phone in both paws as if it were heavy.
Calling her mother felt… complicated. Not because of their relationship, which had always been good. Phone calls, short trips home, with or without Miles, spread across the ten years of distance between her and her family. She felt their presence every day in her heart and her mind, and the mere thought of calling home would have been a simple pleasure in her life.
Still.
She’d been thinking about blood tests and pamphlets and a spare bootie folded in her wardrobe like a secret prayer. She’d been thinking about impossible things.
She scrolled until she found MUM. Her thumb hovered.
Then she hit call. It rang three times.
“Hello?” Her mother’s voice arrived with the sound of a kitchen behind it: clinks of dishes, a radio playing faintly. 
“Mum? It’s me, Lillian.”
“Oh! Love.” The voice warmed instantly. “It’s late for you, isn’t it? You alright? You sound… tired.”
“I’m fine,” Lillian lied, then heard herself and corrected. “Bit off. Nothing dramatic.”
Her mother made a small noise. “When you say nothing dramatic, it’s usually dramatic. Have you been eating? Do you keep going all day on tea and stubbornness?”
Lillian huffed a small laugh. “Aye. I’ve been eating.”
“And Miles? He feeding you properly?”
“Why are you so focused on what I’m eating?” she asked, puzzled. 
“Just joking, love,” her mother commented, with a laugh. “Anyway, how’s Miles?”
“He’s in the shower,” Lillian said. “He’s fine.”
A pause. The way her mother paused when she was making space for the real reason of a phone call. “So,” her mother said gently, “why are you ringing?”
Lillian stared at the tea mug on the counter, the surface of it catching the kitchen light. She could have told the truth. Could have said: I think I’m pregnant. Could have listened to her mother inhale, could have heard the old beliefs rearrange themselves. Instead she said, “Can I ask you something weird?”
“Of course,” her mum replied. “There’s nothing too weird to scare us.”
Lillian’s mouth twitched. “When you were… when you were pregnant with me.”
Her mother went still on the line, as if she’d paused mid-wipe of a dish. “Right,” she said carefully. “That’s a topic. What about it?”
“I’ve just—” Lillian swallowed. “I’ve had one of my students ask me questions. About… early stuff. Bleeding and nausea and that. And it made me wonder. What was it like for you? Did you have morning sickness? Did you—”
“Oh, love,” her mother said, and the tenderness in it made Lillian’s throat tighten. “You’ve never asked me that before.”
“I know.”
Her mother exhaled. “Aye. I was sick. Not like in the films, no dramatic vomiting into a sink with your dad rubbing my back. More just… constant nausea. Like I’d swallowed a bad batch of dough and it wouldn’t budge from my stomach.”
Lillian’s chest felt immediately heavier. Of course her mother would reach for cooking metaphors, considering she worked as a baker. “And bleeding?” Lillian asked, forcing her voice to stay casual. “Did you have any of that? Like… spotting?”
Silence. Then, “Who’s asking you these questions, Lillian?” her mother said.
“I’m just curious,” Lillian lied again. “Students are weird, better be prepared for their questions.”
Her mother didn’t accept it, but she also didn’t push yet. “I did have bleeding,” she said finally. “Early on. Enough to scare me. I thought I’d lost you.” Lillian’s grip tightened on the phone. “Your dad took me to the infirmary at two in the morning,” her mum continued, voice quiet. “We sat under them awful lights with folk coughing and crying and the telly in the corner talking about nothing. The doctor said it might be a miscarriage. Might not. They said to go home and wait.”
In the pause, Lillian could hear the shower shut off in the background of her own flat, the thud of Miles’s footsteps. She turned her body slightly away from the corridor, as if angle could make privacy. “Why didn’t you ever tell me?” Lillian asked.
Her mother made a small, pained sound. “Because you were a wee thing, and you already carried so much in that big serious muzzle of yours. I didn’t want you thinking you’d nearly—”
“Nearly what?”
“Nearly not existed,” her mother said, blunt now, because there was no gentle way through it. “You were a surprise, Lils. A miracle, if you believe in that sort of language.”
Lillian’s ears pricked. “A surprise?”
“Aye,” her mother said. “I’d had two miscarriages before you. Two. And after the second, the doctor told me, very kindly, that I’d likely not carry a baby to term. They said my body just… didn’t do it right. That my uterus was unfavourable. And then they found out about the virus, and—” Lillian’s mouth went dry. “So I stopped trying,” her mother continued. “Your dad and I—we made peace with it. We decided we’d be the aunt and uncle who spoiled other people’s kids. We pretended it was fine.” Lillian closed her eyes. “And then I got pregnant with you anyway,” her mum said, a faint laugh in the words. “No warning. Just nausea and sore breasts and me thinking I’d caught some virus—which I did, but it was not the flu.”
Lillian’s heart hammered. “What happened?” she managed.
Her mother’s voice softened. “I was terrified,” she admitted. “Because wanting it after being told you can’t have it is like touching something hot. You don’t trust it won’t burn you. And then I bled, and I thought: of course. Of course this is how the universe teaches me not to hope.” A lump rose in Lillian’s throat. “But you were stubborn,” her mother said. “You kept going. You stayed. The doctors later told me that it was common for… anthros births and pregnancies, at least at the time.”
“And what about the birth?” Lillian asked. It came out as a whisper.
Her mother exhaled slowly. “You were early,” she said. “Not terribly. A few weeks. You came out furious, and during a thunderstorm so strong the wind was shaking our windows. Your father said you looked like you were ready to argue with the midwife. They whisked you away for a bit—incubator, oxygen, all that. They told me not to worry, but of course I worried. I worried my heart raw.” Lillian pressed her free paw to her belly without thinking. “When you finally came back into my arms, I told you You’re here now. You’re mine. You don’t get to leave.” Lillian’s eyes stung. “I’m not saying this to frighten you,” her mother added quickly. “If this is about someone else. A student. Or a friend. Or—”
“It’s about me,” Lillian uttered. Once the words flew her snout, she felt a flush of cold brushing her heart.
The silence on the other end was complete.
Then her mother breathed in, sharp. “Lillian.”
“I don’t know anything yet,” Lillian said, words coming fast now, as if speed could stop them being true. “It’s early, I’ve done a test, I haenae telt Miles, I dinnae even ken if it’s possible, we were telt it wisnae, ye ken, species, genetics, aw that.”
Her mother made a sound that might have been a prayer. “Oh, love.”
“I’m not telling you because I’m excited,” Lillian said, voice shaking. “I mean—I am, I suppose. Somewhere. But mostly I’m terrified. I can’t— I cannae gie him hope an then tak it awa’.”
Her mother’s voice turned careful, steady in the way it always did when she was doing mothering rather than talking. “Listen to me,” she said. “You’re not doing anything wrong by being hopeful. You’re not cursing it by wanting it. And you’re not protecting Miles by carrying this alone.”
Lillian swallowed. “He’ll blame himself.”
“Aye,” her mother said. “And you’ll blame yourself. As if I don’t know about you two. And the two of you will sit in your wee flat pretending you’re fine until it turns into something that eats you. I know that pattern. I’ve seen families destroyed by this.”
A weak laugh escaped Lillian, half sob.
“And also,” her mother added, voice gentler, “if anyone is stubborn and strong enough to survive against the odds, it’ll be a baby that’s half you and half Miles. Two bloody mules. And I’m not sure the world is ready for that.”
Lillian laughed properly then, wet-faced.
Behind her, a floorboard creaked.
“You alright?” Miles’s voice called softly from the corridor.
Lillian turned her face away, wiped at her eyes quickly. “Aye,” she said, too loud.
Her mum heard it. “Is he there?” she asked.
“In the flat,” Lillian said.
“Then I’ll say this,” her mother murmured. “Whatever happens, you tell him. Not to make it a drama. Not to make it a tragedy. Just… because he’s yours. And you’re his. That’s how you survive.”
Lillian closed her eyes.
“I love you,” her mother said, abrupt and fierce.
Lillian’s chest tightened. “I love you too,” she whispered.
When she hung up, the kitchen felt too quiet.
She stood very still with her hand on the phone, listening to Miles move around the bedroom, to the ordinary sounds of their life continuing. Then she made herself put the kettle back on.
Not a decision.
Just the next task.

 
## Chapter 9: Shutdown

### 3.9.1
As usual for her, Sarah was awake when Faroukh came back after midnight. She lay on her pallet with one ear tuned to the building and one paw resting on the metal bar that wedged the side door shut, as if she could hold the whole warehouse together through contact.
The lock scraped. A breath. Then the soft, careful click of someone trying not to be heard.
Sarah was on her feet before the door opened fully.
Faroukh slipped in sideways, shoulders hunched. The fur on the back of his neck was muddy. His shirt was half untucked. There was a dark smear on his jaw. “What happened?” Sarah hissed.
“Nothing,” Faroukh muttered.
Sarah stepped closer and the warehouse smell shifted—metallic, sharp.
Blood.
“Sit,” she ordered.
Faroukh bristled out of instinct, then obeyed because he’d learned Sarah’s tone meant there was no point wasting energy. He dropped onto an upturned crate with a grimace.
Under the weak lamp light, the damage resolved. His lip was split. One cheek was swelling already. His knuckles were scraped raw and wet, one claw broken. Sarah’s stomach turned over, cold. “Who?” she asked.
Faroukh looked away.
“Who?” she repeated, quieter this time, which was worse.
“No one,” he said. “Just… lads.”
Sarah’s ears flattened. “Connor’s lot?” Faroukh averted his eyes, turning his head away, tail held low. “Faroukh,” she repeated.
“It’s not them,” he snapped, too fast. Then his shoulders sagged. “It’s… not them.”
Sarah waited.
Faroukh reached into his trouser pocket and pulled out a wad of notes, crumpled like they’d been shoved in a hurry. He set it on the crate between them. Sarah stared. “That’s a lot,” she said.
“It’s for the Strays,” Faroukh muttered. “Like always.”
Sarah’s throat went dry. “From him.”
Faroukh didn’t deny it.
She could see the leash now, clearer than ever: the phone, the errands, the money that arrived with bruises. “What did he make you do?” Sarah asked.
Faroukh’s eyes flashed. “Nothing you’ve not done,” he said. “Nothing that would make you look at me like I’m… dirty.”
Sarah held his gaze. “I’m looking at you like you’re bleeding,” she said. “And like you’re fifteen. And, yes, you’re dirty, but at least it’s a dirt that can be cleaned.”
Faroukh’s bravado cracked, just at the edges. He swallowed. “It was just a drop,” he said. “Parcel. I picked it up, I brought it somewhere else. I didn’t even know what it was. Then some guy tried to take it off me in the stairwell and I—” He lifted his scraped hands, the gesture helpless. “I fought. I got it there. And he paid me. That’s all.”
Sarah’s chest tightened. “And the bruise?”
Faroukh’s voice went smaller. “He didn’t like that I was late.”
Sarah’s claws flexed. Anger arrived clean and sharp, a kind she preferred because it didn’t make her feel weak. “He hit you?” she asked.
Faroukh’s eyes dropped.
Sarah exhaled slowly. Her mind started building lists.
First aid.
Money storage.
Rules.
What to do when your child comes home bruised by an adult you can’t name.
“Give me the notes,” she said.
Faroukh’s head snapped up. “No. I earned that.”
“You earned it,” Sarah agreed. “And it’s going in the tin with the rest. Because if you walk around with that in your pocket, Connor’ll smell it on you and rip your arm off for the privilege.”
Faroukh hesitated, then shoved the money toward her with a violent little movement.
Sarah tucked it into the biscuit tin under the crate of tinned tomatoes. Then she turned back. “Hands,” she said.
Faroukh held them out, still averting her gaze.
Sarah cleaned him with water boiled earlier and left to cool, with an old rag and the antiseptic wipes Miles and Lillian had brought. Faroukh flinched when she dabbed the split lip. “Don’t,” she warned.
“I’m not—”
“Don’t apologise,” she snapped, surprising herself.
Faroukh shut his mouth.
When she was done, she wrapped his knuckles in gauze. Not pretty. Serviceable. “You’re not going back out tomorrow,” she said.
Faroukh’s ears pricked. “I have to. He’ll—”
“I don’t care what he’ll do,” Sarah whispered, lips curling to show him her fangs, almost snarling. “If he can reach you, he can reach us. And if he thinks he can put his hands on you, he’ll think he can put his hands on my kids. That’s not happening.”
Faroukh’s throat bobbed.
Sarah felt a flicker of something—fear, maybe. The sense of a door opening onto a corridor of uglier truths. “You hear me?” she asked.
Faroukh nodded once.
“Good,” Sarah said, and swallowed the softness in her own voice. “Go sleep. I’ll take watch.”
Faroukh hesitated as if he wanted to argue.
Then he moved past her, slower than usual, and disappeared into the blankets.
Sarah stayed by the door with one hand on the metal bar. The warehouse breathed around her.

### 3.9.2
The practice hall was empty in that specific post‑class hush—chairs stacked, stands pushed against walls. Juno had locked the door behind her, not because she was doing anything forbidden, but because she couldn’t bear the idea of someone walking in and looking.
She set her flute case on the piano bench, opened it, and took the instrument out with the care of a person handling something that could cut if mishandled. Her fingers were long and sure. Her shoulders rolled once, loosening. She brought the flute to her mouth.
The first note came out clean and bright, suspended in the air like a thread.
Juno closed her eyes.
It wasn’t the warm-up exercises Lillian gave her. It wasn’t scales.
It was a reel, fast and sharp, something her mother had played on a tin whistle in kitchens when the world felt briefly kind. The tune ran through Juno’s fingers like it had always lived there. Her breath was steady. Her embouchure held firm. The notes climbed, spun, landed.
It was—Lillian would have said it if she’d heard—brilliant.
Juno played as if the instrument was the only honest thing in the room. She let it carry her along the hills of her homeland, as if touching grass and then down to Lough Mahon, then breathed through Follow Me up to Carlow, Molly Malone and The Rocky Road to Dublin, then—
The door handle turned.
The sound was small.
But to Juno it was a gunshot.
Her fingers missed a note. The melody snagged.
She froze with the flute still at her lips.
The door opened.
Lillian stepped in, satchel on her shoulder, scarf half loosened, cheeks pink from the cold. She stopped the moment she saw Juno.
For a heartbeat, there was only the tail end of the note dying in the air.
Juno’s chest tightened until it hurt. “I—” Juno started, then nothing came.
Lillian’s face softened in a careful way, as if she was approaching a skittish animal. “You’re early,” she said. “As usual.”
Juno swallowed. Her paws trembled around the flute. “I didn’t mean to—”
“To play?” Lillian asked, gently.
Juno’s mouth opened. Closed.
The shame hit her hot and quick, flooding up her neck under her fur. She wanted to put the flute away. Wanted to evaporate.
Lillian didn’t move closer. She didn’t comment on the locked door. She simply set her satchel down on a chair and sat on the edge of the piano bench at a respectful distance. “That was beautiful,” Lillian said.
Juno’s ears pricked, then flattened again. Compliments were traps. They made you visible. “I messed up,” she whispered.
“You were interrupted,” Lillian corrected her. “That’s different.”
Juno swallowed hard. She kept her gaze on the floor.
“You don’t have to perform for me,” Lillian added, voice low. “But you also don’t have to pretend you can’t play.”
Juno’s hands shook harder. “I just—” she tried.
“You freeze when someone walks in,” Lillian said, not unkindly. “You’re not the first. And you won’t be the last.” Juno’s eyes stung. Lillian leaned forward slightly. “Tell me the truth, love. Does your family do that thing where they only love you when you’re quiet and convenient?”
The question landed like a weight. “They call,” she said, voice flat. “They call and they call and I don’t answer,” she replied, her voice straining more and more while she spoke.
Lillian nodded once, as if she’d expected it. “Alright,” she said. “Then we practise two things, aye? Flute. And answering.”
Juno blinked up at her.
“Not answering them,” Lillian clarified dryly. “Answering me. Answering the room. Answering yourself. You played like someone who knows she belongs in it. Then you stopped because someone might see.”
Juno’s breath hitched.
Lillian’s eyes held hers, steady and warm. “Play it again,” she said. “From the top. I’ll sit here and be boring. You pretend I’m a radiator.”
Juno stared, then—a shaky, unwilling laugh escaped.
“That’s it,” Lillian said, satisfied. “Good. Now. Again.”
Juno lifted the flute.
Her hands still trembled.
But this time, when she started, she didn’t stop when Lillian listened.

 
## Chapter 10: Hiding places 

### 3.10.1 
Lillian bought the first thing without meaning to.
It was a Tuesday afternoon, the kind of grey, damp day that flattened London into a single, soggy mood. She’d finished her classes early after a colleague traded slots, and for once she found herself with an hour between work and band practice. An hour unclaimed.
She let her feet take her along Camden High Street, past the stalls selling bootleg band T‑shirts and incense. The smell of fried food and damp denim hung in the air. She told herself she was heading for the music shop. She even slowed outside, looking at the window display of strings and brass mutes. But she didn’t go in. Her paws kept moving until the crowd thinned and the bright colours gave way to a smaller row of shops: a launderette, a barber, a charity shop with a faded sign.
The charity shop’s window was a jumble of objects: mismatched cups, a mannequin torso in a sequinned dress three sizes too small for it, a stack of romance novels, a child’s wooden train with peeling paint.
Lillian’s gaze snagged on a tiny bootie. Yellow, with two uneven white stripes. Spare, possibly a left one.
Before she knew it, she’d pushed the door open.
The bell jingled overhead. The shop smelled of old books, cloth, and the faint floral powder of whoever was behind the counter. A woman in her sixties glanced up from a magazine and gave Lillian a brief, polite smile before returning to her reading.
Lillian made herself walk past the baby clothes rack twice, as if she were just browsing. Her heart thudded in her throat. She felt ridiculous. She was thirty years old, a badger with a respectable job, a band, a partner of a decade. She’d faced down administrators and hecklers. Yet…
On the third pass, she reached out and touched the bootie.
It felt soft under her paw, slightly fuzzy. She imagined, absurdly, how it would look on a small hind paw, small enough to fit in her own.
“It’s just the left one,” the woman at the counter said, not unkindly.
Lillian’s ears heated. “Aye,” she said. “I know.”
She checked the tag. Two pounds. The number seemed both insultingly low and terrifyingly high. “I’ll take it,” she heard herself say.
At the counter, she fumbled for coins in her purse, her claws scraping against the metal. The woman folded the bootie with the care of someone handling something more valuable than it cost and slid it into a paper bag.
“We get a lot of nice bits in,” she said. “Babies grow so fast. Folks don’t know what to do with all the wee clothes.”
Wee. The word landed in Lillian’s chest like a pebble in a pond. “Aye,” she said.
She stepped back out into the drizzle with the bag tucked close to her chest. The fabric inside seemed to radiate heat through the paper, though she knew that was impossible. She stood in the doorway for a moment, letting the passers‑by flow around her, and tried to breathe normally.
You’re being foolish, she told herself. It’s just a spare bootie. People buy presents in advance all the time.
But she didn’t buy presents that way. She bought them for specific people, at specific times, when there was a solid reason. This… this was buying a possibility. A version of the future she still didn’t fully believe the universe would let her have.
She tucked the bag inside her satchel, between her lesson planner and a stack of unmarked theory worksheets, and set off for the tube.
On the train, she stood by the doors, paws braced, letting the sway of the carriage rock her. Her free hand drifted, without her permission, to her belly, resting there lightly over her coat. No one paid her any mind; people almost stopped caring. Anthro bodies on public transport were just part of the scenery now, something to avoid brushing against if you didn’t want to be reminded of certain political arguments.
Her stomach had settled into a new normal: less nausea now, more a persistent awareness, as if the centre of her body had become slightly denser. The clinic had made room for her that morning after she’d phoned, voice too calm, asking for blood work. The nurse had raised an eyebrow at the positive test she’d brought in, but had not argued. The sample had gone off to a lab somewhere, and Lillian had walked out with a handful of pamphlets about “early pregnancy” that might as well have been written for someone else.
