She hadn’t told Miles. Not yet. Each time she’d considered it, the words had dried in her throat, her mind conjuring a thousand ways it could go wrong.
It’s early, she told herself. Too early. Wait until we ken more.

### 3.10.2
Sarah woke before the others, the way she always did, in that thin grey strip of time after dawn when the city hummed but hadn’t yet cleared its throat.
For a second, she didn’t know where she was. The air smelled of damp concrete, unwashed bodies, and the faint, greasy sweetness of yesterday’s chips. A rat skittered somewhere in the walls. A bus rumbled past in the distance, making the corrugated roof above them shiver.
Then the weight of the kid draped over her right arm reminded her.
“Mmph,” Rafi muttered in his sleep, pressed into the hollow between her side and the pallet that served as their bed. One of his ears twitched against her ribs. On her other side, Mia had curled herself into a tight ball, lynx nose tucked under Sarah’s elbow, breath warm on her fur, her dark hair a curly mass covering the right side of her body.
Sarah nursed the brief, ridiculous wish that she could stay still, just for once. Let the warehouse wake itself. Let someone else worry about the leak in the roof over the east corner, the way the wind had been coming in colder through the broken pane, the fact that they were down to half a box of paracetamol and their last roll of bin bags.
The wish evaporated by the time she’d counted to ten.
She eased her right arm out from under Rafi’s head with practised care, sliding his weight onto a folded hoodie. The boy murmured but didn’t wake. Mia clung tighter for a moment, small claws catching in the fur of Sarah’s forearm, then loosened her grip with a sigh. Sarah swung her legs over the edge of the pallet. The concrete floor was cold enough to make her pads sting. She wriggled her toes once, quietly, to wake them up, then stood.
The warehouse looked different in these early minutes. The shapes of sleeping bodies under blankets and coats made soft mounds across the floor, broken up by the dark ribs of stacked pallets and the skeletal outlines of old shelving units. Someone had left a torch on near the far wall; its beam had sagged, pointing into a corner like a drunken spotlight.
She padded across the floor, stepping over an outstretched leg here, a rumpled tail there. Her own tail stayed close to her, tucked enough that no one could grab it by accident, but not so tight it looked like fear.
Fear was a luxury she couldn’t afford to display.
Two battered camping stoves on a metal table, a bucket for washing, and some plastic crates composed their makeshift kitchen. There she paused and did the inventory in her head before she even opened anything. Bread: six loaves from the grocery store, delivered in rustling plastic bags by the big German Shepherd. Milk: half a crate of long‑life cartons. Fruit: a few bananas, spotted but still holding their shape; some apples, bruised. Veg: carrots, potatoes. Protein: the chicken she’d insisted they cook last night before it turned, now sitting as cold leftovers in a big plastic tub.
Enough for breakfast and lunch. Maybe something thin for dinner if she stretched it.
The memory of Miles arranging the bags on the concrete outside their door flickered at the edge of her mind. She pushed it aside.
Gratitude could wait. Logistics first.
She filled the kettle from the water butt by the wall—a cut‑down oil drum they’d scrubbed and rinsed until it didn’t smell like petrol anymore—and set it on the first stove. Her claws clicked against the stove’s metal edges as she turned the gas on, the hiss catching with a whoomf as the flame took. The sound was a comfort: a controlled fire, where it was supposed to be.
Footsteps scuffed behind her. She didn’t turn; she knew by the pattern who it was.
“Morning,” she said.
“You should’ve woken me,” Faroukh grumbled. “It’s my turn.”
“You slept through your alarm,” Sarah said mildly.
He squinted at the wall where someone had drawn a crude rota in marker. His name sat under that day’s date, a big blurry FAROUKH scribbled next to a list of jobs.
“Still,” he said.
“You can take over the washing,” she offered. “I’ll do the hot stuff, so you don’t set your fur on fire like you did last time.”
He muttered something under his breath that might have been agreement, might have been insult, and shuffled over to the bucket.
As the kettle came up to a simmer, the noise in the warehouse shifted. A cough here, a yawn there, the crackle of sleeping bags. The younger ones woke first, drawn by the smell of heat. The older ones stayed where they were, pretending sleep until they could gauge whether the morning required effort. “Line up, yeah?” Sarah called softly. “Mugs by the bucket. Rafi, don’t stand on Mia’s tail, I can see you.”
Small shapes detached themselves from blankets and padded over. Rafi, Mia, the twins, little Vee who still sucked her thumb when she thought no one was looking. They brought their mismatched mugs and chipped plastic cups, setting them in a row on the table without being asked. Sarah poured hot water over the tea bags she’d rationed out into the kettle yesterday, letting the brew sit dark and strong before topping each mug up. She gave herself whatever was left.
“Is there bread?” Nate asked, nose twitching. One of the twins, half-Labrador, half-German Shepherd.
She couldn’t look at him or his brother Dick without thinking of Miles.
“There’s bread,” Sarah said. “Toast if you wait, plain if you don’t.”
“Toast,” they chorused, as if that settled all of life’s questions.
She popped slices onto the old wire rack balanced over the second stove and flipped them as the edges browned, her paws moving automatically. Faroukh slouched beside her, washing up yesterday’s cups, his movements slower but steady. He might be surly, but he did the work once he was vertical. “You’re up early,” he said after a while. “Earlier than usual, I mean.”
Sarah shrugged one shoulder. “Got things to do.”
He grunted. “You mean him.”
She didn’t look at him. “Mean the food. It doesn’t ration itself.”
“You know what I mean.” He banged a mug a little too hard against the side of the bucket. “Shepherd.”
Her tail twitched once, betraying her. “Miles. He brought stuff. Saves us nicking it and getting banned.”
“For now,” Faroukh said. “People like that, they always want something. You watch.”
She slid three pieces of toast onto Rafi’s plate and handed it over. “Not everyone is like—” she began, then stopped herself.
Like them. Like the ones who had taken one look at her ears and her muzzle and decided she was a problem to be moved on, not a girl to be kept.
The memory rose, unbidden: a hand on her shoulder, firm and impersonal, the smell of antiseptic and burnt coffee. the car ride, the first cold night. Her parents never coming back.
The warehouse came back into focus with the sizzle of bread catching. She flipped the slice, pressing the burnt corner down and scraping it against the rack. Waste not. “He left it and went,” she said. “That’s all. We take the food. We don’t give him anything he can use.”
“Except knowing where we are,” Faroukh muttered. “Except knowing we’re here. That’s already something. And he and the badger woman keep doing this all the time. How long before someone connects the dots and gets here? Comes and sweeps us away?”
She wanted to tell him that the world already knew they were here. The council knew enough to send social workers once in a while, half‑hearted patrols that wrote reports about hard‑to‑reach minors and non‑engagement with services. The police knew enough to move them on from doorways. The neighbours knew enough to lock their bins.
But Faroukh’s kind of anger needed something concrete, not the vague shape of systems. “If he wanted to grass us up, he’d have done it,” she said instead. “He’s had time.”
Faroukh snorted. “Maybe he’s waiting ’til the right moment. People like that are always waiting for the right moment.”
She filed the argument away for later. There would be more of them, she knew. For now, she had toast to not burn and children to feed.
By the time the older teens dragged themselves up and slouched over for their share, the warehouse had filled with a low, warm buzz. Kids perched on pallets and improvised benches, fingers wrapped around hot mugs, chewing with the focused intensity of the hungry. Someone had dug out last week’s newspaper from under a crate and was reading the back page football results aloud. Two of the smaller ones argued quietly over whose turn it was with the only unchipped plate.
Sarah ate standing up, leaning against the table, her own toast chewed more out of duty than enjoyment. Her stomach was a knot of lists: food, medicine, the bloody leak, Faroukh’s suspicion, the Shepherd’s next visit, that strange, unexpected flicker she’d felt when the dog took out a bag full of sketch paper and pencils.
Art, he had said. For the kids.
Sarah had said they didn’t have time for colouring in. Then she’d taken the pad as soon as his back was turned and spent half the night on the roof, the stub of a pencil digging into cheap paper. The sketchpad sat now on an upturned crate near her pallet, its cover closed. She could feel its presence the way she felt the draught from the broken window: not actively pressing on her, but never gone.
“Sarah?” Vee’s small voice tugged at her sleeve. “I spilled.”
Tea had sloshed onto the concrete near the girl’s feet. Not much, but enough that someone might slip later if they didn’t watch it. Sarah handed her a rag. “Clean it up, yeah? You know the drill.”
Vee nodded and knelt, wiping the floor with careful circles, ears drooping with concentration.
Sarah watched her for a moment, then lifted her gaze to scan the room. Twenty-two bodies, counting herself and Faroukh. Five under ten. Fourteen between ten and eighteen. Two over eighteen who could, in theory, walk away whenever they liked, but never had. The warehouse roof loomed above them all, a patched skin against the London sky.
Leader, they called her sometimes. Not to her face, but when they thought she couldn’t hear. It was a joke, half the time. “Ask the leader.” “See what the boss says.” But the word had stuck to her nonetheless, like burrs in her fur.
She hadn’t asked for it. She’d just been the oldest who hadn’t flinched away the night the first social worker had tried to split them up into placements. She’d been the one to say no, to grab Rafi’s paw and bolt. The others had followed. That was all leadership was, in the end: the person who moved first, and the ones who trailed after because they’d rather take their chances with you than with the cold, fluorescent rooms the council offered.
“Oi,” Faroukh said suddenly, nodding towards the high windows. “We’re famous.”
Sarah followed his gaze. In the strip of dirty glass above the loading bay, someone had pushed a leaflet through from the street. It hung half in, half out, flapping in the faint draught.
“Leave it,” she said automatically. “We don’t need junk.”
But curiosity flickered again. Later, when the kids were occupied, she would clamber up the metal shelves and retrieve it, just to see what the world was saying about people like them this week. Another article about feral anthro youth, probably. Another politician’s promise to tackle antisocial behaviour. The kettle clicked as the last of the heat bled out. She flicked the stove off and rolled her shoulders, feeling the early tightness there already.

### 3.10.3
Felix chose the place because it was loud. A kebab shop two streets off Soho with greasy menus and neon lights and a constant churn of people stumbling in after work, after pubs, after whatever they did to make the day tolerable. The air was thick with fried onions and chilli sauce and human sweat.
No one in there looked at you for long. No one in there expected you to be graceful.
Milla sat opposite Felix at the little plastic table, shoulders held too straight. Her hands were tucked under her thighs like she didn’t trust them to behave. She’d tied her hair back, tight, and her eyes kept flicking to the menu as if she were studying for an exam. “You look like you’re about to defend a dissertation on chips,” Felix said.
Milla’s mouth twitched. “I am merely… considering my options.”
Felix snorted. “It’s a kebab shop. Your options are: regret now, or regret later.”
Milla gave a small, brittle laugh.
He watched her carefully.
Milla had always been controlled—classical musicians were built out of discipline and pain tolerance. But lately there was a different tightness to her, something that sat under her skin like a held breath. The sleeves of her jumper were too long now. The collar sat looser around her neck.
Felix hated noticing.
Felix hated knowing.
“So,” he said, leaning back, “tell me about rehearsal.”
Milla’s gaze darted up. Grateful for the shift. “Juno is… excellent,” she said, and her voice softened on the praise. “Her tone is unusually even. She has a very good ear.”
“Yeah,” Felix said. “She also looks like she’s one bad day away from biting someone.”
Milla’s smile was faint. “That too.”
The man behind the counter shouted an order number.
Felix stood. “Right. I’m getting food. You want anything?”
Milla’s eyes flicked to the menu again, too fast. “I’m not very hungry,” she said.
Felix held her gaze. “It’s ten at night,” he said. “You’ve been at the academy since eight. You are hungry.”
“Felix.”
He shrugged, forcing his tone light. “Alright. You want something small? Chips? Falafel? Salad if you’re feeling morally superior?”
Milla’s mouth tightened at the joke. “A wrap,” she said finally. “Chicken. No sauce.”
“Course,” Felix said.
He ordered for both of them, adding extra chips without asking. He’d learned, over the months, that if he asked directly Milla would turn it into a negotiation with herself and lose. When he came back, Milla’s hands were folded neatly on the table, fingers pressed together as if she were keeping them from shaking.
Felix put the food down.
Milla stared at the wrap like it was dangerous.
“You’re not allergic,” Felix said.
“I know.”
“Then eat.”
Milla unwrapped the foil with slow precision, taking care not to let any sauce touch her fingers. She took a bite so small it barely qualified.
Felix watched, frowning.
Milla chewed as if chewing was something she could do wrong. “You’re allowed to enjoy it,” Felix said.
Milla’s eyes flicked up, sharp. “I do not require permission.”
“Could’ve fooled me,” Felix muttered.
Milla’s throat bobbed. She took another bite. A fraction bigger.
Felix ate his own food with less ceremony, because he was always hungry and because he refused to turn survival into a performance.
Halfway through, Milla reached for a chip, hesitated, then pulled her hand back.
Felix pushed the tray a fraction closer.
Milla’s eyes narrowed. “Stop that.”
“Stop what?”
“Watching,” she said. “Pressuring.”
Felix leaned back. “Alright,” he said, voice flat. “I’m not watching. I’m just existing in the same postcode.”
Milla gave a small sound that might have been laughter, might have been pain. Her gaze dropped to her hands. “My wrists hurt,” she said abruptly. Felix went still. “They always hurt,” she added, as if that made it less important. “But lately… it’s worse. And if I eat too much I feel… heavy. Like my hands will be slower. Like the bow will—”
“Like you’ll be punished,” Felix finished, quietly.
Milla’s squirrel tail twitched behind her. “I’m not asking for sympathy,” she said.
“I’m not offering sympathy,” Felix replied. “I’m offering reality. Your body needs food. Even if you hate it. Even if your brain tells you it’s a moral failing.”
Milla’s eyes flashed. “You don’t understand—”
“I understand enough,” Felix said, voice low. “I’ve watched you get smaller and meaner to yourself. And I’ve watched you pretend it’s discipline. It’s not. Not any more, at least.”
Milla’s breathing went shallow.
Felix softened, reluctantly. “Eat the chips,” he said, gentler. “Then tell me you hate me. Then we go home.”
Milla stared at him for a long moment.
Then, slowly, she picked up a chip.
She ate it.
Her eyes closed for half a second like she was bracing for impact.
Nothing happened.
Felix didn’t smile.
He just kept eating, as if this was normal.
As if food was not a battlefield.

### 3.10.4
By late afternoon, the warehouse had the restless feel of a body about to sneeze.
Kids moved faster than usual. Coats were pulled on and shrugged off again. The air held that thin, prickly tension that came before a supply run, before Connor’s lot might show, before anything that required them to leave the safety of their own rules.
Sarah stood by the loading bay with her back to the door and her eyes on the room. She had learned to count people the way some people counted change: automatically, without looking like she was doing it.
Still, when it mattered, she did it out loud.
“Right,” she said.
A few heads turned. Faroukh stood to her left, arms folded, phone in his pocket for once.
Noor hovered nearby, listening with that strategic stillness she had. Bruno sat on a pallet, big shoulders hunched, watching the door like it owed him money. The younger ones clustered together—Vee pressed against Mia, Kimi’s hands never stopping, Tomas already tucking something small into his sleeve.
Sarah drew in a breath. “Rollcall,” she said. Groans. “Don’t,” Sarah warned, and the groans stopped. “Rafi,” she called.
“Here,” Rafi replied instantly, fox ears up.
“Vee.”
“Here,” Vee squeaked.
“Kimi.”
Kimi’s head jerked up. “Here.”
“Tomas.”
Tomas hesitated a beat, then muttered, “Here.”
“Callum.”
“Here,” Callum said, too loud, then flushed.
Sarah nodded.
She moved through the list like a metronome.
Melanie. Josh. Mateo. Iona. Laleh. Ayumi. Harper. Rafa. Elise. Daisy. Anika. Mia. Nate. Dick. Bruno. Noor. And finally—
“Faroukh.”
Faroukh rolled his eyes. “I’m standing right here,” he said.
Sarah’s gaze stayed on him. “Say it,” she replied.
Faroukh’s jaw tightened. “Here,” he muttered.
Sarah nodded once. She took a breath. She scanned the room.
Counted again.
There were no gaps. No missing bodies. No sudden silence where someone should have been.
The relief hit her like a wave. She kept her face neutral anyway. “Alright,” she said. “We’re going in threes. Nobody goes alone. If you get separated, you come back here. You do not improvise. You do not decide you’re brave. You just come back.”
Elise scoffed from the back. “You can’t tell me what—”
Sarah’s gaze snapped to her. Elise shut her mouth. “Good,” Sarah said.
She caught Faroukh’s eye. For a second, there was something between them that wasn’t fighting.
Understanding.
She hated it. She needed it. “Move,” Sarah said.
The warehouse obeyed.

 
## Chapter 11: The Gig 

## 3.11.1
‘The Basement’ looked smaller from the stage.
Miles always thought that—every time they climbed up the narrow steps past the curtain, every time the lights caught in his eyes and the room turned into a blur of faces and glass. From the floor, the stage felt like a place where things became possible.
From the stage, it looked like a room full of people who might decide otherwise.
That night, the room was packed. The air smelled of beer, sweat, and warm cables.
Lillian stepped up to the mic and the noise shifted.
Not silence, not reverence, just attention, gathering.
Asim tuned his guitar with the flamboyant confidence of someone who’d been told too many times to make himself smaller and had decided, instead, to take up more space. Toby hovered near the back of the room, half hidden behind a pillar, smiling like he couldn’t quite believe he was allowed to be there. Felix leaned on his bass like it was a crutch and a weapon both, eyes scanning the crowd with his usual cynical assessment. Milla sat with her cello between her knees, bow poised, posture perfect, hiding how her shoulders held too much tension, the way her jaw stayed clenched between songs.
Miles tapped his sticks together once.
The sound cut through the room like a small command.
They started.
The first song went well.
It wasn’t perfect—nothing live ever was—but the band moved with the old, familiar muscle memory of years together. Lillian’s voice filled the room, rich and grounded, the kind of sound that made people stop talking without noticing they’d stopped.
It looked, from the outside, like they were fine. Like this was simply what they did. A band playing on a small stage while the world continued to be cruel outside.
Miles felt the sweat gathering at the back of his neck. He felt his heart thudding. He felt, in the space between songs, the hum of something not said. But he kept playing.
Because that was what you did.
You kept the beat.

## 3.11.2
After the gig, 
“You were brilliant,” Asim had said after they’d come off stage, wrapping Lillian in a hug. Felix had grunted something that was probably praise. Milla had smiled, small and sharp, then vanished into the crowd as if she couldn’t bear being seen celebrating.
Back home, it was just Miles and Lillian. Miles kicked off his shoes by the door, careful not to step on the pile of sheet music that had migrated to the hallway again. Lillian hung her coat on the chair back instead of the hook, which was unlike her. Her movements were a touch too stiff.
Miles set his drumsticks on the table, aligning them with the edge without thinking. The ring box in his jacket pocket felt like a weight.
Lillian went to the kitchen and put the kettle on. Her fingers trembled when she reached for mugs.
“You alright?” Miles asked, because he couldn’t stop himself.
“Aye,” Lillian said too quickly. “Just tired.”
Miles watched her. Watched the way she avoided looking at him. Watched her free hand drift, unconsciously, toward her satchel.
In the wardrobe, behind her winter jumper, the tiny bootie sat folded in its paper bag. In her satchel, there were pamphlets and blood test appointment slips.
In Miles’s pocket, a ring.
They moved around each other in the small kitchen like two people carrying glass.
“I’m making tea,” Lillian said, as if narrating could make the night safe.
“Right,” Miles replied.
He wanted to tell her. To pull the ring box out and make the moment simple. But the look on her face—tight, guarded—made him hesitate.
He set his jacket on the back of the chair, not hanging it up, and the ring box thudded softly against wood.
Lillian’s ears flicked. “You’ve still got your coat on,” she observed.
“Forgot,” he lied.
Lillian’s mouth twitched. She poured hot water into the mugs, hands steadying as she did something practical.
They sat at the small table with tea between them. The television in the other room continued muttering about nothing.
Miles cleared his throat. “I’ve been thinking about… stuff,” he said.
Lillian’s paw tightened around her mug. “Aye?”
“About houses,” Miles said, choosing the safe topic because he was a coward. “Bigger place. Somewhere with an extra room. For—”
He stopped.
For what?
A kid?
A future?
Lillian stared into her tea. “That’s sensible,” she said.
“Sensible,” Miles echoed, because sensible was easier than hopeful.
They sipped in silence.
He looked up.
Her eyes were shiny.
Not crying.
Not yet.
His paw moved toward hers on the table.
She let him take it.
Her paw was warm.
Her grip was too tight.
Neither of them spoke about the secrets.
They just held on.

### 3.11.3
Faroukh did not like leaving the warehouse in daylight.
It wasn’t that night was safer, but daylight made you visible in a different way. In daylight, adults looked at you and decided you were a problem that belonged somewhere else. In daylight, everybody, including you, could see how dirty you looked.
He tightened the strap of his school bag across his chest and checked his phone again.
No new calls, no new texts.
The screen was dark enough to show him his own face in faint reflection: long Afghan Hound muzzle, tired eyes, the bruise at the edge of his mouth that was just fading from last week.
“Stop looking at it,” Melanie said.
Faroukh snapped his gaze up. Melanie was a weasel, small but sharp, eleven years old and already built like a person who’d learned that being cute didn’t keep you safe. Her fur was smooth and dark, her eyes bright with irritation under a bang of dark brown hair that refused to stay combed. “I’m not looking,” Faroukh lied.
Josh, a rat boy with quick hands and a cautious expression, adjusted the hood of his coat. “He’s looking,” he offered, tone neutral, like a fact about weather.
Faroukh bared his teeth at them in what he meant to be a warning and what came out more like a grimace. “Right,” he said. “Rules.”
Melanie rolled her eyes. “We know,” she said. “Don’t talk to strangers. Don’t look like you’re scared. Don’t run unless you have to.”
“Don’t run at all unless I tell you,” Faroukh corrected.
Josh nodded. “Where are we going?”
Faroukh glanced down the street, then up, then down again. He mapped the route the way Sarah mapped the warehouse, as a list of exit points. “First, the back of the supermarket,” he said. “Not ours. Not Miles’s one. The other one. They throw more out because their manager’s an idiot.”
Melanie snorted. “All managers are idiots.”
Faroukh didn’t argue.
They walked in a loose triangle, Faroukh slightly ahead, the two kids half a step behind but not too close, spaced the way he’d been taught to space himself at school when he wanted teachers to think he was a normal student and not a stray dog with a second life. The pavement was wet from earlier rain. The air smelled of frying oil and car fumes and the faint sweet edge of a bakery.
Faroukh kept scanning: old man on a bench; couple with a pram; two boys in school uniform; a police car parked at the corner, engine off, officer inside staring at his phone.
Josh watched him. “You scared,” he said quietly.
Faroukh’s ears heated. “No,” he snapped.
Josh didn’t flinch. “You are,” he said, still quiet. “It’s fine. I’m scared all the time.”
Faroukh didn’t know what to do with that kind of honesty, so he did what he always did: he turned it into instruction. “Eyes up,” he said. “We’re nearly there.”
Behind the supermarket, the world changed tone. The alley was narrow, brick walls close, the smell of rot sharp enough to make Faroukh’s nose sting. Bins sat in a row like fat, tired animals.
Melanie wrinkled her snout. “Gross.”
“Yeah,” Faroukh said. “Gross is where food lives, sometimes.”
He crouched by the first bin and knocked the lid with his knuckles.
“Listen,” he said.
Josh leaned in.
They all listened.
No scuffle.
No rats.
No glass shifting.
“Safe,” Faroukh decided.
He lifted the lid. Inside, they found bags tied tight, cardboard, packaging.
He frowned, then moved to the next.
“Rule one,” he said. “You don’t rip bags open with your claws like an idiot. If there’s broken glass, you’ll slice yourself and then you’ll bleed and then you’ll have to explain to Sarah why we’re wasting antiseptic. Use something.”
He pulled a flat piece of cardboard from the ground and slid it under a bag to lift it.
“Rule two,” he continued, “don’t take anything wet unless you know what it is. Wet can be milk. Wet can be blood. Wet can be bin juice. You don’t want bin juice.”
Melanie made a gagging noise. Josh, surprisingly, smiled.
They found two still‑sealed packets of bread rolls, squashed but unbroken, and a tray of bruised apples. Melanie held up an apple. “This one’s mouldy,” she declared.
“Then you don’t take it,” Faroukh said. “You take the ones with bruises. Bruises aren’t poison. Bruises are… life.”
He didn’t know why he said it like that. They moved on.
A café that tossed pastries at closing. A hotel that threw out whole trays if one croissant got touched. A corner shop whose owner sometimes left a box by the back door if he was feeling guilty.
Faroukh taught them where cameras tended to sit, how to walk like you belonged, how to keep your shoulders loose.
“Police don’t stop you if you look like you know where you’re going,” he said.
“What if they do?” Josh asked.
Faroukh’s throat tightened. “Then you say you’re running an errand for your mum,” he said. “You say you’ve got a note. You say you’re lost. You don’t say warehouse. You don’t say Strays. You don’t say—”
He stopped.
His phone buzzed again in his pocket.
This time he didn’t check.
He forced himself to keep walking.
There was an errand he had to do. The thing Sarah knew about, sort of, but didn’t know the shape of.
They reached the shop near the main road, the one with the shutter that was always half down and the sign that pretended it sold phone cards. Faroukh stopped with Melanie and Josh in the shadow of a doorway. “Stay here,” he said.
Melanie’s eyes narrowed. “Why?”
“Because I said so,” Faroukh replied.
Josh looked from Faroukh to the shop. “This the other errand,” he said.
Faroukh’s ears flicked. “Just… stay,” he repeated. He crossed the road quickly, head down.
Inside, the air smelled of cheap incense and nervous sweat. The man behind the counter didn’t look up at first. When he did, his eyes slid over Faroukh like he was a tool. “Late,” the man said.
Faroukh’s stomach clenched. “Bus was slow,” he lied.
The man snorted and held out a plastic bag. It was light. Faroukh’s paw closed around it. He didn’t ask what was inside. He didn’t look at the man’s face. He left.
Two streets over, he dropped the bag into the hands of another man outside a newsagent, a brief exchange that looked like any other purchase if you weren’t watching too hard. The man pressed a wad of notes into Faroukh’s palm.
Heavy. Too heavy.
Faroukh’s throat tightened.
He shoved it deep into his bag and walked back to Melanie and Josh with his skin buzzing like it wanted to crawl off him.
Melanie stared. Josh’s eyes tracked the bag.
“What was that?” Melanie demanded.
“None of your business,” Faroukh said automatically.
Josh’s mouth tightened. “Everything is our business,” he said, voice low. “We’re the ones who get in trouble if you don’t come back.”
Faroukh stopped walking. He could feel the city around them—buses, voices, a siren in the distance. He could also feel the two kids beside him, waiting. He swallowed, because Josh was right. “It’s… money,” he admitted, quieter. “For the warehouse.”
Melanie’s eyes widened. “From who?”
Faroukh’s jaw clenched. “Just… someone.”
Josh didn’t let it go. “Someone like Connor?”
“No,” Faroukh snapped. “Not Connor.”
Melanie’s voice sharpened. “Someone hits you?”
Faroukh looked away.
That was answer enough.
Melanie’s small paw closed into a fist. “That’s stupid,” she said, furious on his behalf.
Faroukh laughed once, sharp. “Yeah,” he said. “It is. But it buys shampoo. It buys plasters. It buys a second kettle when Sarah melts the first one because she forgets water exists.”
Josh’s ears dipped. “Sarah doesn’t forget,” he said. “She just… has too much.”
Faroukh’s throat tightened. Because that was true, too.
He glanced at his phone again, then didn’t. He forced himself to keep moving.
“Come on,” he said, voice rough. “We’ve still got two more stops. And you’re carrying that bag wrong. If anyone sees you struggling, you look like a target.”
Melanie adjusted the bag. Josh followed suit.
They walked back toward the warehouse with their hands full of bruised apples and half‑stale rolls and something heavier that wasn’t food at all.
 
# Act IV
## Chapter 12: The Miscarriage 

### 4.12.1
Lillian felt the first cramp halfway through a scale. The upright piano under her paws was old, its keys slightly yellowed, its middle register prone to going honky if you hit it too hard. “Again,” she said, keeping her voice even.
The boy on the bench next to her, a fox anthro of about twelve, sighed dramatically and set his paws back on the keys. His tail drooped over the side of the stool, brushing the floor in time with the metronome. “You’re not being tortured,” Lillian said mildly. “You’re learning F major. Big difference.”
He snorted and launched into the scale again, fingers stumbling on the fourth note.
The cramp slid through her lower belly like a band being tightened, sharp enough that her breath caught. She forced herself not to flinch. Her paws stayed on the piano lid, knuckles pale under fur.
It’s fine, she told herself. Bodies do things. Muscles spasm. You slept funny. You’ve been on your feet all morning. She watched his left paw, corrected the angle with a gentle tap, nodded when he finally got the fingering. “Better,” she said. “Much. Again once more for luck, then we’ll torture you with arpeggios.”
By the third repetition the pain had ebbed to a dull ache, low and deep, like the beginning of a bad period. Except she wasn’t due. She’d been tracking, more carefully than she had in years. The app on her phone was full of tiny, private dots: nausea days, headache days, temperature.
Positive test, she thought, and the words still felt unreal. The NHS pamphlet in her bag, folded three times over as if that might reduce its importance. The nurse’s raised eyebrow at the clinic when Lillian had said her age, their species, and the word “pregnancy” in the same sentence.
The boy finished the scale and looked up at her, wrong‑pawed confidence back in his eyes. “That better?”
“Aye,” she said. “Much. Let’s retry a last time.” Another cramp rolled through her as he played, tighter this time. She pressed her thighs together under the bench, feeling the fabric of her skirt pull. A fine sheen of sweat broke out under her arms. The room suddenly felt warmer, the air thick.
You’re just tired, she told herself. Long week. Warehouse runs. Band. You’ve not eaten properly since Tuesday.
Except she had eaten, carefully, like the pregnancy leaflets had told her. Small snacks through the day. More water. Less coffee and tea. She’d even begrudged herself some of the cheap cheddar she normally inhaled without thinking, cutting thinner slices, swapping in yoghurt.
The metronome ticked with relentless cheerfulness.
When the lesson ended, she praised the boy, scribbled a few notes in his practice book, and saw him out with a joke about not terrorising his parents with scales at breakfast.
“They’d rather I did that than play FIFA all night,” he said, rolling his eyes.
“Tell them I said you can do both,” she replied. “Balance, aye?”
He grinned and loped off down the corridor, bag bouncing against his side.
As soon as the door clicked shut behind him, Lillian let her shoulders drop. Her paw went automatically to the small of her back, kneading. Another cramp, sharper. She exhaled slowly, counting under her breath the way she taught her students before difficult passages. Four in, four hold, four out. She glanced at the timetable pinned above the piano. Ten‑minute break before her next student. It would have to do.
The staff toilet at the end of the corridor was a narrow little room with a single stall and a sink barely wide enough for her paws. The fluorescent light hummed overhead. The tile was that institutional cream colour that made everything look slightly ill.
She locked the door, hung her satchel on the hook, and sat down heavily.
The ache in her abdomen throbbed again, a slow twist. She closed her eyes for a second, then forced herself to look down as she reached for the toilet roll.
A dark stain bloomed on the pad in her underwear. Fresh, wet, more than spotting. The kind of red that didn’t belong here, not today, not now. For a heartbeat, everything went very quiet. The hum of the light faded, the distant murmur of students in the corridor muffled. All that existed was the colour. She knew her cycle. Thirty‑two days, give or take. Heavy for two, light for three. This wasn’t that time. She had counted, obsessively, tapping dates into her phone while Miles slept, paw tracing the calendar squares as if she could hold the weeks still. “No,” she whispered, the word slipping out without permission. “No. Not now.”
She blinked hard, as if that might change what she was seeing.
It didn’t.
Her ears rang. She felt suddenly cold, though sweat still trickled down her spine. The pamphlets had mentioned spotting, a brief, blood‑less euphemism in a list of things marked “normal”. A bit of light bleeding can occur, they’d said. Implantation. Cervical changes. So many neat, clinical phrases.
This didn’t feel neat. It felt like something being unstitched.
Another cramp hit, sharper, making her gasp. Her paws gripped the edges of the seat until her claws scraped porcelain. You’re at work, she told herself firmly, dragging her mind back into the tiny room. You’ve got students. You can’t sit here and fall apart in the staff loo like you’re in a scene cut from Trainspotting.
She tore off paper, working mechanically, cleaning what she could, changing the pad. Every streak of red made her stomach lurch. It might stop, she told herself. It might be one of those things. Bodies do things. The nurse said there can be bleeding and everything still be fine. You dinnae ken yet.
She stood slowly, the world tilting for a moment. Her reflection in the mirror above the sink looked wrong, her face too pale under dark fur, eyes wide behind her glasses. She splashed water on her cheeks, patted them dry with a paper towel, then pressed her paws flat against the cool porcelain of the sink, grounding herself.
Tell someone, a small voice in her chest whispered. Call the clinic. Call Miles. Tell Asim. Tell anyone.
Her throat closed around the thought. What would she say? I’m bleeding at work and I don’t ken if the thing I’ve only half allowed myself to believe in is coming apart. Come and fix it?
No one could fix this. That was the quiet, brutal truth. There was only time, and her body, and whatever it had already decided to do.
The knock on the door made her jump.
“Lillian?” It was a colleague, human, oboe teacher, voice muffled through the wood. “You alright? Next student’s here a bit early. I told him to wait, but just checking you’re okay.”
Lillian swallowed, forcing her voice into its usual register. “Aye,” she called. “Fine. Two ticks. Tell him to go get a drink.”
“Will do.”
Footsteps retreated down the corridor.
She leaned her forehead briefly against the cool wall, eyes closed. Her belly gave a small, traitorous pulse of pain. She straightened, adjusted her skirt, checked three times that there was no visible stain. She smoothed her fur where it had ruffled, pinched colour back into her cheeks, practised a neutral expression in the mirror. “You’re fine,” she murmured. “You’re just tired. Tired and dramatic. Story of your life.”
The joke didn’t land, even in her own ears.
She picked up her satchel, which felt strangely heavy, as if the folded jumper hidden at the back had turned to lead. When she stepped out into the corridor, the noise of the school rushed back in: scales from distant rooms, footsteps, laughter, a clarinet squeaking on a high note.
Her next student, a shy human girl with a clarinet case nearly as big as she was, sat on the bench outside the practice room swinging her legs. “Miss Edgecombe!” she said, brightening.
Lillian smiled, the expression sliding into place like a well‑rehearsed chord.
“Ready to make some noise?” she asked.

### 4.12.2
Miles called his dad because he couldn’t stand the idea of looking at Rightmove again.
He was on his lunch break, sitting on an upturned crate behind the loading bay with a lukewarm tea and a phone that felt too small in his hands. Rain threaded down the metal guttering above him in thin, steady lines. He hit dial before he could think.
“Alright, lad?” Will answered, warm and thick through the speaker. “You at work?”
“Aye,” Miles said. “Break.”
“You eaten?”
Miles huffed a laugh. “I’ve had tea.”
“That’s not eatin’.”
“Dad,” Miles said, trying not to smile.
“Aye, alright.” Will’s voice shifted into something gentler. “What’s up?”
Miles stared out at the yard, at the wet concrete, at the neat stacks of pallets like a grid he could understand. “We’ve been looking at houses,” he said.
There was a pause. Then his dad’s breath came through the line, quiet. “You and Lillian?”
“…well, more like me and me,” Miles replied. “Feels like we’re always taking big steps lately,” he then added.
Will chuckled. “Aye. That’s adulthood for you. One big step, then you look down and realise the ground’s been moving under you the whole time.”
Miles snorted. “You been reading poetry?”
“Don’t be cheeky,” Will said. “I’m just old enough to have noticed patterns.”
Miles hesitated. “It’s not just… wanting more space,” he said. “It’s… practical. Papers and that. Adoption forms. Social workers.”
Will cleared his throat. “So what are you looking at?”
Miles blinked. “What?”
“Houses,” Will said, as if it were obvious. “You rung me about houses. Tell me. What’s the plan. Two bed? Garden?”
Miles exhaled, grateful for the return to tangible things. “Two bed minimum,” he said. “Somewhere we can afford without selling our souls. Lils would love something with a bit of green, even if it’s just a scruffy yard.”
“Aye, she would,” Will said fondly. “She’s got that earth in her, that lass.”
Miles’s throat tightened a little. “She deserves it,” he said.
“You both do,” Will replied. “And listen—don’t let estate agents mug you off. They’ll see you coming, all tall and polite, and they’ll see you’re an anthro and start behavin’ differently. Let’em know you’re not daft.”
Miles laughed. “I’m not polite.”
“You are,” Will said. “You just hide it under that alright voice.”
Miles’s smile faded as quickly as it came. “Dad,” he said, quieter, “how did you know when it was time? Like… when you and Mum bought the house.”
Will’s breath came through the line slow. “We didn’t,” he said. “We just got tired of feeling like guests in our own lives. Same as you, I reckon.” Miles swallowed. “And,” Will added, brisker now as if he’d gone too soft, “your mum’ll be over the moon. So you better tell her before she hears it from your gran and murders me.”
Miles snorted. “Aye. I will.”
“Good lad,” Will said.
Miles stared at the rain and felt, for a moment, anchored.
“Oh, and. Just one more thing, lad,” Will started. “Mortgages aren’t easy to get. You’ve got a good job. Lillian has one, too. But I’m not sure they’ll see you… as enough for them. Bankers, you know.”
“Yes, I’m… working on it.”
His father laughed. “Which means that you’re planning and counting and doing complicated math, right that?”
Miles’ instincts caught up. “Dad, I’m not accepting money.”
“It’s not charity. It’s help. You two want a future together, that? And there’s that girl, too, right?”
“Yes, but—”
“No buts allowed here. We’re family, you’re family, like it or not. I’ll talk to your mother and your gran, we’ll see how much we can muster to help you.” 
He hung up when his break ended. He went back inside.

### 4.12.3
When she reached their building and climbed the stairs, her legs felt leaden. Inside the flat, the familiar clutter greeted her: shoes by the door, a stack of music scores on the coffee table, a mug with Milla’s lipstick mark still faint on the rim from the last band meeting. She shut the door with more force than she meant to. The sound echoed in the small hallway.
Silence waited.
Miles wouldn’t be home for hours. He had a late shift and a warehouse stop after that. Juno had texted about a theory assignment earlier; Milla was rehearsing with the orchestra. No one expected anything of her for the next three hours.
The cramps came again, deep and insistent.
Her paws shook as she hung up her coat. The folded charity‑shop jumper in the wardrobe might as well have been a siren. “Okay,” she said aloud, voice low. “Okay.”
She went straight to the bathroom.
The tiles were cold under her paw pads. The yellow light buzzed, harsh on white porcelain. She shut the door, turned the lock with a firm click, and sat down, heart pounding. When she looked, there was no room for pretending it was nothing.
The bleeding had worsened, seeping through the pad, vivid against white.
Her breath came in a sharp little hiss.
This is happening right now, and you’re alone in the bathroom, and there’s no camera cut, no narrative voice to make it easier.
Her paws braced on her knees. Another cramp twisted through her, worse than any she remembered, a full‑body clench that made her vision blur.
On the shelf above the toilet, the pregnancy pamphlets sat in a neat stack, their pastel covers suddenly obscene. She reached up with shaking fingers, knocked them into the bin.
The sound of glossy paper hitting plastic was small, but it felt like a bell being struck.
She stayed there, on the edge of the seat, listening to her own breathing, to the rush of the cistern, to the quiet tick of the extractor fan.
The next cramp drove Lillian to her knees.
She had just enough time to slide off the toilet seat and turn, fingers catching the rim of the bath, before her body knotted itself around the pain, sharp and deep, like someone was wringing out her insides. “Jesus Christ,” she hissed, the words catching on her teeth.
Her forehead hit the cool side of the tub. One paw clawed at the bathmat, dragging it into a crumpled heap under her knees. The other stayed clamped around her lower belly as if sheer pressure could hold everything in place.
The extractor fan hummed overhead. The bathroom smelled of cheap lemon cleaner and damp towels, the everyday scents of a lived‑in flat. Underneath, another smell threaded through: iron and salt, thick in the air.
Blood.
She squeezed her eyes shut, riding the wave of pain. The muscles across her back and thighs trembled. When the cramp finally eased, she sucked in a breath that shuddered on the way out. Spotting, cramps, all were part of normal body functions, the nurse had said. But the nurse had also said, “If the bleeding gets heavier, if you’re soaking pads, if there’s clots—call us, come in.”
Another pulse of wetness between her legs made the decision for her.
She shifted awkwardly, fumbling for the toilet roll, for the pack of pads under the sink. When she pulled her underwear down again, the sight made her stomach twist. Blood, bright and abundant, soaked the pad. When she wiped, the paper came away red and slick, heavier than any normal cycle. There were darker streaks in it, thicker, viscous. Tissue, she thought, remembering the pamphlet’s clinical diagram. Products of conception. Cells and possibility, reduced to medical language.
Something warm slid from her, landing in the bowl with a soft, obscene sound. For a moment she froze, every muscle locked. Her breath stopped. The world narrowed to the white porcelain and the red in it. “No,” she said, but there was no force behind it now. Just air and pain.
She didn’t look closely. Some small, animal part of her refused. She shut her eyes as she reached blindly for the flush, pressing it down with more force than necessary. Water roared through the bowl, carrying red away in swirling streaks. The sound filled the small room, too loud, a bad imitation of a river. She sat there, pants tangled around her ankles, paws braced on her thighs, listening to the cistern refill.
When it was over, the silence came back, thicker than before.
Lillian breathed, in and out, counting, the way she taught her students. Four in. Four out. Her chest ached. Her belly continued to cramp, less violently now but still in pulses.
She was aware, distantly, of the time. The clock in the hall would be ticking towards five. Miles would finish at the store, maybe nip into the warehouse if he had time. He’d text when he was on the bus. He’d pick up curry like he’d promised the night before, or maybe a bag of chips if he was feeling indulgent.
She had, maybe, an hour before there was another body in this flat.
It wasn’t enough time to process anything. But it was enough time to erase.
That thought arrived without fanfare, solid and heavy. It settled into her like a stone.
She reached for a fresh pad, hands steady now. She moved through the motions she’d learned at fifteen: peel, press, smooth. She cleaned herself as best she could, ignoring the way the blood kept coming, slower but persistent, streaking the toilet paper.
Once she was dressed again she flushed a second time, watching until the water ran mostly clear.
Then she turned to the rest of the room.
The towels hung on the back of the door, blue and grey, familiar. She took the oldest one down, the one already frayed at the edges, and spread it on the floor beneath her. If there was more bleeding, she didn’t want it on the bathmat. The bin beside the toilet already held the pamphlets she’d knocked in earlier. She lifted them out, hands shaking only a little, and stacked them on the windowsill instead. She couldn’t bring herself to throw them away just yet; that felt too final, like tempting some vindictive fate.
She tore open the little plastic bag that had come with the last pack of pads, laying it in the bin like a liner. Another cramp came, weaker. She pressed her paw against her belly, feeling the soft give of flesh under fur. Her body, which she knew so well—the way it took up space on buses, the way it shifted when she sang, the way her belly rested when she sat—felt suddenly alien. Betrayer and victim both.
She sank down with her back against the cool tiled wall opposite the bath, knees drawn up as far as her stomach would allow. The tile dug into her shoulder blades. The towel under her paws scratched at her pads.
Tears burned at the back of her eyes but didn’t fall. It was as if her body had decided that losing blood was enough; it refused to spare any water for crying. She thought of the bootie in the wardrobe, folded behind scores. Of the way Miles’s paw had rested on her belly the night before, unconscious, trusting. Of his face if she told him now, if she said, “There was a baby. It’s gone.”
The thought made her chest seize up, a different kind of pain.
He would blame himself, somehow. That was the kind of man he was. He’d go back over every conversation, every shift he’d taken, every warehouse visit, looking for the moment he should have been here instead. He would try to fix something unfixable and hurt himself on the attempt. And if she told him, she would have to relive this. Say it out loud. Watch it crystallise in his eyes.
Practicalities first, she told herself, clinging to the thought. Clean first. Decide later.
She stripped the bathmat and the towel, rolling them carefully, checking for stains. She peeled off her underwear and skirt, dropping them into the tub for now. Tomorrow she could soak them in cold water, scrub, see what could be salvaged.
Tonight, she would bundle. Bag. Hide. One by one, she put things in a bag: the blood‑streaked towels, the ruined pad wrappers, the toilet roll she’d used to wipe the floor. Each item made a soft thud as it landed. She tied the bag in a knot, double‑looped, making sure it was tight. Then she slid it behind the bathroom bin, out of direct sight.
She would take it down later, when it was dark. When she could slip past the neighbour’s door without having to nod and say hello with a bag of her own blood in her paw.
When the immediate mess was contained, she finally allowed herself to lean over the sink and look at her face in the mirror.
She looked… fine.
Maybe a little paler than usual, the fur around her eyes slightly damp where she’d splashed water earlier. But to anyone else she’d be the same: big badger, round cheeks, glasses slightly askew, hair scraped back hastily. She bared her teeth at her reflection, testing the expression. It made her look like she was about to go on stage and pretend to be fierce for the benefit of a crowd.
“Well done,” she said to the woman in the mirror. “You’ve managed to have the worst day of your life and still look presentable. Gold star for you.”
Her voice shook on the last word.
She turned on the tap, letting the water run until it was as hot as it would get. She held her paws under it, scrubbing with soap, watching pink swirls drain away. The smell of lemon cleaner grew stronger, overpowering the metallic tang in the air.
The pain in her belly settled into a low, continuous throb. A period after the storm.
She switched the water off, dried her paws carefully, and reached for her phone at last.
There were two texts from students about homework, one from Asim sending her a link to an article about anthro musicians being “featured” in a tokenistic photo spread, accompanied by a string of swearing emojis. A notification from the pregnancy tracker app sat above them, cheerfully informing her she was “five weeks and three days” and that something called a neural tube was forming.
She stared at that for a long moment, thumb hovering over the screen, then held down until the app shook and a little cross appeared in the corner.
Delete.
The phone asked if she was sure. All data will be lost, it said, as if that were the problem.
“Aye,” she whispered. “I’m sure.” She deleted it.
Her thumb hovered over the contacts list. The clinic’s number sat near the top. So did Miles’s. So did Milla’s.
She could call the clinic, spend the evening being told about options and follow‑up and how very common this was, really. She could call Milla and pour it out into the phone, listen to her friend’s breathing on the other end, accept the offered sympathy that she wasn’t sure she could bear.
Or she could do what she’d always done when life dealt her something too big: shrink it down. File it away. Make it into a task. You don’t have to decide forever tonight, she told herself. You just have to decide what to do before Miles gets home. The thought of his key in the lock sent a fresh, fragile panic through her. How could she stand there, in this flat, in this body, and show him any of this?
She pictured him walking in: shoulders tense from work, fur smudged with warehouse dust, tail drooping with tiredness. Pictured him dropping his bag, toes skidding on the laminate because he always forgot to take his boots off at the door until she glared at him. Pictured his face when he saw the blood, the towels, the bin bag.
Her decision settled into place with the same dull finality as the stone had earlier. He would not see it.
“I’ll tell him it was a scare,” she said aloud, testing the lie. “A false alarm. Or nothing, if I dinnae have to. I’ll keep this bit. He gets enough of the world’s cruelty. He doesn’t need this too.”
It sounded almost reasonable when she put it that way. Protective, even.
She knew, somewhere under the rationalisation, that it was also fear. Fear of seeing herself broken in his eyes. Fear of changing the way he touched her, the way he looked at her belly, the way he spoke about the future.
But she was too tired to interrogate that.
She stepped carefully out of the bathroom, checking the hallway carpet for stray drops. There were none. The world beyond the door looked the same as it always had: the bookshelf crammed with scores, the sofa with its sagging cushions, the framed gig poster crooked on the wall.
She stripped the sheets off the bed, just in case, and put on fresh ones, moving slowly. She found clean pants and soft, loose lounge trousers, pulled them on with a wince. By the time she’d finished and sat on the edge of the bed, the worst of the bleeding had slowed. Her belly ached dully. Her limbs felt heavy, as if she’d run a race she hadn’t trained for.
Her phone buzzed on the bedside table. Miles.
On the bus. Want owt from the chippy?
She stared at the message, at the casual affection tucked into the dialect, at the unknowing kindness in the question.
Her fingers shook slightly as she typed back.
Just chips and curry sauce, ta. Rough day. Could do with carbs.
Say no more. Be about 40 mins. Love you x
She swallowed.
Love you too x
She set the phone down carefully, like it was made of glass, and lay back on the bed. The ceiling above her was the same off‑white it had always been, hairline cracks tracing maps she’d never bothered to follow.
She placed one paw gently on her belly. It felt the same under her touch as it had that morning: soft, familiar. If anything had changed inside, there was no outward sign. No mark.
“I’m sorry,” she said quietly, though she wasn’t sure who she was addressing—the maybe that had been, her own body, the universe. “I’m so, so sorry.”
No one answered. The flat hummed around her: the fridge in the kitchen, the distant slosh of someone’s music through the wall.
The tears she wasn’t able to shed finally tore through her eyelids, her cry coming out in harsh, merciless sobs as the last straws of hope for whatever was growing within her left her heart.

### 4.12.4
Miles’s key turned in the lock, the flat smelling of bleach and air freshener and the faint, greasy comfort of chip shop curry sauce. Lillian stood in the kitchen, leaning against the counter, paw wrapped around a mug of tea that had long since gone lukewarm. She had changed into an old band T‑shirt and soft trousers, her fur brushed, her glasses cleaned. The bin bag sat by the front door, tied tight.
The cramps had settled into a background ache. Every now and then, a sharper twinge cut through; she rode those out with her jaw clenched, eyes on some neutral point on the wall until it passed. She heard the familiar shuffle and scrape as Miles wrestled the door open with his elbow, arms full. “Evening!” he called, voice bright. “Delivery for one exhausted music teacher.”
She pasted a smile on and stepped into the hallway.
He stood half in, half out of the doorframe, ears perked. A paper‑wrapped bundle of chips and two little plastic tubs of curry sauce balanced precariously in his paws, along with a carrier bag clinking softly with tin cans. “Got you some Guinness as well,” he said. “Figured if it’s been that rough a day you might need the good stuff.”
Something in her chest pinched at the casual thoughtfulness. She smoothed her expression, widening the smile just enough. “You’re a saint,” she said. “Come in before you drop it all, you daft eejit.”
He laughed and shouldered the door the rest of the way open. As he stepped inside, she kicked the bin bag slightly with her heel, nudging it further into the corner so his paws wouldn’t brush it. He toed off his boots without being asked, a small victory she might have teased him about another day. Tonight, she simply noted it, filed it away somewhere bland. “How was work?” she asked, taking the bottles from him and heading for the kitchen before he could ask the same question back.
“Busy,” he said, following. “Thursday late shift. Everyone suddenly remembers they need groceries at half six.” He set the chips on the table, then moved automatically to put the kettle on. “There was this one bloke who tried to argue that buy one get one free meant he should get two free, like… mathematically, I don’t know where he was going with that. Absolute scenes.”
He launched into the story, complete with impressions: the customer’s whine, the supervisor’s clipped politeness. Lillian made the right noises—small laughs, little shakes of her head in disbelief—at the right moments, the way she’d learned to in staff meetings when someone told a joke she’d already heard. Her mind floated somewhere a few inches behind her eyes, observing the scene from a distance.
The kitchen looked the same as it had that morning: mismatched mugs on the draining board, a few crumbs on the counter from breakfast toast, the magnet with Asim’s face on the fridge, pulling tongues in a photo booth strip. The only new element was the folded tea towel over the oven door handle, fresh from where she’d swapped out the one with the faint, stubborn red mark.
“You alright?” Miles asked when his story wound down. “You’re quieter than usual.”
She forced herself to meet his eyes. They were tired, soft, ringed with the faint shadows of too many early mornings. Concern creased the fur between his brows.
“Just knackered,” she said. “Long day. Couple of parents wanting the moon on a stick, you ken how it is.”
He snorted. “Oh, yes. ‘Why isn’t my little prodigy in the advanced ensemble after three lessons?’”
“Exactly.” She poured Guinness into her glass, the fizz loud in the small room. “And the head decided we should consider adding extra evening classes to meet demand, which is code for we’ve no money but can you work more for the same pay?” The irritation was real enough. It slotted neatly over the rawness underneath like a ready‑made mask.
Miles grimaced. “Give over. You say no?”
“I said we’d discuss it at staff meeting,” she replied, which was true. “We will discuss it. Loudly.”
He grinned, some of the tension around his eyes easing. “Wish I could be a fly on the wall for that. You in full union rep mode. Terrifying.”
“You love it,” she said, and that was also true. She kissed him on his cheek, eliciting a tail wag.
They unpacked the chips together at the table, spreading newspaper over the surface. The smell of salt and vinegar and curry sauce filled the air, overlaying the faint chemical tang from the bathroom with something friendlier. Lillian’s stomach twisted at the smell—not with nausea, exactly, but with a complicated knot of hunger and resistance. Her body wanted the food; her mind remembered blood and tile and the way the towel had looked when she’d rolled it into the bin bag.
She made herself take a chip, dunk it in sauce, put it in her mouth. The grease and spice hit her tongue, familiar, almost grounding. “See?” Miles said, watching her. “Cure for all ills.”
She chewed and swallowed and smiled. “I’ll put that on a poster next time you’re at the protest,” she said. “Down with austerity, up with curry sauce.” He laughed, the sound bright and uncomplicated.
They ate. He told her about a new colleague who kept mis‑stacking tins, about an old lady who’d asked for his help reaching the top shelf and then patted his arm and called him a good boy in a way that made him simultaneously delighted and slightly embarrassed.
She told him, in return, about a student who’d tried to claim their metronome was “possessed” because it had ticked on after they’d knocked it, about the head of department’s email reminding everyone to “take self‑care seriously” while also asking them to fill in a dozen new forms.
The rhythm of it was a comfort: his story, her story, back and forth, like trading fours in a solo. She knew where to leave space, when to come in. It had taken years to learn, the easy duet of their shared evenings. Under the table, her legs trembled slightly. She kept her knees pressed together, aware of the pad between her thighs, of the slow, dull seep still happening.
“You’re really sure you’re alright, Lils?” he asked again at one point, squinting at her over a chip. “You look… I dunno. Was going to say peaky but you’re a badger.”
“You calling me stripey?” she said, eyebrows up.
He grinned. “I would never, honey. Fearsome fat badger goddess, that’s you.”
The phrase, which would normally have made her snort and maybe swat at him fondly, landed differently tonight. Goddess. Her body, elevated as a joke, when a few hours ago it had felt like an unstable planet. She made herself roll her eyes. “Flattery willnae get you out of washing up.”
“Worth a try,” he said.
He reached across the table and squeezed her paw. His grip was warm, callused from drumsticks and warehouse pallets. For a second, the contact cracked something in her chest. The urge to tell him surged up, sudden and fierce, like a chord change in a song: I was pregnant. I’m not anymore. It happened this afternoon while you were stacking tins and I was teaching scales.
Her mouth opened. “My day was…” she began, and then the room tilted. In her mind’s eye she saw the bathroom again: the blood, the towels, the bin bag she’d tied with shaking paws. She saw his face, imagined ashen, the way his hands would clench, the way his tail would droop. He would be kind. He would be so kind it would hurt. She swallowed the words back down. “…was just tedious,” she finished, forcing a small shrug. “Nothing dramatic. That’s half the problem, honestly. If it were more dramatic, at least I could justify coming home early.”
He snorted. “You’re not allowed to wish for drama. Every time we think oh, a bit of excitement would be nice, the universe tries to drop a political crisis on our heads.”
“Aye, fair,” she said. “I rescind my request. Bring back tedium.”
He watched her for a moment, eyes searching. Then he nodded, apparently satisfied, and went back to his food. The moment passed. The mask settled more firmly.
After they’d eaten, he insisted on doing the washing up, rolling his sleeves to his elbows, tail swishing contentedly as he hummed under his breath. Lillian stood at the counter, drying plates, stacking them in their cupboard. The domestic chore, the ordinary clink of crockery, felt oddly fragile, as if she were participating in a scene from a life that no longer quite belonged to her.
In the bathroom, the bin bag lurked behind the door. Although she couldn’t see it, she could feel it, as if it was sending out waves of heat she only could perceive. “I’ll take the rubbish down in a bit,” she said as casually as she could when Miles moved towards it later, reaching for his boots.
“I can do it,” he replied automatically.
“No, I’ve got it,” she said, too quickly.
He paused, ears tilting. “You sure?”
“Positive,” she said. “You cooked.” It was barely a joke.
He frowned slightly. “I brought chips.”
“You cooked via proxy,” she said. “Go sit. I’ll make us tea.”
He hesitated, then nodded, dropping onto the sofa with a small groan. “You’re the boss.”
“Don’t you forget it,” she replied.
She made tea—the real, strong kind they both liked—hands steady, movements precise. When the kettle clicked off, she seized the moment, grabbing the bin bag and lifting it carefully. It was heavier than she’d expected. The weight dragged at her paws, at her arms. She tied the knot once more, checking it was secure, then slipped her feet into her shoes.
“Back in a tick,” she called.
“D’you want me to—”
“Sit,” she said, with more force than she’d intended. “Your turn to be knackered. I’ll be two minutes.”
He subsided, looking faintly bemused, but didn’t argue.
The stairwell smelled of damp and someone else’s dinner. Her paws echoed on the concrete steps. She held the bin bag away from her body, though there was no risk of it leaking; she’d double‑bagged it, triple‑knotted it. Outside, the evening had deepened. Streetlights cast puddles of orange onto wet pavement. She crossed the small courtyard to the communal bins, each one yawning open like a mouth.
She lifted the lid on the general waste and dropped the bag in. It landed with a dull, heavy thump, disappearing under other bags: takeaway boxes, junk mail, the detritus of other people’s days. Whatever had come out of her, whatever might have been, was now anonymously wedged between chicken bones and supermarket packaging. She stood for a moment in the cool air, paws hanging at her sides, breath puffing faintly in front of her. Somewhere nearby, a taxi honked. A dog barked three streets over. “That’s that, then,” she said softly to no one.
On the way back up the stairs her legs felt shaky. She moved carefully, one paw at a time, as if she were carrying something fragile inside her that might still break further.
In the flat, Miles had flicked the telly on. Some panel show burbled in the background, laughter track just a beat too loud. He looked up as she came in. “You sure you’re okay?” he asked again, softer this time.
She set the mugs of tea down on the coffee table and sank onto the sofa beside him, tucking her feet under herself. His arm went around her automatically, pulling her close, his paw resting on her upper arm. Her body, traitor that it was, relaxed into him. The warmth of his side, the steady rise and fall of his chest, the familiar smell of him—dog shampoo, lavender and turpentine—wrapped around her.
“I’m fine,” she said, the lie coming more easily this time. “Just… tired.”
He kissed the top of her head. “Then we’ll do nothing tonight,” he said. “No politics, no emails. Just telly and you falling asleep halfway through and me pretending I’m not offended.”
She huffed a small laugh. “You love it when I fall asleep on you. Makes you feel important.”
“Well,” he said. “Gotta take my wins where I can.”
She rested her head against his shoulder and let the panel show wash over her. The jokes bounced off the surface of her brain without quite sinking in.
Inside, in the quiet place she kept for herself, she turned the afternoon over and over: the blood, the pain, the sound of the flush. The moment she’d deleted the app. The knot of resolve when she’d decided not to tell him.
 
## Chapter 13: Cooking and Panic

### 4.13.1
The first night they ate like that, Miles nearly cried over a pot of stew.
He’d brought the ingredients with a plan this time, not just whatever was going spare at the end of his shift. The store had a promotion on stewing beef that no one had read the labels on properly; a whole batch had been mispriced. Technically, it was supposed to be returned to the depot. Practically, he’d persuaded Mr Bahadur that community waste reduction was a worthy cause and that he’d handle the paperwork. “Title it under local outreach,” he’d said. “Looks good for audits.” Mr. Bahadur had grumbled, but he’d signed.
Now the beef sat in a big pot in the warehouse’s makeshift kitchen, alongside chopped carrots and potatoes, onions he’d softened in oil, a couple of stock cubes he’d slipped into his pocket on the way out of work. The smell rose thick and rich, cutting through the damp and dust.
Faroukh hovered nearby, suspicious. “What’s that?”
“Food,” Miles said. “Proper food.”
“Our food is proper,” Faroukh muttered.
“Yeah,” Miles said. “This is just… a bit more proper.”
He handed the ladle to Sarah. It felt important that she do the serving. She stepped up to the pot, eyes narrowed as she gauged portion sizes. The kids had lined up without being told, bowls and chipped plates held out, feet shifting. Someone had put on a tinny radio in the corner; a pop song crackled, almost drowned by the sound of their breathing.
“Small scoop first,” Sarah said. “Then seconds if there’s enough.”
She moved down the line, ladling stew, adding a chunk of bread from the loaf Faroukh had sliced. The little ones’ faces changed as the smell hit them: cautious hope stretching into something almost like joy.
Vee took a careful spoonful, blew on it, then tasted. Her ears shot straight up. “It’s hot,” she said, voice awed. “Like… very hot. Like the place with the plastic trays.”
“School dinners,” Mia supplied.
“Yeah,” Vee said. “But better.”
Miles leaned against one of the pillars, arms folded, watching. He felt oddly out of place now that the hard work was done. His contribution had been the carrying, the chopping, the stirring. The rest of it—organisation, discipline, making sure Rafi’s portion wasn’t bigger than his belly could handle—was all Sarah.
She moved differently tonight. Still wary, still sharp, but there was a looseness in her shoulders he hadn’t seen before. The lines around her mouth had softened a fraction. Each time she handed a bowl to a kid, she met their eyes, checking, counting. When the last of the little ones had been served, she glanced into the pot, did some silent maths, and nodded.
“Alright,” she said. “Seconds for anyone who wants, but if I catch you chucking it in the bin because your eyes were bigger than your stomach, I will personally make you scrub the toilets for a week.”
Groans and laughter met that. Bowls extended again.
She filled one for herself last. Someone had written on the rota board in marker: SARAH EATS WITH US OR WE STRIKE. She’d rolled her eyes earlier, but now she took a bowl and sat on a pallet among the kids, tucking her legs up to make room.
Miles watched her take the first spoonful. Her nose flared. For a second, her eyes closed. He knew that feeling. The way your body lit up when you put something substantial in it after days of scraping by. The way warmth radiated outwards from your chest, uncurling bits of you that had been clenched. He felt it second‑hand now, through them.
“You alright, mate?” Faroukh asked, squinting at him. “You look like you’ve seen a ghost.”
“Fine,” Miles said roughly. He cleared his throat. “Just hot in here.” It wasn’t hot. It was still cold enough that his breath misted if he stood by the walls. But something in his chest felt warm and sore and very, very full. He’d spent his life making sure things worked. Shelves stacked. Rota covered. Drum hardware tightened. His job, in and out of work, had been to carry weight and make sure nothing fell. It was satisfying, in its way. Necessary.
This was… different.
Here, in this drafty, half‑forgotten building, a small group of kids were eating a meal that hadn’t come out of a tin or been scraped together from the back of a bin. They were eating because he’d noticed a line on a shrinkage report and decided to cheat the system.
It felt like a bigger kind of fixing than he’d ever done before, but he wasn’t sure he liked it.
“You going to keep doing this?” Faroukh asked, trying to sound offhand and failing.
Miles considered pretending he hadn’t heard the question, or deflecting with a joke. But he looked at the boy’s face and decided he owed him more than that. “Yeah,” he said. “As long as I can.”
“What if your boss finds out?” Nate asked from the next pallet over, mouth full.
“He half knows,” Miles said. “He prefers not to think about it too hard. Saves him money on waste disposal.”
“And if someone complains?” Faroukh persisted. “Or they say you can’t keep doing it?”
Miles shrugged one shoulder. “Then I’ll find another way,” he said. “Different shop. Different angle. I’m stubborn.”
Sarah glanced up at that, eyes meeting his across the room. Something passed between them—acknowledgement, maybe. She didn’t smile, but she dipped her chin in a small nod. You’re in this now, that look said. No backing out.
He nodded back.
Later, when the bowls had been scraped clean and stacked, when the kids had drifted into little clusters—card games here, quiet reading there, Mia braiding Vee’s ears as the younger girl dozed—he found himself standing alone in the shadow of one of the support beams, listening to the sound of full bellies.
It was in the way their laughter had changed. Less brittle. Less edged with hysteria. In the way arguments flared and died quicker. In the way no one glanced towards the door every time a car went past outside, ready to grab their stuff and run. Something settled in him then, heavy and sure. It didn’t feel like a choice, not really. More like recognising that he’d already crossed a line some visits ago, and that all this talk of “helping out for a bit” had been a fiction he’d told himself to preserve some illusion of distance. He couldn’t pretend these were just “some kids” anymore. They had names now. Faces. Personal habits. He knew that Rafi always tried to sneak extra carrots, that Mia hummed under her breath when she was concentrating, that Faroukh pretended not to care and then spent his nights checking the doors. He knew that Sarah slept nearest the pallet that passed for a door, one paw always within reach of the metal bar they used to wedge it shut.
He knew too much to walk away.
“You should go,” Sarah said quietly, appearing at his elbow while he wasn’t noticing. She moved like someone who’d learned to do so without drawing attention: light on her paws, weight distributed carefully.
“Yeah,” he said. “Early shift tomorrow.”
“You always say that,” she pointed out.
“It’s always true,” he said.
She looked past him, towards the kids. “They’re… happier,” she said, as if the admission cost her something.
“Food helps,” he said. “Tend to get grumpy myself when I haven’t eaten.”
“It’s not just that,” she said. “It’s… the way they sit. Less… ready to jump.” He nodded. He’d seen it too. “You don’t have to keep talking to us,” she said suddenly. “If you don’t want. You could just drop the bags and go. We’d still use them.”
Something in her tone made him look at her properly. Her ears were angled back, not in fear, but in a sort of braced expectation. He recognised it. It was the look people wore when they were waiting for the other shoe to drop. “You want me to?” he asked.
She hesitated. “I want…” She trailed off, jaw tightening. “I don’t want the kids getting used to… this. To you. Coming in, talking, joking. It’s one thing to get used to food. You can find food other places. But people…”
“People leave,” he finished for her, softly.
She flinched, almost imperceptibly. “Yeah.”
He could have told her then that he wasn’t like the people who’d left her. That he’d stuck with Lillian for sixteen years through auditions and jobs and rejections and relocations. That once he’d decided something was his responsibility, he didn’t put it down. But words like that sounded like promises, and promises sounded like lies if you’d heard enough of them broken. “Alright,” he said. “We can do it your way. I’ll still talk to you when I’m here, mind. I’m not going to start pretending you’re not in the room.”
Her shoulders lowered a fraction. “That’s not what I meant,” she muttered.
“I know,” he said. “But if you want less… show, I can do less show. I’ll cut back on my stand‑up routine.”
“Good,” she said. “Your jokes are rubbish.”
He smiled. “Everyone’s a critic.”
He picked up his empty bags and headed for the door. At the pallet, he paused. “If you need anything specific,” he said, “you can leave a note.”
Outside, the night air hit him, damp and cool. He lifted his face to it for a moment, letting the drizzle soak into the fur along his muzzle. His arms smelled of onions and stock; his clothes smelled of warehouse. He imagined what Lillian would say when he walked in like this.
You’ve been feeding half the city again, aye?
He couldn’t help but smile.
On the walk home, he thumbed out a text.
Kids had proper dinner tonight. Like, actual stew.  
We need to talk about what comes next.  
He hesitated before adding a line.
Feels like we’re past the point of no return, that.  
He hit send before he could overthink it. By the time he reached Mandela Street, she’d replied.
We were past that the minute you walked into that warehouse, love.  
Tea’ll be on. Bring your stories.  
He looked up at the window of their flat. Light glowed through the curtains. The world outside was still as sharp and unfair as ever, but there, in that small, yellow‑walled room, there would be warmth, and Lillian’s voice, and the beginnings of a plan that might be called a family, if you squinted.
He squared his shoulders, shook some of the rain off his fur, and climbed the stairs.

### 4.13.2
Juno let the phone ring itself into silence. It was the third call that night. The screen lit her small flat in a pale, accusing blue: MAM. Then, a minute later, DAD. Then MAM again, as if repetition could turn obligation into affection.
She sat on the edge of the sofa in her hoodie and leggings, flute case on the coffee table like a closed mouth. Her hands were damp with sweat. Her chest felt too tight for the air available.
She watched the call end. Watched the screen go dark.
The quiet after was not relief. It was a vacuum.
Her heart thudded once, hard, and then began to race. Juno tried to breathe the way Lillian had taught her.
Four in.
Four out.
Her body didn’t listen.
Her stomach rose as if she were going to be sick. Heat rushed up her throat. Her fingers tingled, then went strangely numb, as if she’d been holding ice.
No, she thought. Not now. Not again.
Her phone vibrated with a new message. Her mother.
Pick up. We need to talk.
The words on the screen made her vision blur at the edges.
Need to talk meant: I’ve decided you’re mine again.
Need to talk meant: We can do this nicely if you behave.
Need to talk meant: You don’t get to set the terms.
Juno’s hands started shaking.
