She stood too quickly, the room tilting. She paced to the kitchenette and back, as if movement could outrun memory. Her tail whipped once behind her, agitated. She pressed her palm to the counter. “I’m twenty,” she whispered into the empty room.
The words sounded absurd. Twenty didn’t mean safe.
The phone rang again. This time she hit decline so hard her thumb hurt.
The silence that followed was immediate, brutal.
Her chest clenched tighter. The air felt thick. Her breath made a thin, high sound that didn’t belong to her.
She slid down the cupboard doors to the floor, knees up, arms wrapped around them. Her fur along her spine prickled with sweat. She tried to breathe and couldn’t find a rhythm. The room narrowed.
Her mind did that thing it always did when she panicked: it found a door and opened it.
A hallway in Cork. A raised voice. A plate breaking. Her mum’s hand on her shoulder, not gentle, and the message underneath it: don’t you embarrass me. Juno pressed her forehead to her knees. “Stop,” she told herself.
Her body didn’t care.
Somewhere in the mess of sound and blood pressure and shame, she remembered Lillian’s voice: You don’t have to answer them. You can answer yourself.
Juno fumbled for her phone with shaking hands.
She didn’t call her mother. She didn’t call her father. She opened her contacts and scrolled until she found LILLIAN.
Her thumb hovered.
She couldn’t bear the idea of being a burden. Then another breath caught in her throat, sharp and panicked, and the thought broke.
She hit call.
It rang once.
“Juno?” Lillian answered, voice already alert. “Love? What’s happened?” Juno tried to speak. All she managed was a thin, broken sound. “Alright,” Lillian said immediately, no hesitation. “Listen to me. Put your back against something solid. Floor’s fine. Aye. There you go. Now—breathe with me, yeah? I’ll do it and you follow. One, two, three, four—”
Juno clung to the sound of her like it was a rail. Her lungs started to remember how.

## 4.13.3
Asim knew Toby was late the moment the flat started to feel too quiet.
Not silence—London didn’t do silence. There was always a bus exhaling outside, always a neighbour’s television bleeding through the wall, always the faint hum of a fridge that sounded like a distant amp. But Toby’s absence had its own texture.
Asim sat on the sofa with his guitar across his lap, fingers moving in lazy patterns over the strings. He wasn’t properly practising. He was occupying his hands so his mind didn’t gnaw itself raw. He was wearing the indigo kaftan Toby bought him the previous year: cozy, colored, warm, still smelling of both their scents combined, plus the wildflower-scented soap Toby loved to use.
The kettle had boiled and clicked off ten minutes ago. He hadn’t made the tea. His phone sat on the armrest.
Miles’s earlier call replayed in his head like a riff he couldn’t stop returning to.
Kids.
Warehouse.
Strays.
Asim had laughed at first, because that’s what you did when your friend brought you a problem the size of the city. He’d made a joke about Miles adopting the whole of London one damaged child at a time.
Then Miles had gone quiet.
Asim had heard it. That sound. The one Miles made when he’d already decided.
Asim’s fingers stilled on the strings.
He got up and crossed to the table by the window, where a folded newspaper sat unopened. He’d picked it up at the corner shop on autopilot, mostly for the crossword. The headline on the front page made his stomach tighten.
STRAYS: “FERAL” ANTHRO YOUTH OVERWHELM SERVICES
There was a grainy photo of a girl in a hoodie, face blurred, strings of fur slipping out of the blur showing her anthro identity. A police officer’s hand hovered near her elbow. The caption used words like “antisocial” and “burden” and “rising costs.”
Asim opened the paper. Inside, it was worse.
Quotes from politicians who’d never spent a night cold. Stats, presented like the kids were an infestation. A paragraph about “urban anthro communities” that read like an anthropologist describing monkeys.
Asim’s throat went hot. He kept reading anyway.
There—halfway down the page—was a mention of a warehouse near Coldharbour Lane. “Reports of minors sheltering on industrial estate property,” it said, and the rest was speculation and handwringing.
Asim stared at the words until they swam.
So it’s in the papers now, he thought.
Miles wasn’t just helping hungry kids. He was stepping into something the whole city had decided to narrate as a problem.
Asim’s phone buzzed. Toby.
Sorry baby. Train delayed. Be home soon. Don’t start without me x
Asim stared at the message for a moment.
He typed back.
Where are you?
Then deleted it.
He typed:
Ok. Hurry.
He hit send. He went back to the sofa and picked up the guitar. He played a chord harder than he meant to.
In his head, the warehouse became a place with walls and children and a girl named Sarah, and Asim felt the old, familiar urge to do something—anything—when the world got cruel.
When Toby finally came through the door, damp-haired and apologetic, Asim already had the newspaper open on the table. “We need to talk,” Asim said.
Toby blinked. “Oh God. Did I—”
“Not you,” Asim said, and then softened. “Not you. Miles.”
He tapped the headline with one claw.
Toby’s face changed as he read. “Oh,” he whispered.
“Yeah,” Asim said. “Oh.”

## 4.13.4
In the morning, when the warehouse was half awake—kids rubbing sleep from their eyes, the kettle rattling on the stove, someone arguing quietly over whose turn it was to fetch water—Sarah sat on an upturned crate with a biro and a torn exercise book and did the thing that made her feel like the building was less likely to collapse.
Inventory.
Faroukh hovered nearby, pretending not to hover.
“Read the top one,” Sarah said.
Faroukh peered at her scribbles. “Food,” he said.
“Good,” Sarah replied. “So what do we have?”
Faroukh sighed like she’d asked him to recite poetry. Then he started. “Tins,” he said. “Beans, tomatoes, soup. Pasta. Rice.”
“How much rice?” Sarah asked.
Faroukh glanced at the corner where the sack sat, rolled down and tied with string. “Half a bag.”
Sarah wrote it down in block capitals, neat and hard, like she could force the world to behave through pen pressure.
“Bread,” Faroukh continued. “Two loaves left.”
Sarah’s eyes narrowed. “There were four yesterday.”
Faroukh shrugged. “Kids ate,” he said.
Sarah stared. Then, because she didn’t have the energy for a fight this early, she nodded once and moved on. “Soap,” she said.
Faroukh made a face. “That’s not supplies,” he argued.
“It is if you want no one getting scabies again,” Sarah replied.
Faroukh’s mouth tightened. He walked over to the plastic crate they kept the “clean things” in—combs, wipes, a half-used bottle of shampoo, toothbrushes in a jam jar. He picked up a comb and squinted at it. There was hair stuck between the teeth. There was also something darker. “Who’s is this?” Faroukh demanded.
Sarah glanced up. “Which?”
“This,” Faroukh said, holding it between two claws like it was a dead rat.
Sarah shrugged. “Probably Rafi’s,” she said. “Or Vee’s. Or mine. Does it matter?”
“It does if it’s disgusting,” Faroukh snapped.
Sarah’s eyes narrowed. “We live in a warehouse,” she reminded him.
“We can still not be gross,” Faroukh shot back.
Sarah leaned forward, elbows on her knees, pen poised. “What are you actually saying?” she asked.
Faroukh blew out a breath, frustrated at himself. “I’m saying… hygiene,” he said. “If we share combs and brushes, we share… things. Fleas. Lice. Fungal stuff. You know.”
Sarah stared at him. Then she snorted. “You’re worried about lice,” she said.
Faroukh’s ears heated. “I’m worried about us itching ourselves to death,” he said. “And before you say it, yes, I know, we have bigger problems. But bigger problems don’t stop you having skin.”
Sarah’s mouth twitched. It was almost a smile. “Alright,” she said. “So what do we do?”
Faroukh blinked, caught off guard by the fact she’d listened. “We sort them,” he said. “Personal ones and shared ones. If it touches your head, it’s yours. If it touches someone else’s head, it’s theirs.”
Sarah’s brow furrowed. “What about little ones who lose stuff?”
“Then we teach them,” Faroukh said. “That’s the point of the inventory. Not just counting. Teaching.”
Sarah’s gaze moved across the warehouse.
Kids in blankets.
Kids in too-big hoodies.
Kids who didn’t know, sometimes, that a body needed care and not just survival.
She swallowed.
“Callum!” she shouted. Callum, the collie-spaniel cross who memorised bus routes like prayers, looked up from the corner where he was tracing a map on cardboard. “Come here,” Sarah said.
He padded over, wary.
Sarah held out the comb Faroukh had been inspecting. “Whose is this?” she asked.
Callum stared at it like it was a trick question. “It’s… everybody’s,” he said cautiously.
Faroukh made a noise of disgust.
Sarah looked at Callum, expression softening a fraction. “We’re going to change that,” she said. “You’re good at organising things. You’re going to help me.”
Callum’s ears pricked. “Like a rota?” he asked.
“Like a rota,” Sarah confirmed.
Callum nodded like he’d been given a purpose.
Faroukh watched the exchange.
“Okay,” Sarah said, ticking boxes on her list. “Combs and brushes: we label. Toothbrushes: we already label. Soap: we ration but we actually use it. Wipes: what’s left?”
Faroukh dug through the crate. “Three packets,” he said.
“Sanitary pads?” Sarah asked, without looking up.
Faroukh froze with a packet of baby wipes in his paw. He blinked at her. “What?”
Sarah looked up finally, expression blank. “Sanitary pads,” she repeated. “How many.”
Faroukh’s face did something odd—horror mixed with bafflement. “Why are you asking me?” he demanded.
“Because you’re holding the list,” Sarah said, as if that settled everything.
Faroukh stared at her for a long moment.
Then, because the warehouse demanded function over awkwardness, he said, “One packet.”
Sarah nodded, wrote it down.
Faroukh watched her hand move, then cleared his throat. “You… you need more than that,” he said, voice stiff.
Sarah’s brow furrowed. “No,” she said. “I don’t.”
Faroukh blinked again. “What do you mean, you don’t?”
Sarah shrugged, as if discussing tins. “I haven’t had… that,” she said.
Faroukh stared.
Melanie, listening from a few feet away, snorted. Josh choked on his tea.
Faroukh turned slowly back to Sarah. “You’re sixteen,” he said.
“Yeah,” Sarah replied.
“And you haven’t—”
Sarah’s expression tightened. “No,” she said. “I don’t know. I don’t… I don’t know what it’s supposed to be like, okay? Nobody told me. And when I was little, the women in Connor’s lot used to… joke, and I didn’t ask.”
Faroukh’s anger softened into something helpless. He rubbed a paw over his muzzle. “Right,” he said, voice rough. “Okay. We’ll… ask someone. Lillian would know.”
Sarah’s eyes flashed. “We’re not asking Lillian about my… body,” she snapped.
“Then who?” Faroukh demanded.
Sarah stared at her list.
The pen hovered.
Her voice came out smaller than she wanted. “Does it… does it matter?” she asked.
Faroukh went still. “What do you mean?” he said.
Sarah swallowed. She kept her eyes on the paper because looking at people when you said things like this made you feel like you might tip over. “I mean,” she said slowly, choosing the words like she chose routes, “maybe I’m just… broken. Or maybe I’m normal. I don’t know. I don’t know anything. Like—” She swallowed again. “Like loving. I don’t know what that is either.”
Faroukh’s breath caught. Around them, the warehouse kept moving—kettle whistles, kids talking, someone laughing.
Sarah’s world narrowed to the biro in her paw and the sting behind her eyes.
Faroukh sat down on the crate opposite her without asking.
He didn’t touch her.
He just sat, knees drawn up, shoulders hunched. “You love the kids,” he said, quietly.
Sarah flinched. “No,” she said too fast. “I just… I keep them alive.”
“That’s love,” Faroukh replied, voice rough. “I don’t care what poets say.”
Sarah stared at him.
Faroukh’s jaw clenched. “You get up first,” he said. “You count tins. You make sure Vee’s got her rabbit toy. You scream at Nate when he tries to steal bread and then you make sure he still eats. You stayed up all night when Rafi had a cough because you were scared he’d stop breathing.”
Sarah’s throat tightened. She stared hard at the paper until the letters blurred. “That’s not love,” she whispered. “That’s… responsibility.”
Faroukh nodded once, like he’d been expecting the answer. “Sometimes it’s the same thing,” he said.
Sarah’s mouth twisted, half-sneer, half-sob. “That’s stupid,” she muttered.
“Yeah,” Faroukh said. “It is. Welcome to being alive.”
Sarah let out a shaky laugh.
It sounded wrong in her own ears.
But it existed.
She blinked hard, then picked up the pen again. “Right,” she said, voice steadier. “Inventory.”
Faroukh nodded. He picked up the comb again like it wasn’t an insult.
 
## Chapter 14: Promotion and Relief 

### 4.14.1
Miles was in aisle seven, the tinned goods canyon, clipboard balanced on one paw, pen tucked behind his ear. The fluorescent lights buzzed overhead; the shelves smelled faintly of dust and tomato sauce. He had fallen into a rhythm: count, mark, straighten, move on.
“Miles!” came the bark from the end of the aisle.
He looked up. Mr Bahadur, the store manager, stood there in a too‑tight shirt and a tie with cartoon vegetables on it, clipboard of his own clutched to his chest like a shield.
“Yeah?” Miles said. “You alright?”
“Got five minutes?” Bahadur asked. “Office.”
The brisk, falsely casual tne made the fur along Miles’s neck prickle. Five minutes in the office rarely meant anything simple. He pictured rota changes, budget cuts, some new corporate policy about customer engagement that would involve him handing out flyers at the door in the rain.
“Ok,” he said. “Let me just finish this shelf and I’ll be up.”
“Now would be better,” Bahadur said, already half‑turned away.
Miles set the clipboard down, aligning it carefully with the edge of the shelf to ground himself. “On my way.”
The office above the shop floor was a small, overheated box with a window that looked out over the aisles like a control tower. A faded poster about health and safety peeled at one corner; the computer on the desk hummed with effort. Bahadur shut the door behind them and gestured at the spare chair. “Sit, sit,” he said. “How’s things?”
Miles lowered himself into the chair, which creaked under his weight. His tail had to tuck awkwardly around the legs; the space was clearly designed with humans in mind, like most things. “Fine,” he said. “Busy. You know well how it is.”
“Yeah, yeah,” Bahadur said, waving a hand. “Listen, this won’t take long. Got a call from regional this morning.”
Miles’s stomach did a small, anticipatory twist. He sat up a bit straighter despite himself.
“They’re restructuring the management rota,” Bahadur went on. “New emphasis on diversity and inclusion.” He said the words like they were a company slogan, which they probably were. “We’ve been asked to identify promising colleagues from, you know…” he made a vague, encompassing gesture at Miles’s fur “…under‑represented backgrounds for development opportunities.”
There it was. The thing Miles had been waiting to hear ever since the company had started putting anthro faces on their posters about community engagement. “Right,” he said, keeping his voice neutral.
Bahadur shuffled some papers, found the one he wanted, and glanced at it as if it might lend him authority. Yet he looked at Miles with steady and warm eyes. “They’ve seen your numbers,” he said. “Stock losses down since you took over the warehouse checks. Staff feedback’s good. Customers mention you by name on the survey thing. Friendly,” he read, squinting. “Helpful. Calm under pressure.”
“That’s just code for doesn’t shout when the tills crash,” Miles said automatically.
Bahadur chuckled. “Well, quite. I knew you were the right person when I suggested you for that management workshop, years ago; you’ve got what it takes, Miles, and people know it. They’re impressed. Regional’s looking to promote a couple of people to deputy manager over the next quarter, and you’re on their list.”
The words hung in the air between them for a moment. Deputy manager. More money. More responsibility. More meetings in rooms like this, with the air slightly too warm and the coffee slightly too bitter.
Also: more leverage.
Miles felt the shape of that thought before he fully articulated it. Leverage with schedules, with donations, with what could be written off and sent quietly out the back door to places that needed it more than this branch did.
He pictured the warehouse. The Strays. Sarah’s too‑thin face, the jutted angle of her shoulder blades under a jacket that had once been cream and was now a tired grey. The smaller kids’ eyes when he turned up with a van full of food.
“What would that actually look like?” he asked, buying himself time.
Bahadur brightened, relieved to have moved onto the script he’d clearly rehearsed. “Bit of training first,” he said. “I know, more training, but it’s… mandatory. I don’t make the rules. Online modules, couple of days at head office. You’d shadow me on rota planning, deliveries, that sort of thing. We’d bump your pay grade.” He named a figure. Miles did the mental arithmetic automatically: rent, bills, the small cushion in their savings account growing a little faster. The mortgage. “We’re really keen,” the man added. “Head office is very focused on, uh, reflecting our customer base at all levels of the organisation. You’d be an excellent, you know…” He hesitated, clearly searching for the right phrase. “Role model.” Anthro on the poster, Miles translated silently. The good dog on the leaflet. Proof they weren’t racist, classist, speciesist, or any of the other things the tabloids muttered about in code. “They’ve, ah, mentioned possibly using you in some of the internal comms stuff as well,” Bahadur went on. “Profiles of successful colleagues, that sort of thing. Only if you’re comfortable, of course.”
Miles thought of the last company newsletter he’d seen. Smiling faces in badly lit photos, quotes in italics about “team spirit” and “going the extra mile”. He imagined his own snout under a headline about “anthro leadership”, the implication that his species was the interesting part, not his competence. His tail flicked once, betraying his irritation. He forced it still. “Right,” he said again.
Bahadur leaned forward, elbows on his desk. “Look, Miles,” he said, dropping his voice as if they were co‑conspirators. “Off the record, I think you’d be good at it. You already do more than half the work. This just means you’d get paid for it. And… well, it’s the way things are going, isn’t it? Diversity. Better to be on board, shape it, than have it done at you. I know this is all… furwashing, right? It’s just corporate speak. But you can try and… exploit it before they exploit you. I know a thing or two about this, Miles,” he added, hinting at his Indian heritage. “They all do it with us. They don’t see us as people. They do it on humans like me for my skin, my family, the place I come from and so on; they do it on you because you have fur, paws and a muzzle. They see us as… tools. Scores. I don’t like it, I can’t accept it, but I can exploit it just like they exploit me. Do you understand what I mean?”
The phrasing—done at you—was clumsy, but the core of it made something tighten in Miles’s chest.
Be on board, shape it.
He thought of the adoption section of the reference guide he and Lillian had skimmed in bed a few nights earlier. Married couples prioritised. Stable employment favoured. “Anthro applicants welcome,” the leaflet had said, the font trying too hard. He thought of the woman at the council office last time they’d gone in about volunteering, how her gaze had lingered on his fur, on his height, before flicking to Lillian’s weight, then away.
He thought of Sarah’s drawings taped up in the warehouse office, the ones he and Lillian had found on their last visit: two adult figures sketched in charcoal, one broad and badger‑shaped, the other taller with a Shepherd’s ears. The smaller silhouettes clustered around them like satellites. “Is there a reason you’re telling me this now?” he asked slowly. “As in, do I have to decide today?”
Bahadur shook his head. “No, no. Plenty of time. They’ll want an answer in a couple of weeks, mind. They’ve got targets.” He grimaced. “They always have targets.”
“Of course they do,” Miles said.
He sat back, letting the idea settle. Deputy manager. More pay. More clout. More corporate nonsense. More sideways glances from customers who still weren’t used to anthros as anything but shelf‑stackers or novelty acts.
He hated the thought of being trotted out as a success story for a system that still made it harder for people like him and Lillian and Sarah. But if he took it, he might be able to bend that system, even a little. He knew how stock moved, how budgets were coded, where the slack in the numbers lived. He already was manipulating numbers to feed Strays, so he knew how and what to do and what it looked like.
If he said no, someone else would take it. Probably a keen human graduate who talked a good game about “diversity” in meetings and then quietly signed off on policies that hurt anthros at the coalface. “I’ll think about it,” he said at last.
Bahadur’s relief was visible. “Good man,” he said. “Take the weekend. Talk to your partner. Let me know Monday, yeah?”
“Aye,” Miles said. “I’ll do that.”
“Oh, by the way, Miles. I’ve checked your… uh… numbers on waste management. I don’t know what you are doing, but I trust you, Miles. You’re a good person, and I trust that whatever you do is fine and good. So I won’t check further or enquiry about it.” Bahadur’s gaze hardened. “I can trust you with it?”
Miles nodded. “Yes, sir. Thanks for your trust.”
As he left the office and walked back down the stairs, the shop floor noise swelled around him again: tills beeping, kids whining, someone arguing about multi‑buy offers at checkout three.
He finished counting tins. His paws moved automatically; his mind was elsewhere, running numbers, scenarios. Extra money each month. How quickly they could reach the threshold the adoption agency pamphlet had suggested. What it would mean for his hours, for the time he spent at the warehouse.
When his break came, he took his tea out the back by the loading bay. The air there was always cooler, tinged with diesel and cardboard. Pallets were stacked in neat rows, the mundane geometry of his working life. He leaned against the wall, mug cradled in both paws, and let his eyes travel over the familiar shapes: crates, cages, the ramp down to the yard where delivery lorries rumbled in.
He imagined another lorry, the one he borrowed when he took food to the Strays. The calculations he already did—what he could fit, what expiry dates he could justify shuffling.
As deputy manager, the numbers would be his. He’d see the spreadsheets before they got sanitised. He’d know exactly how much surplus there was, how much could be diverted without triggering alarms. Weaponise tokenism, he thought, the phrase surfacing from an article Asim had sent him months ago. Take the spotlight they tried to shine on you and angle it somewhere useful.
He pulled his phone out of his pocket, thumb hovering over Lillian’s name.
Tell her now, he thought. Get her reaction before you twist yourself into knots about it.
He pictured her at the academy, in some cramped practice room, coaxing long tones out of a nervous clarinettist. She’d had that look lately, a tiredness around the eyes, lines in her forehead deeper than they used to be. He’d asked if she was okay; she’d brushed him off with jokes about students and politics. He didn’t want to add another weight to her shoulders mid‑afternoon. Better to talk properly tonight, over tea, when they could spread the numbers out together on the kitchen table the way they always did.
He slid the phone back into his pocket.
Later, then.
He took a sip of tea. It had gone lukewarm already.

### 4.14.2
Felix hated hospitals. He hated their smell—bleach and plastic and other people’s fear. He hated the waiting rooms with their cheerful posters and their tired magazines and their clocks that always felt like they were ticking at you personally. He hated, most of all, that a building could make his mum look small.
Linda Murano sat in the chair beside him with her hands folded in her lap like she was being polite to the room. She wore her best coat—navy wool, buttons polished—and a scarf that made her look like she was going to church rather than oncology. “Stop frowning,” she whispered.
Felix looked at her. “I’m not.”
She raised her eyebrows in a way that said: don’t lie to your mother, even if she is human and you are a rat and the world is stupid about it. “I’m just concentrating,” Felix muttered.
“On what?”
“On not killing the next person who says how are we today? like it’s a weather report,” Felix said.
Linda snorted softly. “You always were dramatic.”
“I learned from the best,” Felix replied.
They sat.
Minutes passed. Then more.
Felix’s knee bounced.
Linda reached out and put her hand over it, palm warm, a quiet command. “Fermo,” she murmured.
Felix forced himself still.
When the doctor finally called her name, Felix stood too.
“Only one of us needs the scan results,” Linda said.
“Yeah,” Felix replied. “And I’m the only one who can punch someone if they’re stupid about it.”
Linda’s mouth twitched. “Fair.”
The office of Dr. Braithwaite, Linda’s oncologist, was small and bland, a computer on the desk and an anatomical poster on the wall showing lungs in colours nobody’s lungs were ever meant to be.
The doctor spoke gently. He used the word ‘stable’ first. Then ‘no progression.’ Then, because doctors liked to build relief in careful increments, he said, “In fact, the lungs appear to be healthy. No other masses. All scans are clean. Ms Murano, you’re tumor-free.”
Felix blinked.
His mum’s hand tightened around her bag strap. “So,” Linda said, voice oddly calm, “it’s… it worked.”
“Yes,” the doctor said.
Felix felt something in his chest crack.
Outside the office, in the corridor, Felix leaned his forehead briefly against the wall like he could hide there. Linda touched his cheek. “Hey,” she said. “I’m still here.”
Felix swallowed hard. “Yeah,” he managed. “Yeah. You are.”
“Come on,” she said briskly, the Italian immigrant matriarch snapping back into place. “We get coffee. We tell your nonna before she starts a pilgrimage to Lourdes.”
Felix let out a laugh that turned into something wet.
He pulled his phone out.
He didn’t call the band.
He didn’t call Milla.
He called the one person who would understand this particular relief without making it pretty. “Asim,” he said when the wild dog picked up. “You’re not going to believe this.”

### 4.14.3
On the bus home, he stood in the same spot he always did, paws braced, tail tucked in so he wouldn’t whack anyone’s knees. He let the sway of the road lull his body while his mind ran on. Deputy manager. It felt like someone else’s title. He pictured telling his mum on the phone; she’d be proud and would make a fuss about it. He pictured telling Asim, who would immediately come up with three ways to use it for the band’s benefit. He pictured telling Sarah, someday, in the warehouse office: “I can get more food now. More blankets.”
And he pictured telling Lillian.
He imagined her reaction. She’d stiffen, maybe, at the words “diversity” and “role model.” She’d make a sharp comment about window‑dressing. But she’d also see the practical side. She always did. She’d ask how much extra they’d have, what it meant for their mortgage prospects, for the adoption forms. He thought of their conversation a week earlier, lying in bed in the dark, staring at the ceiling as they’d talked about “later”. About houses with more than one bedroom. About no longer feeling like guests in their own country’s systems.
The bus juddered to a stop near their flat. He climbed off and cut through the side street, past the graffiti‑tagged bins and the smell of fried chicken. At the bottom of their building’s stairs, he paused, key in paw. It occurred to him, suddenly, that this conversation—about work, about promotion, about using the company’s new love of diversity buzzwords to their advantage—might be the beginning of a whole new chapter for them.
He wanted it to be a good thing. Wanted to bring her something concrete, something hopeful, something other than the endless grind of fighting for crumbs from systems that had never been built for them. He took a breath, squared his shoulders, and climbed.
Inside, the flat was warm. The air smelled faintly of bleach and chips. The bin by the door was empty; the bag must already have gone down.
“That you?” Lillian called from the kitchen.
“Yeah,” he said, hanging up his coat. “Got news.”
He meant to lead with it. That had been the plan on the bus. But when he stepped into the kitchen and saw her leaning against the counter, mug in paw, expression tired but carefully bright, the words caught.
“News?” she echoed. “Good news, I hope.”
“Maybe,” he said. “Bit complicated.”
He told her then, about Bahadur, about regional, about “diversity initiatives” and deputy manager titles and training weekends. As he spoke, he watched her face.
She listened, head tilted, eyes sharp. At the mention of “role model” her mouth tightened briefly; at the salary bump she raised her eyebrows; at the potential for “sorting out more of that waste stock, properly” she nodded slowly.
“So they finally noticed you’re holding the place together,” she said when he finished. “Took them long enough.”
“Well,” he said. “They noticed my fur and my teeth as much as anything.”
“Then we make that work for us,” she replied, matter‑of‑fact. “If you want it.”
“That’s the bit I’m stuck on,” he admitted. “Feels… weird. Being the token.”
“You already are,” she said, not unkindly. “This just puts it in writing. And puts a bit more cash behind it.”
He huffed a small laugh. “When you put it like that.”
She stepped closer, resting a paw briefly on his chest. The contact was light, almost formal. “If you can stomach the meetings,” she said, “and if they’re going to do it anyway, I’d rather it be you than some guy who thinks diversity is making sure there’s a woman in the Christmas party photos.”
“You’re right,” he said.
“And you deserve, it, love. You work as hard as a horse.”
He wanted to pull her into a proper hug then, to bury his nose in her fur and let the day’s tension bleed out. But something in her posture—an almost imperceptible stiffness, the way her weight stayed more on one foot than the other—held him back. “You sure you’re okay?” he asked, for what felt like the hundredth time in recent days.
“Aye,” she said. “Just… long day. Tell you what, you say yes to the promotion, and when your fancy new pay packet comes through we’ll celebrate with something other than chips.”
He smiled. “Deal.”
He said yes on Monday.
He emailed Bahadur from the staff computer before his shift, fingers hovering over the keyboard for a moment before he hit send. Happy to accept the development opportunity, he typed, using their language. Looking forward to contributing to the store’s continued success, he continued, spouting corporate lingo. He cringed at it, then shrugged. If he was going to play their game, he might as well learn their moves. He could translate later, in the warehouse, in the adoption office, in the quiet places where real decisions got made.
When he walked back onto the shop floor, the shelves looked the same, the customers the same mix of busy parents, students, pensioners. Nothing had changed, outwardly.
Inside, something had shifted. He had stepped a little further into a system he mistrusted, not out of faith in it, but because he believed he could use it.
Later, when he lay in bed beside Lillian, listening to her breathing while she stared silently at the ceiling, he thought about the future he was trying to engineer. A future where their names on an adoption form came with job titles that made social workers relax, where surplus food went first to kids like Sarah instead of the bin.

 
## Chapter 15: Connor Attacks 

### 4.15.1
At first it was silence.
On a normal afternoon the warehouse had a constant background noise: kids arguing over cards, someone kicking a ball against a wall, a baby crying, a teenager playing music too loud on a phone with speakers. Even in the middle of winter, with everyone huddled in their coats, there was sound.
That day, when Sarah pushed open the side door after a supply run to the cash‑and‑carry, the air felt wrong. Thick. Quiet.
She paused just inside, bags cutting into her paws, ears straining.
No laughter. No shouting. No music. Just the distant drip of water from the leaking roof and the faint hum of traffic outside. Her fur prickled: that was not normal. “Oi?” she called. “Where is everyone?”
Her voice echoed back at her from the concrete, too loud in the stillness.
She dropped the bags where she stood and moved forward, steps light, claws clicking softly. Her tail stood high, every sense alert. When she reached the main hall, the damage hit her like a punch.
The crates that usually held their food stocks were tipped over, contents spilled and trampled. Broken biscuits ground into the floor. A bag of rice burst open, grains scattered like teeth. The cardboard boxes Miles had carefully labelled last week were ripped, the marker pen words smudged. The youngest kids huddled in a corner, pressed together like a flock, eyes wide. Mateo the badger had a smear of something on his cheek that might have been sauce or blood. Rafi clutched a dented tin to her chest like a toy. Faroukh, Daisy and Anika, the older kids, stood in front of them, shoulders squared, as if they’d tried to form a human shield. Bruises were already starting to bloom on Faroukh’s cheek.
“What happened?” Sarah demanded, voice sharper than she meant. “Who did this?”
“Them,” Rafi whispered, pointing with a trembling paw.
Sarah followed the line of her finger.
The older Strays occupied the other side of the space. Connor’s pack. His ears pricked as Sarah entered.
“Afternoon, Princess,” he said. “Nice of you to join us.”
“What did you do?” Sarah asked, keeping her voice level.
“Just taking our due,” Connor said. “You lot have been hogging the good stuff. Thought we’d even it up.”
He jerked his chin at the mess on the floor. Several tins were dented beyond use; a carton of long‑life milk had burst, slicking the concrete with white that would sour by morning.
Sarah’s paws curled into fists. “We had rules,” she said. “We divvy things up. Nobody’s hogging anything—”
“You think you can keep this place going without us?” he asked. “You and your little charity cases?”
“We’ve managed so far,” she said.
“Things gotta change,” Connor said. “Gotta keep up with the times.” He gestured behind him with his chin.
That was when Sarah saw it.
The pinboard in the office doorway, visible from here if you knew where to look. Her drawing, the one she’d pinned up yesterday, torn in half.
Half of Lillian’s face remained, glasses askew, mouth mid‑smile. The rest dangled from a thumbtack by one corner, charcoal lines smeared, the paper crumpled. Something cold settled in Sarah’s stomach.
“We were just redecorating,” Connor said lightly. “Didn’t figure you’d mind. You’ve got more paper, right? You can draw them again.”
His tone hit its mark. Them. No names. No acknowledgement that she’d given those adults shape on the page, that she’d taken the risk of letting hope have a body. Behind her, she felt the younger kids stir. They didn’t know what the drawing had been, but they heard the taunt. “Leave it,” she said, voice flat. “You’ve made your point.”
“I’m not sure I have,” Connor replied. “See, we’ve been talking. Me and the lads, all of them. And we reckon it’s about time the food situation got… recalibrated.” He walked slowly towards her, boots crunching on broken biscuits. “This place?” he went on. “It’s valuable. Roof, four walls, stash. People bring stuff. People like your dog man and your big badger. They turn up because you smile at them and say thanks and make them feel all heroic. That’s a skill. I respect that.”
He stopped an arm’s length away, close enough that she could see the faint scar along his jaw. “But you’re soft,” he said, quietly now. “You feed the kids first. You give away the good blankets. You’re making them weak.”
“They’re children,” Sarah said. “They’re already weak. That’s why we look after them.”
“Nah,” Connor said. “They’re… liabilities. Mouths to feed. You want to survive out here, you cut your dead weight.”
There was a flicker, just for a second, of something in his eyes that looked like an old hurt. Sarah had heard bits of his story, passed along in whispers: care home fights, police station cells, a foster placement that had ended with a smashed window and a court date. “We’re not cutting anyone,” she said. “You want a share, you help. You watch the kids, you fix the roof, you do runs. You don’t smash up what we’ve got. We already set things up, you can’t—”
Connor’s smile sharpened. “Can’t what, exactly?” he asked. “Tell you what to do? And who’s gonna stop me: you and your toy soldiers, there? You’ll tell your big friends?”
Sarah’s cheeks burned under her fur.
He’s right, a treacherous little voice in her head whispered. You rely on them. On Miles’s van and Lillian’s bags of paints. On whatever they can scrounge from their world of shops and salaries and heating.
Connor leaned in closer, his breath hot and smelling faintly of cigarettes. “Here’s how it’s going to go,” he said, low enough that only she could hear. “When the dog man comes next time, you tell him to leave the crates in the yard. No more quarters: we take first pick. You can have what’s left.”
Sarah’s stomach lurched. “That’s not happening.”
“It is,” he said. “Because if it doesn’t…” He let the sentence trail off, glancing meaningfully at the huddle of younger kids behind her. Kimi had started to cry silently, tears turning the smudge on her cheek into a streak. One of the other girls hugged her, whispering something into her ear.
Sarah’s heart hammered.
If she said no, if she pushed, Connor would push back. Maybe not right now, with everyone watching, but later. At night. In some dark corner of the building. A missing stash. A mysteriously broken ankle. A fire set where no one was looking.
If she said yes, she let him turn the Strays into his resource. Her leadership into a joke. The kids into bargaining chips. She stalled for time.
“We’ll talk about it,” she said. “Me and the others. We’ll—”
Connor shook his head. “No committees,” he said. “No meetings. Just you. You’re the one they listen to. You say yes, we’re good. You say no…” He shrugged, then straightened, turning his back on her to survey the damage as if he was the one in charge. “Should clean this up,” he said loudly. “Can’t have the little ones slipping and breaking their necks. Bad optics.” The joke got another round of laughter from his crew. He jerked his chin at his lads. “Come on. We’ve got other places to be.” They filed out in a loose, confident line, brushing past her without a glance. 
When they were gone, the silence settled again, heavier.
Sarah’s paws were still clenched. Her claws had dug crescents into her own palms. “You okay?” Faroukh asked, voice small.
She made herself unclench her fists, flexing her fingers. “Course I am,” she said. “We’ve got a mess to sort, that’s all.”
She moved, because moving was easier than thinking. She righted the crates, scooped up spilled rice with a dustpan, set the least‑damaged tins aside. The kids joined in without being asked, picking up wrappers, sweeping crumbs, working in a quiet, focused way that made her throat tighten.
At some point her eyes fell again on the pinboard in the office.
Her drawing hung in ragged halves. Someone had taken a marker and scribbled across the bottom: NOTYOUS
Not yours.
She took it down slowly.
The rip was clean, right through the centre. Miles on one side, Lillian on the other. The little kid shapes at the edges were mostly intact, which felt like a bad joke.
NOTYOUS
She traced the letters with one claw, charcoal smearing under the ink, making a grey halo.
She could throw it away. That would be the sensible thing. Admit it had been a stupid indulgence. Burn it in a tin on the roof and grind the ashes under her heel. Instead, she folded the two halves together, carefully, matching the torn edges, and slid the paper into her rucksack.
Later, when the kids had eaten what they could salvage and curled up in their nests of blankets, she sat on the roof again, pad in her lap, wind cold on her face. She took the torn drawing out and spread it in front of her.
NOTYOUS
Connor’s barely literate words: not yours. The world’s words. The council’s words, printed in polite language on leaflets about “eligibility” and “waiting lists”.
She looked at the adults she’d drawn, at the way their shoulders leaned towards each other. At the little cluster of kid‑shapes around them. Her throat burned. “Maybe not,” she muttered. “Maybe never.”
But she didn’t believe it, not fully. Because some stubborn part of her, the part that had kept her alive this long, didn’t know how to stop wanting.
She took fresh paper, sharper charcoal. This time she drew something different: not Miles and Lillian, not any adults at all. Just kids. The Strays, as they were here and now. Melanie with her too‑big hoodie. Faroukh with his scowl. She drew them standing together, backs to an empty space where adults should have been. When she finished, she added a thin, faint outline behind them. Not fully formed, just a suggestion. Like a shadow of someone not yet arrived.
She didn’t pin this one up. She folded it and put it carefully with the torn drawing.
The balance of power in the warehouse had shifted. Connor had made that clear. Miles and Lillian’s visits, once a simple bright spot in the week, had just become a bargaining chip in someone else’s game. Sarah tucked her knees up to her chest, arms wrapped around them, and watched the city lights flicker on. She would have to think differently now. Protect the little ones without provoking Connor into something she couldn’t control.
Leadership, she realised, wasn’t just about feeding hungry kids and patching leaks. It was about knowing when to pick a fight and when to step back. When to ask for help. When to risk admitting you couldn’t hold it all alone.
She wasn’t ready to ask yet.
Not from Connor. Not from the overworked social workers who’d already written them off. Maybe, one day soon, from the two adults who kept turning up with food and patience and eyes that didn’t look away. For now, she sat on the roof, clutching torn paper, and listened to the distant sound of trains, carrying other people to other lives. 
# Act V
## Chapter 16: Adoption Interview

### 5.16.1
Miles had never seen a waiting room trying that hard to look cheerful.
He and Lillian sat on a sagging sofa upholstered in a fabric that was trying, and failing, to be neutral. The walls were painted a pale yellow that someone had probably described as “sunny” in a meeting. Posters about “Forever Families” and “Every Child Deserves a Home” covered every flat surface, the smiling stock‑photo human kids on them all suspiciously clean.
In the corner, a plastic box of toys sat under a poster that said PLEASE PUT TOYS BACK WHEN FINISHED. Miles noticed immediately that none of the toys had fur on them. The only anthro in the promotional materials was a fox‑girl in the background of one picture, half‑cropped out by the frame.
Miles’s tail was tucked as small as he could make it on the sofa, wedged between his leg and the armrest. Even so, it brushed the elbow of the woman sitting next to him every time he shifted. She didn’t flinch exactly, but her body tilted minutely away.
Lillian sat on his other side, back straight, hands folded around her bag, placed on her belly like a shield. She wore a dark blue dress that skimmed rather than clung over her body and a cardigan with no holes. Her glasses had been cleaned so thoroughly the frames squeaked. Her fur lay smooth except for one rebellious tuft at the back of her head. He could tell she was nervous because her accent had thickened on the way here. Every other word had been “aye” or “och”, vowels stretching wider than usual. She was quiet, lips pressed together, eyes scanning the posters with the clinical focus she usually reserved for bad marking.
“Ferguson?” called a voice from the doorway. “Mr Miles Ferguson and Ms Lillian Edgecombe?”
Miles rose, double‑checking that his shirt was buttoned, his tie straight. He hated wearing a tie, but they’d agreed—it was the kind of place that expected ties. They were playing a part.
The social worker—medium height, neat bob, glasses on a chain—smiled the professional smile of someone who spent a lot of time telling people news they didn’t want to hear. “Hi,” she said. “I’m Ms Hargreaves. Thanks for coming in. Tea or coffee before we start?”
“We’re fine, ta,” Miles said. “Thanks.”
She looked, briefly, at his paws, then at Lillian’s, as if cross‑checking their names against some mental list. “Come through,” Ms Hargreaves said.
Her office was smaller than he’d expected, cluttered with files and ring binders and a few framed photos. One showed her with two teenage boys in school uniforms; the other was a seaside shot with a dog whose species Miles couldn’t immediately place.
There were chairs on either side of the desk. She gestured them towards the ones opposite, then seemed to think better of it. “Actually,” she said, “why don’t we sit over there?” She nodded towards a small round table by the window with three chairs around it. “Less formal.”
Miles knew a tactical move when he saw one. Sitting at a desk meant I hold the power here. Round table said we’re all in this together. He appreciated the attempt even as he saw through it. They sat. The chairs were slightly too small for him; his knees ended up higher than he’d like, tail awkward under the seat. Lillian squeezed in delicately, trying not to knock anything over.
Ms Hargreaves opened a file on her lap. “So,” she said, smiling again. “Tell me a bit about yourselves. How long have you been together?”
“Ten years,” Lillian said. “Met in college sixteen years ago.”
“Living together,” Miles added.
“And you’re not married?” Ms Hargreaves asked, making a note.
“No,” Lillian said. “We’ve been… considering it.”
“Right.” Another note. “And you’re both working?”
“Yes,” Miles said. “I’m a deputy manager at the grocery store in Camden. Full‑time.”
“Music teacher,” Lillian said. “Part‑time at the academy, part‑time freelance. I teach in schools as well, outreach stuff.”
“So, flexible hours,” Ms Hargreaves said, pen hovering.
“Flexible,” Lillian agreed, with a small smile that didn’t quite reach her eyes. “Which is handy when children get ill.”
“Mm.” Ms Hargreaves glanced at another paper. “And you’re both, ah—”
She hesitated.
“Anthro,” Miles supplied.
Her smile tightened, just for a heartbeat. “Yes,” she said. “That’s… noted.” Miles bit his tongue on half a dozen responses. Of course it was noted. It was the first thing people noted, no matter how politely they pretended otherwise. “We do have anthro families on our books,” she added quickly. “Just so you know. It’s not a barrier in itself.”
In itself.
Lillian’s paw tightened on her bag. “We read the guidance,” she said. “Equality Act and all that. You’re not supposed to discriminate on species.”
“Of course,” Ms Hargreaves said. “We take our responsibilities very seriously. The key things we look at are stability, capacity, and fit. For any couple.”
“Fit with who?” Lillian asked.
Ms Hargreaves smiled in what she probably hoped was a reassuring way. “We try to match children with families who can meet their needs,” she said. “So we consider all sorts of factors—background, religion, culture… Obviously, anthro identity can play into that in various ways.”
She said the last part carefully, as if she was tiptoeing across a frozen pond.
Miles resisted the urge to fold his arms. Instead he rested his paws on his knees, posture open. Non‑threatening. Cooperative. “We’re interested in adopting an older child,” he said. “Teenage, if possible.”
Ms Hargreaves blinked. “That’s… good to hear,” she said slowly. “Most prospective adopters are focused on younger children. Babies, toddlers. We do have a number of older children who are waiting.” She glanced at her file. “Can I ask what’s led you to that preference?”
Sarah’s face rose unbidden in Miles’s mind. Her eyes, cool and assessing, the way she’d stood between the younger kids and danger. The way her paws trembled when she thought no one was looking. He exchanged a quick glance with Lillian. “We’re connected to some of the Strays,” he said carefully. “Kids who’ve fallen through cracks. We do runs to a… place. Bring food. Clothes. We’ve got to know them a bit.”
Ms Hargreaves’s pen stilled. “The ‘Strays’ situation has been noted,” she said, voice going a shade more official. “It’s… complicated.”
“That’s one word for it,” Lillian muttered.
Miles shot her a warning look; she raised her eyebrows, unapologetic. “We’re not here to debate policy,” he said, keeping his tone mild. “We’re here because we want to start the process properly. Above board. We know we can’t just… take a kid home.”
“That’s correct,” Ms Hargreaves said. “Any child leaving an unregulated environment needs to go through the proper channels. There are safeguarding concerns, legal liabilities…” She trailed off, perhaps realising she was drifting into jargon. “In practical terms,” she went on, “today is just an initial information session. We’ll talk through the process, the expectations, the kinds of children who are currently waiting for placements. If you decide to proceed, there are forms to fill in, references, checks—medical, financial, home visits.” She looked at them over the top of her glasses. “I won’t lie,” she said. “It’s a thorough process. It needs to be. We’re talking about vulnerable children.”
“We teach vulnerable children,” Lillian said. “We live with being vulnerable adults in your paperwork. We’re not afraid of forms.”
“Good,” Ms Hargreaves said. “That will stand you in good stead.” She pulled out a glossy booklet and slid it across the table. A white couple beamed up from the cover, holding a mixed‑race toddler between them like a prize. “This outlines the stages,” she said. “You’ll see that being married is… viewed positively. It’s not an absolute requirement, but it is something panels look on favourably.”
Miles felt rather than saw Lillian’s flinch.
“We’ve been living together for ten years,” Lillian said quietly. “We have shared accounts and two steady incomes. We’ve more evidence of stability than half your married couples, I’d bet.”
Ms Hargreaves’s smile thinned. “As I say,” she repeated, “it’s not a requirement. But given the additional… factors… in your case, anything that demonstrates long‑term commitment can help.”
Additional factors. Anthro. Fat. Stray‑adjacent. Working‑class. Take your pick.
“We’re committed,” Miles said, with a steadiness he hoped masked his anger. “Ring or no ring.”
“I don’t doubt that,” she said quickly. “I’m simply outlining how panels tend to operate.” She moved on to practicalities, perhaps sensing it was safer ground. “Can you talk me through your flat?” she asked. “How many bedrooms, outdoor space…?”
They did. One bedroom, upstairs. Enough space for a child to sleep if they were okay with sharing for a bit, but not ideal on paper. Lillian mentioned the possibility of moving somewhere with more rooms; Miles nodded, mentally recalculating the budget with his new salary. “We’re realistic about the challenges,” he said. “Foster‑to‑adopt, teenagers with trauma, all that. We’re not coming into this thinking it’s going to be some fairytale.”
“We wouldn’t be letting you leave if you were,” Ms Hargreaves said dryly, and for a moment the professional mask slipped enough to show the woman underneath. Tired. Wry. A little bit on their side, despite herself. “You understand that anthro children can have additional needs,” she added. “Medical, social… we do our best, but resources are… limited.”
Austerity, Miles thought. Cuts. Services pared back. Anthro support groups run by volunteers on a shoestring.
“We’re anthro,” Lillian said. “We understand.”
“Of course,” Ms Hargreaves said. “I just… have to say it.”
There were other questions. Some were reasonable: how they handled conflict, what their support network looked like, whether they were open to siblings. Some were couched in neutral language but carried an edge. “How do you feel your experiences of prejudice might impact on your parenting?” for instance.
“We’ll be better at spotting it,” Lillian said, before Miles could open his mouth. “And less likely to tell a kid they’re imagining it when someone stares at them on the bus.”
“Do you think your involvement in political activism could expose a child to risk?” was another.
“We think it could teach them how to stand up for themselves,” Miles said. “We’re not planning on taking a toddler to the front line of a riot, if that’s what you’re asking.”
They navigated it, just about. They swallowed the impulse to snap, to argue. They made themselves sound steady and sensible and deserving. After an hour, Ms Hargreaves closed the file. “Okay,” she said. “I think that’s enough for today. As I said, the next step, if you choose to proceed, is the formal application. I do need to be honest with you, though.” Here it comes, Miles thought. The bit where she tells us not to get our hopes up. “There are a lot of couples on the list,” she said. “The process can take time, especially for first‑time adopters. We do prioritise married couples and single carers with particular matches for specific children. Anthro couples… it’s not that we don’t approve them,” she added quickly, seeing Lillian’s jaw tighten. “It’s just that panels can be… cautious.”
“Cautious about what?” Lillian asked.
“Perceived stability,” Ms Hargreaves said. “Perceived… resilience. Concerns about how an anthro couple might cope with discrimination their child could face. There’s a narrative—” she grimaced “—that it’s adding extra complexity.”
“Right,” Miles said, managing not to bare his teeth. “Because two humans who’ve never had a slur shouted at them in their life are better placed to help a kid deal with being called a beast on the bus.”
Ms Hargreaves looked, for a moment, like she wanted to agree. Then the professional mask lowered again. “I’m not saying it’s fair,” she said. “I’m saying it’s the system we’re working in.”
“So what you’re telling us,” Lillian said slowly, “is that if we go through all this, jump every hoop, there’s still a good chance some panel of mostly humans will look at us and decide we’re a risk because we’re like the kid, not like them.”
“I’m telling you,” Ms Hargreaves said carefully, “that if you decide to embark on this, it will be a marathon, not a sprint. And that you’ll need thick skins. Thicker than you already have.”
Miles thought of the Strays again. Of Sarah on the roof, shoulders squared against the wind. Of the way his chest had felt when she’d left the little note in the crate: Thank you for pads. “We’ve already been living the marathon,” he said quietly. “We’re just asking to do it with a kid whose name is on your books instead of on some social worker’s list of missing, presumed sofa‑surfing.”
Ms Hargreaves’s shoulders dropped a fraction. “I hear you,” she said. “I do. And if you apply, I’ll be honest with you at every stage. I can’t promise outcomes. But I can promise to tell you what’s happening and why.”
It was more than he’d expected. It still wasn’t enough.
When they left, booklet in hand, the waiting room was empty. The toy box sat undisturbed. Outside, the sky had clouded over, the light flat.
They walked in silence for a while, the sounds of the city wrapping around them: buses, shouting, a distant siren. Lillian’s steps were measured, careful. Miles matched his pace to hers. “Well,” she said eventually. “That was… enlightening.”
“In a we’re screwed sort of way,” he replied.
She snorted. “Aye.”
They reached a pedestrian crossing. The green man lit up; they crossed with the small crowd, shoulders brushing strangers.
“We can still do it,” Miles said. “Apply. Fill in the forms. Give them more paperwork than they’ve ever seen. Make it harder for them to say no.”
“You want to?” she asked.
He thought of the warehouse again, of kids sleeping under thin blankets, of Sarah’s eyes when she pretended not to care. “Yeah,” he said. “I do.”
She was quiet for a few steps. “Me too,” she said finally. “I dinnae ken if I can handle another system trying to tell me I’m no’ enough. But I cannae leave thae weans tae it either.”
He reached for her paw. She let him take it, fingers weaving through his. “We’ll do it,” he said. “Together. We’ve done harder things.”
She squeezed his paw once, hard. “We have,” she said. “We will again.”
They walked on, hand in paw, leaflet heavy in Lillian’s bag. Between them and any future child lay forms and panels and prejudice disguised as caution. Behind them, in a warehouse that smelled of damp and defiance, a girl they already loved was weighing up her options.

### 5.16.2
Jonathan’s new studio always smelled of turpentine, coffee, and rain, on top of his own scent. It was on the top floor of an old warehouse a few streets from ‘The Innards’, a space that had once stored something industrial and anonymous and now held canvases, easels, and the accumulated detritus of a working artist’s life. Light poured in through high windows, grey‑white on days like this, sharp and golden on others.
Lillian liked it because it had no mirrors. Only paintings and photographs, Jonathan’s last passion, which reproduced reality through his own vision, which she often found more true than reality itself.
“You’re late,” Jonathan called as she let herself in with the spare key he’d given her. “By which I mean, perfectly on time, but I like pretending you’re unreliable. It makes me feel better about myself.” He appeared from behind a stacked pair of canvases, cloth tied around his waist, a streak of ultramarine on his cheek. Badger, just like Lillian, leaner and sturdier, hair perpetually on the verge of needing a cut, he moved with the distracted energy of someone whose mind was already halfway back to the work.
“I brought biscuits,” Lillian said, holding up a packet.
“Ah,” he said. “All is forgiven.”
He took them, dropped them on a table that already held a mug of coffee and a chipped mug with brushes—“I chipped that on purpose,” he specified one day, “so that I don’t try to drink tea from the wrong mug.”
“Tea?” he asked. “Coffee? Turpentine?”
“Tea would be grand,” she said. “I’ll pass on the solvents.”
While he fussed with the kettle, she shrugged off her coat. Underneath she wore a plain black vest and soft leggings, clothes that were easy to move in. Modelling clothes, they’d joked, as if there was a catalogue somewhere for “fat badger who sits very still.” The canvas on the easel faced away from her. She was glad. She preferred to see paintings when they were at a point he was willing to show, not half‑formed ghosts of herself on the page.
“How’s the council thing?” he asked over his shoulder. “Did you scandalise them?”
“Probably,” she said. “I said ‘backfat’ in front of a councillor. I think she nearly fainted.”
He laughed. “You’re a menace.” He brought her a mug of tea exactly how she liked it—strong, a wee bit of milk, no sugar—and took his coffee back to the easel. “Right,” he said. “Same set‑up as last time? Chair, three‑quarter turn, looking out the window like you’re thinking deep thoughts?”
“I’m always thinking deep thoughts,” she said, deadpan.
“Obviously,” he said. “Sit however you’re comfortable. I’ll work around you.”
That was one of the reasons she kept coming back. He never tried to twist her into poses that hurt. He never asked her to suck anything in. He had, on their second session, gently moved her arm to reveal the crease where her side met her back, the soft fold she had spent years trying to hide. “That’s interesting,” he’d said, like he was talking about a shadow on a bowl of fruit. “Don’t hide that from me.”
Now, she settled into the battered armchair he kept for her, letting herself spill into it fully. Belly soft, thighs spread, tail curled around one leg. She rested her paws on the arms, shoulders dropping. “There,” Jonathan said. “Perfect.”
He picked up a stick of charcoal and began to sketch, quick, economical strokes. She watched his hands, the way they moved, the way he stepped back, squinted, stepped forward again. “You look tired,” he said after a few minutes, not unkindly.
“That’s because I am,” she said. “Panel last night, lessons this morning, adoption forms, life.”
“Ah yes,” he said. “Trying to convince the state you’re worthy of caring for children. My favourite bureaucratic pastime.”
“You joke,” she said. “But that’s basically it.”
He made a sympathetic noise. 
“How’s Miles?” he asked. “Still being heroic in supermarkets?”
“He’s deputy manager now,” she said. “Got himself promoted.”
“Look at him,” Jonathan said. “Climbing the greasy pole.”
“He thinks he can use it,” she said. “For the Strays. More say over what gets chucked.”
“I wouldn’t bet against him,” Jonathan said. “He’s sneakier than he looks.”
She smiled faintly.
They fell into a companionable silence: he drawing, she holding herself steady, shifting occasionally when a muscle twinged. The light shifted slowly across the floor. Somewhere outside, thunder rumbled, distant. “Storm coming,” Jonathan murmured.
“Aye,” she said. “Been building all day.”
He looked up from the canvas. “You can feel it?”
“Always could,” she said. “Even as a kid. You ken when the air goes heavy, like a held breath?” He nodded. 
“My dad used to say I was thunder’s daughter,” she went on, surprising herself. She hadn’t thought about that phrase in years. “Said I was born in a storm and that’s why I was so loud.”
Jonathan’s eyes lit. “Thunder’s daughter,” he repeated. “That’s a song title.”
“Maybe,” she said. “Or maybe it’s just my da talking shite.”
“Write it down,” he said. “Even if you never use it. That’s too good to lose.”
She filed it away in the mental drawer where she kept phrases that might become lyrics.
“What did the storm look like where you grew up?” he asked.
“Grey,” she said. “And wild. Edinburgh rain’s different from London. It comes at you sideways. You cannae pretend it’s romantic. It just is.”
He smiled, charcoal flicking. “That’s what I like about painting you,” he said. “You just are. Never pretending to be something else.”
She snorted softly. “Tell that to the adoption forms.”
“Sod the forms,” he said. “We’re talking about art.”
He switched to paint, mixing colours on the palette with practiced ease. He didn’t reach for pinks or peaches; he built her fur from greys and browns and warm blacks, catching the way the light hit the white stripe down her face, the soft paler patches at her throat.
“What are you actually painting?” she asked. “I mean, beyond Lillian in a chair looking knackered.”
“Which would make a perfect title for a painting, by the way,” he commented. He tilted his head, considering. “Weight,” he said. “In all senses. How you occupy space. The way you sit like you’ve decided the chair belongs to you. The way your body holds what it’s held.”
She felt a flicker of discomfort, not at the word weight itself—she’d reclaimed that long ago—but at the idea of him seeing the unseen things she carried. “You’re not making this easy,” she said.
“It’s not supposed to be easy,” he said gently. “It’s supposed to be true.”
She looked away, out of the window. The sky had darkened further, clouds stacked like bruises. Rain ticked against the glass. “You ever paint thin folk?” she asked.
“All the time,” he said. “They’re just less interesting. Fewer planes.”
“You’re a menace as well,” she said.
He grinned.
“Do you ever worry,” she went on, “that you’re… I dinnae ken… fetishising? Making a ‘thing’ out of big bodies? Out of anthro bodies?”
He considered this seriously, brush poised. “I worry about turning people into symbols,” he said. “Into messages. I try very hard not to do that. Bodies are bodies. I like painting what’s actually there. If the world has a problem with some bodies, that’s the world’s neurosis, not mine. But if you ever look at a painting and feel like I’ve made you into a poster, you tell me, and I’ll bin it.”
The simplicity of it steadied her. “You won’t have to,” she said.
He painted in silence for a while. The sound of rain grew louder. Thunder muttered again, closer this time.
“Sing something for me,” he said suddenly. “If you’ve got the breath. Helps me with rhythm.”
She rolled her eyes. “Bossy.”
“Artist’s prerogative,” he said.
She cast about in her mind for something that fit the mood. Not the band’s usual set—‘The Innards’’ crowd liked big choruses and swear words. Something older, slower. A tune her granny had hummed when she was a cub, maybe. Or that new line that had been rattling about since the jumper, since the bathroom, since everything.
She started with a low hum, feeling for notes. The words followed, half‑formed.

I am the daughter of thunder's roar
Born from ancient stone and peat
My claws dig deep through Highland floor
Where old roots and bedrock meet
These mountains know my mother's name
She carved the glens with storm and flame

Her voice filled the studio, soft at first, then stronger. Jonathan’s brush moved in time, strokes matching the rise and fall. “That,” he said quietly, “is something.”
“It’s nothing yet,” she said. “Just… noise.
“Noise is where everything starts,” he replied. “Even life starts like… like that. A bunch of words in a bedroom, a bunch of cells in a womb.”
The truth in his words stung Lillian; she hid the sensation by shifting her gaze from Jonathan. She sang the verses again, adjusting a word here, a note there. By the third time through it had settled into a shape that made her chest ache in a not entirely painful way.
When she stopped, the room felt charged, like the pause before lightning.
Jonathan stepped back from the canvas. “Come look,” he said. She hesitated. Seeing herself on a canvas always felt like standing next to a stranger and being told they were family. “Come on,” he said, gentler. “I promise I’ve painted your worst angle. Full honesty.”
She snorted and got up, stretching her cramped legs. The walk to the easel felt longer than the few steps it was.
The painting was still rough, underpainting visible, lines sketchy. But there she was: sprawled in the chair, belly soft, thighs spread, shoulders solid. Her stripe caught the light; her glasses glinted. He hadn’t slimmed her. He hadn’t softened the creases at her side. He hadn’t made her pretty in the way magazines understood it.
He’d made her whole.
She swallowed.
“You look like you could tell someone to fuck off and then make them tea,” he said.
She laughed, unexpected and sharp. “That’s basically my job description,” she said.
“Then I’m doing my job,” he replied.
She lifted a paw, not quite touching the canvas. “You painted my belly bigger than it is,” she said. “Cheek.”
“No,” he said. “I painted it the size it feels. It’s the centre of gravity.”
She thought of the bathroom. Of blood and tile and the way her hand had rested there in apology. “It’s got a lot going on,” she said quietly.
He didn’t ask. He just nodded, as if she’d said something about a muscle strain. “We can stop there for today,” he said. “You’re flagging.”
“Bossy,” she repeated, but she didn’t argue.
He made more tea; they drank it perched on stools, looking at anything but the canvas. They talked about logistics for the next gig, about Juno’s nerves, about Milla’s upcoming audition. Ordinary things, small as pebbles, steadying.
As she put her coat back on, thunder cracked properly overhead, loud enough to make the windows rattle. “There you go,” Jonathan said. “Your relatives saying hello.”
“Tell them to bugger off,” she said. “I’ve got enough going on.”
He opened the door for her, mock‑solemn. “Go in peace, thunder’s daughter,” he said.
She rolled her eyes, but she carried the phrase with her down the stairs, out into the rain.

 
## Chapter 17: Phone Lessons

### 5.17.1
Faroukh always had a swagger to him, even when he was scared. It was a weapon and a shield: shoulders wide, mouth sharp, a way of making the world flinch first.
That night he sat on the edge of a pallet with his knees up and his elbows on them, gaze fixed on the small phone in his paw like it might bite. He had stopped posturing. The bruise on his cheek had yellowed at the edges.
Sarah came over without asking. “What’s that long face?” she said.
“Huh-uh,” he replied. “I’m not taking the piss over my snout, princess.”
She sat at his side. “Bad joke. Sorry.”
“Not bad,” he said. “Just stupid. But thanks anyway for trying.”
Sarah noticed he was gripping the phone like it was some kind of small, incredibly nasty beast. “You always holding that phone.”
“I need it.”
“For what?”
“You know,” he commented. “But… I was… thinking at something else.” He passed it over. It was a battered Nokia, the kind with a screen the size of a postage stamp and buttons worn smooth. It turned on. That alone was a small miracle.
“Why are you showing me?” she asked.
Faroukh’s mouth tightened. “Just…” He swallowed. “Just in case.”
Sarah stared at him for a beat, the irritation and the fear tangling together. “In case of what?” she asked, though they both knew.
Faroukh exhaled through his nose. “In case someone gets hurt and you stop being stubborn about it,” he said, the words coming out rough. “In case Connor does something worse. In case we need to ring that dog man and I’m… I’m not here.” The last words landed harder than the rest.
Sarah’s throat went tight. “Alright,” she said quietly. “Teach me.”
Faroukh blinked, as if he hadn’t expected her to agree. The warehouse around them was its usual night‑shift hum: kids whispering, someone turning over in a blanket nest, the distant drip of the leak into the bucket. He scooted closer and pointed with one claw. “This is the main menu,” he said, suddenly all competence. “You press this. Not hard. It’s temperamental.” Sarah pressed. The screen flickered.  “Right. Emergency number,” Faroukh said. “It’s 999. But if that doesn’t work—like if the network’s being a dick—you can do 112. Same thing.” Sarah repeated them under her breath. “You press the green button to call,” he continued. “If you press the red one you hang up. If you panic and press both you’ll probably break it.”
“Helpful,” Sarah said.
Faroukh’s mouth twitched.
He took the phone back, thumbs moving quickly. “Contacts,” he said. “You can save numbers so you’re not trying to remember them when you’re freaking out.”
Sarah watched him scroll.
“Miles,” Faroukh said. The name appeared on the tiny screen.
Sarah’s chest tightened, surprise and something like relief moving under her ribs. “You already saved him,” she said.
Faroukh shrugged too hard. “Obvious, innit.”
“Put Lillian as well,” Sarah said.
Faroukh looked up. “Why?”
Sarah’s jaw clenched. “Because she answers. Because she doesn’t…” She stopped, breath catching. Because she sounds like someone who won’t leave, her mind finished, and the thought was dangerous.
“Fine,” Faroukh muttered.
He added LILLIAN. He handed the phone back.
Sarah held it in her palm. It was warm from his hands, the plastic slightly greasy. It felt ridiculous that something so small could be so heavy.
“Right,” Faroukh said, voice quieter. “And you keep it on you. Not in your bag. Not in the office. On you. If Connor comes and you go down first, at least one of the little ones can grab it.” Sarah’s ears flattened. “Don’t look at me like that,” Faroukh muttered. “It’s just… planning.”
Sarah swallowed. Planning. That was what Miles did. Maybe it was what she had to do too. “Alright,” Sarah said. “I’ll keep it.”
Faroukh nodded once, as if accepting a contract.
Sarah tucked the phone into the inside pocket of her hoodie, close to her chest.
Neither of them said thank you.
They sat for a moment in the thin dark of the warehouse, listening to the leak count down the night.

## 5.17.2
The problem with loving Lillian was that Miles could feel her leave a room even when she stayed in it.
They were in the warehouse again, late afternoon light leaking through the high windows in grey strips. Kids drifted around them in loose orbit—curious, hungry for adult attention, careful not to ask for too much. Sarah had been quieter since Connor’s last visit. Faroukh kept scanning the door like he expected it to open into a fight.
Lillian stood beside Miles with her hands in her coat pockets, shoulders squared, face composed.
From the outside, she looked fine. Miles could still feel the thinness under it.
He’d been noticing it for weeks now—little misalignments. The way she laughed a fraction too late. The way her eyes sometimes went distant when he said something ordinary. The way she slept with one hand on her belly like she was guarding a bruise.
He’d asked.
Are you alright?
And she’d said aye.
Miles didn’t know what else to do with an aye that didn’t mean yes.
Sarah came over, hands on hips. “Connor’s been back,” she said.
Lillian’s head snapped up. “When?”
“Yesterday,” Sarah replied. “Didn’t come in. Just… hung about outside. Mickey threw a bottle at the wall. Like a reminder.”
He glanced at Lillian. Her face stayed calm, but her paw had clenched inside her pocket. There it was again—that distance. Not from the warehouse. From him. Like there was a second conversation always happening behind her eyes and he wasn’t invited. He couldn’t fix whatever that was.
But Connor?
Connor was fixable. Not in the moral sense—Miles wasn’t naïve. But in the practical sense. Doors. Lights. Routes. Boundaries. Things you could measure. “Right,” Miles said, voice steady. “We need to treat this place like what it is.”
Sarah frowned. “A dump?”
“A target,” Miles corrected.
He crouched by the side door and ran his hand over the latch. It was a joke, really. A bit of metal and a padlock that could be cut with the right bolt cutters. “We do three things,” Miles said, because saying it like that made the panic in his chest behave. He held up a finger. “One: entry points. You’ve got the side door, the loading bay, and that window with the loose frame.” He looked at Faroukh. “You can get up there, yeah?”
Faroukh’s chin lifted. “Course.”
“Good,” Miles said. “We fix it. Screw it shut. If it can’t be fixed, we block it from the inside with something heavy. Pallets. Shelving. Anything.” Second finger. “Two: visibility. You need light outside the doors. People do more stupid things in the dark. We get a motion sensor light, battery if we have to. Stick it above the loading bay.”
Sarah’s eyes narrowed. “Where do we get that?”
“I’ll find one,” Miles said. He didn’t say: I’ll steal it if I have to.
Third finger. “Three: protocol,” he said. “If Connor shows, nobody goes out alone. Nobody. Not for a smoke, not for a piss. You keep the little ones out of sight, you send one person to watch, and you don’t talk unless you have to.”
Faroukh snorted. “You saying we should hide like rabbits?”
“I’m saying you older ones are outnumbered and you’ve got kids,” Miles replied. “That’s not cowardice. That’s prioritising. And,” Miles added, softer now, “you keep a phone charged.”
Sarah’s eyes flicked to the inside pocket of Faroukh’s hoodie.
Lillian looked at her sharply, then at Miles. Miles held her gaze.
For a moment, in the middle of a warehouse full of children, they had a silent conversation.
You didn’t tell me.
Not about the phone.
Not about whatever else was living in her.
Lillian’s mouth tightened. Then she said, to Sarah, “If he comes inside again, you ring us. Immediately. Dinnae negotiate. Dinnae try to handle it alone.”
Sarah’s chin lifted, stubborn as ever. “I’m handling it now.”
Lillian’s voice stayed calm. “Aye. And you’re doing well. But you’re sixteen. You’re allowed to need help.”
Sarah flinched at the number, like it had been thrown.
Miles felt something shift in his chest.
This—this was Lillian. Not distant. Not gone. Present and fierce.
He clung to it. “We’ll bring a proper lock next time,” Miles said, forcing the conversation back onto the rails. “And I’ll talk to Bahadur about the light. Maybe we can write it off as security for the shop.”
“You’re always talking to your boss about things you’re not supposed to be doing,” Sarah said.
Miles shrugged. “He’s always talking to me about things I’m not supposed to be doing. Seems fair.”
Sarah’s mouth twitched.
Miles stood.
He didn’t know how to fix the gap between him and Lillian. But he could, for now, make sure Connor didn’t widen it with a knife.

### 5.17.4
Juno rang Asim. Not because he had been knowing him for long, but because Lillian told her so. 
She stood outside the academy gates with her phone pressed to her ear, coat collar up against a wind that smelled like rain and diesel, and listened to the line ring.
It rang.
It rang.
It rang.
Then a voice answered. “Hello?”
Juno blinked. “Asim?”
A pause. “No, sorry,” the voice said. “This is Toby. He’s in the shower. Who am I talking to?”
“Uh… it’s… it’s Juno. Juno Radcliffe. I’m…”
“Oh, Lillian’s pupil, right? Yes, Asim told me about you—well, Lillian told him and then he told me, so, well, happy to meet you, Juno. You were looking for Asim, right? He’s taking a shower.”
Juno’s cheeks heated under her fur. “Oh,” she said. “I—sorry. I can call back.”
“You can,” Toby said. “Or you can tell me what’s up and I can pass it on. I’m a reasonably secure communications system.”
Juno made a small sound that might have been a laugh. “Right,” she said. “It’s just— I need someone to… go shopping with me.”
Toby didn’t hesitate. “Okay,” he said. “I’m asking him. Asim!” Toby’s bark was too loud for Juno’s sensitive hearing even through the phone. She heard them discuss from a distance, although she couldn’t really make the words. “Hello? You still there?”
“Yes, I am.”
“Oh, cool, thanks for waiting. Asim’d love to come with you, but he’s got a shift in one hour and he’s already late.” There was a beat. “But I’m free, if you want.”
“No, I-I-I’ve been…”
“Look, I’d love to come out for a walk and do some shopping. I need some fresh air. Would you be okay with it?”
“A-Are you sure Asim wouldn’t mind?”
“Oh, yes, he would, but he knows you’ll be in good paws if I’m coming with you.” Then Toby added, “Give me twenty minutes to put clothes on that aren’t a crime, and I’ll meet you outside the academy.”
Juno swallowed. “Okay,” she said.
Twenty minutes later, Toby appeared at the gate with damp eyebrows and his scarf on crooked.
He was a Riesenschnauzer, tall and soft around the edges, with a face that looked permanently concerned even when he was smiling. “Sorry,” he said. “I had a fight with the hair dryer and lost.”
Juno stared. “Your eyebrows are wet,” she observed.
Toby touched them, startled, then laughed. “They are,” he agreed. “Right. Shopping. Where are we going?”
Juno hesitated, then admitted, “I don’t know.”
Toby nodded like that was a perfectly reasonable starting point. “Okay,” he said. “Then we start with: what do you need?”
Juno’s throat tightened. “Clothes,” she said. “Something… not this.” She tugged at her hoodie, the fabric stretched across her belly and chest, sleeves pulled down over her paws.
Toby’s gaze flicked over her outfit, not judgmental—analytical, like he was reading a brief.
