He took in all he needed: her height, her body structure, the colors of her badger fur, the green tips of her short bob, even the small triskelion earrings. “Alright,” he said. “We can do that. And while we walk, you can tell me about your life, because Asim just told me something about you and Lillian and he told me that you looked like you wanted the floor to swallow you.”
Juno’s mouth twitched. “Asim talks too much,” she muttered.
“He does,” Toby agreed warmly. “It’s one of his charms.”
They took the tube. In the carriage, Juno stood with her back against the wall, trying to make her body smaller despite the fact she could not, physically, be smaller. Toby stood beside her and didn’t crowd. He held the pole with one paw and kept the other loosely at his side. “So,” Toby said, as if they were discussing weather, “I’m a graphic designer.”
Juno blinked. “Like… posters?”
“Posters,” Toby said. “Websites. Logos. I make things look like they make sense.”
Juno snorted quietly. “That sounds weird.”
Toby grinned. “It is a bit,” he admitted. “But it pays the rent. And it means I spend a lot of time looking at shapes and colors and thinking about how people see themselves. Which is useful, because right now you are dressed like you’re trying to apologise for existing.”
Juno’s throat tightened. She stared at the carriage floor.
Toby didn’t push. He simply said, “Let’s find you clothes that feel like you.”
They started in a charity shop on Brick Lane because Toby claimed it was good for the soul and bad for the fashion industry. “They just want people to feel miserable.”
Juno trailed through racks of fabric, fingers brushing sleeves.
Everything seemed designed either to hang off narrow human bodies or to squeeze large bodies into shame. She caught herself reaching for the biggest hoodie in the place.
Toby touched her elbow lightly. “Not that,” he said.
Juno flinched. “What’s wrong with hoodies?”
“Nothing,” Toby said. “I live with hoodies on. The question is: why would you need a hoodie?”
Juno’s jaw tightened. “It’s just… easier,” she admitted.
Toby nodded. “I know,” he said. “But easier isn’t always kinder. Especially on you and with your colors. Green, black, grey. They work well together, but what I see is the lack of something else that would make you—”
“Visible,” Juno continued.
“See? That’s why you’re here, then. You want people to see you.” Juno nodded, biting her lips. Toby held her paw with a soft firmness that made her feel weird, and stared at her from below. “You dress like someone who wants to disappear, yet you called Asim because some part of you wants to be seen. Which part you want to feed, Juno? Let me guess: the part that made you disappear didn’t really help you through your life, uh?”
“Uh… I guess not.” Her gaze shifted from Toby’s muzzle to the shop. She suppressed a shiver, and exhaled. “Let’s do this, then.”
Toby clapped his paws once. “Good. But I need you to trust me. I do this as a job—well, not looking for clothing, but checking for colors and composition—so I know a thing or two about it. To start, let me check for something else to change your hoodie.” 
They found a soft, bright yellow long shirt that ran down to the middle of her thighs, a boat neck and sleeves that never reached her elbows. Juno stared at it in the mirror, breathing shallow. Her chest sat heavy, undeniable. She could feel the old panic rise.
Toby leaned against the changing room doorframe, eyes on her reflection but not on her body like a weapon. “Tell me something,” he said. “That sports top you’re wearing under your bra.”
Juno’s ears heated. “What about it?”
“It’s flattening you,” Toby said gently. “And it looks like it hurts.”
Juno’s jaw clenched. “It’s fine,” she lied.
Toby didn’t argue. He simply said, “Take it off.”
Juno stared at him.
“Look, this is the most heterosexual thing I’m going to say in my whole life, but you have breasts. Large, beautiful girls that don’t deserve to live compressed in a top, because it’s not good for your health, it doesn’t help your figure and doesn’t justice to who you are. Take that bloody top away and breathe, Juno.”
Juno’s throat tightened. She looked at herself again.
Big.
Visible.
Still alive.
Her paws shook as she reached under her hoodie and peeled the compressing top off.
The relief was immediate—like taking off a too-tight bandage. Juno sagged against the wall.
Toby nodded once, like she’d done something brave, which made her want to be furious and made her eyes sting. “Better?” he asked.
Juno swallowed. “Yeah,” she whispered.
They bought the shirt.
Two cheap bras that could fit her breasts without restraining them.
A scarf.
A green pleated gown that Toby sweared “would do numbers with your dark calves.”
Nothing dramatic.
Just choices.
Afterwards, still carrying the paper bag, Juno allowed herself to breathe. “See?” Toby said. “You’re still alive. This is not the endpoint, though, but it’s a start.”
Juno nodded, trying to avoid his stare. “Thanks, Toby. I… nobody ever did this for me.”
Toby lifted both eyebrows. “Well, there’s always a first time.”
While walking to the tube, Juno froze outside a music shop. The window displayed instruments like they were jewellery.
Flutes, too. Sleek and silver.
Juno’s chest tightened.
Toby followed her gaze. “Do you need strings?” he asked, then corrected himself immediately. “No. Not strings. Sorry. I’m an uneducated barbarian.”
Juno let out a small laugh. “It’s woodwinds,” she said.
Toby nodded. “Do you want to go in?”
Juno’s mouth went dry. “I can’t afford—”
“We can look,” Toby said. “Looking is free.”
Inside, the shop smelled of polished wood and metal and the faint dust of old sheet music.
A clerk, human, middle-aged, looked up and smiled in that neutral retail way. “Can I help?”
Juno’s throat stopped working. Toby stepped in smoothly beside her. “She’s looking for a transverse flute,” Toby said, like he’d been born knowing the phrase. He gave Juno a sideway glance. “Asim speaks too much.”
Juno blinked.
The clerk’s gaze flicked to Juno’s paws. “We can try a few,” he said.
He brought out two. Juno lifted the first one and immediately felt the problem—keys too close, spacing wrong for her thick fingers. The second was better. The clerk watched her grip. “You’ve got strong hands,” he said. Something fluttered within Juno’s chest. No one had ever said that about her body without it being an insult. “We can find you something that fits.” He disappeared in the back of the shop, then came back half a minute later with a third flute that had slightly widened key touches and a sturdier body. “Here. You’ll fight less with this.”
She held the third flute.
Her fingers settled.
Her shoulders dropped.
She raised it to her mouth and blew.
The note came out thin at first, then steadied.
The shop held its breath with her.
Toby’s eyebrows lifted.
Juno lowered the flute, cheeks hot.
“I’ll take it,” she said, before she could talk herself out of wanting.
Toby looked at her, tail wagging slowly. “You sure?”
Juno’s throat tightened. “Yeah,” she said. “I’m tired of… fighting everything.”
Toby nodded. “Okay,” he said. “Then we do it.”
Juno paid with cash she’d saved and money Toby quietly added without making it a gift.
Outside, the wind hit them again.

 
## Chapter 18: Community Meeting

### 5.18.1
Asim’s throat already felt raw. It wasn’t from singing. It was from rehearsing arguments in his head on the bus from the flat in Holloway to the estate off Seven Sisters Road where the Somali centre rented space on Thursday nights.
The community hall smelled of instant coffee, floor polish, and damp coats. Plastic chairs had been set out in rows facing a low stage with a table and three microphones. Someone had tried to make it welcoming with a cloth banner that said STRAYS ARE OUR CHILDREN in hand‑painted letters, but the paint had bled on the fabric, turning the words slightly fuzzy.
Asim stood for a moment in the doorway, letting his eyes adjust from grey afternoon to fluorescent glare.
Different worlds had already claimed their corners.
At the front, a row of adult anthros in their best clothes sat stiffly, paws folded, expressions wary. Behind them, younger anthros in hoodies and band T‑shirts sprawled, legs extended, tails flicking. A cluster of human parents near the middle checked phones, whispered, glanced around as if hoping to see someone they knew.
On the right, a group from the local mosque had gathered, in kufis and neat hijabs. On the left, a couple of councillors in suits muttered to each other, leaflets in hand.
Asim’s stomach tightened. His father would be somewhere in the front row on the right. He could feel the gravitational pull of that presence even before he saw him. His uncle couldn’t be present due to tight work schedules, which made the experience much more uncomfortable for him.
“Asim!” called a voice.
He turned. Toby waved from near the back, two cups of coffee in his paws. Asim threaded through the chairs to him, weaving between elbows and tails.
“Thought you’d done a runner,” Toby said, handing him a cup.
“Tempting,” Asim replied. “But then who would explain intersectionality to Councillor Jenkins in words of one syllable?”
Toby snorted into his coffee. “You’re a saint.”
“I am not,” Asim said. He took a sip. The coffee was burnt and too sweet, but he needed something to do with his paws.
“Your dad’s here,” Toby murmured, nodding towards the front.
“Of course,” Asim said. “I felt the temperature drop.”
“Want me to go say hi?” Toby asked, half joking.
“Please don’t,” Asim said. “I would like the meeting to start with only one shouting match.”
Toby’s smile flickered, then steadied. “Asim, look… he drove the entire way from Birmingham to come and listen to you.”
“So?”
“I mean… I know he said you have to repent, that he doesn’t accept your life and all. I… I’m not really into the nuances of your religion, so I’m not going to lecture you on anything, but… a father driving three hours only to listen to his son at a panel… doesn’t really look like someone who—”
“This is a conversation I don’t really want to consider now, Toby,” Asim cut him off. Toby’s smile faltered. Asim shook his head and grabbed his paw. “I apologize, love. I’m very nervous. You didn’t deserve it.”
“You know I’ll be here after,” he said, quiet.
Asim nodded once, grateful and sick all at once.
He made his way up the side of the hall to the stage. The MC for the night—a human woman from a local housing charity—stood by the table, shuffling notes. Her name, he remembered, was Priya. “Asim,” she said, spotting him. “Thanks for coming. The councillors are twitchy and the elders are giving me looks and we haven’t even started.”
“I’ll be very soothing,” he promised. “I’ve rehearsed my reasonable face.”
“You do have a very good reasonable face,” she joked. “Go sit. We’re starting in five.”
He took his place at the table between her and a local pastor, a black human man in a collar who ran a youth club. The microphone in front of Asim smelled faintly of dust and fear.
He scanned the room again.
His father sat on the right, as expected. He hadn’t looked at Asim yet, but his whole body radiated disapproval: of the meeting, of the Strays, of his son’s life in London, but mostly of his son’s life as a whole.
On the other side, Asim recognised familiar faces: Juno in the second row with a school friend, notebook already out; a couple of the older Strays who’d drifted in, arms folded, curiosity hidden under boredom; one of the warehouse volunteers; a teacher from the school where Lillian worked.
This was what community looked like, he thought. Not some neat circle of joined hands, but a room full of people who would normally be glaring at each other on the bus, forced into proximity by crisis.
Priya tapped the mic.
“Alright,” she said. “Thanks, everyone, for coming. We know you’re busy; we appreciate your time. We’re here because we have a problem we can’t ignore: young people sleeping rough, kids on our estates with nowhere to go, so‑called ‘Strays’ in warehouses and squats.” She glanced at Asim. He nodded. “We use the word ‘Strays’ tonight because it’s what the kids are calling themselves,” she said. “Not because we think they are less than. They’re ours. Our responsibility.” Murmurs. A couple of elders nodded; one councillor pursed his lips. “We’re not here to blame,” Priya went on. “We’re here to talk about what we can do. Asim Ganacsade, who many of you know from the band Venus in Furs and from his work with youth across the borough, is going to start us off.”
Applause, thin but present.
Asim took a breath and leaned towards the mic. “Salaam alaikum,” he said first, because he needed his elders to hear it. “Peace be upon you all.” Some answered the greeting; others sat stony‑faced. 
“You all know the headlines,” he said. “Anthro kids sleeping in warehouses. Abandoned children. ‘Strays’ as if they’re dogs you can just… leave somewhere and hope they survive.” He kept his voice level, not preaching, not shouting. “You also know,” he went on, “that this didn’t happen in a vacuum. It happened because of cuts. Because services shut. Because families were told to cope alone. Because it is easier for certain politicians to blame ‘bad parents’ and ‘culture’ than to admit they’ve wrecked the safety net.” He saw one councillor wince. Good. “But we’re not here tonight to wait for Westminster to grow a conscience,” he said. “We’re here because the kids are here. Now. On our streets. In our warehouses. In our mosques and churches and youth clubs, if we’re honest.” He looked at his father then, just briefly. Their eyes met, flared, moved away. “So the question is,” Asim said, “what do we do? As neighbours. As people of faith. As people who say we care.”
He stepped back. The room shifted, restless.
Priya opened the floor.
The first speaker was a young jackal girl Asim knew from the mosque, ears tucked under her hijab, hands clasped tight. “We can’t just… take them in our houses,” she said, in a thick Egyptian accent. “We’re not social services. We’re struggling as it is. My flat is two rooms, five people. Where do we put them?” There were nods, murmurs of agreement. “We can support,” she went on. “Bring food, clothes. But bringing them into our homes?”
“The Strays are our own,” someone from the back muttered. “Who else will claim them?”
Next, a human father stood up, hair receding, hands shaking slightly. “My son got mugged by a group of them,” he said. “They took his phone. One was a greyhound, tall, lanky. They had knives. You want me to feel sorry for them when my boy’s scared to walk home?”
“Not feeling sorry,” Asim said quietly into the mic, “doesn’t mean pretending they’re not there. Also, plenty of humans do the same, yet nobody has ever abandoned them.”
“They’re dangerous,” the man insisted. “You’re asking us to bring danger into our buildings. Into our stairwells.” Someone clapped. Someone else booed.
One of the younger activists stood. “You know who else is dangerous?” they said. “Landlords who won’t rent to anthros. Councils that sell off youth centres. Papers that call kids ‘feral’ so they can sell copies.” 
The room splintered.
Asim watched the arguments bloom and multiply like mould. Human parents talked about fear. Youth workers talked about funding. 
Through it all, his father sat like a stone. When he finally stood, making the chair legs screech on the floor on purpose, the room quieted a notch. “We are people of faith,” his father said. “We care for the orphan, the stranger. This we know. But we do not invite wolves into our homes.” The use of the word made something in Asim’s chest tighten. Wolves. Strays. The same animal metaphors, over and over. “These children,” his father went on, “some of them have been on the street since they were small. They have learned to survive with violence. Drugs. Theft. We must be careful not to endanger our families in our eagerness to help.”
“And what do you suggest, Abbo?” Asim asked, unable to keep quiet. “We bring them food at the door and then close it again?”
His father’s gaze fixed on him, hard. He rarely called him by his childhood nickname in public; hearing it in this room, in that tone, made Asim’s stomach drop. “We support organisations,” his father said. “Proper ones. Not bands and warehouse parties.” A ripple of laughter and disapproval. “We lobby the council to do its job. We do not take this on ourselves in ways that will bring police to our door.”
The councillor—Jenkins again, miraculously present at everything these days—seized the opening.
“We are absolutely committed,” she said, “to working with community leaders to tackle this issue. There are budget constraints, of course, but we’re exploring—”
“You cut the budget,” someone shouted. “You!”
Voices rose. Old grievances spilled out: housing allocations, racism, Islamophobia, anthro discrimination.
Asim’s head throbbed. This was what he’d feared: that a meeting ostensibly about kids with nowhere to sleep would turn into everyone emptying their bins in public. “Okay,” he said into the mic, raising a paw. “Okay. Can we… breathe, please?” Some did. Some didn’t. “Look,” he said. “We can argue about roots all night. Some of you will blame parents, some will blame politicians, some will blame God. That's… fine. I love a good theological fight as much as the next wild dog. But the kids are still in the warehouse while we’re doing it.” He looked at his father again, made himself hold that gaze. “We are not asking every family here to adopt a Stray,” he said. “We are asking what we can do with what we have.” He turned to the youth worker. “You’ve got space in your boxing club two afternoons a week,” he said. “Could we make that Stray‑only time? Somewhere they can be inside without being hassled?”
The man considered, then nodded slowly. “If we can get some funding for extra staff. I’m not taking that on alone.”
“Councillor?” Asim said, turning his head slightly.
Jenkins shifted in her seat. “We can… look at small grants,” she said. “There might be a pot.”
“Not might,” Asim said. “Will.”
She gave a tight smile. “We’ll see what we can do.”
He moved on.
“Mosque,” he said, nodding towards his father’s cluster. “Church. You both have halls that stand empty some evenings. Could we open them as safe spaces? Supervised. With rules: no dealing, no fighting, just somewhere warm and dry, somewhere to rest and learn.”
There was murmuring, heads turning.
His father’s eyes narrowed. “Our hall is not meant for this,” he said. “It is a place of prayer.”
“So is the street when someone has nowhere else to say please help me,” Asim said, heat finally creeping into his voice. “We keep saying these kids are our responsibility, but every time we’re asked to do anything specific, we say not here, not us.” Silence thudded.
His father’s lips thinned. “You live with your… friend,” he said, the pause heavy. “You play music in bars with people who drink and take drugs. You think you can lecture us about responsibility?”
There it was. The ultimatum behind them, dragged into the light.
A few heads swivelled between them like watching tennis.
“I live with my partner,” Asim said, evenly. “I play music with people who are trying to survive the same cuts as everyone else. And I’m standing here because I care about the kids you say you pray for. My life is haram, I acknowledge it. But I do my best every day to be the best person I can be: I give what I have to the community, I go and help kids left behind by parents who didn’t want them for… for what they are. I do this not only because it’s what the Prophet asked us to do, but because it’s the most human thing to do.” He felt Toby’s gaze on him like a hand on his shoulder. “We’re not going to agree on my life tonight,” Asim said. “We’ve had that fight already. You gave me your choice: family or honesty. I chose honesty. I chose to make it just between me and God. Fine.” The words sat between them, heavy and irrevocable. His father flinched, just slightly. “But this,” Asim went on, gesturing around, “isn’t about me. Or you. It’s about them. The Strays. Kids. Kids. The ones who will be in our youth groups in five years if they survive, or in our funeral prayers if they don’t.” He took a breath, forced his voice back into that reasonable register. “So can we,” he said, “please, for one night, stop pretending someone else will fix it? Because if we don’t, nobody else will.”
The hall hummed. No miraculous consensus emerged. But something shifted. The youth worker came up after and pressed his number into Asim’s paw. A couple of aunties whispered about cooking extra and sending food out with trusted kids. Jenkins promised, more quietly this time, to meet with him and Priya about funding. Some priests started discussing about spaces in their oratories.
His father left without speaking to him, coat zipped up, shoulders rigid.
Toby found Asim leaning against the outside wall afterward, rain misting his fur. “That went… well?” Toby said.
Asim huffed a laugh. “For a given value of well.”
“You did good,” Toby said. “Even when your dad was being a—”
“Careful,” Asim said, but there was no heat in it.
They stood in silence for a moment, listening to the rain tick on the paving.
“You alright?” Toby asked eventually.
Asim stared up at the orange‑glow sky.
“Ask me after I pray,” he said.

### 5.18.2
The mosque was quieter at night.
Not the big Friday noise—rows full, kids fidgeting, the rhythm of collective recitation—but the low hum of a few stragglers, the swish of mops, the murmur of private prayers.
Asim had grown up in spaces like this. Before London, before bands and protests and Toby’s kitchen, there had been evenings in Birmingham mosques with fluorescent lights and threadbare carpets, the smell of other people’s socks and perfume, his father’s hand heavy on his shoulder.
He hadn’t been back to this particular mosque in weeks. Not since his father’s ultimatum had hardened into a quiet, cold thing—no shouting, just a set of conditions: you are welcome if, you are family if, you are my son if.
That evening he left Toby at the bottom of the street with a promise to message when he was done.
“You sure you don’t want me to come in?” Toby had asked.
“Not tonight,” Asim had said. “I need… words with someone else first.”
Now he stood just inside the door, letting the familiarity wash over him. The hum of a fridge in the small kitchen. The rustle of plastic as someone refilled the shoe rack. The low, melodic recitation from the main prayer hall.
He took off his trainers, lined them up. The carpet under his paws was rougher than he remembered, but the pattern of greens and browns, little arches marking spaces, was the same. A few men sat scattered across the hall, some with Qur’ans open, some with heads bowed. No one paid him much attention; he was just another body slipping in late.
He found a space near the back, facing the qibla. The niche in the wall glowed softly in the low light. He raised his paws. “Allahu akbar,” he said under his breath.
The motions came back to him like music he hadn’t played in a while. Hands to ears. Bending, bowing, pressing his forehead to the carpet. His back ached a little in sujood; he let the discomfort anchor him.
He prayed the familiar words first, the ones everyone knew. The Fatiha. Verses about mercy, about guidance, about the straight path. Then, sitting back on his heels, he let the scripted bits go and spoke in his own voice, in Somali and English and the private slang that lived between. “You see this mess,” he murmured. “Don’t pretend you don’t. Stray kids in warehouses, my dad wanting a version of me that doesn’t exist, councils that talk about complexity like it’s a disease.” His paws rested on his thighs, palms up. “I’ve tried,” he went on. “You know I have. I did the straight boy act for years. I did the good Muslim son act. I dated girls I liked as friends and pretended that was enough. I wore long sleeves to cover fur and bruises both.” He stared at the carpet in front of him, at the tiny pattern where the dye had faded. “And then I came here,” he said. “And I met people who saw me. All of me. The band. Toby. The ones who don’t flinch when I say I’m anthro and Muslim and gay in the same sentence.” His voice cracked on the last word. He swallowed. “My dad says I’ve chosen this over family,” he said. “But I didn’t feel like I chose. I feel like I stopped lying. There’s a difference.” 
The hall was quiet around him. Someone coughed in the distance.
“Here’s my problem,” he said, almost wry. “I love you lot. I love my dad, even when he’s a stubborn old man. I love my faith. I love the way my chest feels when I hear Qur’an recited right, like a door opening. I loved the smell of Eid in Mum’s kitchen. I love the rhythm of salah. I love all of it.” He took a breath, let it out slowly. “But I also love Toby,” he said. “I love his stupid socks and his stupid jokes and the way he makes the tea properly. I love the way he looks at me like I’m allowed to exist.” The words sat between him and the mihrab, as bold and vulnerable as anything he’d ever shouted into a microphone. “And I’m tired,” he said softly. “I am so tired, Allah. Of having to pick.” He closed his eyes. “So here’s what I’m asking,” he said. “Give me a way to keep you and keep him and keep myself. I’m not clever enough to work that out alone. And if there isn’t one…” He swallowed. “Then give me the strength to choose what’s right, not what’s easiest. Even if that means walking away from people who think they’re speaking for you when they tell me I’m haram for existing.”
It wasn’t tidy. It wasn’t a neat three‑point request. It was a splatter of feelings, a tangle.
He sat with it, letting the silence answer in its own way.
At some point he became aware of someone moving closer. A soft thud of paws on carpet. He kept his eyes closed, not ready to see who it was. “Asim,” said a voice, low.
His eyes snapped open. His stomach dropped. His father stood a few feet away, hands clasped behind his back, expression guarded. “You shouldn’t be listening to other people’s conversations with God,” Asim said, more sharply than he meant.
“You were not exactly whispering,” his father replied.
They looked at each other across the small distance. The air between them felt thick, charged. “You came to the mosque,” his father said at last.
Asim almost laughed. “You say that like I’ve been avoiding prayer,” he said. “I’ve been praying a lot. Just… not in Birmingham.”
His father’s jaw tightened. “This is your place,” he said. “Your people.”
“My people are the ones who don’t ask me to cut myself into parts to fit their idea of acceptable,” Asim said, quietly. “I would have loved that to be you. It is not.”
Pain flickered across his father’s face, there and gone.
“You think I don’t love you?” his father asked.
“I think you love a version of me that doesn’t exist,” Asim said. “And you’re willing to lose the actual one over it.”
They stood in the prayer hall, framed by verses about mercy written in green on the walls.
“The Qur’an is clear,” his father said, automatically. “Men with men—it is—”
“Do not quote Lot at me like I haven’t read it,” Asim snapped. “I know the verses. I also know the ones about no compulsion in religion, and about God creating us in nations and tribes so we may know each other, and about the oppressed on earth who cry out, ‘Lord, take us out of this town of wrongdoers.’” His voice echoed slightly, bouncing off tile and plaster. He lowered it. “I am not asking you to perform a gay wedding,” he said. “I am asking you to not throw your son away because you’re scared of what the aunties will say.”
His father flinched again.
“It is not about the aunties,” he said.
“It is always about the aunties,” Asim said, then took a breath. “And about fear. I get it: you’re scared for me. For my afterlife, for my safety. I am scared too,” Asim confessed. “But I am more scared of living a lie so well that I forget how to tell the truth.” He felt suddenly exhausted. “I’m not going to stop being me,” he said. “Anthro. Muslim. Gay. Activist. Annoying. Loud. All of it. I could choose some of these things. Others just happened. If you can’t live with that, that’s your choice, I can’t make it for you. But I’m not coming back here to sit in the back row and pretend I’m something I’m not just so you can look like a respectable elder.”
His father’s shoulders sagged, just slightly. “Your mother misses you,” he said.
Asim’s throat tightened. “I miss her too,” he said.
Silence.
“Bring Toby,” his father said suddenly, almost choking on the name. “For tea. No… touching. No… talk of… that. Just tea. So she can see you are alive. Just one Sunday.”
It was not acceptance. It was a concession. A tiny, fragile one.
Asim blinked. “That what God told you in your prayer?” he asked, half‑joking because the alternative was crying.
His father’s mouth twitched. “God told me I am not Him,” he said. “I cannot throw my son into hell just because I am scared.”
The sentence hung between them, strange and holy.
Asim laughed once, wetly. “Alright,” he said. “We’ll come for tea. Toby will be very polite. He will bring cake. He will probably over‑season the rice.”
His father made a face. “He cannot cook our rice.”
“We’ll negotiate,” Asim said. They stood there for another moment, trying to map a path across decades of expectation. “Thank you,” Asim said quietly.
His father shook his head. “Do not thank me,” he said. “Thank the One who made your mother so stubborn she shouted at me for an hour about her missing son and persuaded me to drive three hours to stay with you.”
Asim smiled, unexpected and soft. “I will,” he said.
After his father left, he stayed a while longer, forehead to carpet. “Okay,” he whispered. “I see you. I see what you did there. Sneaky.”
He left the mosque lighter than he’d gone in. Not because everything was fixed—it wasn’t—but because a door he’d thought was shut had cracked open.
Outside, Toby leaned against the wall, hood up, ears down against the drizzle.
“How’d it go?” he asked.
Asim slipped his paw into Toby’s without thinking. “We need to arrange a tea on a Sunday,” he said.
Toby’s eyes widened. “At your parents’?”
“Aye,” Asim said. “Under strict no‑touching rules and with a side of theological tension. You in?”
Toby’s tail wagged uncontrollably. “Wouldn’t miss it.”
As they walked away, paws swinging between them, Asim felt something settle inside him. He had not chosen faith over himself, or himself over faith. He had chosen both, and trusted God to live with the complexity.

 
## Chapter 19: Forms and Panel

### 5.19.1
At home that night, he watched Lillian sleep. Her face looked younger in rest, the lines smoothed out. One paw lay on her stomach. He reached over and laced his fingers through hers, gently, careful not to wake her.
“Whatever it is,” he whispered, to the dark, to her, to the ceiling, “I can take it.”
She didn’t stir.
The next morning, he was out early for a delivery. The van smelled of cardboard and earth; he’d insisted they start carrying more fresh veg out to the warehouse when they could, not just tins and pasta. Kids needed greens as well as calories. As he drove, a song came on the radio, some pop thing with a beat that made his paws tap on the wheel. He thought of Lillian’s new melody, the one she’d been humming under her breath lately. Something about thunder.
We believe in different things, he thought. She believes in words and notes and speeches that make rooms go quiet. I believe in pallets and spreadsheets and making sure the fire exits aren’t blocked. Between us, we might just keep a kid alive. If only he could bridge the gap between them long enough to say it.
At the warehouse, Sarah met him at the door, shoulders hunched against the wind. There were dark circles under her eyes.
“You’re early,” she said.
“Couldn’t sleep,” he admitted. “Figured I’d make myself useful.”
She snorted. “You and Lillian are obsessed with usefulness.”
“We wouldn’t know what to do with ourselves otherwise,” he said.
As they unloaded crates together, his muscles doing what they were built for, he felt some of the tension in his chest ease. There, at least, he knew what his job was. Carry. Sort. Plan. Keep kids from starving. Keep the building from falling down. Be the adult who turned up when he said he would.
Back at the flat that evening, Lillian was out—Jonathan wanted to run something for an exhibition, she’d texted. He heated some leftovers, ate it standing at the counter, listening to the hum of the fridge and the faint sounds of the neighbours’ telly through the wall.
The adoption forms lay where they’d left them. He picked one up, scanning the questions.
Describe your relationship in your own words.
He sat down, pen hovering.
Solid, he wrote. Long‑term. Built over ten years of shared bills, shared grief, shared joy, and shared washing‑up. We argue about politics and laundry, not about whether we’re staying.
He thought for a moment, then added: We give each other space when we need it. We are still learning how to ask for help.
He put the pen down.
Maybe that was the distance, really. Not a lack of love, but a lack of the right question, asked at the right time.
He hoped they’d find it before the gap widened into something they couldn’t cross.

### 5.19.2
Milla found the photo by accident. It was tucked into the back of a drawer in the academy office where they kept old gig flyers and spare instrument strings, the kind of drawer that collected the past in layers. She’d been looking for a rosin block and found, instead, an envelope with the band’s name scrawled on it in Asim’s handwriting.
VENUS IN FURS.
Inside were a handful of glossy prints. Milla frowned and pulled the first one free.
It was them.
Six years ago, by the look of it. The lighting was too bright, the outfits too earnest. They were standing outside ‘The Innards’ with arms thrown around each other, faces flushed from the stage and the cold. Lillian in a dress that glittered. Miles with his arm slung around her shoulders, grin wide. Asim doing something theatrical with his hands. Felix in the back making a face like he’d rather die than admit he was having fun.
And Milla.
Milla stared.
She’d been rounder then.
Not in a way that anyone had ever commented on openly—anthros in their scene came in all kinds, and Milla had always been “small” compared to most. But there was softness in her cheeks in that photo, a slight fullness in her waist, the way her jumper sat on her body without hanging.
More than that—her face.
She was smiling.
Not a polite curve of the mouth.
A proper smile. Eyes crinkled. Shoulders loose.
She looked down at herself.
Her hands were thin. The tendons stood out when she flexed her fingers. Her wrists looked delicate in a way that didn’t feel like grace; it felt like fragility. She told herself, automatically, that it was good.
Lighter meant easier on the joints.
Lighter meant faster.
Lighter meant control.
She stared at the photo again.
She had looked… happy.
The thought was treacherous.
Milla slid the prints back into the envelope with care she didn’t feel.
On the way home, rain soaking the cuffs of her coat, she couldn’t stop seeing her own face from six years ago—open, unguarded. At her flat, she went straight to the wardrobe.
She pulled out the dress she wore for gigs sometimes. Black, simple, meant to make her look like a serious musician rather than a squirrel playing in a band. She’d bought it two winters ago and it had been snug then. Now it slid over her shoulders too easily.
She fastened it.
The fabric hung. At the waist, there was space.
Milla stared at herself in the mirror. Her ears angled back.
There was a part of her that felt satisfaction—clean, sharp.
Then, underneath, something else.
Fear.
Because if the dress was too big, that meant she had changed. It meant she could keep changing. It meant she didn’t know where the line was.
She grabbed the dress at the sides and pulled it tight, as if she could force it to fit. Her breath came shallow. The mirror showed her bones.
And it showed, behind that, the ghost of a softer version of herself who had once stood outside ‘The Innards’ laughing, arm around Lillian’s waist, not thinking about calories or punishment.
Milla’s hands shook. She stepped out of the dress as quickly as she could, folding it with furious precision and putting it back in the wardrobe.
She sat on the edge of the bed, elbows on knees, and stared at the floor until her breathing slowed.
Then she got up and made herself a cup of tea.
No sugar.
No biscuits.
Control, she told herself.
But her eyes kept returning, unwillingly, to the place in her mind where the photo lived.
Happy.

### 5.19.3
The first time Lillian saw the photo, she thought it was a mistake.
She’d gone to the community centre straight from work, music bag over one shoulder, stomach a tight knot of nerves and residual ache. The council had asked her—“invited” her, really, in that way that wasn’t quite a request—to speak on a panel about Healthy Bodies, Healthy Minds as part of some vague initiative. “You’re a respected local educator,” the email had said. “And a visible anthro figure. It would be wonderful to have your perspective.”
Visible anthro figure. Translation: fat badger who sings.
She’d almost said no. She had enough on her plate: lessons, band rehearsals, adoption forms now spread across the kitchen table like a second job. But Asim had nudged her. “If you don’t go, they’ll get some human gym bro to talk about discipline,” he’d said. “At least you’ll say something useful.”
So she’d agreed, on the condition that she saw the questions in advance and that the event stayed local, not some filmed spectacle.
She hadn’t been told about the projector.
The foyer of the centre was the usual mix of outdated noticeboards and leaflets about everything from yoga for seniors to debt advice. A table at the back held little paper cups of squash and a plate of slightly stale biscuits. The main hall, though, had been transformed. Rows of plastic chairs faced a makeshift stage at one end, where a long table with four microphones waited. Behind it, a large white screen hung from the ceiling.
As Lillian stepped in, smoothing her dress, the screen flickered. A PowerPoint slide came into focus: the council logo, the words HEALTHY BODIES, HEALTHY MINDS in a font that tried too hard to be friendly.
And then, as she watched, the slide changed.
Her own body filled the screen.
The photo was from a gig at ‘The Innards’. She recognised the angle: someone must have taken it from the side of the stage, catching her mid‑song, paw raised, eyes closed. Her dress clung to her belly, folds of backfat visible under her arms. The stage lights had picked out every line, every curve. She had liked that photo when Jonathan had shown it to her—in his camera, in the context of his careful eye, it had felt like art. Real. Honest. True.
Up here, blown to the size of a billboard, with HEALTHY BODIES, HEALTHY MINDS in bold above it, it felt like something else.
A display.
A specimen.
She stopped dead.
“Oh!” someone exclaimed to her left. “You’re here. Wonderful.”
A woman in a bright blazer approached, hand extended. Human, mid‑forties, the kind of haircut that said professional but approachable. “Ms Edgecombe?” she asked. “I’m Councillor Jenkins. We’re so grateful you could make it. Isn’t that a fantastic photo of you?” She gestured cheerfully at the screen.
Lillian’s mouth was dry. “Where did you get it?” she asked, voice low.
“From social media,” Jenkins said. “One of our team found it. It really captures… vitality, don’t you think? And it breaks the mould of what people expect when they see the words healthy body.”
Lillian swallowed. The words breaks the mould echoed nastily. “Did anyone ask me,” she said carefully, “before you put it up there?”
“We assumed you’d be thrilled,” Jenkins said. “It’s so empowering. Showing different body types, all that.”
Empowering. Like a fire alarm. It made a lot of noise, but didn’t actually put anything out. “You should have asked,” Lillian said. “It’s my body. My image.”
Jenkins’s smile faltered, then snapped back into place. “Of course,” she said. “If you’d prefer we don’t use it, we can always turn the projector off.” As if the damage wasn’t already done. Half the chairs were occupied; heads had turned when Lillian came in, glancing from her up to the giant version of her on the wall and back again, performing the mental game of spot‑the‑difference.
“Leave it,” Lillian said after a beat. Her voice surprised her with how level it sounded. “I’ll talk about it.”
Jenkins blinked. “Wonderful,” she said, clearly relieved not to have to scramble for an alternative. “Shall we get started?”
The panel—two physicians, a physical education teacher from the local secondary, and Lillian—took their seats at the table. Lillian positioned herself at the end, under the corner of the projected image. If she looked up, she saw her own arm looming above her.
The first part was predictable. The doctors talked about obesity rates and diabetes and the importance of “lifestyle changes.” The male, slim and grey‑haired, said “we” a lot when he meant “you people.” The female, harried but kinder, tried to stress social factors, food deserts, stress.
The teacher—late twenties, tracksuit, earnest—waxed lyrical about the benefits of exercise for mental health. “Just getting out there and moving,” he said, “does wonders for your self‑esteem. We try to show the kids that any body can be a sports body.”
Lillian listened, jaw tight, hands folded on the table.
When the chair—the same councillor—finally turned to her, the projector hummed the image of her body above them, mid‑song, belly soft, arms strong. “Lillian,” Jenkins said, “you’re a musician, a teacher, and a well‑known local figure. Can you tell us about your own journey with health and body image?”
There it was. Journey. As if she’d walked a straight line from shame to acceptance, with a handy three‑point moral at the end.
Lillian adjusted her glasses. The microphone smelled faintly of dust and someone else’s breath. “Aye,” she said. “I can.” She let the silence sit for a moment, felt the room lean in. “First thing,” she said, “I want to name the elephant in the room. Or the badger on the wall.”
A ripple of uncomfortable laughter.
She pointed up at the photo. “That picture,” she said. “Of my body. That was taken at a gig. It was a good night. We were loud, the crowd were daft, I was sweating like a sinner in church. I saw that photo later, in a friend’s camera, and I liked it. Because it looked like how it felt from inside my skin.” She looked back at the audience. “Nobody asked me before putting it up there tonight,” she went on. “So I’ve had the experience of walking into a room and seeing my fat belly and my backfat and my double chin staring down at me under the words HEALTHY BODIES, HEALTHY MINDS without warning.” She heard someone cough. Another chair squeaked as someone shifted. “Now, I’m a grown woman,” she said. “I’m thirty. I’m a badger. I’ve been called animal and sow and disgusting enough times that it barely raises my blood pressure these days. I’ve had strangers on the internet tell me to kill myself because they don’t like the shape of my body. So this?” She gestured at the screen again. “This I can handle.”
Her voice was calm. She wasn’t going to shout for them. Shouting made them think you were the problem.
“But imagine,” she said, “being sixteen. Or thirteen. Or ten. Imagine you’re a fat kid, or an anthro kid, or both, sitting in this hall. Imagine you see a body like mine blown up that size under the word healthy and your first thought is, They must be taking the piss. Because everywhere else you’ve seen a body like mine, it’s been the butt of the joke or the warning picture on a leaflet.” She heard Asim’s voice in her head, from a planning meeting months ago: Say the words. Don’t dance round them. “So let’s be clear,” she said. “My body is fat. I have a belly. I have backfat. I have a double chin. I am an anthro woman in a world that still thinks our bodies are either threats or fetishes. I’m also healthy in the ways that matter for my life. I can teach a full day of music, rehearse with my band till midnight, and still walk home and carry my own amplifier up the stairs.” The PE teacher shifted in his seat, eyes on the table. “Health is not a moral category,” Lillian went on. “It’s not a reward for being good. It’s not something you earn by hating yourself enough on the treadmill.” A few people in the audience laughed, short and bitter. “And if you’re using bodies like mine as visual aids,” she said, “you need to ask us first. Because we’re not public property. We’re not posters. We’re people.”
She saw Jenkins’ cheeks flush faintly. Good. Not to humiliate her—though part of Lillian wasn’t above a little satisfaction—but because embarrassment meant the point had landed somewhere beyond the surface.
“If you want to talk about healthy minds,” Lillian said, “let’s talk about what it does to your mind to grow up in a body that every headline tells you is a problem. Let’s talk about sitting in a GP’s office with chest pain and being told to lose weight before they check your heart. Let’s talk about anthro kids being told their fur is unhygienic at school, that their claws are dangerous, that their tails tak up ower muckle space on the bus, whan it’s juist a pairt o their body.” Her Scots thickened as she went on; she didn’t try to smooth it. “Let’s talk about austerity,” she said. “Aboot closin the swimmin baths an’ cuttin free schuil meals an’ then wringin yer hauns whan fowk can only afford shite scran.” She took a deep breath to soothe her nerves. “Let’s talk about telling parents to send their kids out to play in the park when you’ve sold half the parks off to developers.”
One of the GPs cleared his throat. “We do have to acknowledge personal responsibility,” he began, but Lillian raised a paw.
“I’m not saying individual choices dinnae matter,” she said. “Of course they do. I choose to walk when I can. I choose to eat veg when I can afford it. I choose to go to therapy, when I can get a slot. But you cannae preach healthy choices at people whose choices you’ve already narrowed to the bone.” She looked out over the room. A few faces were rigid with discomfort. Others were intent, hungry. A young anthro lad near the back—a hyena, maybe—had his arms crossed tight. “I’m here tonight,” she said, “because I care about kids. My students. The Strays. The ones you already call difficult before you’ve learned their names. I don’t want them to grow up thinking their bodies are problems to solve before they’re allowed to have a life.” Her gaze flicked briefly to the photo again. “So if you’re going to use my image,” she said, “let me at least give it context. That’s me working. That’s me doing my job. That’s me joyful and sweaty and alive. That’s a healthy body. Not because it’s a certain size or shape, but because it’s doing what I need it to do so I can do what I believe in.”
She let the words hang.
Then, more softly, she added, “And on some days, a healthy mind is just one that gets you out of bed and into the shower. I’ve had those days too. Nobody projects those on a wall.”
Silence hummed. The chair cleared her throat.
“Thank you, Lillian,” Jenkins said, voice a little huskier than before. “That was… powerful.”
Questions followed. Some were earnest: parents asking how to talk to their kids about weight without hurting them, an anthro woman in her late twenties asking about navigating gyms that treated her like a curiosity.
One young man, hoodie up, phrased his question clumsily. “Do you worry,” he asked, “that by saying fat is healthy you’re encouraging people to… you know… stay that way?”
Lillian took a breath. “Och, the good old glorifying obesity. I’m not telling anyone what to do with their body,” she said. “I’m saying if you hate yourself, you’re less likely to care for yourself. Shame is a terrible motivator. It might get you to skip a meal. It won’t get you to build a life.”
When it was over and the chairs were scraping back and people were collecting their coats, Jenkins approached again, fiddling with the edge of her blazer. “I deeply apologize,” she said quietly. “About the photo. You’re right, we should have asked.”
“Aye,” Lillian said. “You should.”
“Would you… mind if we took it down now?” Jenkins asked. “I know it’s late, but…”
Lillian looked up at her own frozen image, arm raised mid‑note, mouth open. “Leave it,” she said. “Let folk walk out past it having heard what I said. Otherwise it’s just a big fat badger under a slogan.”
Jenkins winced at the word fat; Lillian let it sit between them.
Outside, the air was cold enough to bite. Lillian walked home instead of taking the bus, letting the night soak into her fur. Her paws ached by the time she climbed the stairs to their flat.
Inside, the kitchen table was still scattered with adoption forms and pamphlets. Miles had left a note on the counter saying he’d be late—warehouse run, extra stock to sort.
Lillian leaned on the table, looking down at the neat piles. HEALTH AND LIFESTYLE QUESTIONNAIRE, one heading read. She laughed once, a short, humourless sound. “You want healthy,” she muttered. “You’ve no idea.”
She pulled a chair out and sat, pen in paw, and began to fill in boxes about her weight, her exercise habits, her medical history. She wrote in steady, clear letters, the way she always did when the world tried to compress her into categories.
If they wanted her body on a wall, on a form, in a file, they were going to get the truth. Every uncomfortable inch of it.

 
## Chapter 20: Worries  

### 5.20.1
Lillian chose the moment carefully. Not straight after rehearsal, when everyone was buzzing or snappish, nor in the middle of a lesson, when Juno would freeze at the slightest shift in tone, and absolutely not in public.
She waited until the practice rooms were emptying, the corridor noise thinning into that end‑of‑day hush. She’d just finished with a student; the girl had left with her clarinet case bouncing against her leg, cheerful and oblivious.
Juno lingered by the water fountain, pretending to read a noticeboard. Lillian walked over and leaned beside her, casual. “You got five minutes?” she asked.
Juno’s shoulders tensed. “If it’s about the call—”
“It’s not,” Lillian said gently. “Well. Not directly.”
Juno’s eyes flicked to hers.
Lillian swallowed, feeling her own nerves stir. She was good on a stage. She was good in front of councils. But asking someone to join your life in a way that mattered—asking for closeness—always scraped at the soft parts. “We’ve got a gig coming up,” she said. Juno nodded cautiously. “The band,” Lillian continued, “is considering… adding someone.”
Juno’s mouth tightened as if she expected the next words to be: not you.
Lillian watched it happen and felt a small, fierce anger at whoever had trained her to expect rejection as the default. “We want you,” Lillian said. Juno blinked. “Not as a favour,” Lillian added quickly. “Not as charity. Because you’re good. Because you make the room different when you play. Because when you don’t freeze, you’re—” She stopped herself. Compliments were a kind of pressure.
Juno’s hands had gone very still. “Why?” Juno asked, voice thin.
Lillian frowned. “Why what?”
“Why me,” Juno said. “You’ve got people. You’ve got—”
“We’ve got five,” Lillian said. “And sometimes five isn’t enough to hold what we’re trying to do. And also,” she said more softly, “because I like you.”
Juno’s breath hitched.
The corridor felt suddenly too bright.
Lillian kept her voice practical. “We’d start slow. A couple of songs. Flute and voice. You wouldn’t have to talk to the crowd if you didn’t want. Asim will, obviously. He can’t help himself.”
Juno made a small sound that might have been laughter. Then her face changed. Her eyes went distant. “I can’t,” she whispered.
Lillian’s stomach tightened. “You can,” she said automatically, then stopped. “You might not want to. That’s different.”
Juno’s throat bobbed. “If I’m… seen,” she said, words careful, as if she was choosing them from broken glass, “he’ll see me.”
Lillian went very still. “Your dad,” she guessed.
Juno nodded once, sharply. “He calls,” Juno said. “And I don’t answer. And then I… I can’t breathe. And you say it’s not my fault, and I believe you for five minutes, and then I don’t.”
Lillian kept her own breathing steady. “What does he do when he sees you?” she asked, softly.
Juno’s mouth opened.
Closed.
Then, as if the words had been waiting for a crack, they came. “He used to hit me,” Juno said.
The corridor hummed with distant piano scales, muffled through walls.
Lillian’s hands curled into fists inside her cardigan pockets. “When?” she asked, voice low.
“When I was a kid,” Juno said, as if that made it less real. “When I talked back. When I cried. When I was… too loud. Too much.” Her voice thinned. “He said I made him ashamed.”
Lillian’s throat went tight. “And when you left?” Lillian asked.
Juno’s laugh was short and ugly. “He said I owed him,” she said. “That I was ungrateful. That he’d made me. That he could unmake me.”
Lillian felt something cold settle in her chest. “Does he know where you live?” she asked.
Juno shook her head quickly. “No.”
“Good,” Lillian said. She exhaled slowly and forced herself into the shape she wore when a child told her something terrible: steady, contained, safe. “Listen to me,” she said. “You don’t owe him your body. You don’t owe him your voice. You don’t owe him your silence either.”
Juno’s eyes shone.
“If joining the band makes you feel unsafe,” Lillian continued, “then we don’t do it. End of. But I want you to know the offer is real. It’s not a test. It’s not something you have to earn by being brave.”
Juno’s shoulders shook once.
Lillian stepped a fraction closer, not touching. “And,” she added, “we can make a plan. Not just for gigs. For calls. For when your chest goes tight and you can’t breathe. For if he ever does show up.”
Juno swallowed. “What kind of plan?”
Lillian’s mouth tightened. “The kind where you’re not alone,” she said.
Juno stared at her. Then, very quietly, “Okay,” she whispered.
Lillian nodded once. She didn’t say: I know what it’s like to bleed alone and not tell the person who loves you. But she felt the shape of that truth under her tongue.

## 5.20.2
Miles found Asim in the back room of ‘The Innards’, guitar case propped against the wall and two pints of golden ginger ale sweating on the table between them.
Asim looked better than he had in weeks. Not less tired—everyone was tired—but looser. Like someone had been holding their breath for a long time and had finally exhaled.
Miles slid into the seat opposite him.
Asim lifted his pint. “Alright, Deputy Manager,” he said.
Miles groaned. “Don’t start.”
“I will start and I will continue,” Asim replied brightly. “It’s my love language. Congratulations on becoming the corporate face of anthro inclusion. How does it feel?”
“Like I want to bite someone,” Miles said.
Asim grinned. “That’s the spirit.”
Miles took a sip of his pint. He tasted nothing.
Asim’s eyes sharpened. “Okay,” he said, tone shifting. “That’s not a normal I want to bite someone. That’s a my chest is full of bees one. What’s happened?”
Miles stared at the condensation on the glass. “Lillian’s… off,” he said finally.
Asim leaned back. “Define off,” he said. “Because Lillian’s baseline is capable of committing arson for social justice.”
Miles huffed a laugh despite himself. “She’s there,” he said. “She’s doing everything. Work. Rehearsals. Forms. She’s talking to Sarah like she always does. She’s holding it together.”
“And?” Asim prompted.
Miles’s jaw tightened. “And she’s… not with me,” he said quietly, toying with his golden ginger ale pint. “Not properly. Like she’s standing two inches to the left of herself and I can’t reach.”
Asim didn’t make a joke this time. He nodded slowly. “Have you asked her?” he said.
Miles’s mouth twisted. “A hundred times.”
“No,” Asim said, and the gentleness in it was almost worse. “Not are you alright, bruv. Have you asked her what’s wrong?” Miles stared. Asim pointed with one claw. “Because are you alright is the question you ask your mate at the bar when he looks a bit sad and you want the socially acceptable lie,” he said. “It’s not the question you ask your partner of ten years when you think she’s drowning.”
“What if she doesn’t want to tell me?”
Asim snorted. “Then she’ll say no,” he said. “And you’ll still be in the same position. But at least you’ll have stopped doing that thing you do where you try to fix the world with padlocks and spreadsheets instead of words.”
Miles’s ears heated. “That’s unfair,” he muttered.
Asim’s eyes softened. “It’s also true,” he said.
Miles stared into his alcohol-free pint. “I don’t want to push,” he admitted. “She’s… she’s been through things. People have pushed her whole life. Doctors, teachers, strangers. I don’t want to be another person trying to get something out of her.”
Asim’s expression shifted, something tender in it. “Miles,” he said. “You are literally the least extractive person I’ve ever met. You make tea for other people when you’re the one bleeding. The problem isn’t you pushing. The problem is you leaving her alone in the dark because you’re scared of being the bad guy.” Miles swallowed. Asim took a sip of his pint, then added, casual as if it were nothing, “Also, I’m feeling very pro‑communication lately.”
Miles blinked. “Why?”
Asim’s mouth twitched. “Because my dad invited Toby for tea,” he said.
Miles stared. Asim spread his hands. “I know,” he said. “I know. It’s like God woke up and chose plot development.” Miles let out a surprised laugh. “No touching,” Asim added. “No gay talk. Just tea. Like we’re in a BBC drama from 1998.”
Miles shook his head. “You alright about it?”
Asim’s gaze went briefly distant, then returned, bright. “I’m terrified,” he said. “And I’m happy. Which is annoying.” Miles smiled, small. “So,” Asim said, tapping the table lightly, dragging them back. “Here’s my advice. Go home. Sit her down. Say: I can feel you going away from me and I’m scared. I don’t need you to be fine. I need you to be honest.”
Miles’s chest tightened. “And if she says ‘nothing’?” Miles asked.
Asim leaned in. “Then you say: Okay. But I’m here. And I’m not leaving. And I’m going to keep asking, because I love you.”
Miles snorted. “You make it sound easy.”
Asim grinned. “It’s not easy,” he said. “It’s intimate. And you two are famously allergic to saying the mushy bits out loud unless you’re singing.”
Miles rolled his eyes. “We’re not.” Asim raised his eyebrows. Miles sighed. “Alright. Maybe we are.” His eyes moved to the TV set Jonathan installed high on the wall, right behind the bar. A football match was playing, the comment set on a volume so low even Miles had trouble understanding. Yet, he immediately spotted two things: the colors of the Manchester City uniforms and the player who just scored a goal. “Oi, that’s Novak!” he yelled, pointing at the player.
Asim turned his head. “He’s an anthro,” he commented. “I didn’t know there were anthro football players.”
Miles nodded, his eyes gleaming enthusiastically. “He’s a Czechoslovakian Wolfdog, the team bought him like two years ago. One of the first anthro players—and one of the best. He’s revolutionised the team, he thinks of the players as a pack. We’re going to win the next Premier League with him, right that? And we’re going to get at least to the semifinals in the Champions League.”
“I’ve never seen you so involved in football.”
Miles averted his eyes from the TV. “Oh, yes.” He passed a paw on his neck, visibly embarrassed. “It’s a family thing. When I was a pup, dad didn’t want me to go to the stadium because he felt it would have been too dangerous for me, so we started following matches from the TV. The entire family, three generations… huddling in our small living room, cheering for the team. I’m not that involved any more, but dad’s still a great fan and… matches are fond memories.” He looked at his watch. “But it’s time for me to get back home, or Lils is going to start roaming the city with a knife and too many questions to find me.”
Asim’s smiled. “Go be brave,” he said. “Or at least go be honest. You’re good at honest, when you remember to do it.”
Miles stared at his pint for a moment longer. Then he stood. “Thanks,” he said.
Asim waved him off. “If you two start communicating properly, I’ll have to find new entertainment,” he said. “Don’t do it too well. Leave me some drama.”
Miles shook his head, but the tightness in his chest eased by a fraction.
He went out into the damp London night with Asim’s words in his ears, trying to work out how to ask the right question.
 
# Act VI
## Chapter 21: Repairs  

### 6.12.1
The flat was quiet. Miles had turned the television down to background volume and still the room seemed too loud with absence: the tick of the cheap clock on the wall, the low hum of the fridge, the occasional bus exhaling at the stop outside. Lillian was at the academy and Miles was alone with his own hands.
The ring box sat in his palm. It was ridiculous, how small it was. He’d been holding it for minutes, maybe longer, his thumb pressed against the edge through the fabric of the little square like contact could keep the decision from floating away. He stared at it.
Then, because his body never tolerated stillness for long, he got up.
He checked the lock.
He straightened the pile of sheet music on the sideboard.
He aligned the remote control with the edge of the coffee table.
He made tea he didn’t want.
The kettle clicked, the mug warmed his fingers, and the ring box stayed in his pocket, a weight pulling his jacket slightly off balance. He stood in the middle of the living room and tried to name what he was doing. “Proposing. Marrying.”
A decade together and still, the word marriage made something in him tighten like a bolt.
He loved Lillian. That was the easiest part. He loved her in the way he loved gravity: quietly, constantly, with the certainty that if it vanished he would simply fall. But love didn’t automatically turn into paperwork. Love didn’t automatically turn into a family that the state recognised. Love didn’t automatically turn into Sarah being allowed to stop sleeping under a leaking roof.
That was the brutal part, the part that scared him more than anything else. 
Miles sat down on the sofa, careful, as if the cushions might break under the weight of his thoughts. He took the ring box out again: it sat on his palm like a small engine part. A component, a hinge. He knew what he’d told himself: this makes it easier. If we’re married, they’ll take us seriously. If we’re married, we can adopt. If we can adopt, Sarah can have a home. If Sarah comes home, maybe the kids in the warehouse will follow her out like ducklings.
Maybe.
Maybe was not a plan.
He ran his thumb over the edge of the box again. “Am I doing this because I want it?” he asked himself. “Or because I need it?” The question made his stomach tighten.
He looked around the flat. Their flat. Mandela Street, yellow walls, scuffed skirting board, the framed gig poster they kept meaning to straighten. It smelled like Lillian even when she wasn’t there: cardamom and iodine and a faint, ghostly memory of freshly-dug soil. It smelled like him too—his fur shampoo, turpentine and lavender. A home built out of small consistencies.
He could picture her walking in, shrugging off her coat, complaining about the BBC, humming as she filled the kettle. He could picture her hands on the piano keys. He could picture her face when she was tired and trying not to show it.
He could picture her face when she met Sarah. Tears. Rage. A softness that had made Miles’s chest ache.
He loved her.
He wanted her.
The ring was not a trick.
Still.
He thought of Sarah’s eyes. Sixteen and already carrying the kind of authority that came from being the only one who turned up. Sixteen and already having to be the adult in charge. He thought of the way she’d said it’s my warehouse like it was both pride and a curse.
He thought of Vee clinging to someone’s sleeve. He thought of the smell of damp fur and bleach and fear.
He didn’t want to be a saviour. He didn’t want to walk into the warehouse with bags and feel good about himself while the kids clung to him like gratitude was a payment. He just wanted them to stop being hungry, to stop having to negotiate safety. He wanted Sarah to have a bed that wasn’t concrete, to have homework that was just homework, to be allowed to be sixteen.
On the television, an advert cut in. Bright colours, a kitchen that looked like a showroom. A human woman—thin, clean, smiling—lifted a baby out of a high chair. A man kissed her cheek. Two blond children laughed over cereal as if laughter was a product you could buy. Miles stared.
No anthros.
Of course.
Anthro families didn’t sell. Anthro families were either tragedy or debate.
He felt something bitter curl in his chest.
The advert faded into the next segment. A news anchor, crisp suit, serious tone.
AUSTERITY MEASURES.
CUTS TO SOCIAL SERVICES.
“Local authorities have warned,” the presenter said, “that reduced funding will impact vulnerable young people…” 
Miles’s fingers tightened around the ring box. Vulnerable young people. He pictured Sarah again, holding a pencil like it was a weapon.
He pictured the warehouse. He pictured the council offices that would file the kids away into categories—hard to place, complex needs, high risk—then close at five and go home. He pictured the police car that drove past and didn’t stop. He pictured the social worker who might show up, once, with a clipboard, and then vanish under a stack of caseload. He pictured nobody coming.
And he realised that his question was, in itself, a kind of luxury. The right reason was that Sarah was real. The kids were real, the warehouse was real, the state’s absence was real.
Miles exhaled slowly. He opened the ring box. The band inside caught the dull living-room light, simple and solid, built to be lived in. He held it between thumb and forefinger and imagined it on Lillian’s hand.
Not as a symbol.
As a tool.
A piece of metal that would change how other people treated them.
He hated that.
He accepted it.
He leaned back and closed his eyes for a moment.
Was he ready to be a father?
The question returned, quieter now. He thought of his own dad’s voice: You do it proper.
He thought of his mum: Life is complicated. We’ll manage.
He thought of Lillian asking him, half joking, half terrified: Dae ye ever think aboot bein a faither?
He thought of how he’d answered. Be good at it. That was still what he wanted—to be good at it. Not because it would make him a hero. Because if you were going to take a kid out of a warehouse, you didn’t get to be half‑arse about it.
He opened his eyes. He looked at the ring.
He looked at the flat. He pictured Lillian coming home.
He pictured her taking off her shoes, sighing, saying something rude about the government, as usual. He pictured her looking at him with that steady warmth that always made him feel like he could stand up straighter. “I love you,” he said aloud, to the empty room, testing the words like a measurement.
It felt true.
It felt small.
It felt enormous.
He closed the ring box and slid it back into his pocket.
The television kept talking about budget cuts. Miles reached for the remote and turned it off. In the sudden quiet, he could hear his own breathing. 
He went to the kitchen and started chopping onions for dinner, because if Lillian came home tired he wanted there to be something warm waiting. 
Care, he reminded himself, was mostly logistics. And love—love was the thing you did anyway, even when you were scared.

### 6.21.2
The leak over the far corner of the warehouse had gotten worse. Sarah lay awake listening to it. Drip, drip, drip, into the bucket she’d put under it. Each drop sounded like time passing. Or like something wearing a hole in the roof, slowly.
She was on the mezzanine, as always, where she could see the whole hall if she sat up. Below her, the younger kids slept in their nests of blankets and mattresses, breaths soft and uneven. Someone snored. Someone whimpered in a dream. Her own mattress was thin, springs digging into her back. Her coat served as a blanket. Her rucksack, packed with essentials, sat by her head. Ready, in case.
Her paws itched with the urge to move, to check yet again that the doors were bolted, that Connor’s crew weren’t lurking anywhere inside. She knew they weren’t; she’d watched them leave, swaggering, after the last confrontation. But the itch didn’t care.
She sat up slowly, careful not to wake the kid curled at the end of her mattress—a small rabbit anthro who’d been dropped off three nights ago by an aunt who’d said, “Just for a bit,” and never came back.
She pulled her hoodie on and slid down the metal stairs, avoiding the squeaky step without looking. Down on the floor, the air was cooler. She walked between the sleeping kids, checking automatically: was that blanket slipping? Did that tail have enough space? Were there feet sticking out from under covers?
She tucked a hoodie around Lila’s shoulders, nudged Faroukh’s foot back onto his mattress. He muttered and kicked even in sleep. At the far corner, the bucket was half full. She emptied it into the sink in the tiny toilet block, watching the water swirl down.
On the way back, she paused by the office. The door hung a little crookedly; the frame had warped with damp. Inside, the pinboard was bare. She’d taken down the torn drawing weeks ago. The memory of NOTYOUS still burned in her mind. She sat at the old desk, knees bumping the underside, and pulled her sketchpad from her rucksack. Paper was running low. Charcoal too. She rationed it like food.
She opened to a blank page and stared at it.
She’d been drawing more lately, not less. Faces mostly. Kids’ faces, caught mid‑laugh, mid‑glare. Connor’s face, sometimes, when she needed to burn off anger. Miles and Lillian, always, in different configurations. That night, the lines refused to come. Her paw hovered over the page, then dropped.
She leaned back, staring at the stained ceiling.
“I want out,” she said, quietly, to the empty room. The words felt like a confession. She’d thought it before. On cold nights when the wind cut through the cracks in the walls. On days when the older Strays pushed and sneered. On mornings when she woke up so tired her bones felt hollow. But she’d never said it out loud. “I want out,” she repeated. “I’m sixteen. I’m so tired.”
Her voice sounded small in the echo. She rubbed her eyes with the heels of her paws. It wasn’t that she wanted some fairy‑tale life. She wasn’t stupid. Even if she left, there would still be forms and stares. But when she thought about the van and the flat and the way Miles had once described their kitchen, something in her chest clenched.
A room with a door that locked properly. Heating that worked without having to hotwire the system. A bed that was hers, not just whatever mattress hadn't got soaked that week. Shoes that weren’t two sizes too big, held on with string. A table where adults drank tea and argued about politics instead of whose turn it was to stay up on watch.
And the kids. Always the kids.
Lila, who still flinched at loud noises but had started humming under her breath when she thought no one was listening. Faroukh, who pretended not to care but always made sure the little ones got the last of the biscuits. The rabbit boy, who sucked his thumb in his sleep and clung to his rucksack with both paws. If she left, what happened to them?
Connor would move in proper. He’d already pushed, tested boundaries. The “tax” on food. The smashed drawing. The threat in his eyes. She could picture it: one night she wasn’t here, one night she chose herself over them, and the next morning there’d be bruises and missing tins and maybe worse.
“You’re not their mum,” she muttered to herself. “You’re not their social worker. You’re a kid as well.”
It didn’t matter. She was the one who’d been here longest. The one who had a head for logistics. The one who knew which pipes rattled because they were old and which rattled because someone bigger was sneaking in. She had made herself the person they looked at when something went wrong. “I did this to myself,” she said bitterly.
She thought of Miles and Lillian again. The way they moved together. The way they always came back. She thought of the drawing Connor had torn. She’d drawn them walking away, she remembered. Away from the warehouse, towards something else. “What would you do?” she asked the empty room, addressing their imagined selves. “If you were me? If you were stuck in here and people were looking at you like you were the only thing between them and the dark?”
She could almost hear Miles’s answer: Plan. Assess. Risk‑manage. Make lists, cause that’s what he did for everything from food stocks to gig logistics.
She could almost hear Lillian: Tell someone. Don’t carry it alone. Ask for help before it breaks you.
The thought of asking for help made her skin crawl. Pride wrapped itself tight around her ribs. But there were cracks in the pride now. Hairline fractures where exhaustion had seeped in.
She flipped the sketchpad to a new page. Instead of drawing, she wrote.

Pros of leaving:  
Not freezing to death.
Not getting stabbed by Connor.
Back to school.
Warm bed.
Less responsible for everyone else’s shit.

Cons of leaving:  
Kids get hurt.
Connor takes over.
Guilt that never goes away.
Miles and Lillian thinking I’m selfish.

She stared at the lists. She could argue with herself all night. She’d done it before. It always came back to the same stuck point: she couldn’t save everyone. She couldn’t save anyone if she went down with the building. Last week, one of the younger boys had tripped on a broken pallet and cut his head open. Blood had poured down his face, startlingly bright. She’d pressed a towel to it with shaking paws, shouting for someone to fetch the first‑aid kit Miles had left.
The cut had needed stitches. But the thought of taking him to A&E, explaining who they were, had made her stomach knot. They’d cleaned it, taped it, watched for infection. He’d been lucky. “What happens when we’re no lucky?” she whispered.
Her paw moved almost of its own accord. She wrote another heading.

Other options:
Talk to Miles. Ask about one of the younger ones staying somewhere else. 
Find a way to get Connor out.
Burn the whole place down and start again. (She scribbled that last one out, though the image held a certain dark appeal.)

Somewhere deep inside, the truth sat like a stone: she wanted to go with them. Miles and Lillian. She wanted to get out of the warehouse, not just for a night, but properly. Admitting it felt like betrayal. She put the pen down and pressed her paws over her face. “Okay,” she said, to herself, to the room, to whatever was listening. “Line in the sand.” She pictured it: a literal line on the warehouse floor, chalked between the kids and Connor’s crew. A boundary. A point past which she would not retreat. “If someone else gets seriously hurt,” she said quietly, “we’re done. I’m done letting this place be the only option.” The promise settled in her chest, heavy and fragile.
It wasn’t an answer. It was a condition.
It scared her, how much she meant it.
She closed the sketchpad gently, slid it back into her rucksack. On impulse, she took out the folded, torn drawing—Miles and Lillian, ripped in half—and smoothed it on the desk.
She found tape in the drawer. Carefully, she aligned the edges and pressed them together. The tear was still visible, a scar through the middle. But the figures stood again. Two adults, side by side, walking towards something she couldn’t quite see. “If I walk with you,” she murmured, “someone has to walk with them.”
She didn’t know yet who that would be. Maybe no one. Maybe she was lying to herself. But the line was drawn now, even if it was only in her head. She folded the repaired drawing and slipped it into the inside pocket of her hoodie, close to her chest. On the way back to the mezzanine, she paused by the leak. The bucket was already filling again.
“Tomorrow,” she told it. “I’ll fix you tomorrow. One more day.”
She crawled back onto her mattress, the rabbit boy curling automatically towards her warmth. She rested a paw lightly on his back, feeling the rise and fall of his breathing.
“One more day,” she whispered. “Then we see.”
Sleep didn’t come easily. When it did, it brought dreams of lines drawn in chalk on wet concrete, washing away under rain.

### 6.21.3
It was a Friday night. Lillian and Miles sat at the kitchen table in their flat, adoption forms spread out between them like a second dinner. They had been working on them for the whole time, and the pile of documents they both provided was enough to prove them capable of virtually anything. On the whiteboard they used for shopping lists, Miles had drawn columns: CURRENT, POST‑PROMOTION, WITH CHILD. Under each he’d put numbers—wages, rent, bills, rough estimates for “kid stuff.” Lillian’s hair was scraped back in a bun, glasses perched low on her nose. She had the look she got when marking exams: fierce concentration with a thread of irritation. “You did this for fun before we even thought about adoption,” she said, tapping the board with the end of her pen.
“Some of us find comfort in knowing how much loo roll we can afford,” Miles said. “It’s called being responsible.”
She made a non‑committal noise, half amusement, half exhaustion.
He watched her as she scribbled notes on the side of a form titled FINANCIAL STABILITY. Her paw trembled slightly when she thought he wasn’t looking. The social worker’s words from the adoption office ghosted between them: Panels view marriage positively. It demonstrates stability.
They’d mocked it on the way home, in the way they always mocked bureaucratic nonsense. Anthros needing extra paperwork. Fat anthros, doubly so. Anthro couples, triply. But the phrase had stuck in his head.
Marriage as a tick box.
He hated it. He also couldn’t ignore it.
He cleared his throat.
“I’ve been thinking,” he said.
She didn’t look up. “Dangerous.”
“About the marriage thing,” he said.
Her pen stilled. She looked up slowly, eyes narrowing.
“If you’re about to propose because some social worker thinks rings are sexy, I swear to God, Miles—”
“Hey,” he said, palms up. “Let me finish before you throw the stapler at me.”
She snorted despite herself.
He took a breath. “We’ve been together ten years,” he said. “We share a flat, a bank account, a band, a social life. We argue about whose turn it is to do the bins. We’ve already done all the bits people say marriage is supposed to be practice for.”
“You forgot shared trauma,” she said dryly.
“Aye, that too,” he said. “We’ve got a solid portfolio there.” She huffed. “We’ve not got married,” he went on, “because neither of us needed a registrar to tell us what we already knew. We love each other. We’re in. We’re committed. This is it.” She nodded slowly. “But the system,” he said, jerking his chin at the forms, “is built for people who like bits of paper. And I keep thinking… if a ring on our fingers and a certificate in a drawer makes some panel look at us and go, ‘Oh, good, stable, married,’ and that’s what gets us Sarah—or any kid—out of a warehouse and into a bed in a real room and with a hot dish on a kitchen table… then maybe it’s not the worst thing in the world to play their game.”
He watched her face as he spoke, looking for signs of anger, hurt, anything. Her eyes stayed on him, unreadable. “So this is a legal strategy,” she said. “A tactical marriage.”
“Partly,” he said.
“Romantic,” she said, but there was no real bite in it.
