He swallowed. “It’s also,” he said, words coming slower now, “because I want to. For its own sake. I want to stand in a room with our people and say, ‘This is the person I’ve built my life with.’ I want you to see my name on forms where that helps and I want to see yours where it matters. I want to dance with you to some daft song at ‘The Innards’ while Asim makes filthy jokes on the mic.” Her mouth twitched. “Mostly,” he said, “I want to put in writing what’s already true: you’re my family. Have been since we were twenty and I spilled curry on your music scores.”
She laughed at that, a short, surprised sound. “I still haven’t forgiven you,” she said. “Those were Schubert.”
“He’d have appreciated the spice,” Miles said.
The laugh faded. She looked down at the forms, then back at him. “You ken we’d be doing this in part for a child,” she said. “Some Stray, some kid in the system. That’s a weird thing to say about a wedding.”
“Aye,” he said. “It is. But we’ve never been big on doing things the normal way.”
She tilted her head. “And you’re sure,” she said slowly, “that you’re no feeling… I dinnae ken… pushed into this? Like you have to make a grand gesture to make up for something I haven’t told you?”
The sentence hung between them, odd and sharp. He frowned.
“What haven’t you told me?” he asked.
She swallowed, looked away. For a moment he thought this is it, this is the thing she’s been carrying. Then she shook her head. “Nothing,” she said. “Forget it. I’m just… tired.”
The wall between them flickered into view, then invisible again. He let it go, for that moment. Pushing hadn’t worked so far. He reached into his pocket. “I was going to do this somewhere fancier,” he said. “Restaurant, maybe. Or the rehearsal room. But this is where we actually live.”
He pulled out the ring box. It wasn’t fancy. A simple silver band in a plain little box. Lillian stared at the box, then at him. “If you get down on one knee, I will kick you,” she said, but her voice quivered for a second, standing on unsure legs.
“I can’t get down on one knee anyway,” he said. “I got hurt yesterday moving crates.”
Her mouth twitched again.
He flipped the box open.
“Lillian Edgecombe,” he said. “Will you marry me so social workers will shut up, and also because I love you more than anyone else on this stupid island?”
Her eyes gleamed suspiciously. “You said the L‑word,” she said.
“Aye,” he said. “Get used to it.”
She looked down at the ring, then back up at him. Her breathing had gone shallow, glasses fogging slightly at the edges. “Say it again,” she whispered.
He rolled his eyes, but his chest warmed. “I love you,” he said. “You stubborn, brilliant badger. I have loved you since you corrected my rhythm in theory class and then shared your chips with me at the chippy.”
Her paws trembled as she reached for the ring. “Fine,” she said, voice rough. “I’ll marry you. But if any social worker says congratulations like they made it happen, I’m biting them.”
“Deal,” he said.
He took her paw and slid the ring on. It looked right there, snug against her fur, catching the kitchen light. She looked at it for a long moment, then at him. “We’re really doing this,” she said.
“Looks like,” he said. “We’ll have to tell the others. Asim will explode.”
She laughed, properly this time, the sound filling the little kitchen. “Juno will write an essay about it,” she said. “Milla will cry. Felix will pretend he doesnae care and then turn up with a bottle.”
“Jonathan will paint it,” Miles said.
“Of course he will,” she replied. “On the outer wall of St. Paul, overnight, as a cubist masterpiece.” Her expression softened. “There’s one more reason I’ll say yes,” she said quietly. “Not just the social workers and the romance.”
“Yes?” he asked.
“Because I want Sarah,” she said. “Or whoever ends up being our kid. I want them to ken that we chose every part of this on purpose. That we built the paperwork we needed because the system’s broken, but underneath it, this—” she gestured between them “—was always about us deciding to be each other’s person.”
He felt something loosen in his chest. “Then we’ll tell them that,” he said. “Someday.”
They sat for a while, hands linked across the table, forms forgotten for the moment.

 
## Chapter 22: Family Meetings

### 6.22.1
Sarah’s day had no beginning.

It only had the first problem—then the next one climbing over it, then the next one, and the next—until the warehouse felt less like a building and more like a mouth she had to keep feeding so it didn’t bite.

The air was cold enough to sting the inside of her nose. Someone had opened the loading bay a crack earlier to let smoke out and forgotten to seal it again; the gap let in February damp and the smell of wet tarmac, and it dragged the heat from the kids’ bodies like a thief.

“Iona,” Sarah called.

A tarp shifted near the far wall. Iona’s seal head emerged, eyes half‑lidded. She was thirteen, big in a way that would have looked luxurious in another life—smooth grey skin, a heavy softness over muscle—but in here her bulk worked against her. Without a bath, without clean water, her skin cracked along the folds at her neck and under her arms. She scratched and pretended she wasn’t.

Sarah had seen the cracks this morning and her stomach had dropped the way it did when she realised the roof leak had got worse.

Iona didn’t answer.

That wasn’t defiance. That was the slow, flat withdrawal of someone whose body was starting to hurt in ways she didn’t have language for.

Sarah hauled the tarp across the concrete again, dragging it toward the corner where the one working lamp made a tired circle of light. The tarp was meant to be a roof patch, a piece of blue plastic scavenged from a skip behind a building site. Sarah had nicked it with her heart in her throat, hands shaking on the fence as if stealing plastic could get her arrested the same way stealing a person’s childhood had.

She’d nicked water too.

Not much.

A jerry can, half full, taken from a construction site tap that hadn’t been locked.

It sat by her feet now, heavy and precious.

She crouched, knees protesting, and started to tie the tarp corners to four stacked crates. She’d planned it in her head like a diagram: tarp as basin, crates as legs, water in, Iona in, quick wash, skin softened, cracks cleaned, ointment if she had any.

A field hospital.

A bath made of bin liners and stubbornness.

The knot slipped.

“Fuck’s sake,” Sarah muttered, then bit the word back because Vee was only six and listened like the world was a secret she could learn.

“Sarah?” Vee’s rabbit voice came from behind her.

Sarah didn’t turn. “Not now,” she said.

“Something broke,” Vee whispered.

Of course it did.

Sarah’s mind flicked through a catalogue: lamp, plate, kettle, the little mirror Laleh used to pluck her eyebrows because it made her feel like she still had a face that belonged to her.

“What broke?” Sarah asked.

Vee hesitated. “The… the light thing,” she said.

The lamp.

Sarah’s jaw tightened.

The lamp was not just light. The lamp was safety. Light meant you could see who was moving at night; it meant you could spot bruises and infections and the way Tomas’s eyes went too wide when he thought food was disappearing.

Sarah forced her hands to keep working.

“Iona first,” she told herself, like saying it out loud made it true.

Behind her, Elise’s voice cut through the warehouse murmur, sharp and clear.

“This is stupid,” Elise said.

Sarah’s shoulders went tight. Elise was seventeen, ibex, horns that made her look older than she was and a spine like a fencepost. She didn’t challenge for fun; she challenged because she could smell weakness like damp.

“Which bit?” Sarah asked without looking up.

“The quarter,” Elise said. “Connor’s lot. Why are we still doing that? We need it more.”

A couple of older teens went quiet. Daisy, big cow body leaning against a pillar, watched with tired eyes. Noor’s ears angled forward. Bruno’s gaze stayed on his hands, but Sarah could feel his attention like weight.

This was never just one question.

It was always an audition.

Sarah tightened the knot again. This time it held.

She breathed in.

She stood.

Then she turned to Elise.

“You want the honest answer?” Sarah asked.

Elise lifted her chin. “I want the smart answer,” she said.

Sarah nodded once, as if she’d been offered a deal.

“They’ve got five kids under ten,” she said. “Same as us. Hungrier than us, some nights. If we don’t give them something, they take it. Not politely. Not with a rota.” She let the word hang there—rota, like a joke on the edge of a knife. “This way, we choose. We control what leaves this building. We keep Connor outside more often than not. And we make sure the little ones in their lot don’t die to prove a point.”

Elise’s mouth tightened.

“That’s not fair,” Elise muttered.

“It’s not fair,” Sarah agreed. Her voice stayed level. “It’s not fair that we’re feeding anyone with stolen water. But we don’t get to pick a fair world. We get to pick the least bad option.”

Noor made a small approving hum. Daisy’s shoulders loosened, fractionally.

Elise stared at Sarah for a long second, then looked away like she’d lost interest.

She hadn’t.

She’d been answered.

Sarah turned back to the tarp.

“Help me,” she said.

Mateo, the badger boy who could dig a sleeping hollow into the warehouse wall like it was soft earth, stepped forward without being asked. “Where?” he said.

“Hold that corner,” Sarah told him. “Keep it high.”

He did.

Sarah hauled the jerry can up and tipped it.

Water sloshed into the tarp basin with a sound that made half the room look up.

The smell of clean water hit the air and the warehouse felt, for a second, like a body smelling food.

Tomas’s eyes flicked to it.

Sarah saw.

She always saw.

“Tomas,” she called, sharp.

Tomas froze.

He was nine, hedgehog, small spines flattened with fear. He had a way of collecting things—buttons, broken pencils, stale biscuits—in hidden corners as if the act of hoarding could build walls around him.

Sarah’s chest tightened.

She’d found his stash yesterday.

A bag of bread crusts and half a tin of peaches.

Food she’d counted as available.

Food she’d already rationed out in her head.

The discovery had changed the maths, which meant it had changed the week.

“You’ve been hoarding again,” Sarah said.

Tomas’s spines lifted in a small defensive flare. He didn’t speak.

Sarah kept her voice calm because calm was sometimes the only mercy she could afford.

“I’m not angry that you’re scared,” she said. “I’m angry that you didn’t tell me. Because when you hide food, I think we’ve got more than we do, and then everybody eats less later.”

Tomas’s mouth trembled. “I—” he started.

Sarah held up a paw. Not stop. Just: wait.

“We’ll fix it,” she said. “You and me. After Iona. You bring me what you’ve got, and we’ll put it somewhere you can see it without it being yours alone. A reserve. Alright?”

Tomas blinked.

It was the first time his eyes had looked like a kid’s in days.

He nodded once.

Sarah filed it away.

Reserve. Portion recalculation.

Seven days.

Twenty‑two mouths.

Plus Connor’s quarter.

Plus the small unknown of what Miles might bring.

Kimi.

Sarah’s mind dragged itself back to Kimi like a sore tooth.

“Where’s Kimi?” she asked.

No one answered.

Vee’s ears flattened.

Sarah felt her own stomach drop again.

Kimi was eight, weasel, hands that never stopped moving. When Kimi melted down, the warehouse went brittle.

“Nate,” Sarah said.

Nate, thirteen, lab-shepherd cross, looked up immediately. Conflict‑avoidant, loyal, the kind of kid who tried to smooth the air with his own body.

“Find Kimi,” Sarah said. “Don’t lecture. Don’t ask questions. Just sit with him.”

Nate nodded and slipped away between pallets.

Sarah turned back to the tarp bath.

“Iona,” she said again, softer now.

Iona crawled out of her tarp nest with slow, heavy movements, her body built for water and forced into concrete. She looked at the makeshift basin with a mixture of longing and embarrassment.

“I don’t want everyone watching,” she muttered.

Sarah’s throat tightened.

“We’ll make a screen,” she said.

Melanie and Laleh, without being asked, grabbed a couple of blankets and held them up between two pillars, turning a slice of warehouse into privacy.

Sarah nodded at them, gratitude that didn’t have time for words.

She crouched again, testing the tarp edges with her claws.

Then the tarp gave a quiet, betrayed sound.

A thin line of water ran out and dripped onto the concrete.

Sarah’s breath caught.

Not much.

But enough.

Enough to matter.

Enough to mean that in ten minutes, the bath would be a puddle and Iona’s skin would still be cracking and Sarah would have wasted stolen water for nothing.

She swallowed.

Her hands moved.

She grabbed a strip of duct tape from the supplies crate—Miles’s duct tape, from the last bag of “random useful things” he’d insisted was not charity—and pressed it over the leak with the heel of her palm.

Her fingers were shaking.

She made them stop.

“One more strip,” she told herself.

“Sarah,” Bruno said quietly, from behind her.

She didn’t look up. “Not now,” she said, already bracing.

Bruno’s voice stayed low, almost casual. “My hands are hurting,” he said.

Just that.

Like it didn’t matter.

Like pain was a thing you mentioned the way you mentioned weather.

Sarah’s jaw tightened.

She pictured him, seventeen, rottweiler, big shoulders and careful restraint, rewiring stolen extension cords with bare paws and a screwdriver that didn’t fit.

She pictured his hands, cracked pads, sore joints.

She pictured not having him.

“Show me later,” she said, still working the tape down. “I’ll figure something out.”

“Alright,” Bruno replied, like he hadn’t asked for anything at all.

Sarah finished sealing the leak.

She pressed again.

Held.

For now.

“Okay,” she said.

She looked up and found Faroukh standing near the pillar, glancing at the clock on his phone and then away as if time itself might attack.

Sarah felt her chest tighten.

Phone call soon.

Gone for hours.

One less reliable pair of hands at evening feeding.

She could already feel the gap.

She forced herself not to look at him too long.

If she looked too long, she’d have to ask questions she couldn’t afford.

“Iona,” she said. “In.”

Iona climbed carefully into the tarp basin. Sarah steadied her by the elbow, the touch brief and practical. The water rose around Iona’s body, making her sigh in a way that sounded like relief and shame braided together.

Sarah poured a little more water over Iona’s shoulders with a plastic cup, careful not to splash too much, careful not to waste.

“Slow,” she murmured. “Breathe.”

Iona nodded.

The warehouse noise softened.

Even Elise went quiet.

For ten minutes, the warehouse was a bath.

A screen of blankets.

A girl’s cracked skin eased back toward something survivable.

Sarah’s shoulders stayed tight anyway.

She was still doing sums.

Half a tin per person per day.

Bread on alternate mornings.

Reserve visible to Tomas.

Connor’s quarter.

Faroukh’s absence.

Kimi’s broken lamp.

She was calculating while she poured water and while she listened to Iona’s breathing and while she watched the door.

Because doors were always the real problem.

When Iona climbed out again, skin damp and less angry, Sarah handed her an old towel and nodded toward the blankets. “Sit,” she said. “Warm up. No scratching.”

Iona’s mouth twitched. “Yes, boss,” she murmured.

Sarah pretended not to hear the affection.

She turned.

She followed a scent trail without meaning to.

Something clean.

Soap and outside air.

Bread.

She looked toward the loading bay.

Miles stood there, just inside the gap of the door, holding a carrier bag in one paw like he wasn’t sure where to put it.

He’d seen everything.

The bath.

The tape.

Elise’s challenge.

Tomas’s panic.

Bruno’s quiet pain.

Her own voice holding the whole room up.

Miles’s tail wagged slowly when their eyes met.

Not eager.

Not frantic.

Just… there.

Sarah went still.

The old reflex fired—have I done something wrong; is he going to tell someone; is he going to walk away because this is too messy.

Miles didn’t move closer.

He didn’t look past her like she was a door.

He just said, in a voice so simple it made her throat hurt:

“How long have you been doing this alone?”

Sarah stared at him.

For a second, the warehouse was silent enough that she could hear the leak in the roof, drip by drip, counting out their life.

Then she swallowed.

Her mouth opened.

The truth came out without drama.

“Since I was ten,” she said.

Miles’s ears angled forward.

Something in his face—shock, grief, respect—shifted like a light turning on.

Sarah wanted to look away.

She didn’t.

Not this time.

### 6.22.2
Asim spent the entire bus ride rehearsing how not to be dramatic.
It was ridiculous. He had stood on stages in front of rooms full of strangers and made them sing along to songs about being unwanted. He had argued with councillors on microphones. He had faced his father’s stare and survived it.
And yet the idea of walking into his mother’s kitchen with Toby at his side made his stomach twist like he’d swallowed a live cable.
Toby sat beside him, hands clasped around a paper bag from the bakery. Cake. Peace offering. He’d gone for something conservative—plain sponge with jam, nothing too modern, nothing too messy. The sort of cake an auntie could approve of even while disapproving of everything else. “You’re doing that thing with your jaw,” Toby murmured.
Asim blinked. “What thing?”
“The clenching thing,” Toby said. “Like you’re trying to crack your own molars.”
Asim exhaled. “I’m fine,” he lied.
Toby’s mouth twitched. “Sure,” he said. “And I’m the Royal Baby.”
Asim snorted despite himself. The humour didn’t fix the fear. It just made it breathable.
When they got off the bus and started the walk, Asim’s body remembered the route on its own: the corner shop, the halal butcher with the fluorescent lights, the row of semi‑detached houses with tidy front gardens and satellite dishes like small, suspicious eyes.
His parents’ house looked exactly as it always had.
That was the problem.
He stood on the front step for a moment too long.
Toby nudged him gently with his elbow. “Want me to knock?”
Asim shook his head. “No,” he said. “If anyone’s going to be rejected on this doorstep, it’s me. Tradition.”
Toby huffed a laugh.
Asim knocked.
The door opened almost immediately.
His mother was there in a patterned headscarf and slippers, her eyes already shining with the kind of emotion she usually hid under briskness.
For a second, Asim forgot every sentence he’d rehearsed.
“Hooyo,” he said.
Her face softened, then tightened again like she was trying not to cry on principle. “Asim,” she said, and his name sounded like a scolding and a blessing at once. Her gaze flicked past him.
Toby stepped forward slightly, not too eager. “Hi,” he said. “I’m Toby. Thank you for having me.” His tail was wagging earnestly.
Asim’s mother looked at him for a long, assessing beat. Then she opened the door wider.
“Come in,” she said.
Asim’s lungs unclenched by a fraction.
The hallway smelled of cumin, frying onions, and detergent. Shoes lined up neatly by the rack. A framed photo of Asim as a cub sat on the side table, grinning in a too‑big school jumper. His father’s voice came from the living room. “Who is it?”
Asim’s mother didn’t answer immediately. “Take your coat off,” she told Asim instead, as if he were twelve again. “And you,” she added, eyes on Toby, “you can put yours there.”
Toby obeyed, careful and polite.
They stepped into the living room.
His father sat in the armchair, newspaper folded on his lap like a shield. He looked up.
The temperature changed. Asim felt it in his fur.
His father’s gaze flicked from Asim to Toby and back again.
“Salaam,” Toby said quietly.
Asim’s father didn’t return it.
Asim opened his mouth—ready, always, to fight.
Before he could, his mother clapped her hands once, sharp.
“Tea,” she said. “Everyone will have tea. We are not doing this in the doorway like animals.”
Asim stared.
Toby’s eyes widened, then he swallowed a smile.
They sat.
Tea appeared like it always did in that house: too hot, too sweet, poured with the force of habit. Plates. Biscuits. The cake Toby had brought, unwrapped and sliced with ceremonial care.
His mother tasted it and nodded once. Approval.
Asim watched Toby’s shoulders drop by a millimetre.
His father stared at the tea as if it had personally betrayed him.
No one touched anyone. No one made any declarations. But Asim’s mother kept refilling Toby’s cup.
She asked him questions—where he was from, what he did, whether his parents were still alive, whether he knew how to fix a broken boiler. Toby answered in the careful, respectful tone he’d saved for probation officers and head teachers.
At one point Asim’s mother turned to Asim and said, very simply, “You look tired.”
Asim laughed once, surprised and raw. “Yes,” he admitted. “I am.”
She reached across the table and patted his paw, quick and firm, like she was checking he was real.
His father watched the gesture.
Didn’t stop it.
Asim felt his throat tighten. Not acceptance. But a crack.

### 6.22.2
Band planning happened in Lillian and Miles’s kitchen, because it always did.

It was the only room in the flat that could hold all of them if everyone stood at the right angles and nobody tried to open the cupboard without warning.

Miles sat at the table with a notebook, drawing boxes and arrows as if a set list was a kind of plumbing.

Felix leaned against the counter, arms folded, making a face at everything.

Milla perched on a stool, posture precise even in a domestic space, a mug of tea cooling between her paws.

Asim sat on the floor because he claimed chairs were “a conspiracy against tall people,” guitar leaning against his shoulder.

Juno hovered by the door for a moment with her new flute case pressed against her chest like contraband.

Lillian looked up from the sink where she was rinsing a mug and smiled. “Hullo, love,” she said. “You look like you’ve stolen something.”

Juno’s ears heated. “I did,” she said.

Miles glanced up. “Should we be worried?”

“Only if you’re the kind of person who hates music,” Juno replied.

Felix snorted. “I hate loads of music,” he said.

“Then you’re in trouble,” Juno shot back.

Lillian dried her paws on a tea towel and came closer, eyes flicking to the case. “Go on then,” she said. “Show us.”

Juno set the case on the table with careful reverence and opened it.

The flute inside caught the kitchen light.

Asim’s face softened immediately, like someone had reached inside him and tweaked a knob.

“Och,” Lillian breathed, then covered it with a cough. “That’s lovely.”

Juno’s throat tightened. “It fits,” she said, simple.

Milla leaned forward. “The spacing is better,” she noted, and Juno’s shoulders loosened at the fact Milla’s praise came in the form of practical observation.

Miles nodded. “Good purchase,” he said.

Felix made a noise that might have been approval.

Juno swallowed, then fished a folded sheet of paper from her coat pocket.

“I’ve got songs,” she said.

Felix groaned. “Christ.”

Juno ignored him.

She held the paper out to Lillian.

Lillian took it and adjusted her glasses.

“What have you got?” Lillian asked.

Juno’s cheeks heated. “Irish,” she admitted, bracing for dismissal.

Lillian’s mouth curved. “Of course you do,” she said, fond.

Juno pointed at the first title. “Follow Me Up to Carlow,” she said.

Lillian’s eyes scanned the lyrics.

Her brows lifted.

Then her mouth twitched.

Then she let out a laugh that was half cough.

“Oh,” Lillian said.

“What?” Juno demanded, immediately defensive.

Lillian read aloud, deadpan, “Now the English are all frightened,” then looked up at Juno over the top of the page. “Love,” she said, “this is a bit… direct.”

Juno’s ears flattened. “It’s history,” she insisted.

“Aye,” Lillian said, still smiling. “So’s the Highland Clearances. Doesn’t mean we open a central London gig by explicitly asking for the slaughter of the English.”

Felix barked a laugh. “That’d be a short set,” he said.

Asim covered his mouth with his hand, eyes bright.

Miles looked pained. “Please don’t start a riot in my kitchen,” he muttered.

Juno stared at Lillian, torn between embarrassment and indignation. “It’s a good song,” she protested.

“I’m not saying it’s not,” Lillian said, voice warm. “It’s belting. But it’s… politics with a knife in it.”

Juno’s jaw clenched.

Lillian’s smile softened into something conspiratorial. “Also,” she added, lowering her voice as if the walls might be listening, “I’m Scottish. I’m legally bound to hate the English, but we can’t tell them we want to kill them, ken?”

There was a beat.

Then Juno let out a startled laugh.

It broke something in her chest.

Asim cackled.

Felix shook his head. “You’re all mad,” he said, but his mouth twitched.

Juno rubbed a paw over her muzzle, mortified and amused at once. “Fine,” she said. “Not Carlow.”

“Not Carlow,” Lillian agreed, solemn.

Juno stabbed the paper with her finger. “What about The Rocky Road to Dublin?” she offered, quick, like a second chance.

Lillian’s eyes moved to the next set of lyrics.

Her brows lifted again.

This time with interest.

“Oh,” she said, softer.

Milla leaned in. “That one’s fast,” she observed.

“It is,” Juno said, a little proud now.

Lillian read the first verse, then looked up at Juno. “This,” she said, “is fascinating. It’s got rhythm in its teeth. And it’s not asking anyone to die.”

Juno’s mouth twitched. “That’s the minimum,” she muttered.

Lillian smiled. “It is,” she agreed. “But we can work with minimum.”

Miles tapped his pen against his notebook. “Tempo?” he asked, already thinking in counts.

Juno brightened, then caught herself, then let the brightness stay. “I can do it,” she said. “On flute. And maybe voice.”

Lillian nodded. “Aye,” she said. “And we’ll arrange it so you’re not fighting to be heard.”

Juno’s throat tightened.

Not fighting to be heard.

It sounded like music.

It sounded like a life.

Lillian folded the paper and handed it back. “Bring a few more,” she said. “Not the murder ones,” she added, eyes twinkling.

Juno rolled her eyes. “No promises,” she said.

“As long as the promises you break are not about murder,” Miles muttered, and Felix laughed again.

The kitchen settled back into planning.

Boxes.

Tempo.

Tea.

Outside, London carried on.

Inside, they built a set list that made room for Juno’s voice.


## 6.22.1
Felix cooked because it was easier than talking.

He’d found a way to make pasta for two feel like a decision rather than a negotiation: put the pot on, keep his hands busy, let the smell of garlic and oil do some of the work.

Milla sat at his little table with her shoulders held too straight, hands folded in her lap like she was waiting for an exam to begin. She’d taken her coat off, but not her scarf.

“You’re wearing that indoors,” Felix observed.

“It’s cold,” Milla replied.

Felix looked around. The radiator was on. His mum’s old blanket was over the back of the chair. “Sure,” he said.

He plated the pasta and put it down in front of her.

Milla stared at it.

“It’s not poison,” Felix said.

Milla’s mouth tightened. “I didn’t say it was.”

“You don’t have to,” Felix replied. “Your face does whole speeches.”

Milla picked up her fork and twirled a tiny amount of pasta like she could make it smaller by willpower.

Felix watched, the anger arriving in him like it always did—clean, protective, helpless.

“You’ve been doing this for weeks,” he said quietly.

Milla’s fork paused. “Doing what?”

“Shrinking,” Felix said.

Milla’s eyes flicked up, sharp as a blade. “Don’t.”

Felix exhaled through his nose. “I’m not trying to be a prick,” he said. “I’m trying to be your boyfriend. Which is apparently a job that involves pretending I can’t see basic reality.”

Milla’s jaw clenched. “You don’t understand what it feels like,” she said.

“Then tell me,” Felix said.

Silence.

The pasta cooled.

Milla’s voice, when it came, was precise. “It feels like failure,” she said. “It feels like my hands will betray me. Like my body will get heavier and heavier until I can’t do the only thing I’m any good at.”

Felix stared at her.

“You’re good at loads of things,” he said.

Milla’s laugh was short. “Name one.”

Felix swallowed.

“You’re good at staying upright,” he said. “You’re good at showing up when you’re in pain and still being kind. You’re good at making a room quieter just by existing in it. You’re good at caring about people even when you pretend you don’t.”

Milla looked away quickly.

Felix pushed on, gentler. “And you’re strong,” he said. “Not the gym poster kind. The kind that keeps living when your head is telling you you don’t deserve to.”

Milla’s throat bobbed.

“Stop it,” she whispered, and the words sounded like please.

Felix set his fork down. “I can’t,” he said. “Not anymore. Not if it means watching you disappear.”

Milla’s fingers tightened around her fork.

“You think I want this?” she snapped. “You think I like being… like this?”

Felix’s chest tightened. “No,” he said. “I think you’re terrified. And I think you’ve decided the only safe feeling is control.”

Milla’s eyes shone with sudden fury. “Control is the only thing that’s mine,” she said.

Felix nodded once. “Alright,” he said. “Then keep it. But let me in, yeah? Let me be on your side. Even if all I can do is sit here and be annoying.”

Milla’s breathing went shallow.

Felix waited.

Finally, she lifted her fork again.

She took a bite that was bigger than the first.

Her eyes closed for a fraction of a second as if bracing.

Nothing terrible happened.

Felix didn’t smile.

He just sat there and ate with her, making it normal.

After a minute, Milla’s voice came small, as if it cost her. “I love you,” she said.

Felix froze.

Milla stared at her plate as if the words were a stain. “I do,” she said, firmer. “Even when you’re… brutal.”

Felix’s throat went tight. “Yeah,” he managed. “I love you too.”

Milla’s shoulders dropped by a fraction.

She took another bite.
 
## Chapter 23: Grief 

### 6.23.1
The registry office smelled like carpet glue and old paper.

The waiting room chairs were the same kind of soft‑plastic misery you found in GP surgeries: designed to wipe clean, designed to discourage lingering. A fake plant drooped in the corner, doing its best.

Miles sat with his knee bouncing, tie too tight, ears too alert to every sound.

Lillian sat beside him in a dark dress and a cardigan with no holes, hands folded around a small bouquet of supermarket flowers because neither of them had wanted anything that looked like a performance.

It still felt like a performance.

Across from them, Felix slouched with his arms folded, suit jacket unbuttoned, expression fixed in a look that said I am only here because I love you and I hate that about myself.

Milla sat with perfect posture, hands resting neatly on her lap. She’d worn a simple dress and a thin necklace. Her eyes looked too bright.

“You sure you don’t want, like, a priest or something?” Felix muttered.

Lillian raised an eyebrow. “Are you offering?”

Felix snorted. “I’ll burst into flames.”

Miles’s mouth twitched.

The door opened.

“Mr Ferguson? Ms Edgecombe?” a woman called.

Miles stood too fast, chair scraping.

Lillian’s paw slid into his, grounding.

They followed the registrar down a corridor that tried very hard to look festive with framed prints of flowers and a poster about “Celebrating Love.”

The room they were led into was small. Two chairs. A table. A flag in the corner. A window showing a sliver of grey sky.

The registrar smiled with the careful warmth of someone who had seen every kind of marriage: joyful, strategic, doomed, defiant.

“This is a civil ceremony,” she said. “It’s brief. It’s legal. You can have a larger celebration later if you wish.”

Miles squeezed Lillian’s fingers.

Brief.
Legal.

They’d built their entire lives on those two words without saying them out loud.

“Do you take—” the registrar began.

Miles stared at Lillian.

Not at the version of her the world saw—fat badger, loud voice, political nuisance.

At the version he knew: tea and jokes, a hand on his shoulder when he was tired, a song hummed under her breath while she washed dishes.

“I do,” he said.

Lillian’s eyes glistened. “Aye,” she said. “I do.”

The registrar nodded, satisfied.

Felix and Milla stood as witnesses.

Felix’s hand found Milla’s without thinking.

Milla squeezed once, hard.

When the papers were signed, the pen scratching loud in the small room, the registrar smiled. “Congratulations,” she said. “You are married.”

Lillian stared at the certificate as if it might bite.

Miles let out a breath that sounded like laughter and grief tangled together.

Felix cleared his throat. “So… do we clap or is that cringe?” he asked.

Lillian laughed, wet. “Clap, you idiot,” she said.

They did.

## 6.23.2

The benefit gig started in the street.

Not with music.

With shouting.

The venue was a small hall over a pub, the kind that hosted wedding receptions and darts tournaments and community fundraisers with raffles. A hand‑painted sign by the door read: STRAYS BENEFIT. FOOD + BLANKETS + LEGAL FUND.

Outside, a knot of protesters had formed, faces half hidden by hoods and scarves.

SAVE OUR STREETS.

NO TO FERAL ANTHROS.

GO HOME.

The words were familiar in their ugliness.

Miles felt Lillian stiffen beside him.

Asim, of course, was already talking.

“Evening,” he called, voice bright. “Thanks so much for coming. Love community engagement. Do you want a leaflet about austerity or are you more of a shouting‑in‑the‑rain type?”

“Pervert,” someone spat.

“That’s not the slur you think it is,” Asim replied cheerfully.

Felix muttered, “He’s going to get punched one day.”

“Aye,” Lillian said. “And he’ll deserve it, and we’ll still bail him out.”

Inside, the hall smelled of spilled beer and cheap disinfectant. Folding chairs filled quickly. Donation buckets clinked. Kerry from the youth centre stood by the door with a clipboard, her face set in the calm, determined expression of someone who would fight a bear for a child.

When the band went on, the noise softened into attention.

They played tight, like every note mattered.

Because it did.

Halfway through the set, Lillian stepped back from the mic.

“We’ve got someone joining us for one song,” she said, voice steady.

Juno walked onto the stage.

She wore black trousers and a simple top, hair pulled back. Her face was pale, but her chin was lifted.

The room shifted.

Lillian watched her, the fierce protective warmth in her chest cutting through everything else.

Juno raised the flute.

“Rocky Road to Dublin,” Asim announced, grinning. “Because sometimes the only appropriate response to the state is a good Irish tune and a raised middle finger.”

The first notes rang out, bright and clean.

Juno played like she was running.

Not away.

Through.

The tune drove the room forward—fast, sharp, joyful in a way that was also defiant.

For the first time in a long time, Miles saw Juno’s face change mid‑performance.

Not freezing.

Not shutting down.

Opening.

When the song ended, the crowd erupted.

Someone whooped.

Someone shouted her name.

Juno’s eyes went wide.

For a beat, Miles thought she might bolt.

Then she bowed—small, awkward, real—and stepped back.

Lillian squeezed her shoulder as she passed.

Juno didn’t flinch.

## 6.23.3

The bootie was waiting for her.

Lillian found it by accident, which was how the universe liked to do its cruellest work.

They’d come home after the benefit gig buzzing and wrung out, clothes smelling of rain and beer and adrenaline. Miles had made tea automatically. Asim had sent seventeen texts in a row about the donation totals and how he wanted to fight everyone outside personally.

Lillian had laughed.

She’d even kissed Miles in the kitchen like she meant it.

Then she went to put away laundry and opened the wardrobe.

The paper bag slid out from behind her jumper and landed on the floor with a soft, stupid rustle.

Lillian froze.

She stared at it as if it were a grenade.

Her paw moved without permission.

She picked it up.

Inside, the yellow bootie sat folded like a secret.

She remembered the charity shop. The bell above the door. The woman saying it’s just the left one.

She remembered how the possibility had felt in her chest—bright and terrifying.

Now the bootie felt like evidence.

Miles’s voice came from the kitchen. “You alright, love?”

Lillian couldn’t answer.

Her throat closed.

She slid down the wardrobe door to the floor, bootie clutched in her hands.

The grief that had been kept in boxes—tidy, stacked, labelled—spilled out anyway.

It came hot and animal.

She pressed the bootie to her face.

The fabric smelled of nothing but paper and old wool.

She sobbed.

Not pretty crying.

Not dignified.

The kind that made her shoulders shake until her muscles hurt.

Miles’s footsteps came closer.

The bedroom doorframe filled with him.

He took in the scene—Lillian on the floor, the little bag, her hands locked tight.

“Lils?” he said, voice soft.

She couldn’t look at him.

She shook her head, because words still wouldn’t come.

He crossed the room and sat on the floor beside her without touching yet.

“Alright,” he murmured. “Alright. I’m here.”

Lillian let out a broken sound.

Miles’s arm came around her slowly, giving her the chance to refuse.

She didn’t.

She turned into his chest like she’d been holding herself upright by spite alone and had finally run out.

He held her.

He didn’t ask what it was.

He didn’t try to fix it.

He just stayed while she grieved the future she’d bought for two pounds and then lost without ever naming it.

 
## Chapter 24: Firebreak

### 6.24.1
Wind whipped down the alley leading to the warehouse, driving grit into Sarah’s eyes as she wrestled the side door shut against a gust. The tarps on the roof flapped and snapped like angry flags. Inside, the air felt damp and metallic, full of static. “Stay away from the windows,” she told the kids. “You’ll catch worse than a cold.”
They mostly obeyed, huddling in blankets, playing cards, bickering in low voices. The younger ones clung closer than usual, sensing her tension. She’d barely slept. The promise she’d made herself the night before—that if someone else got badly hurt, she’d stop pretending she could fix this—had sat on her chest like a weight. She didn’t know, waking up, whether some part of her had made a deal with the universe. She hoped not. Bargains with fate never ended well.
Faroukh hovered near the crates, kicking the metal leg of a shelving unit with the side of his trainer. “We’re low on noodles,” he said. “And the bread’s mouldy.”
“Cut the mould off,” she said. “Toast it. You won’t taste it as much.”
He pulled a face. “Disgusting.”
“You’ll live,” she said. “We’re not due a delivery till tomorrow.”
He scowled at the crates, then at the door. “They’ll be back,” he muttered.
She didn’t pretend not to know who he meant. “We’ll deal with it,” she said, though her stomach twisted. As if summoned, the side door rattled. The bolt juddered under the impact of a fist.
“Open up, Princess,” Connor’s voice called, muffled by wind and metal. “We’re cold out here.”
Sarah’s heart thudded. The kids looked up, all conversations snapping off. “Go upstairs,” she told the littles. “Now. Faroukh, you too.”
“I’m no kid,” he protested.
“You are when there’s Dobermans at the door,” she said. “Move.”
Reluctantly, he herded the smaller ones towards the stairs. Lila clung to his shirt; he didn’t shake her off. Sarah checked the bolt one more time, then opened the small hatch carved into the door—an old ventilation panel someone had repurposed. Connor’s snout appeared in the gap, grinning.
“Hey, neighbour,” he said. Rain matted the fur on his head; his jacket was soaked. The boys behind him huddled in their hoodies, teeth chattering.
“What do you want?” Sarah asked.
“Same as always,” he said. “Bit of warmth. Bit of food. Bit of respect.”
She swallowed.
“You broke the last deal,” she said. “You smashed the crates. You scared the kids.”
He shrugged, rainwater running down his neck. “We were sending a message,” he said. “You didn’t die. Consider yourself lucky.” He leaned closer. “Let us in,” he said, voice dropping. “We’ll take what we need. No drama. Or you can play hard‑arse again and see how long you keep your little nursery.”
Her paws shook on the edge of the hatch. She tightened her grip. “You’re no coming in,” she said. “Not today.”
He smirked. “Then we’ll wait you out,” he said. “Storm like this, you’ll have to open up eventually. Roof’s leaking, yeah? Kids’ll get cold. Hungry. We’ll still be here.”
He knocked once more, a lazy thud, then stepped back. The shapes of his crew blurred in the rain.
Sarah shut the hatch. Bolted it. Rested her forehead briefly against the metal, breathing hard. She could feel the eyes on her when she turned. “They’re not coming in,” she said, loud enough for the hall. “We’ve got blankets. We’ve got food. We’ll ride it out.”
The kids’ expressions ranged from frightened to sceptical. They’d been here for enough storms to know promises didn’t mean much against physics.
The afternoon dragged. The leak got worse. Buckets had to be emptied more often. The concrete floor grew slick in patches. The cold seeped into bones and fur. By early evening, the noodles were gone. The bread was mostly green. The last tin of beans sat on the shelf like a joke. Sarah rationed it out: a spoonful here, a crust there. She skipped her own turn without comment; Faroukh noticed, but said nothing.
The door rattled again.
“Getting late,” Connor called. “Kids must be starving.”
“Don’t answer,” Sarah told Faroukh when he looked at her. “He’ll get bored.”
She wasn’t sure she believed it.
By the time the sky outside had gone from grey to black, the kids’ faces had taken on that pinched look she recognised too well. Blood sugar dropping. Energy ebbing. Fear turning into listlessness. She tried to distract them—told stories, started a game of cards, got Lila to draw—but everything felt thin. The line she’d drawn in her head the night before pulsed.
If someone gets hurt, we’re done.
She didn’t get to choose how that happened. It was one of the littlest ones who broke first. The rabbit boy, the one who’d come with his rucksack and no name. He had been quiet all day, eyes tracking the older kids, flinching at loud noises. Now, as the door rattled again, louder this time, he snapped. He grabbed his rucksack and bolted down the stairs, ears flat, feet scrambling.
“Wait—” Sarah shouted, but he was already at the door, little paws fumbling with the bolt.
He wasn’t thinking of Connor, she realised with a jolt. He was thinking of getting out, away from the noise, away from the tension. Kids ran towards threats sometimes if the room behind them felt worse.
She was halfway across the floor when the bolt slid back.
The door flew inward on a gust of wind. The rabbit boy stumbled backwards with it, knocked off his feet. Connor and his crew flooded in on a wave of cold air and rain, like they owned the place.
“Evening,” Connor said cheerfully. “Told you we’d be back.”
“Get away from him,” Sarah snapped, putting herself between Connor and the boy.
Connor raised his hands. “Relax,” he said. “We’re just here for our tithe.”
Spines and the others fanned out, eyes already scanning the room for food.
“You touch the kids, I break your nose,” Sarah said, voice shaking with fury.
Connor cocked his head. “Look at you,” he said. “Little general. Still playing mum.” He flicked his gaze towards the crates. “Food first,” he told his crew. “Leave the toddlers. We’re not animals.”
They laughed. The rabbit boy whimpered, clutching his rucksack, eyes huge.
“Upstairs,” Sarah told the kids, not taking her eyes off Connor. “Now.”
Some obeyed. Some froze. Faroukh stood his ground at the bottom of the stairs, fists clenched. “Move them,” Sarah hissed.
He swallowed and started hauling littler ones up by their sleeves. Connor watched, amused. “You’re good at this,” he said. “You’d be useful on our side. You, me, we could run this whole estate.”
“I’m already running something,” she said. “It’s called ‘basic decency.’ You should try it.”
He chuckled. “Decency doesn’t keep you warm,” he said. “Food does. And look—” he gestured at the crates “—your decency’s left you low.”
Spines had already prised open the last tin of beans, eating them with his fingers. The rat stuffed packets of biscuits into his hoodie. 
Sarah’s claws dug into her palms. “Take what you came for and go,” she said through gritted teeth. “You’ve made your point.”
“I don’t think I have,” Connor said. “See, you keep trying to pretend this is your kingdom. But you’ve got no walls. No guards. Just kids and leaks and the occasional charity van.” He stepped closer. The rabbit boy cowered behind Sarah’s leg. “Thing is,” Connor said softly, “if anything happens to them while you’re here, that’s on you.” He let the implication sit. Sarah’s heart hammered.
“Last warning,” she said. “Get. Out.”
He didn’t move. “Make me,” he said.
For a second she saw herself launching at him, claws out, teeth bared. The two of them rolling on the wet concrete, his strength against her speed. She also saw the kids behind her, wide‑eyed, pressed against the railings. Even if she landed a hit, even if she knocked him down, the others would pile in. She’d be outnumbered. She’d lose. And then what?
Connor must have read the calculations on her face, because he smiled. “Didn’t think so,” he said. He turned his back on her and walked towards the crates, as if she were already irrelevant.
That was when the rabbit boy moved.
Maybe it was panic. Maybe he was trying to get to the door, to bolt past them all and run. Whatever the reason, he darted sideways. His foot hit a slick patch of water from the leak. His legs went out from under him. He fell, hard, the back of his head cracking against the corner of a crate with a sickening thunk.
Everything stopped.
For a heartbeat, there was only the sound of the rain.
Then blood.
It soaked into his white fur, bright red against grey concrete. His eyes rolled back. He made a small, broken sound and went limp.
“Shit,” someone breathed.
Sarah was on her knees beside him before she realised she’d moved.
“Hey,” she said, voice too high. “Hey, hey, stay with me.”
His chest rose and fell in ragged little jerks. She pressed a paw to the wound, fingers slippery.
“Get me towels,” she snapped. “Now.”
Faroukh scrambled, hands shaking, bringing the first thing he could grab. She pressed the towel to the boy’s head, feeling the warm, awful bloom of blood against her pads. “Ambulance,” she said. “We need an ambulance.” The word hung in the air like a threat.
“We can’t call an ambulance,” one of the older girls whispered. “They’ll ask questions. They’ll call social services.”
“He might die,” Sarah spat. “I don’t give a shite what questions they ask.”
Connor shifted his weight, eyes darting.
“This is why we don’t like kids running about,” he muttered. “Clumsy little—”
Sarah’s head snapped up. “You,” she said, pointing at him with a blood‑slick paw. “Out.”
“We should go,” Spines muttered. “This is bad heat.”
Connor hesitated, then jerked his chin.
“Fine,” he said. “We’re done here anyway.”
As they slunk towards the door, he threw a last look over his shoulder. “Told you this place was dangerous,” he said. “You can’t protect them, Princess.”
She wanted to launch herself at him, tear his throat out. She didn’t move, because the boy under her paws made a small, terrible noise. She heard the door slam. The warehouse felt suddenly huge and empty. “Phone,” she said. “Who’s got a phone with credit?”
Silence. Then Faroukh, reluctantly, held his out. “Mine,” he said. “I… someone helps me topping it up.”
“What’s the number for ambulance again?”
He stared at her.
“Nine‑nine‑nine,” Lila whispered. “Everyone knows that.”
Sarah punched the numbers in with her free paw. Her fingers left smears of red on the screen. The operator’s voice was calm, almost bored. “Emergency. Which service do you require?”
“Ambulance,” Sarah said. “We’ve got a kid with a head injury. Anthro. He’s bleeding. He fell. He’s not awake properly.”
The questions came in a steady stream. Where are you? How old is he? Is he breathing? Is he responsive? She answered as best she could, voice shaking, trying to sound like an adult. Not a kid in a warehouse full of other kids with no responsible grown‑ups.
“Is there an adult with you?” the operator asked at one point.
“I’m sixteen,” Sarah said. “I’m in charge.”
There was a pause. “Okay,” the operator said, adjusting tone. “Ambulance is on its way. Keep pressure on the wound. Talk to him if you can.”
She kept thinking he might stop breathing between one breath and the next. Each rise and fall of his chest felt like borrowed time. Sirens wailed in the distance. Got louder. Cut off.
Flashing blue light spilled in through the high windows as the ambulance doors opened outside.
The medics came in with big bags and bigger eyes, taking in the scene: damp concrete, kids huddled on the stairs, one rabbit boy on the floor bleeding through a towel held by a too‑young golden retriever. “What happened?” one asked, dropping to his knees.
“He slipped,” Sarah said. “Hit his head. The floor’s wet. We tried to—”
“You did good,” he said, already checking the wound, barking orders at his colleague. “We’ll take it from here.” They moved with brisk efficiency: oxygen mask, neck brace, questions about allergies no one could answer. They asked for his name. No one knew that either.
“We call him Bun,” Lila whispered. “Like… like bunny.”
The medic’s mouth tightened. They loaded him onto a stretcher. Sarah moved to follow on instinct.
“Sorry, love,” the second medic said gently, stepping in front of her. “You can’t come with us unless you’re his next of kin.”
“He has nobody,” Sarah snapped. “That’s the problem.”
He hesitated. “Name and date of birth?” he asked. “For our records?”
She opened her mouth, closed it. Shame burned hot under her fur. “We don’t know,” she said. “He just… turned up. Has been left here.”
The medic’s eyes flicked around the hall, taking in the lack of adults, the makeshift beds, the patched‑up roof. “Social services will need to be involved,” he said carefully. “There’ll be questions.”
“Aye,” she said. “There usually are.”
He looked like he wanted to ask more. He didn’t. They didn’t have time. “We’ll do our best,” he said. “You did the right thing calling.” The doors banged behind them. The siren faded into the night.
The warehouse felt even bigger now. The kids stared at her, eyes huge.
“Is he gonna die?” Lila whispered.
Sarah swallowed. The honesty she’d prided herself on tasted like sawdust. “I don’t know,” she confessed. “But he’s got a better chance now than if we’d left him here.”
She realised she was still gripping the bloody towel. Her paws shook as she dropped it into a bucket. Her hoodie stuck to her arms; she was suddenly aware of how cold she was.
The line she’d drawn in her head glowed bright.
Someone has been seriously hurt.
This place had crossed from “barely viable” into “actively lethal.” She could patch leaks. She could ration food. She couldn’t control gravity. She couldn’t control Connor. She couldn’t keep waiting for luck. She looked up at the mezzanine, at the kids huddled together. “We can’t stay here,” she said, voice rough. “Not like this.”
Faroukh’s eyes widened. “Where else we gonna go?”
Sarah thought of the van. The flat. The way Lillian had looked at her drawings. The way Miles had spoken about adoption like a plan, not a fantasy. “I don’t know yet,” she said. “But we’re not doing another winter in this death trap.” Her paws were still shaking. She wiped them on her jeans, leaving smeared pawprints. “Tomorrow,” she said, mostly to herself. “I’m calling them.”
Them. The only adults she trusted, even a little.
Miles and Lillian.
She didn’t know what she’d say. Didn’t know what they could do. All she knew was that the line in her head had stopped being hypothetical and turned into a crack in the floor.
If she stayed on this side, pretending she could hold everything together, more kids would bleed.
She was done.

### 6.24.2
He was in the stockroom, half‑inside a pallet cage of tinned tomatoes, when his phone buzzed against his hip. He finished counting the row, marked the tally on his clipboard, and only then fished the handset out of his pocket.
Faroukh. The name still looked wrong on a screen. In his head he was usually “the boy from the warehouse”, or “Sarah’s friend”, or just a knot of worry that lived somewhere behind his ribs. He frowned. Faroukh never called: it was Sarah using his phone. She didn’t ring unless he was on the way already, unless they’d arranged a drop.
By the time he got to the staff corridor and enough quiet to listen, the call had already gone to voicemail. One new message. The little icon blinked at him, patient. He pressed play.
“Um. It’s me.” Her voice was rough around the edges, stretched thin. “We had… a thing. At the warehouse. One of the littles slipped. Hit his head. There was blood everywhere. I called an ambulance. They took him. Social services are gonna come sniffing. Connor’s lot were here. They’re saying it’s my fault. I—” The recording juddered, as if she’d moved the phone away for a second. “I can’t do this, Miles,” she said, quieter now, her voice shaking. “I thought I could, but I can’t. This place is eating us. I need… I can’t. A plan. Something. Call me back. Please.”
The line clicked dead.
For a moment all he could hear was the hum of the strip lights and the distant whine of a floor buffer out on the shop floor. His reflection in the fire door glass looked the same as it always did: big shoulders, dark fur, hi‑vis vest, barcode scanner in one hand. A man who could solve anything with enough spreadsheets.
Head injury. Ambulance. Social services.
He’d known, of course, that it would come to this. You didn’t stack children in a condemned warehouse and expect physics or bureaucracy to look the other way forever. He’d known and he’d kept bringing crates anyway, because not bringing them felt worse.
Now the line he hadn’t wanted to draw had been drawn for him.
He hit call.
She picked up on the second ring.
“Yeah?”
“It’s me,” he said. “Are you safe right now?”
There was a shuffling sound, the echo of a big room.
“Depends what you mean by safe,” she said. “Roof’s still leaking. Connor’s lot pissed off when the sirens came. Kids are… quiet.” Quiet was worse than screaming, in his experience.
“Any adults there apart from you?” he asked, already knowing the answer.
“No,” she said. “Just me and Faroukh and the big ones. And the big ones are barely hanging on themselves.” 
He pressed his thumb into the soft rubber of the phone case until it squeaked. “Okay,” he said. “Listen. I’m at work. I can’t walk out right this second, but my shift finishes at four. I’ll be there by half‑past. Can you meet me outside? Just you, or you and Faroukh if you trust him.”
“Why outside?” Suspicion flickered up at once, reflexive.
“Because if social services or the council turn up while we’re talking, I want them to see you as a kid who called for help, not as the manager of an illegal hostel,” he said. “And because I need you to be able to walk away if you need to.”
There was a pause. When she spoke again, her voice was smaller. “I don’t want to walk away,” she said. “That’s the problem.”
“I know,” he said. “We’ll talk. Half four. Yeah?”
Another long beat.
“Yeah,” she said. “Half four.”
He hung up and put his forehead briefly against the cool metal of the fire door. The smell of cardboard and pallet wrap pressed in on him. He could keep pretending this was something he could manage in his spare time—a charity case, a side project. Or he could admit what the warehouse had become: a fire that was going to jump the line if he didn’t cut it off.
He’d learned about firebreaks in a training video years ago, back when the chain had flirted with selling garden furniture and barbecue kits. A bloke in a hi‑vis had stood in a field talking about controlled burns, about deliberately clearing strips of land so a wildfire had nothing to eat when it reached them.
Right now, the warehouse was the scrubland. Connor’s lot, social services, the press—they were all different tongues of the same flame.
He needed a firebreak.
“Miles?” His deputy manager, a human woman with a buzzcut and a clipboard, poked her head round the door. “You all right? Frozen peas delivery’s early. Need you on the dock.”
He straightened up.
“On my way,” he said.
He moved through the rest of the shift in a kind of double exposure. On one layer he was the efficient regional guy: checking temperatures, signing invoices, answering emails about promotional endcaps. On the other, he was mapping the warehouse in his head, overlaying hazard symbols: leaks here, dodgy electrics there, stair rails loose, exit routes blocked by junk.
He texted as he walked, thumbs moving fast.
To Asim:  
You free tonight? Need to talk Strays, urgent. Not just food.

To Juno:  
Kid from warehouse hurt, ambulance called. Don’t panic, they’re in hospital. Might need extra blankets + flasks later. Ask your friend if the church hall is ever empty weekday evenings?

To Lillian:  
Running a bit late. Warehouse stuff. Will explain later. Love you.

He deleted the last words twice before leaving them in. They sat there on the tiny screen, earnest and inadequate.
He didn’t tell her about the head injury. Not yet. She’d sounded so tired the last few weeks, voice hoarse even on good days, shoulders pinched. He kept telling himself he’d sit her down, ask properly how she was. He kept not doing it, because every time he looked at her she was in the middle of carrying something—lesson plans, set lists, someone else’s worries. He didn’t want to be another weight.
He finished his shift exactly on time, signed himself out, and jogged to the staff car park. The van’s engine coughed to life with a wheezy growl. He drove with the radio off, ears full of his own heartbeat and the imagined drip of water from the warehouse roof.
Sarah was waiting by the corner when he turned into the alley, hood up despite the break in the rain. She looked smaller than he remembered, standing away from the door as if the bricks themselves might bite.
He parked at the end of the alley and got out, hands visible, posture loose. They’d learned each other’s edges over months; he knew better than to come in hot.
“Hey,” he said.
“Hey,” she said. Her eyes were ringed with grey, fur clumped where she’d clearly scrubbed blood off in a hurry. The smell of disinfectant and damp concrete clung to her. Her tail was held low.
“Can we walk?” he asked. “Just up to the corner and back.”
She hesitated, glancing at the door, then nodded and fell into step beside him.
For a while they just walked, the estate’s familiar mix of chip‑shop grease, exhaust fumes and wet dog filling the air. “Tell me everything,” he said at last. “Not the polished version. The truth.”
She laughed once, bitter. “There is no polished version,” she said. “Kid slipped. Hit his head. Blood everywhere. I called nine‑nine‑nine. Medics came. Took him away. Social’ll get told there’s a group of kids living in a death trap with no adults in sight. Connor’s lot were there before that, taking food, scaring the littles. Roof’s getting worse. We’re out of decent blankets. Faroukh’s talking about moving away to someone he knows to get away from it all, and… I don’t think it might be a friendly place.” She swallowed. “That enough truth for you?”
He listened, counting problems the way he counted tins. One, two, three—structural, social, legal. “How often do Connor’s lot come?” he asked.
“Couple of times a week now,” she said. “Sometimes just for food, sometimes to remind us they can.”
“Any adults you trust within two streets?” he asked. “Anyone with a front room they’d let some of the littles kip in, even short‑term?”
She shook her head immediately, then faltered. “Maybe Mrs Kaur,” she said. “She used to give us leftovers when she worked the late shift at the takeaway. But she’s got her own grandkids. And folk talk when there’s too many anthro kids in a flat. You know what the neighbours are like.”
He did. He’d had complaints about “loitering youths” in every branch he’d ever managed. The definition of loitering rarely included human teenagers in rugby hoodies. “Okay,” he said. “And social services. What’s your worst case scenario if they turn up?”
She stopped walking. Her paws balled into fists. “They take the littles,” she said. “Split them up. Shove them into homes where folk don’t understand them. The big kids get left to rot here till they age out and Connor’s lot swallow them. Nobody sees the whole picture. Nobody cares that we’re… us. Together.” Her voice cracked on the last word.
He nodded slowly. “And your best case?” he asked.
She gave him a look like he’d asked her to recite a fairy tale. “Best case?” she said. “They magically find a big warm house and let me bring everyone, and nobody gets called a beast or a lost cause, and nobody gets filmed for a sad wee documentary to make humans feel better about themsel—” Her voice broke entirely. She stared at the pavement. “It’s daft,” she muttered. “There is no best case.”
He let the silence sit between them for a few breaths. “Right,” he said at last. “So. There is no good outcome where you keep running this place on your own until you drop. That’s what I’m hearing.” She flinched, then nodded once. “What if,” he said carefully, “we treat this like a firebreak?”
She blinked. “A what?”
“You know when there’s a wildfire,” he said, “and folk burn strips of land on purpose so there’s nothing left for the flames to eat? It looks brutal in the moment, but it stops the whole forest going up. This place—” he nodded towards the warehouse “—it’s already burning. Maybe the only way through is to clear a strip. Move the littles somewhere safer, even if it’s not perfect. Cut Connor’s access. Give you a way out that isn’t abandoning them.”
Her ears flattened. “Where?” she demanded. “You got a secret spare house lying around?”
“No,” he said. “But I’ve got a van, a community that trusts us, and more leverage with the council than I used to. I’ve got Asim, who knows half the activists in London. I’ve got a church hall that’s empty some nights and a youth worker who owes me a favour. And I’ve got a social worker who’s already half convinced we’d make decent parents.”
She looked at him sharply. “You’re talking about adoption again,” she said.
He lifted a paw. “I’m talking about options,” he said. “For the littles, that might be emergency placements with people we can keep an eye on. For you… that might be us. But I’m not asking you to decide that tonight. Tonight I’m asking if you’ll let me step in properly. Not just as the bloke who brings noodles, but as an adult who will put his name on forms and talk to people who make decisions.” 
The word adult made her flinch as if he’d slapped her. “You think I’m no an adult?” she snapped.
“I think you’ve been doing an adult’s job on a teenager’s body for too long,” he said, voice gentle but firm. “And I think that if something happens to you, there’s no backup. That scares the hell out of me.”
They walked in silence to the end of the street and back. A bus rumbled past; the driver didn’t look at them.
“If you talk to social,” she said eventually, “you’ll tell them what, exactly?”
“That there’s a group of kids who have kept each other alive in impossible conditions,” he said. “That they called for help when it really mattered. That they need safe accommodation yesterday. That some of us are willing to step up as kinship carers instead of letting them vanish into the system.”
Her mouth twisted. “They’ll say we’re trouble,” she said. “They always do.”
“Then we’ll give them better stories to believe,” he said. “Evidence. Witnesses. Lesson plans. Gig schedules. Whatever it takes to make them see you as a person, not a statistic.”
She huffed out something that might have been a laugh if it hadn’t sounded so close to a sob.
“You make it sound so tidy,” she said. “Like stacking crisps.”
“It won’t be tidy,” he said. “It’ll be messy and slow and full of folk who don’t call back. But we can’t keep doing… this.” He gestured vaguely towards the warehouse. “The fire’s coming whether we plan for it or not.”
They stopped a few yards from the side door. From inside came the faint, muffled sounds of kids trying to be quiet: a cough, a clatter, a shushed argument over a blanket. “What do you need from me?” she asked.
“I need you to keep the kids as safe as you can tonight,” he said. “No deals with Connor. No letting anyone in. If social or police turn up, you call me before you open that door. Tomorrow, I’ll start making calls. Asim, the youth centre, the social worker. We’ll work out how to move the littles in stages. And I need you to start imagining a world where you’re not the one holding the roof up.”
Her jaw clenched. “I don’t know how to do anything else,” she whispered.
He swallowed. “Then we’ll learn together,” he said.
She studied his face for a long time, eyes searching for the catch. She must have seen something—steady, stubborn—because her shoulders dropped a fraction. “Okay,” she said. “You want to play fireman, big dog, you do it. But if they try to split my lot up without me there, I’ll haunt you.”
“Deal,” he said. “You can haunt me from our sofa, with a mug of tea.” He watched her slip back through the door, bolt sliding into place. The warehouse loomed against the grey sky, every missing tile and cracked window now a problem to be solved, not just endured.
In his head, he began a new list.

Firebreak Plan:
1. Call Asim.  
2. Call youth centre.  
3. Call social worker, frame warehouse as emergency, not scandal.  
4. Talk to Lillian. Really talk.  
5. Get the kids out before the next storm hits.

He climbed into the van, started the engine, and pulled away from the kerb. The dashboard clock ticked over to 17:01.

 
## Chapter 25: Evacuation and Collapse

### 6.25.1
Lillian stood at the front of the practice room, one paw on the edge of the upright piano, listening to the corridor outside fill with the usual cacophony: lockers slamming, chairs scraping, teenagers shouting good‑natured abuse at each other. The smell of dust and resin and adolescent deodorant hung in the air.
She could usually tell how her day had gone by how heavy her glasses felt on her nose. Today they might as well have been bricks.
“You all right, Miss?” Juno hovered in the doorway, clutching her clarinet case. Her black‑and‑white fur was fluffed slightly, whiskers twitching in concern.
“Aye, hen,” Lillian said automatically. “Come on in. Let’s make some noise.”
The thirty Year Tens filed in, a flurry of blazers and tails and half‑hidden piercings. A badger boy in the back started tapping a rhythm on the radiator; a human girl shushed him with theatrical patience. “Instruments out,” Lillian called, clapping her paws once. The sound rang sharper than she’d intended. Her own ears flinched. “Warm‑ups from bar seventeen, you ken the drill.”
She turned to the whiteboard and picked up a pen. The words she’d written at lunch—“Dynamics: Telling Stories With Volume”—swam in front of her eyes for a second before snapping back into focus. She had slept badly. She’d been sleeping badly for months, if she was honest, but last night had been a particular mess of half‑dreams and cold sweats. Every time she closed her eyes she saw blood on tile, towels piled high, the little flicker of an ambulance siren reflected in a warehouse window.
She hadn’t told Miles about the call from him the night before, the way his text had landed with a thump in her chest—warehouse stuff, will explain later. She hadn’t told him that the word warehouse now lived in the same part of her brain as the word bathroom, both of them tagged with the bright red sticker of things we don’t talk about.
“Miss?” Juno again, closer now. “You’re holding the pen upside down.”
Lillian looked down. Inkless end against the board.
“Right,” she said, flipping it round. “That would help.” The class tittered. She smiled, a thin strip of expression she could put on without thinking. “Okay,” she said. “Eyes up. Today we’re talking about how we use loud and soft to tell folk how we feel, even when the words are saying something different. So. Bar seventeen. Juno, can you give us the first line?”
Juno lifted her clarinet, adjusted the reed with practised care, and played. The melody of the piece they’d been working on—a rearranged folk tune Lillian had dug out of an old book, all rising fifths and unexpected minor turns—filled the room. Lillian listened, trying to settle into the familiar groove of teaching. Usually it worked. Usually the muscle memory of call‑and‑response, of praise and correction, was enough to carry her even when her insides felt thin.
That day, every note seemed to land on bare nerve.
“Good,” she said when Juno finished. “Can we try that again, but this time imagine you’re playing it for somebody who’s just had…”
She stopped. The words had lined themselves up—just had news they weren’t expecting, just had a loss they can’t speak about—but her throat refused to shape them.
“Just had what?” one of the boys asked, half teasing, half genuinely curious.
Her vision blurred for a second. The room swam again, edges softening. “Just… had a long day,” she finished lamely. “Tired. All right? Make it sound like that.”
They played. She clapped the beat, counted them in, corrected the cellos on their timing. She could feel herself from the outside, watching the way her shoulders hunched, the way her tail tucked tight against the backs of her legs.
Halfway through the second run‑through, one of the trumpets split a note. The harsh crack of sound went through her like an electric shock.
The room tilted.
For a moment she was back in the bathroom, tile cold under her knees, the smell of bleach and iron and the washing machine going like a train. She could hear her own breath, ragged and too loud, the way it had been when she’d clung to the sink and decided, in that horrible, clear way, that she would never tell Miles what had happened in this room.
The present snapped back in a rush. Thirty teenagers looking at her. Clarinet reeds drying. A radiator hissing.
“Miss?” Juno again, softer now. “You’ve gone a bit… pale.”
Her paws were gripping the edge of the piano so hard her claws creaked.
“I’m fine,” she said, though the word felt like gravel in her mouth. “Just—just carry on. From bar twenty‑three. I’m going to sit for a wee moment, all right?”
She made it as far as the piano stool before her legs stopped belonging to her. The room narrowed to a tunnel: Juno’s worried face, the blur of the whiteboard, a little patch of peeling laminate on the floor where a chair had gouged it years ago. Her heart was doing that thing again, the hummingbird in a shoebox. Sweat prickled under her fur despite the chill.
“Okay,” she said, but there was no air behind it. The edges of her vision fizzed black.
Then the world went.
When she came back, she was on the floor.
The acoustic tiles on the ceiling stared down at her. Someone had put a folded blazer under her head. There were faces leaning over her—Juno’s, white‑faced and wide‑eyed; a human boy from the brass section; Ms Chatterjee from next door, her sari a flash of colour amidst the grey.
“Lillian? Can you hear me?” Ms Chatterjee’s voice cut through the murmur. “Blink if you can hear me.”
Lillian blinked. Her glasses were gone; the room was fuzzed at the edges.
“What happened?” she croaked.
“You fainted,” Ms Chatterjee said. “One second you were at the piano, the next you were on the ground. Scared the life out of us.”
“She went all wobbly,” someone offered from the back. “And then Juno caught her. Sort of.”
“I didn’t catch her,” Juno said indignantly. “I just stopped her hitting her head on the stool.”
Lillian tried to sit up. A wave of nausea rolled through her. She lay back down again, breathing carefully. “Right,” she muttered. “That’s embarrassing.”
“It’s not embarrassing,” Ms Chatterjee said firmly. “It’s your body telling you it’s had enough.”
The words landed with a quiet, awful accuracy.
“Kids,” Ms Chatterjee called, raising her voice without looking away from Lillian. “Pack up your instruments, please. Quietly. You can leave them locked in the cupboards if you don’t feel safe carrying them. Juno, Faroukh, you stay a moment. Everyone else, off you go. No gossiping in the corridor, please; Miss Edgecombe’s fine, she just needs air.”
The rustle of movement, the clink of instrument cases, the soft squeak of chair legs. A few concerned looks over shoulders. Then the door clicked shut, and the room was smaller.
Juno hovered by Lillian’s feet, clarinet case dangling, tail low.
“Miss,” she said, dropping the formality without seeming to notice. “You scared me.”
“Sorry, hen,” Lillian said. Her voice sounded wrong in her own ears, thinner than usual. “Didnae mean to.”
Ms Chatterjee perched on the edge of a student desk, bangles chiming softly. “How long’s this been going on?” she asked.
“What?” Lillian said. “Collapsing mid‑crescendo? First time, promise.”
Ms Chatterjee gave her a look that would have withered a less stubborn anthro on the spot. “The shadows,” she said. “Under your eyes. The way you’ve been holding yourself like you’re expecting someone to drop a piano on you. You’ve lost weight, then you haven’t, then you’re living on coffee and biscuits. Talk to me as your colleague, not your Head of Year. How long?”
Lillian stared at the ceiling tiles. She counted three stains in the nearest square, the ghosts of old leaks.
“A while,” she admitted. “Since… spring, really.”
“Since the warehouse?” Juno blurted. “You and Miles have been going there loads, I know you have, some of the kids mentioned—”
“It’s not just that,” Lillian said quickly. The warehouse was a neat hook to hang the conversation on; she could feel the other truth sitting behind it like a closed door. “It’s… everything. Cuts. Extra duties. Folk being arseholes on the telly. And aye, the warehouse. Those kids.”
Her chest tightened as she thought of Sarah’s voice in the van, bright and brittle, the way she’d said I’m in charge like it was a battle cry and an apology at once.
“There’s a limit to what you can carry,” Ms Chatterjee said. “Even you. You do know that, don’t you?”
Lillian laughed, a short, humourless sound. “No’ really my skill set,” she said. “I’m good at carrying. It’s the letting folk help that I’m shite at.”
Juno shifted her weight from paw to paw. “You could… tell Mr Ferguson,” she said cautiously. “He looks at you like you hung the moon. I’m pretty sure he’d do the carrying if you asked.”
The idea of telling Miles—really telling him—hit Lillian like a physical thing. For a second she saw his face as it would be if she said the words I was pregnant and now I’m not and I kept it from you.
Her stomach lurched.
“Some things,” she said, before she could stop herself, “if you tell them out loud, you cannae take them back. You ken?”
Juno’s eyes widened. Ms Chatterjee’s expression softened, then sharpened again. “Whatever those things are,” Ms Chatterjee said, “they’re currently flattening you. Secrets are heavy, Lillian. They don’t get lighter if you pretend they’re not in your bag.”
Lillian swallowed. Her throat felt raw. “I’m fine,” she lied. “It was just… low blood sugar. I skipped lunch. I’ll grab a sandwich, see the GP if it happens again. You ken how it is.”
“I know that if you were one of my pupils, I’d be phoning home right now,” Ms Chatterjee said. “As it is, I’m phoning Occupational Health and telling them you need a proper appointment, not a tick‑box questionnaire. And if you faint again in my corridor, I’m dragging you to A&E myself.”
There was warmth under the steel. It made Lillian’s eyes sting. “Aye, aye,” she said. “Bossy.”
Juno crouched down so they were more or less at eye level.
“Miss,” she said quietly. “You always tell us that music’s about breathing. In, out, in, out. You can’t play if you hold your breath ‘til you fall over. Maybe you should listen to your own advice.”
Lillian huffed, somewhere between a laugh and a sob. “Traitor,” she said. “Quoting my own patter back at me.”
But the words lodged. Breathing. She realised, with a small shock, that she’d been taking tiny, shallow sips of air for months, as if anything more might dislodge something awful.
Ms Chatterjee squeezed her shoulder. “Take the last period off,” she said. “I’ll cover. Go sit in the staffroom, or the chapel, or wherever you go when you need to pretend you’re not made of meat and nerves for an hour. Then go home. And talk to someone who loves you before you crack all the way through.”
The phrase before you crack all the way through landed with the inevitability of a title card. Lillian let them help her up. Her legs shook, but she stayed vertical. Juno shoved her clarinet into its case one‑handed, the other hovering as if ready to catch her again.
In the staffroom, the fluorescent lights hummed and the coffee tasted like burnt mud as usual. Someone had left a half‑finished crossword on the table. The radiator rattled. She sat on the sagging sofa and stared at the vending machine, at the rows of crisps and chocolate bars she’d told herself she didn’t deserve more times than she could count.
Her phone buzzed. Miles.
You okay? Can swing by school before rehearsal if you need a lift.
She stared at the message. Her thumb hovered over the keyboard.
I fainted, she could type. I’ve been falling apart quietly for months. I lied to you about the biggest thing that ever happened to my body and now it’s eating me from the inside out.
She typed something else.
Bit of a day. Kids are wild. Might take the bus and meet you at the studio. X
She watched the words sit there, neat and cowardly. Then she hit send.
Her chest ached.
In the chapel later, with its cheap electric candles and vaguely nondenominational posters about hope, she sat on a pew and tried to breathe properly.
In, two, three.
Out, two, three, four.
The memory of the bathroom came anyway, vivid and uninvited. The curve of her belly that hadn’t lasted. The moment she’d realised there would be no baby, only blood and bleach and the decision to protect Miles by pretending nothing had changed. “Och, Lillian,” she muttered to herself. “What have you done?”
She felt the crack then—not the dramatic shattering she’d always imagined, but a thin fracture running the length of her, from crown to belly to paws. Invisible from the outside. Impossible to ignore now she’d noticed it. In the quiet, she made herself a promise she didn’t quite believe.
Soon. I’ll tell him soon. Before this secret is the only thing we are.
Outside, somewhere across the city, a warehouse roof dripped onto concrete. Somewhere, a girl with a sketchbook was counting blankets.
Lillian closed her eyes and tried, for once, not to carry both rooms at once.

### 6.25.2
Sarah saw the letter the next morning, as soon as she came back from the shop with a bag of reduced‑to‑clear bread and one carton of juice. A plastic wallet, taped over the rusted metal, the council crest printed at the top.
“What’s that?” Faroukh asked, leaning over her shoulder. He still had sleep‑creases in his fur, hoodie zipped up to his chin.
She read the heading once, twice.
PROHIBITION NOTICE — UNSAFE PREMISES.
The words slotted into her spine like ice. “They’re shutting us down,” she said, the numbness hitting quicker than the fear. “Or they’re gonna try.”
