She skimmed the rest. Fire safety violations. Structural integrity concerns. Evidence of unauthorised habitation. Requirement to vacate immediately.
“Immediate?” Faroukh repeated. “Like, today?”
“They always say immediately,” she said, though she had no idea if that was true. “It means as soon as they can get their arses in gear.” She snatched the notice down and folded it into quarters before the littles could see.
Inside, the warehouse looked the same as it had yesterday and the day before: mattresses along the walls, blankets hung for privacy, buckets under drips. Bun’s absence left a small, raw space by the crates where his bed had been. Lila had lined his patch with her drawings, as if he might step back into them. “Is Bun dead?” she’d asked, twice in the night.
“No,” Sarah had said both times, even though she didn’t know. “He’s in hospital. That’s better than here.”
Now Lila peered over the railings from the mezzanine, ears limp.
“What’s on the door?” she called.
“Just boring grown‑up stuff,” Sarah lied. “Nothing you need to worry about.” Her tongue tasted like metal. She dropped the folded paper into the pocket of her jeans and turned to the crates. “Right,” she said. “Breakfast.”
There wasn’t much to work with. The bread was speckled with green again; she cut the worst bits off, toasted the rest on the little camping stove, sliced it into thin pieces so it looked like more. The juice she watered down, stretching one carton between eight cups.
The kids chewed obediently. No one complained. That was somehow worse.
“I don’t feel right,” one of the smaller girls muttered after a few bites. She was a fox kit with a cough that hadn’t shifted in weeks.
“You’re just tired,” Sarah said automatically, hand flying to the girl’s forehead. Warm, but not burning. “We’ll get you some proper food soon.”
Soon had become a slippery word. Soon when, exactly? Before the council brought bulldozers? Before Connor’s lot decided to stamp their claim even harder now the official paperwork had arrived? Faroukh’s phone buzzed.
Asim.
Heard about the ambulance. Anything you need right now?
Miles had clearly started his calls.
She typed back with one thumb.
Got a letter. Prohibition notice. They want us out.
Okay. Not surprising but still shit. Can swing by later. You around 6?
She glanced around the hall at the huddled shapes, at Faroukh trying to teach two of the younger boys how to knot their shoelaces properly.
Yeah. But come quiet. Don’t want Connor or the neighbours sniffing.
She shoved the phone back in her pocket before he could reply.
The day dragged. Every sound outside made her flinch. Every car door slam sounded like an official visit. Every distant siren twisted her stomach. At lunchtime one of the boys, a skinny rat kid with a permanent sniff, started shivering. He hadn’t stopped coughing since the cold snap. Now there was a new sound in his chest, a wet rattle. “You all right, mate?” Faroukh asked, thumping him too hard on the back. “You look like death.”
“Cheers,” the boy croaked.
Sarah crouched beside him. His nose was streaming. His eyes had that glassy sheen she recognised from winters past. “When did you last feel proper warm?” she asked.
He thought about it. “Dunno,” he said. “Summer?”
She swallowed. “Blankets,” she told Faroukh. “Two extra on him, and he’s sleeping upstairs tonight where it’s less damp.”
“Upstairs is freezing,” the boy protested weakly.
“It’s less damp,” she insisted. “We’re doing triage, all right? You’re damp‑sick, you go up. You’re cough‑sick, you get the corner away from the draught. You’re drama‑sick, you go sweep the stairs.” Her attempt at a joke fell flat.
Faroukh gave her a look. “You all right?” he asked quietly once the boy had shuffled away with his blankets. “You’ve gone… weird.”
“Normal weird or extra?” she asked.
“Extra,” he said. “Like you’re not here, even when you’re shouting.”
She told him about the letter then, in a low voice by the crates, showing him the folded paper. His eyes went wide as he scanned the lines. “They can’t just chuck us out,” he said. “Where are we meant to go?”
“Council b&b,” she said. “Hostels. The system. You know the patter.”
He swore under his breath. “We should burn it,” he said suddenly, scrunching the paper in his fist. “Notice never happened.”
She snatched it back. “It’s not a magic spell, Faroukh,” she snapped. “They’ve got copies. Somewhere in an office there’s a wee line on a spreadsheet saying ‘death trap full of kids, sort later.’”
He flinched.
“Sorry,” she muttered. “I’m just…” He waited. “Tired,” she finished. “And done letting other folk decide when we get looked at.”
Outside, clouds gathered again, low and heavy. By mid‑afternoon, the first fat drops started to hammer on the patched‑up roof. The leak they’d been fighting all winter picked up from sullen drip to a steady patter. Buckets filled faster. Lila’s drawings curled at the edges, the pencil lines blurring.
“No, no, no,” Lila whispered, pressing her paws to the damp paper as if she could hold the water back. “They’ll go all smudgy.”
Sarah grabbed a stack of sketches and moved them to a drier patch, teeth gritted. “We’ll make new ones,” she said. “Better ones. I promise.”
She didn’t know if she believed herself any more.
By the time Asim arrived, the sky had gone that ugly yellow‑grey that meant the weather was thinking about turning from rain to something worse. He slipped in through the side door with his hood up, shaking droplets from his ears. His wild dog fur was plastered flat in places, his hymn‑book under one arm like he’d come straight from a meeting. “Hey, squad,” he called softly. “Permission to enter?”
The littles liked him; they flocked to his legs at once. He bent to their level, exchanging fist bumps and in‑jokes, before working his way over to Sarah. “You look wrecked,” he said without preamble. “I mean that in a loving, community‑organiser way.”
“Cheers,” she said. “Always nice to feel radiant.”
He took the letter from her and read it, lips thinning. “Right,” he said. “So they’ve finally put it in writing. Miles told me he was pushing them, asking what their plan was. This is them covering their backs in case anything else happens.”
“They don’t have a plan,” she said. “They’ve got a notice and a file and no clue what it feels like to sleep with a leak dripping in your ear.”
“Agreed,” he said. “Which is why we’re going to make the plan, and then bully them into pretending it was their idea.”
She wanted to laugh. She couldn’t find the energy.
“I called an ambulance,” she said instead. “Social’ll come. They’ll ask why there’s a bunch of kids with no adults living in a condemned building. I have no answer that makes any sense.”
Asim’s gaze softened. “You do,” he said. “It’s just not one they like hearing. ‘We were made a problem and then left to solve ourselves.’”
“Aye, that’ll go down well at the case conference,” she said. “‘Hello, Mr Social Worker, I’d like to indict capitalism.’”
He snorted. “Miles is working his logistics magic,” he said. “He rang me on his break today, talking about firebreaks like he’s planning a military operation. He’s got a youth centre contact who might be able to take some of the littles short‑term, with supervision. There’s a church hall that’s under‑used. We can get sleeping bags, proper food, volunteers.”
“And then?” she asked. “We scatter. Folk get put in boxes. I lose everyone.”
“Or,” he said carefully, “some of you go into safer boxes while we fight for better ones. And some of you go somewhere that isn’t a box at all.”
He didn’t say with Miles and Lillian. He didn’t have to.
Her chest clenched. “I can’t leave them,” she whispered. “They’re mine.”
“They’re not possessions,” he said gently. “They’re people. You’ve kept them alive for years, Sarah. That’s… I can’t even find a word for that. But if you burn yourself out completely trying to keep doing it inside a literal condemned building, what message does that send them? That their only worth is in being loyal to a sinking ship?”
She glared at him. “You’re meant to be on my side,” she said.
“I am,” he said. “And I’m on theirs. Those two sides are not the same, and that’s the hardest part.”
The rain intensified, drumming on the roof like an angry crowd. Somewhere in the back corner, a bucket overflowed, water spreading in a slick.
The coughing boy started up again. Each hack sounded wetter. “Have you had him looked at?” Asim asked quietly.
“By who?” she snapped. “The magic anthro doctor who doesn’t ask awkward questions?”
He sighed. “We need to get him somewhere warm,” he said. “Soon. If the council shut this place, they might do something decent by accident. Or they might just nail boards over the door and call it safeguarding.”

### 6.25.3
Two hours later, the day tipped from bad to something else.
Connor’s lot arrived just as the light was going. No dramatic pounding this time, no shouted threats. Just the door easing open, the smell of wet fur and cheap cigarettes sliding in with them. “Evening, Princess,” Connor said, as if they hadn’t nearly watched a kid die on this floor two nights ago. “Heard the council’s finally noticed your wee orphanage.”
Sarah’s hackles rose. “You can turn round and walk back out,” she said. “We’re busy.”
He glanced at the buckets, the blankets, the letter Asim was still holding.
“Looks like you’re running out of busy,” he said. “Word is you’ve got social workers sniffing and activists fluttering about. All very heart‑warming. But you know they’ll look at you and see trouble, right? Barely legal, past caring age, mouth on you. They’ll want the wee cute ones. You’ll get left with us either way.”
His crew sniggered. One of them kicked at a bucket, sloshing water onto the floor.
“Oi,” Asim snapped. “Pack that in.”
Connor clocked him, eyes narrowing.
“Oh, it’s the singer,” he said. “Here to do a benefit concert? Pass the hat round for the poor little beasts?”
Asim’s jaw tightened, but he kept his voice level. “I’m here because these kids deserve better than you and better than this,” he said. “And because I’m nosy.”
Sarah put herself between Connor and the nearest bunch of littles, paws shaking. “We’re done with your tithe,” she said. “No more food, no more blankets. We’re closing up.”
“Council’s closing you up,” he corrected. “And when they do, where d’you think the ones they don’t fancy are gonna go? They’ll end up in the same alleys as us. Might as well get them used to it.”
It was the casual way he said it that did it. As if the kids she’d raised were inventory he was planning to scoop up in the clearance sale.
Something in her snapped.
“Get out,” she said. “Now. Before I do something I regret.”
He leaned in, close enough that she could smell the stale beer on his breath.
“You regret plenty already, Princess,” he murmured. “You just haven’t worked out what you actually control.”
That night, after they finally left—after Asim physically stood between them and the food crates, after Faroukh guided the littles upstairs, after Lila cried herself out in a nest of damp blankets—Sarah sat on the concrete by the door with the letter crumpled in her paw.
Her body hurt in places she hadn’t known could ache. Her head throbbed. Every time she closed her eyes she saw Bun on the floor, blood blooming under her paws. She had spent ten years telling herself that as long as the kids were alive and together, she was doing enough. That the leaks and the mould and the knife fights in the alley were just texture.
Now there was a piece of paper in her hand saying, plainly, that the building itself might kill them. There was a coughing boy in the corner who might not survive another winter here. There was a girl upstairs drawing new pictures over old stains as if she could will her friend back from the hospital.
And there was the simple, brutal fact that she was sixteen. Her back hurt from lifting kids and crates. Her chest hurt from holding in fear. Her world had shrunk to this hall and the walk to the cheap shop and the occasional van that brought the only adults who didn’t make her skin crawl. She had always imagined that if she ever left, it would be in some blaze of glory. Kicked out, maybe. Dragged away. Forced. Something that proved she hadn’t chosen to abandon them.
Sitting there with the notice digging into her palm, she realised that staying might be the bigger betrayal.
If I stay, she thought, Bun won’t be the last. Somebody else will slip, or get sick, or pick the wrong fight. And everyone will say, afterwards, that we should have seen it coming.
Tears pricked at her eyes, hot and furious. “I’m done,” she whispered to the empty air. “I’m so bloody done.”
She pulled Faroukh’s phone out with stiff fingers.
Miles’s last message—Call me if anything changes—glowed on the screen.
She stared at it for a long time. The idea of asking for help still sat wrong in her mouth, like a word in a language she didn’t trust.
Then she thought of Bun’s blood on the concrete. Of the damp cough in the corner. Of Connor’s voice saying you’ll get left with us either way.
Her thumb hovered, then tapped.
She didn’t text. She hit call.
He picked up halfway through the first ring, like he’d been sitting staring at the phone.
“Sarah?” he said.
Her throat closed around his name for a second. When she forced words out, they came in a rush. “They put a notice on the door,” she said. “Council. Saying we’ve got to get out. The roof’s getting worse. The littles are sick. Connor’s sniffing around like it’s the end‑of‑season sale. I can’t hold this any more. I can’t—”
The last word came out as a sob. She hated herself for it.
There was a small pause on his end. She could hear the clink of something—keys?—and the rustle of a jacket. “Okay,” he said. His voice was steady in a way that made her want to punch him and cling to him at the same time. “Okay. You did the right thing calling. I’m coming now.”
“Now?” she echoed. “You’re at work.”
“I’m not,” he said. “Not any more. I’ve finished my shift. I’ve already spoken to Asim, the youth centre, the social worker. We’ve got a few things lined up. We were going to do this tomorrow, nice and structured.” She could hear him moving fast, breath coming a little shorter. “But if this is how today is looking, we do it today. I’ll be there in twenty minutes.”
“What are you going to do?” she asked, voice small.
“Firebreak,” he said. “We move the kids. We get you out. We give the council a situation where the only face they can put on this is ‘responsible adults stepping in’. And we make sure Connor has nothing left to feed on.”
Her heart hammered against her ribs. “What if they take them somewhere I can’t see them?” she whispered. “What if they take them and I’m just… left?”
“We’re not letting you vanish,” he said. “Lillian and I meant what we said. We want you. If you’ll have us. But we can talk about the shape of that once everyone’s out of immediate danger. One thing at a time. Right now, your job is to keep everyone calm and not let anyone open that door unless it’s me. Can you do that?”
She closed her eyes. Her head felt light, like she might float off the bucket she was sitting on. She had been doing everything for so long—cooking, bargaining, patching, mediating—that the idea of having just one job felt almost indecent. “Aye,” she said hoarsely. “I can do that.”
“Good girl,” he said, and the praise didn’t make her bristle the way it might have a year ago. “I’m on my way.”
The line clicked off.
She sat for a moment longer, phone clutched to her chest, listening to the rain and the coughs and the muffled whispers from the mezzanine.
Lowest day, she thought. That’s what this is. The bottom of the well.
Somewhere out there, an engine turned over. Somewhere, a badger and a German Shepherd were moving towards her, carrying their own cracks and secrets.
Sarah wiped her eyes roughly on her sleeve, stood up, and went to tell the kids to pack what they could carry.

### 6.25.4
He was in the stockroom, half‑inside a pallet cage of tinned tomatoes, when his phone buzzed against his hip. He finished counting the row, marked the tally on his clipboard, and only then fished the handset out of his pocket.
Faroukh. The name still looked wrong on a screen. In his head he was usually “the boy from the warehouse”, or “Sarah’s friend”, or just a knot of worry that lived somewhere behind his ribs. He frowned. Faroukh never called: it was Sarah using his phone. She didn’t ring unless he was on the way already, unless they’d arranged a drop.
By the time he got to the staff corridor and enough quiet to listen, the call had already gone to voicemail. One new message. The little icon blinked at him, patient. He pressed play.
“Um. It’s me.” Her voice was rough around the edges, stretched thin. “We had… a thing. At the warehouse. One of the littles slipped. Hit his head. There was blood everywhere. I called an ambulance. They took him. Social services are gonna come sniffing. Connor’s lot were here. They’re saying it’s my fault. I—” The recording juddered, as if she’d moved the phone away for a second. “I can’t do this, Miles,” she said, quieter now, her voice shaking. “I thought I could, but I can’t. This place is eating us. I need… I can’t. A plan. Something. Call me back. Please.”
The line clicked dead.
For a moment all he could hear was the hum of the strip lights and the distant whine of a floor buffer out on the shop floor. His reflection in the fire door glass looked the same as it always did: big shoulders, dark fur, hi‑vis vest, barcode scanner in one hand. A man who could solve anything with enough spreadsheets.
Head injury. Ambulance. Social services.
He’d known, of course, that it would come to this. You didn’t stack children in a condemned warehouse and expect physics or bureaucracy to look the other way forever. He’d known and he’d kept bringing crates anyway, because not bringing them felt worse.
Now the line he hadn’t wanted to draw had been drawn for him.
He hit call.
She picked up on the second ring.
“Yeah?”
“It’s me,” he said. “Are you safe right now?”
There was a shuffling sound, the echo of a big room.
“Depends what you mean by safe,” she said. “Roof’s still leaking. Connor’s lot pissed off when the sirens came. Kids are… quiet.” Quiet was worse than screaming, in his experience.
“Any adults there apart from you?” he asked, already knowing the answer.
“No,” she said. “Just me and Faroukh and the big ones. And the big ones are barely hanging on themselves.” 
He pressed his thumb into the soft rubber of the phone case until it squeaked. “Okay,” he said. “Listen. I’m at work. I can’t walk out right this second, but my shift finishes at four. I’ll be there by half‑past. Can you meet me outside? Just you, or you and Faroukh if you trust him.”
“Why outside?” Suspicion flickered up at once, reflexive.
“Because if social services or the council turn up while we’re talking, I want them to see you as a kid who called for help, not as the manager of an illegal hostel,” he said. “And because I need you to be able to walk away if you need to.”
There was a pause. When she spoke again, her voice was smaller. “I don’t want to walk away,” she said. “That’s the problem.”
“I know,” he said. “We’ll talk. Half four. Yeah?”
Another long beat.
“Yeah,” she said. “Half four.”
He hung up and put his forehead briefly against the cool metal of the fire door. The smell of cardboard and pallet wrap pressed in on him. He could keep pretending this was something he could manage in his spare time—a charity case, a side project. Or he could admit what the warehouse had become: a fire that was going to jump the line if he didn’t cut it off.
He’d learned about firebreaks in a training video years ago, back when the chain had flirted with selling garden furniture and barbecue kits. A bloke in a hi‑vis had stood in a field talking about controlled burns, about deliberately clearing strips of land so a wildfire had nothing to eat when it reached them.
Right now, the warehouse was the scrubland. Connor’s lot, social services, the press—they were all different tongues of the same flame.
He needed a firebreak.
“Miles?” His deputy manager, a human woman with a buzzcut and a clipboard, poked her head round the door. “You all right? Frozen peas delivery’s early. Need you on the dock.”
He straightened up.
“On my way,” he said.
He moved through the rest of the shift in a kind of double exposure. On one layer he was the efficient regional guy: checking temperatures, signing invoices, answering emails about promotional endcaps. On the other, he was mapping the warehouse in his head, overlaying hazard symbols: leaks here, dodgy electrics there, stair rails loose, exit routes blocked by junk.
He texted as he walked, thumbs moving fast.
To Asim:  
You free tonight? Need to talk Strays, urgent. Not just food.

To Juno:  
Kid from warehouse hurt, ambulance called. Don’t panic, they’re in hospital. Might need extra blankets + flasks later. Ask your friend if the church hall is ever empty weekday evenings?

To Lillian:  
Running a bit late. Warehouse stuff. Will explain later. Love you.

He deleted the last words twice before leaving them in. They sat there on the tiny screen, earnest and inadequate.
He didn’t tell her about the head injury. Not yet. She’d sounded so tired the last few weeks, voice hoarse even on good days, shoulders pinched. He kept telling himself he’d sit her down, ask properly how she was. He kept not doing it, because every time he looked at her she was in the middle of carrying something—lesson plans, set lists, someone else’s worries. He didn’t want to be another weight.
He finished his shift exactly on time, signed himself out, and jogged to the staff car park. The van’s engine coughed to life with a wheezy growl. He drove with the radio off, ears full of his own heartbeat and the imagined drip of water from the warehouse roof.
Sarah was waiting by the corner when he turned into the alley, hood up despite the break in the rain. She looked smaller than he remembered, standing away from the door as if the bricks themselves might bite.
He parked at the end of the alley and got out, hands visible, posture loose. They’d learned each other’s edges over months; he knew better than to come in hot.
“Hey,” he said.
“Hey,” she said. Her eyes were ringed with grey, fur clumped where she’d clearly scrubbed blood off in a hurry. The smell of disinfectant and damp concrete clung to her. Her tail was held low.
“Can we walk?” he asked. “Just up to the corner and back.”
She hesitated, glancing at the door, then nodded and fell into step beside him.
For a while they just walked, the estate’s familiar mix of chip‑shop grease, exhaust fumes and wet dog filling the air. “Tell me everything,” he said at last. “Not the polished version. The truth.”
She laughed once, bitter. “There is no polished version,” she said. “Kid slipped. Hit his head. Blood everywhere. I called nine‑nine‑nine. Medics came. Took him away. Social’ll get told there’s a group of kids living in a death trap with no adults in sight. Connor’s lot were there before that, taking food, scaring the littles. Roof’s getting worse. We’re out of decent blankets. Faroukh’s talking about moving away to someone he knows to get away from it all, and… I don’t think it might be a friendly place.” She swallowed. “That enough truth for you?”
He listened, counting problems the way he counted tins. One, two, three—structural, social, legal. “How often do Connor’s lot come?” he asked.
“Couple of times a week now,” she said. “Sometimes just for food, sometimes to remind us they can.”
“Any adults you trust within two streets?” he asked. “Anyone with a front room they’d let some of the littles kip in, even short‑term?”
She shook her head immediately, then faltered. “Maybe Mrs Kaur,” she said. “She used to give us leftovers when she worked the late shift at the takeaway. But she’s got her own grandkids. And folk talk when there’s too many anthro kids in a flat. You know what the neighbours are like.”
He did. He’d had complaints about “loitering youths” in every branch he’d ever managed. The definition of loitering rarely included human teenagers in rugby hoodies. “Okay,” he said. “And social services. What’s your worst case scenario if they turn up?”
She stopped walking. Her paws balled into fists. “They take the littles,” she said. “Split them up. Shove them into homes where folk don’t understand them. The big kids get left to rot here till they age out and Connor’s lot swallow them. Nobody sees the whole picture. Nobody cares that we’re… us. Together.” Her voice cracked on the last word.
He nodded slowly. “And your best case?” he asked.
She gave him a look like he’d asked her to recite a fairy tale. “Best case?” she said. “They magically find a big warm house and let me bring everyone, and nobody gets called a beast or a lost cause, and nobody gets filmed for a sad wee documentary to make humans feel better about themsel—” Her voice broke entirely. She stared at the pavement. “It’s daft,” she muttered. “There is no best case.”
He let the silence sit between them for a few breaths. “Right,” he said at last. “So. There is no good outcome where you keep running this place on your own until you drop. That’s what I’m hearing.” She flinched, then nodded once. “What if,” he said carefully, “we treat this like a firebreak?”
She blinked. “A what?”
“You know when there’s a wildfire,” he said, “and folk burn strips of land on purpose so there’s nothing left for the flames to eat? It looks brutal in the moment, but it stops the whole forest going up. This place—” he nodded towards the warehouse “—it’s already burning. Maybe the only way through is to clear a strip. Move the littles somewhere safer, even if it’s not perfect. Cut Connor’s access. Give you a way out that isn’t abandoning them.”
Her ears flattened. “Where?” she demanded. “You got a secret spare house lying around?”
“No,” he said. “But I’ve got a van, a community that trusts us, and more leverage with the council than I used to. I’ve got Asim, who knows half the activists in London. I’ve got a church hall that’s empty some nights and a youth worker who owes me a favour. And I’ve got a social worker who’s already half convinced we’d make decent parents.”
She looked at him sharply. “You’re talking about adoption again,” she said.
He lifted a paw. “I’m talking about options,” he said. “For the littles, that might be emergency placements with people we can keep an eye on. For you… that might be us. But I’m not asking you to decide that tonight. Tonight I’m asking if you’ll let me step in properly. Not just as the bloke who brings noodles, but as an adult who will put his name on forms and talk to people who make decisions.” 
The word adult made her flinch as if he’d slapped her. “You think I’m no an adult?” she snapped.
“I think you’ve been doing an adult’s job on a teenager’s body for too long,” he said, voice gentle but firm. “And I think that if something happens to you, there’s no backup. That scares the hell out of me.”
They walked in silence to the end of the street and back. A bus rumbled past; the driver didn’t look at them.
“If you talk to social,” she said eventually, “you’ll tell them what, exactly?”
“That there’s a group of kids who have kept each other alive in impossible conditions,” he said. “That they called for help when it really mattered. That they need safe accommodation yesterday. That some of us are willing to step up as kinship carers instead of letting them vanish into the system.”
Her mouth twisted. “They’ll say we’re trouble,” she said. “They always do.”
“Then we’ll give them better stories to believe,” he said. “Evidence. Witnesses. Lesson plans. Gig schedules. Whatever it takes to make them see you as a person, not a statistic.”
She huffed out something that might have been a laugh if it hadn’t sounded so close to a sob.
“You make it sound so tidy,” she said. “Like stacking crisps.”
“It won’t be tidy,” he said. “It’ll be messy and slow and full of folk who don’t call back. But we can’t keep doing… this.” He gestured vaguely towards the warehouse. “The fire’s coming whether we plan for it or not.”
They stopped a few yards from the side door. From inside came the faint, muffled sounds of kids trying to be quiet: a cough, a clatter, a shushed argument over a blanket. “What do you need from me?” she asked.
“I need you to keep the kids as safe as you can tonight,” he said. “No deals with Connor. No letting anyone in. If social or police turn up, you call me before you open that door. Tomorrow, I’ll start making calls. Asim, the youth centre, the social worker. We’ll work out how to move the littles in stages. And I need you to start imagining a world where you’re not the one holding the roof up.”
Her jaw clenched. “I don’t know how to do anything else,” she whispered.
He swallowed. “Then we’ll learn together,” he said.
She studied his face for a long time, eyes searching for the catch. She must have seen something—steady, stubborn—because her shoulders dropped a fraction. “Okay,” she said. “You want to play fireman, big dog, you do it. But if they try to split my lot up without me there, I’ll haunt you.”
“Deal,” he said. “You can haunt me from our sofa, with a mug of tea.” He watched her slip back through the door, bolt sliding into place. The warehouse loomed against the grey sky, every missing tile and cracked window now a problem to be solved, not just endured.
In his head, he began a new list.

Firebreak Plan:
1. Call Asim.  
2. Call youth centre.  
3. Call social worker, frame warehouse as emergency, not scandal.  
4. Talk to Lillian. Really talk.  
5. Get the kids out before the next storm hits.

He climbed into the van, started the engine, and pulled away from the kerb. The dashboard clock ticked over to 17:01.


 
# Act VII
## Chapter 26: Evacuation

### 7.26.2
The estate always smelled different in the rain.
Miles noticed it as soon as he turned off the main road and into the narrower streets. Wet tarmac, overflowing drains, kebab fat cooling in the gutter. The kind of damp that got into your fur and stayed there.
He drove with both paws tight on the wheel, wipers beating time with his pulse. In the back of the van, bungee cords creaked around the hastily loaded cargo: sleeping bags from the youth centre, a crate of past‑sell‑by bread and fruit his store would have binned otherwise, two big flasks of soup Lillian had insisted on putting on before he’d left the flat.
She hadn’t argued when he said he needed to go. She’d just looked at him for a long moment, something raw behind her glasses, then kissed his jaw and told him to be careful. “Bring them home,” she’d said, voice low. “However that looks.”
Home. The word was doing a lot of work these days.
He turned into the alley and killed the headlights before the van’s nose cleared the corner. No point advertising more than he had to. The warehouse squatted at the end of the lane, a darker shape against the dark, one high window leaking a thin strip of light.
He parked, grabbed his hi‑vis—he’d found people let you get away with all sorts if you looked Official—and shrugged it on over his hoodie. The bright yellow sat oddly against the grey evening.
As he stepped out into the rain, his phone buzzed.
Asim:  
We’re two streets away with youth workers + minibus. Say when.
He texted back.
Wait for my signal. Don’t want a scene if we can help it.
He didn’t bother texting the social worker again. She knew the situation; he’d made sure of that. He wanted her to walk into a crisis already halfway stabilised, not the other way round. He walked down the alley, boots splashing in shallow puddles, and knocked on the side door with a pattern they’d agreed on months ago—two short, two long.
The bolt scraped back. The door opened just enough for a pair of wary golden eyes to peer out. “You came,” Sarah said.
“I said I would,” he replied.
She let him in. The smell hit him first: damp, unwashed clothes, too many bodies in one space, the metallic tang of old blood barely scrubbed from concrete. The kids were clustered on the mezzanine, bags at their feet, blankets bundled under arms. Some clutched soft toys; others held onto each other’s sleeves like lifelines.
“You’ve been busy,” he said softly.
“Told them it was a fire drill,” she said. “Only with bags.”
Pride and heartbreak tugged at him in equal measure.
He scanned the space, eyes doing their usual inventory: leaks (worse), exposed wiring (still there), exits (two usable, one blocked by junk), trip hazards (everywhere). The coughing boy sat hunched in a nest of blankets in the corner, cheeks flushed, breath whistling.
“First things first,” Miles said. “No one’s leaving this building in a panic. We’re going to do this like we’re evacuating a shop with dodgy electrics. Calm, in order, with a plan.”
Faroukh appeared at his elbow, jaw set. “We’re ready,” he said. “Bags packed. Little ones have got their toys and the important stuff. We hid the rest. If Connor wants to nick a mattress, he can fight the bedbugs for it.”
“Good boy,” Miles said. “Right. Listen up, everyone.” He raised his voice just enough to carry without sounding like a shout. Years of training staff in busy stockrooms had taught him the difference. “Here’s what’s going to happen,” he said. “In a few minutes, my friend Asim is going to bring a minibus round the corner. Some nice, boring youth workers are going to help us get the younger kids into somewhere warm and dry for the night. You are not being arrested. You are not in trouble. You are being moved because this building is not safe, and for once the adults are actually doing something about it.”
Murmurs rippled across the mezzanine.
“What about us?” one of the older girls called. “We’re not littles.”
“We’re working on that,” he said. “There are beds at the centre, and there are other options that might fit better. But nobody’s getting left standing outside in the rain while we have that conversation. Agreed?”
There was a reluctant rumble of assent.
Sarah stood beside him, arms folded, trying to look like this was all her idea. He could see the tremor in her paws. “And Connor?” she muttered. “What if he shows up with his lot and decides he likes the look of a free bus?”
Miles’s jaw tightened. “Then we give him nothing to grab onto,” he said. “No chaos. No kids scattered. Just a boring queue of people leaving an unsafe building under supervision. Predators like confusion. We’re not giving him any.” He’d hoped not to have to test that theory. The universe, naturally, had other plans.
They’d just started downstairs—little ones first, each with a bag and a bigger kid assigned—when the side door rattled. “He’s early,” Faroukh muttered.
“That’s not Asim’s knock,” Sarah said. Her fur bristled.
The door swung open before anyone could reach it. Connor slid in, bringing a gust of wet air with him. Two of his crew flanked him, hoods up, eyes bright with curiosity.
“Well, well,” he said, taking in the bags, the line, the tension. “Looks like someone’s having a party and forgot to invite us.”
Miles stepped forward, slotting himself between Connor and the kids almost without thinking. The hi‑vis vest helped; people were used to not looking past the neon. “Evening,” he said, voice deliberately mild. “Building’s closed. Health and safety. You’ll have to come back another time.”
Connor’s gaze flicked over him. The smirk faltered as he realised this wasn’t a random do‑gooder but the same German Shepherd who’d been bringing crates for months. “Look who’s playing manager,” he said. “Thought you just did deliveries, mate.”
“I do a lot of things,” Miles said. “Right now I’m making sure a group of minors don’t die of pneumonia in a condemned warehouse. You can help by not getting in the way.”
Connor laughed, short and sharp. “You hear that?” he said to his crew. “Council finally notices the kiddie kennel and suddenly we’ve got a proper rescue mission. Cameras coming next, are they? Sad music? Donations?”
Miles didn’t rise to it. “There are no cameras,” he said. “Just a youth centre that’s agreed to open its doors and a council that doesn’t want a scandal on their hands after an ambulance call. You showing up here tonight makes you part of the liability, not part of the solution.”
Connor’s eyes narrowed. “Big words,” he said. “Thing is, when they’ve finished feeling good about themselves, the nice humans will go home. The kids who don’t fit their idea of adoptable will still be here. And then they’ll come find us. They always do.”
Behind Miles, he could feel the kids listening. This was the story they’d been told their whole lives: you belong to us or to nobody. He took a breath, in through his nose, out slowly. When he spoke, his voice was quieter. “Maybe,” he said. “Or maybe this time they have other choices. Maybe this time some of the adults stick around. Maybe this time someone draws a line and says ‘this far and no further’.” He met Connor’s gaze and held it. “Firebreak,” he said softly, more to himself than anyone else. “We’re not adding more fuel.”
Connor snorted. “You think you can cut me out?” he said. “You’re just one bloke with a van.”
“One bloke with a van and a paper trail,” Miles said. “Council officers on their way. Social workers who know my name. Youth workers who’ve already got these kids on their registers. If anything happens to them after tonight, there’ll be people asking questions in rooms you don’t even know exist.”
He let that sink in. Connor wasn’t afraid of a fight; he was afraid of attention he couldn’t control.
“You want to keep your little empire,” Miles went on, “you’re better off not having your face associated with this building on the day it gets cleared.”
For a heartbeat, the only sound was the rain and the soft wheeze of the coughing boy in the corner.
Connor clicked his tongue, annoyed.
“Tch. Boring,” he said at last. “Used to be some romance in this. Now it’s all forms and councils and case notes.”
“That’s adulthood for you,” Asim’s voice called from the door.
He stepped in, shaking rain off his fur, followed by a human woman with a youth centre lanyard and a man carrying a clipboard.
“Evening,” the woman said briskly. “We’re from the youth service. We’ve got a minibus out front and another one on the way. Who’s in charge?”
Thirty pairs of eyes flicked between Sarah and Miles. “She is,” Miles said, nodding at Sarah. “She’s been keeping this place running. I’m here to make sure she doesn’t have to do it alone any more.”
The youth worker—Kerry, her badge read—took in the scene with practised efficiency: bags, leaks, too‑thin kids, older boys hovering on the edge. “Right,” she said. “Little ones first. Anyone under twelve, you’re with me. Over twelves, you’re with Steve. We’ve got warm food, clean bedding and people who actually get paid to worry about you. You’ll get more info once we’re at the centre. Nobody’s being forced into anything tonight; you’re being offered a safer bed. That’s it.”
Connor rolled his eyes. “You lot have fun with your safeguarding,” he said. “We’ll be around when the novelty wears off.”
He jerked his head at his crew. They melted back into the rain, shadows among shadows.
Miles resisted the urge to sag with relief. There’d be consequences later, of course. Connor wasn’t the type to let a loss slide. But for tonight, the door was theirs.
The next hour was a blur of movement. Miles lifted bags, carried a kid who fell asleep halfway down the stairs, steadied Faroukh when he nearly slipped on the wet concrete. He repeated the same reassurances over and over—no, you’re not going to prison; yes, you can bring your toy; no, they won’t shave your fur off—as if they were lines in a song.
He watched Sarah move through the crowd, touching shoulders, counting heads, murmuring instructions. She looked like a general overseeing a retreat she hadn’t wanted but knew they’d die without.
At one point she disappeared upstairs. When she came back down, she had Bun’s pillowcase clutched to her chest, empty and clean. “For when he gets out,” she said, voice flat.
They loaded the littles onto the first minibus in ones and twos. Lila insisted on sitting by the window so she could wave at the warehouse as it slid out of view. “Say bye,” she told the building solemnly. “Thank you for not killing us.”
Sarah laughed, a short, jagged sound. “That’s my girl,” she muttered.
The older ones were harder. Some balked at the idea of going anywhere official. Others were too proud to climb onto a vehicle labelled YOUTH SERVICE.
“You can stay with us,” Faroukh said to one boy who kept shaking his head. “We’ll make it not lame.”
In the end, peer pressure and the promise of hot food did what adult logic couldn’t. One by one, they filed out, leaving the warehouse emptier than Miles had ever seen it.
When the last youth worker had gone and the second minibus’s brake lights had turned the rain red, only three figures remained in the hall: Miles, Sarah, and the coughing boy, who’d rallied just enough to shuffle along under his own steam. “Hospital for you,” Miles said gently, nodding at the boy. “Proper one, this time, where they keep you long enough to fix the infection instead of patching you and sending you back to the mould.”
The boy grimaced. “Hospitals smell weird,” he muttered.
“So does this place,” Sarah said. “At least over there the smell means someone’s cleaning.”
Miles called an ambulance, making sure to use the words unsafe premises and pre‑existing notice and emergency removal. The operator’s tone shifted accordingly. When the medics arrived, they looked less startled than the last crew had. Progress, of a sort.
“You again,” one of them said to Sarah, recognition flickering. “You’re making a habit of this.”
“I’m trying to make a habit of not letting folk die on my watch,” she said.
They took the boy away with murmured reassurances. Miles watched the doors close and felt something unclench in his chest.
Then there were two.
The warehouse felt massive in its emptiness. Every drip echoed. The cold crawled up from the floor.
Sarah stood in the middle of it, arms wrapped around herself. Her bag sat at her feet, the strap frayed where she’d chewed it as a kid. “That’s it, then,” she said. “I’ve officially shut down my kingdom.”
He stepped beside her, close enough that their shoulders almost touched.
“You did what leaders are meant to do,” he said. “You got your people out alive.”
She barked out a hollow laugh. “And now what am I?” she asked. “Just… a stray with no strays?”
He thought of the adoption pamphlets still on their kitchen table, the stupid cheerful fonts. Of the night Lillian had cried quietly into his chest after telling him nothing was wrong. Of the empty space in their flat that had felt, for months, like it was waiting for someone who never arrived.
“Now,” he said carefully, “you get to decide who you are when you’re not holding up rotten beams. There are kids at that centre who will still listen when you talk. There’s a hospital where Bun’s file will say ‘brought in by peer who called early’, not ‘found alone’. There’s a flat across town where there’s always food and blankets and two idiots who care about you more than you seem able to believe.”
She stared at him, eyes shining. “You keep saying you want me,” she said. “Like it’s that simple. You’ve seen me, right? I’m a walking risk assessment. Trauma, attachment issues, tendency to bite folk who try to help.”
He smiled despite everything. “We’ve met,” he said. “We’ve done the risk assessment. We still want you.”
Her breath hitched. “What about the others?” she asked. “The wee ones? The big ones who don’t fit in the nice boxes? You’ll just… leave them at the centre and hope?”
“We’ll visit,” he said. “We’ll check in. We’ll turn up at review meetings and parents’ evenings and the gigs they’re too young to be in. We’ll build something that means ‘Stray’ isn’t just another word for ‘forgotten’. But we can’t adopt all of them. Nobody can. That’s not a failure. That’s the truth.”
She swallowed hard. “Feels like a failure,” she said.
“Feels like a firebreak,” he said. “You lit one today. You stopped the flames here so they don’t take everything.”
They stood together in the empty hall, listening to the rain. Finally, she bent, picked up her bag and slung it over her shoulder. “Where are we going?” she asked, voice small.
“Tonight?” he said. “We’ll go to the youth centre, make sure everyone’s settled. Then I’d like you to come back to the flat with me and Lillian. Properly. Not just as a guest.”
She snorted. “What, you want me to move in same day as a building evacuation?” she said. “No even a cup of tea first?”
“There’ll be tea,” he said. “And biscuits. And a sofa you don’t have to defend with your life.”
Her lips twitched. “Pushy,” she muttered.
“Hopeful,” he corrected.
As they stepped out into the alley, he glanced back once. The warehouse loomed behind them, shadowed and leaking, a carcass of a building.
He didn’t feel triumph. He felt something closer to grief.
“Thank you,” he said under his breath, unexpected. “For holding them as long as you did.”
Sarah followed his gaze.
“It was never enough,” she murmured.
“Maybe not,” he said. “But it was what you had. Now it’s someone else’s turn to carry the load.”
He locked the door out of habit, even though it meant nothing now, and walked her towards the waiting minibus lights, bright in the rain.
The fire had been contained. The real work, he knew, was just beginning.

### 7.26.3
Sarah went back for her tin.

It was ridiculous, she knew. The warehouse was empty now, stripped of bodies, stripped of noise. The youth centre minibus lights were already turning away at the end of the alley, rain making everything smear.

But the tin was still in the office drawer.

The biscuit tin where they kept money, plasters, the last of the paracetamol, the scraps of control that made survival feel organised.

Miles waited by the door, giving her space.

“Two minutes,” she said.

He nodded. “I’m here.”

She moved through the warehouse with her bag on her shoulder, footsteps loud in the emptiness. The mezzanine looked wrong without kids draped over it. The pallets looked like bones.

In the office, the desk chair sat at an angle.

She opened the drawer and pulled the tin out.

It was heavier than she remembered.

Inside were coins, a few notes, a scrap of paper with someone’s handwriting: TOILET ROLLS. Another: PADS.

Sarah swallowed.

She snapped the lid shut.

When she stepped back into the main hall, Faroukh was there.

He stood by the pillar where he’d once washed up mugs and complained about Miles’s jokes. His hands were shoved deep into his pockets. His face was blank in the way that meant he was trying not to fall apart.

“You coming?” Sarah asked.

Faroukh’s jaw worked.

He looked towards the door.

Then back towards the shadows at the far end of the hall.

“I’m not—” he started, then stopped.

Sarah waited.

Faroukh swallowed. “If I go back to my lot,” he said, voice rough, “I’ll end up back with him. With… the bloke. The one who gives me cash.”

Sarah’s stomach tightened.

“And if I don’t,” he continued, words coming faster now, “my mum’ll say I’m ungrateful and my dad’ll pretend I never existed and my uncle’ll—” He cut himself off. His ears flattened. “They weren’t exactly safe before, yeah? Everyone thinks I’m a hard case. I’m not. I’m just… practiced.”

Sarah’s throat burned.

She thought of the phone.

Of the way he’d said in case you’re not there.

“You don’t have to go back,” she said.

Faroukh gave her a look that was half anger, half disbelief. “Where else am I supposed to go?”

Sarah nodded towards the door. “With us,” she said. “To the centre. For now.”

Faroukh’s nostrils flared. “You’re going with the posh badger and her big dog,” he said, like it was an insult.

Sarah snorted. “They’re not posh. They just own a kettle.”

Faroukh’s mouth twitched.

She stepped closer. “You can defer them,” she said quietly. “Your family. Your… whoever. You can take a breath. Nobody gets to drag you back into their mess just because they share your blood.”

Faroukh looked away.

“You’ll be safe?” he asked, too softly.

Sarah’s chest tightened. “Safer,” she said. “And you can be too.”

He stood there for another beat, then nodded once, sharp.

“Fine,” he said. “But if anyone tries to make me go to therapy I’m setting something on fire.”

“We’ll start with tea,” Sarah said.

They walked out together.

Miles held the door.

Faroukh brushed past without looking at him.

Then, at the threshold, he paused.

He glanced back at the warehouse.

“Fuck you,” he muttered under his breath.

Then he followed Sarah into the rain.

 
## Chapter 27: The Offer 

### 7.27.1
The youth centre smelled of beans on toast and floor polish.
Sarah sat cross‑legged on a plastic chair in the corner of the rec room, knees pulled up to her chest, watching the littles bounce between the pool table and the television. The telly was showing some talent show rerun; the sound was down, subtitles rolling silently across the bottom of the screen.
Lila had claimed a beanbag near the radiator and was half‑asleep, thumb in her mouth, one ear flicking every time someone raised their voice. The coughing boy—Eli, they’d finally learned, because hospitals liked to put names on bracelets—was in a ward across town, hooked up to machines that beeped and hissed. The thought made Sarah’s skin itch.
Kerry, the youth worker, moved through the room with the smooth efficiency of someone used to controlled chaos. She handed out bowls, refereed a minor argument over the PlayStation, checked inhalers. Every so often her gaze flicked to Sarah, assessing. “You sure you don’t want something hot?” she asked as she passed. “Soup? Tea?”
“I’m good,” Sarah lied. Her stomach felt hollow, but she wasn’t sure she could keep anything down without choking on it. In the doorway opposite, Miles leaned against the frame, talking quietly to Asim and a woman in a council lanyard. His ears were tipped forward, alert. He looked like he always did when dealing with head office: calm, polite, just stubborn enough that you couldn’t quite shift him.
Lillian sat on the radiator beside Juno, a mug cupped in both paws. She’d come straight from rehearsal; there was a smudge of eyeliner under one eye, her fur flattened where her glasses had rubbed. When Juno made a joke, she smiled properly, the kind that crinkled the corners of her eyes instead of pulling them thin. Seeing them both in the same room, surrounded by kids who weren’t technically theirs, felt wrong and right at once. Like someone had taken a drawing out of her sketchbook and made it three‑dimensional.
Asim clapped Miles on the shoulder and peeled off, heading towards the door with a last grin at the crowd. The council woman checked her watch and left too, promising follow‑up appointments and assessments and all the other words that meant bureaucracy.
Miles crossed the room, weaving around a cluster of boys arguing about whose turn it was on the pool table. “Hey,” he said.
Sarah shrugged one shoulder. “Hey,” she replied.
“Can we grab you for a minute?” he asked. “Me and Lils? Somewhere quieter.”
Her throat went tight. “What, like a meeting?” she said. “Do I need a lawyer?”
He huffed. “Just a chat,” he said. “We’ll bring biscuits. That usually helps.”
She hesitated, glancing at the littles. Faroukh had parked himself near Lila and was teaching one of the younger boys how to flick the cue ball without taking anyone’s eye out. “Go,” he called over. “We’ve got this. I’ll tell you if anyone tries to burn the place down.”
“They’d have to find a match first,” Lila muttered sleepily.
Sarah slid off the chair, legs unsteady, and followed Miles across the hall.
Kerry opened the door to a side room—a small office with two mismatched chairs and a desk covered in flyers. Lillian was already inside, mug steaming in front of her. Someone had lit a cheap vanilla candle; the sweetness sat oddly over the institutional smell.
“All right, love?” Lillian asked.
Sarah shrugged again.
“Define all right,” she said.
Miles closed the door gently behind them. The sudden quiet made her ears ring. Out in the rec room, the muffled sounds of kids and telly continued, a reminder that the world hadn’t stopped just because her bit had cracked.
“Sit,” Miles said, gesturing to one of the chairs. “Please.”
She perched on the very edge, muscles coiled to bolt.
Lillian and Miles exchanged a look over her head. It wasn’t one of those pitying grown‑up glances she hated; it was more like two musicians checking they were in the same key. “Okay,” Lillian said. “Here’s the thing. There’s a lot we could talk about. Council meetings. Case conferences. All that shite. But that’s not what we wanted you in here for.”
Sarah’s claws tapped a staccato rhythm on her knees. “What did you want, then?” she asked. “Because so far today folk have mostly wanted to tell me what I can’t do. Can’t stay in the warehouse. Can’t keep everyone together. Can’t punch Connor. It’s a theme.”
Miles sat down opposite her, paws loosely folded. “We wanted to talk about what you can choose,” he said.
She wanted to scoff. The word choose made her think of multiple choice quizzes and charity shop clothes: options that only looked like freedom from a distance.
Lillian must have seen something on her face, because she leaned forward, mug balanced on her knee. “You’ve spent your whole life making choices that dinnae look like choices to anyone else,” she said. “Who gets the last slice of bread. Who sleeps near the door in case the police come. When to call an ambulance and when to risk it. Those are choices too. Brave ones. Clever ones. They’ve kept folk alive.”
Compliments slid off Sarah like water most days. This one snagged. “And?” she said, wary.
“And now you’re being offered another set,” Miles said. “None of them perfect. All of them real.”
He counted them off on his fingers, like stock options. “One,” he said. “You stay here. Youth centre as your base. You help Kerry and the others. You keep being everyone’s big sister. We visit. We bring food and art supplies and shout at social workers on your behalf. You keep your independence and your distance. You don’t move in with us.”
Her chest tightened. The idea of them walking away at the end of the night, of watching them go back to their flat and their music while she curled up on a centre mattress, made something in her howl. She kept her face blank.
“Two,” Lillian said, picking up the thread. “You say, ‘Aye, all right then, I’ll give this a go.’ You come stay with us—for a bit or for longer. See what it feels like to have a room that’s yours and rules that were written for you, not against you. We talk to social about kinship care, about fostering, about all the boring legal words that add up to ‘you can keep coming back to the same front door and it’ll always open.’”
Sarah’s claws dug into her knees hard enough to hurt. “And the littles?” she asked. “They just… what? Become a youth centre problem? I can’t do that.”
“They become a youth centre responsibility,” Miles said. “With us and Asim and half the band nagging from the sidelines. That’s still better than a dripping roof and Connor at the door.”
“It’s no the same as me being there,” she said, heat flaring. “They’ll be scared.”
“They’ll be scared either way,” Lillian said softly. “Change is scary. But you know what else is scary? Watching their big sister cough herself to death because she thought she had to be a roof and a boiler and a therapist and a security system on legs.”
The words landed like a slap. Sarah flinched.
Silence stretched. The world outside the office door carried on, oblivious.
“There’s a third option,” Miles said eventually. “You leg it. You decide you don’t trust any of us, you walk out that door and disappear. Sleep on someone’s sofa, find another warehouse, join Connor, hop a train out of London. We’re not locking you in. But I’d be lying if I said the thought doesn’t scare me.”
“Why?” she demanded. “You’ve got each other. You’ve got your band. You’ll feel bad for a bit and then you’ll get on with your lives.”
Miles shook his head. “You’re in our lives,” he said. “Have been for months. Every time I walk into a warehouse now, I see the fire exits first because of you. Every time Lillian writes a set list, she thinks about whether you’ll be in the front row. You’ve bent our orbit, Sarah. That doesn’t unbend just because you decide you’re too scared to trust it.”
Lillian’s throat worked. When she spoke, her Scots came out stronger. “We want you,” she said simply. “Not as a project. Not as a wee charity case we can feel proud about at dinner parties. As our lassie. Our family. That’s the choice on the table. We cannae make it for you.”
The word family sat in the air between them, heavy as a stone and fragile as glass.
Sarah stared at her paws. The scar on her right knuckle—Connor’s ring, three years ago—caught the light. She thought of all the times she’d stood between his crew and the littles, heart slamming, telling herself that fear was just part of the job.
She thought of Bun on the floor, blood in his fur. Of the prohibition notice in her pocket. Of Kerry moving calmly through the rec room, taking some of the load without even knowing it.
She thought of the look on Lillian’s face the first time she’d seen the sketchbook—equal parts awe and fury that anyone had made this girl feel like she had to draw in secret.
She thought of the van, the crates, the way Miles always counted heads before he left, even when he pretended he wasn’t.
“What if I say yes,” she said slowly, “and then I… can’t? I freak out. I nick stuff. I scream. I run away. You kick me out. Then what?”
“Then we deal with it,” Miles said. “We put things back on shelves. We say ‘that wasn’t all right’ and ‘you can’t do that’ and ‘we still love you’ in the same breath. We get help. We do family things. Messy, annoying, boring family things.”
“You can’t promise you’ll no kick me out,” she said, voice trembling. “Nobody can promise that.”
Lillian’s eyes shone. “You’re right,” she said. “We cannae promise no’ to make mistakes. But we can promise this much: if things got that bad, if we genuinely couldnae keep you safe or each other safe under the same roof, we’d still be there. At your reviews. At your gigs. At your shite student flats. We’d be the folk the social worker rolls their eyes about because we never bugger off. We’re no going anywhere, even if where you live changes.”
The idea of someone sticking around even after things went wrong was so alien it made Sarah feel dizzy. She curled tighter on the chair, breathing in shallow bursts. “I don’t know how to be someone’s kid,” she whispered. “I only know how to be someone’s mum.”
“Good,” Miles said. “Half of parenting is just remembering what you needed when you were younger and trying not to be a dick about it. You’ll be excellent at lobbying for your own rights.”
Lillian snorted wetly. “You dinnae have to shrink to fit,” she added. “We like that you’re mouthy. That you’re stubborn. That you don’t let folk talk shite about you or the kids. We’re no asking you to turn into some weird Stepford daughter. We’re asking if you’ll let us be your backup for once.”
Something in Sarah cracked then—not the violent shatter she’d expected, but a slow, reluctant giving way, like ice underfoot softening into water.
She saw, for a flicker, a life that wasn’t this. A bedroom with posters on the wall that weren’t flapping because of a draught. A key she didn’t have to hide. A table with more than one chair where food turned up at the same time every day. Arguments that ended with someone storming into another room and then, eventually, coming back.
She saw, with equal clarity, all the ways it could hurt. The first time Lillian lost her temper and said something in Scots that cut too deep. The first time Miles put his job before her crisis. The first time a social worker asked her to list her traumas like they were shopping.
And under all of it, stubborn and bright as a pilot light, the simple fact that they were here. That they were asking, not assuming. That they’d given her three options and meant all of them. “If I say yes,” she said, barely audible, “can I still see them? The littles. Faroukh. Bun. I can’t pretend they don’t exist.”
“Of course,” Miles said. “We’ll be the annoying parents who always have extra plates out. Kerry will get sick of seeing our faces. We’ll build in visits and sleepovers and Sunday dinners, whatever that looks like.”
“And you’ll no get pissed off if I’m… difficult?” she pushed.
Lillian’s mouth twitched. “Oh, we’ll get pissed off,” she said. “That’s family. But we’ll tell you why. And we’ll expect you to tell us when we’re being eejits. That’s part of the deal too.”
Silence again. The candle flickered, scent sickly sweet.
Sarah took a breath that felt like it scraped her lungs on the way in. “Okay,” she said.
Neither of them moved, as if they were afraid of breaking the spell.
“Okay what?” Miles asked gently. “I’m a man of forms, you know. I need the full sentence.”
She glared at him, eyes wet. “You’re a pain,” she said. Then, very quietly: “Okay. I’ll… I’ll come. To yours. Try it. See if I can… be… that.”
Lillian’s paw flew to her mouth. Her glasses fogged.
“Right,” Miles said, voice thick. “Right. Good. Excellent. We can work with that.” He stood up too fast, chair scraping. “We’ll need to tell Kerry,” he said, half to himself. “And the social worker. And we should get you a bag that isn’t held together with string. And keys. And a proper coat. And—”
Lillian reached out and caught his paw. “Breathe, love,” she said. “One thing at a time.” She turned back to Sarah. “You can change your mind,” she said. “I want you to hear that as well. If you come and it’s too much, if you need to slow down, that’s allowed. Saying yes now isn’t signing something in blood. It’s just us starting.”
Sarah nodded, dazed. “I don’t think I’ll change it,” she said. “If I run, it’ll be because I panic. No because I don’t want to be there.”
“We’ll try to tell the difference,” Miles said.
They left the office together. The noise of the rec room crashed over her—laughter, game sounds, the clatter of cutlery. For a moment she wanted to spin round and run back into the quiet. Instead she walked across the room on legs that felt like they belonged to someone else.
“Oi,” Faroukh called as she approached. “You look like you’ve been told off.”
“Kind of the opposite,” she said. “I’m… going with them. To their flat.”
Lila sat up so fast she nearly toppled off the beanbag.
“You’re getting adopted?” she gasped.
Heat rushed to Sarah’s face. “It’s no that simple,” she said. “There’s forms and hearings and all sorts. But I’m going. For now.”
Lila’s eyes filled. “You’re leaving us,” she whispered.
The words hit harder than any of Connor’s digs. Sarah dropped to her knees, taking Lila’s paws in hers. “I’m not leaving you,” she said fiercely. “I’m changing how I look after you. You’ll have folk here who get paid to worry. I’ll still be around, you ok? I’ll come for dinner. I’ll buy you shoes that don’t leak. You’re no getting rid of me that easy.”
Lila sniffed. “Promise?” she asked.
Sarah swallowed past the lump in her throat. “I promise,” she said. “Cross my heart, hope… well, you know.” She couldn’t quite bring herself to say die. Not tonight.
Faroukh stared at her. “See,” he said finally, forcing a grin. “Told you you’d end up somewhere posh. You’ll come back sounding all BBC.”
“Aye, right,” she said. “First time I say ‘bath’ instead of ‘baff’ you can slap me.”
He pulled her into a hug before she could brace for it, arms tight around her shoulders. She froze, then hugged back, burying her nose in his hoodie. It smelled like damp and cheap detergent and something that was just him. “Don’t let them make you soft,” he muttered in her ear. “Or if they do, make sure it’s the kind of soft that can still bite.”
She laughed, shaky. “You’re a poet now?” she said.
“Shut up,” he said, voice thick.
Fifteen minutes later, she was in the back of Miles and Lillian’s van, bag at her feet. The centre’s fluorescent lights fell away behind them as they pulled out into the wet night.
London slid past in blurred streaks—shops closing up, buses hissing, umbrellas bobbing down pavements. She watched the reflections in the window more than the streets themselves. Three figures in the glass: big dog driving, badger in the passenger seat, golden retriever huddled between sacks of sleeping bags and crates of donated cereal.
Halfway home, Lillian twisted in her seat, reaching back with one paw.
“Here,” she said. “Hold this.”
It was a key. Plain, brass, with a little strip of blue tape round the top.
Sarah stared at it. “You’re just… giving me it?” she asked.
“Aye,” Lillian said. “It’s only a bit of metal. But it means you don’t have to knock like you’re begging every time you come to the door. You live there, you get a key. That’s the rule.”
Sarah closed her paw around it. The edges dug into her pads, real and solid.
“If you change your mind,” Miles added, eyes on the road, “you don’t have to hand it back in a dramatic scene. You can keep it. We want you to know you’ve always got at least one door in this city that’ll open.”
Something in her chest loosened, painfully.
When they reached the flat, the stairwell smelled of someone’s curry and someone else’s laundry powder. A string of fairy lights that definitely didn’t meet any fire regulation hung along the banister, left over from Christmas.
“Right,” Lillian said at the top of the stairs, slightly out of breath. “It does the job.”
Inside, the flat was exactly as she remembered and completely different. Same battered sofa, same music stands, same fridge covered in gig posters and student drawings. But there was a third pair of hooks by the door now, empty. A third mug on the draining board. A new blanket folded over the back of the chair in the corner, patterned with stars. “That one’s yours,” Lillian said casually, nodding at the blanket. “If you want it. We can get another. It just… looked like you.”
Sarah ran her paw over the fabric. It was softer than anything she owned.
Lillian showed her the small bedroom at the end of the hall—the one that had been a dumping ground last time. The boxes were gone. In their place: a bed with a proper mattress, a chest of drawers, a desk. An empty corkboard on the wall, waiting.
“We didn’t want to presume too much,” Lillian said, suddenly shy. “Thought you might want to stick your own stuff up. Drawings. Gig tickets. Shite postcards.”
Sarah stepped inside on legs that didn’t feel like they belonged to her. The room smelled faintly of fresh paint and laundry. The window looked out over the communal garden, where a few stubborn plants clung to life in plastic tubs. On the desk, someone had put a sketchbook and a tin of pencils. The sight of them made her throat close. “You can’t just… have this ready,” she said hoarsely. “Like you knew.”
Miles leaned against the doorframe, hands in his pockets. “We didn’t know,” he said. “We hoped.”
She sat on the edge of the bed. The mattress dipped, springy. Her whole body, used to concrete and thin foam, misread it as sinking for a split second. She grabbed the duvet, heart thumping, then laughed at herself. “I’m going to fall out of this,” she said. “It’s too soft.”
“We’ll put pillows on the floor,” Lillian said. “Make it a health and safety compliant fall.”
Sarah looked at them both. At the bed. At the key in her paw. “This feels like stealing,” she whispered.
“It isn’t,” Miles said. “It’s redistribution.”
Lillian snorted. “Welcome home, hen,” she said.
The words hit harder than any speech.
Later, when the flat had quietened, when mugs had been washed and pyjamas found and teeth brushed, Sarah lay on her back staring at the ceiling. The hum of the fridge down the hall, the distant rush of traffic, the murmur of Lillian and Miles talking quietly in the living room—they all blended into a strange new lullaby. Under the window, on the sill, a small terracotta pot sat with dark soil pressed smooth. Lillian had brought it in from the tiny shared garden that afternoon, explaining that she and a friend were going to plant bulbs soon, “for reasons.”
Sarah didn’t know what would grow there—tulips, daffodils, something she couldn’t name. Right now it was just a pot of earth, waiting. She rolled onto her side and reached out, resting her paw lightly on the cool ceramic.
Tomorrow there would be social workers and forms and arguments and probably at least one panic attack. There would be gigs and meetings and fights with Miles about curfews. There would be visits to the youth centre where littles clung to her hoodie and asked when she was coming back. There would be courtrooms and judges who needed convincing that an anthro couple and the girl from the warehouse could be a family on purpose.
Tonight there was this: clean sheets, a key on the bedside table, the murmur of two adults who had chosen her every time they’d been given the chance.
She closed her eyes.
“Okay,” she whispered to the dark. “Okay. I’m in.”

### 7.27.2
Faroukh showed up at the youth centre an hour after Sarah left.

He came in dripping rain, hood up, eyes bright with the kind of anger that was mostly fear.

Kerry clocked him immediately. “You here to start trouble or eat toast?” she asked.

Faroukh blinked. “What?”

“Toast,” Kerry repeated. “We’ve got a kettle and a loaf and I don’t have time for teenage melodrama unless it comes with a signed consent form.”

Faroukh’s mouth twitched despite himself.

Sarah looked up from where she’d been sitting with Lila curled against her side.

For a second, relief hit her so hard she felt sick.

Faroukh saw it.

His jaw tightened, defensive even now. “Don’t look at me like that,” he muttered.

“Like what?” Sarah asked.

“Like you thought I’d leave,” he snapped.

Sarah swallowed. “I did,” she admitted. “That’s why I’m looking.”

Faroukh’s shoulders rose and fell once, as if he was shaking off something wet. “I’m not staying in the warehouse,” he said. “And I’m not going back to my family. And I’m not going back to him.”

Sarah’s ears pricked. “Him,” she repeated.

Faroukh’s eyes flicked away. “The benefactor,” he said, as if saying the word made it less personal. “It was never free. You were right. It was always… strings.”

Sarah nodded slowly.

“He kept saying I was special,” Faroukh muttered. “That I was smart. That I deserved better than the warehouse. And then he’d—” He cut himself off, throat working. “He’d put his hand on my neck like he was checking a dog’s collar and tell me to be grateful.”

Sarah’s claws flexed.

“And your family?” she asked quietly.

Faroukh laughed, harsh. “My family are just a different kind of string,” he said. “You do what we want, you get fed. You behave, you get to stay. You don’t talk about what your uncle did. You don’t tell anyone why your mum drinks. You don’t say you’re scared.” He swallowed. “I’m sick of it.”

Sarah stared at him.

“So,” Faroukh said, voice smaller, “I’m staying here. For now. I’ll… defer them.”

“Defer?” Sarah repeated.

Faroukh shrugged, irritated at his own vocabulary. “Delay. Avoid. Whatever. I’m not dealing with them until I’m not… like this.”

Sarah nodded once. “Okay,” she said.

Faroukh shifted his weight. “You’re still going with them?” he asked.

Sarah’s throat tightened. “Yeah,” she said. “I think so.”

Faroukh’s mouth twisted. “Figures,” he muttered.

Sarah watched him carefully. “You can come too,” she said.

Faroukh’s eyes snapped up.

“Don’t,” he said.

“I’m serious,” Sarah replied. “They’ll take you. If you want.”

Faroukh stared at her like she’d offered him a planet.

“I’m not your little,” he said, and his voice broke on the last word.

Sarah’s chest burned.

“I know,” she said quietly. “You’re my friend.”

Faroukh’s jaw clenched hard.

Then he nodded once.

“Toast,” he said gruffly.

Kerry shoved a plate into his hands without ceremony. “Welcome to civilisation,” she said. “Don’t nick the cutlery.”

Faroukh snorted.

Sarah watched him eat, the simple, ordinary act of it making her eyes sting.

For a minute, the world wasn’t warehouses and Connor and blood.

It was bread and heat and a boy choosing, for once, not to be alone.
 
## Chapter 28: Benefit Confrontation 

### 7.28.1
Juno had never been so aware of her own hands.

Backstage, the hall smelled of hairspray, damp coats, and the bitter edge of nerves. The muffled thump of the crowd came through the thin wall like a second heartbeat.

Juno sat on a folding chair with her flute case open on her lap, fingers moving over the keys without fully pressing them, mapping a shape she already knew.

Lillian crouched in front of her, close enough to be felt, not close enough to trap.

“You dinnae have to do this,” Lillian said.

Juno looked up. “I do,” she said, surprised at her own steadiness. “I want to.”

Lillian’s eyes softened. “All right,” she said. “Then we make it safe.”

Juno swallowed. “How?”

Lillian ticked it off like a lesson plan. “One: you walk on with me. Nobody’s announcing you like a circus act. Two: you play one tune. One. Not the whole set. Three: if you freeze, I’ll take it. I’ll sing. I’ll cover you. No shame.”

Juno’s throat tightened. “And if she’s there?”

Lillian’s mouth went hard. “Then she can listen like everyone else,” she said. “She doesn’t get to come backstage. She doesn’t get to touch you. She doesn’t get to make a scene that becomes your job to clean up.”

Juno nodded, the words settling.

Asim’s voice called from the doorway. “Two minutes, ladies. Felix has done his traditional pre‑gig sulk and Miles has done his traditional pre‑gig hydration lecture. We’re ready.”

Felix stuck his head in behind him. “I’m not sulking,” he said.

“You are literally a sulk with a bass guitar,” Asim replied.

Juno huffed a shaky laugh.

When they walked onto the stage, the lights hit her like warmth and exposure at once.

The crowd was bigger than she’d expected.

Not huge.

But present.

People packed into folding chairs, standing along the walls, hands wrapped round plastic cups. There were Strays from the youth centre in the back, eyes bright, bodies restless. There were teachers and nurses and exhausted parents. There were older anthros with soft faces and hard eyes.

Outside, even through the thick windows, Juno could hear the faint, distant roar of protesters.

Lillian spoke into the mic, calm and fierce.

“Thanks for coming,” she said. “Thanks for putting money where your mouth is. We’ve all read enough headlines about feral kids. Tonight we’re doing the boring work instead. Food. Blankets. Legal help. Being present.”

Applause.

Juno’s chest tightened.

Asim leaned into his mic with a grin. “And if you’re one of the people outside shouting, you’re welcome to donate too,” he said. “We take cash and existential guilt.”

The room laughed.

The band started.

Juno stayed at the side for the first songs, half in shadow, listening.

Then Lillian turned slightly and nodded.

Juno stepped forward.

Asim announced the tune.

“Rocky Road to Dublin,” he said. “Because sometimes the road is rocky and the only reasonable response is to play it faster.”

Juno lifted the flute.

Her breath went in.

The first note rang out—clear, bright, undeniable.

The tune caught and ran.

Juno played as if she were chasing herself back into her own body.

The room clapped along, off‑beat at first, then finding the pulse.

She didn’t freeze.

She didn’t disappear.

When she hit the final phrase and landed it clean, the applause rose like heat.

Juno’s eyes stung.

She lowered the flute.

She bowed.

And for a second she felt, impossibly, like she belonged.

## 7.28.2

Juno saw her mother in the crowd before the song had even finished.

Not because she was loud.

Because Juno’s body recognised her the way it recognised pain.

Her mum stood near the back, half in shadow, hair pulled tight, coat too nice for the venue. Her face was unreadable.

Juno’s hands went cold.

She kept playing.

She forced herself through the last bar.

When the applause hit, it felt like surf.

Juno stepped back behind the mic stands, heart hammering.

Lillian’s hand brushed her shoulder, steadying.

“You saw her,” Lillian murmured.

Juno nodded, throat too tight for words.

“Alright,” Lillian said, voice low. “You don’t have to do anything.”

But Juno’s feet were already moving.

Not toward her mother.

Toward the exit.

She needed air.

In the corridor outside the hall, the sound of the crowd dulled. The walls smelled of stale smoke and floor polish.

Juno leaned against the wall and tried to breathe.

Footsteps.

“Juno.”

Her mother’s voice.

Juno’s stomach flipped.

She turned.

Her mum stood two metres away.

Close enough to reach.

Not close enough yet.

“What are you doing here?” Juno managed.

Her mother’s eyes flicked over her face, her clothes, the flute case. “I came to see you,” she said, as if it were obvious. “You’ve been ignoring my calls.”

Juno’s jaw clenched. “Yes,” she said.

Her mother’s expression tightened. “You can’t ignore family,” she said.

Juno felt the old script rise—apologise, soothe, make it smaller.

She swallowed it.

“I can,” she said. “I have.”

Her mother’s mouth opened in shock, then snapped shut.

“After everything I’ve done for you,” her mother said.

Juno’s hands trembled.

“After everything you did to me,” Juno said.

The words came out calm.

That was the most frightening part.

Her mother’s face flushed. “Watch your mouth,” she hissed.

“No,” Juno said. “You don’t get to correct me anymore.”

Her mother took a step forward.

Juno’s body flinched.

She didn’t move back.

Behind her, the door opened.

Lillian stepped out, followed by Miles, then Kerry from the youth centre.

Lillian took one look at Juno’s face and positioned herself beside her without touching.

Miles’s ears angled forward, alert.

Kerry’s gaze flicked between mother and daughter and hardened.

“Can I help?” Kerry asked, tone flat.

“This is family business,” Juno’s mother snapped.

“It’s happening in my hallway,” Kerry replied. “So it’s my business.”

Juno’s mother’s eyes flicked to Lillian.

Recognition.

Contempt.

“Oh,” she said slowly. “Of course. You’ve found yourself a little gang.”

Juno’s chest tightened.

“They’re my friends,” Juno said.

Her mother laughed, short and ugly. “Friends,” she repeated. “They’ll leave. Everyone leaves. Come home.”

Juno stared at her.

For a moment she saw her own childhood in her mother’s face—fear disguised as authority.

She didn’t have to fix it.

“No,” Juno said.

The word was clean.

Her mother’s eyes widened. “Juno—”

“No,” Juno repeated. “You don’t get to come here and claim me because you heard me play and decided I’m worth something again.”

Her mother’s face twisted. “I am your mother.”

“And you were cruel,” Juno said.

Silence.

Then her mother’s mouth tightened into something hard. “You’re embarrassing yourself,” she spat.

Juno felt the old shame reach for her.

Lillian’s voice cut in, quiet but steel‑edged. “She’s not embarrassing herself,” she said. “You are.”

Juno’s mother turned on her. “Who the hell are you?”

“Someone who believes her,” Lillian replied.

Kerry stepped forward. “Right,” she said. “That’s enough. You need to leave the building.”

Juno’s mother scoffed. “Or what?”

Kerry gestured to the door where two security lads—borrowed from the pub downstairs, bored and built like wardrobes—had appeared, alerted by the raised voices.

“Or you’ll be removed,” Kerry said. “And if you come back and harass our performers, I’ll call the police. This is a youth benefit, not a family therapy session.”

Juno’s mother stared, chest heaving.

For a second, Juno thought she might lunge.

Instead her mother’s mouth curled. “Fine,” she said. “Stay with your little charity cases.”

She turned sharply and walked toward the stairs.

The security lads followed at a polite distance.
