When she was gone, the corridor felt suddenly too bright.

Juno’s knees went weak.

She leaned against the wall.

Lillian’s hand hovered near her elbow. “You okay?” she asked.

Juno’s throat worked. “I don’t know,” she admitted.

Miles nodded once. “That’s alright,” he said. “You held the boundary. The rest is… aftermath.”

Juno pressed her forehead briefly to the wall, breathing.

Then she lifted her head.

“Can we go back in?” she asked.

Lillian’s eyes warmed. “Aye,” she said. “We’ll go back in.”

They returned to the hall.

The music started again.

 
## Chapter 29: Moving Day 

### 7.29.1
Sarah stood in the narrow hallway, still smelling of fresh paint, and tried to map it. Front door, stairs straight ahead, living room to the left, kitchen at the back. The kind of layout you saw in estate agent windows, only this one had scuffed skirting boards and a patch of wallpaper in the hall where a previous child had clearly measured their height in biro lines.
 “Mind the box,” Miles called from behind her.
She jumped aside just in time as he squeezed past with a crate of mugs balanced precariously in his paws.
“Put them in the kitchen?” she asked.
“That’s the plan.”
“I thought the plan was thirty‑seven pages,” Lillian said, following him in with a laundry basket full of pans. “This looks suspiciously like improvisation.”
“The plan is pages one to thirty‑seven,” Miles said. “This is page thirty‑eight: we’re in it now, it’s chaos, we adapt.”
Sarah stepped out of the way, back pressed to the wall, and watched them move.
Miles’ movements were precise even with the box. He’d plotted the route in his head: van to door, door to kitchen, kitchen to cupboards he’d already measured. Lillian was less linear, bouncing between rooms with an armful of cushions one minute and a box of books the next, commenting aloud so she didn’t forget what she was doing.
“Kettle first,” she said now. “No civilisation until the kettle’s on.”
“The kettle is in Box D,” Miles said.
“Which is where?”
He frowned. “Somewhere.”
Sarah grinned despite herself. “That’s not very engineer of you.”
He gave her a mock‑stern look. “It’s in the system. The system is just… temporarily distributed.”
She padded through into the living room.
The floorboards were bare for now, dust and paint flecks catching the light. The big front window looked out onto the street where the van was parked, its hazard lights blinking. A radiator sat under the sill, clunking as it warmed. The walls had been painted a soft off‑white that Lillian had insisted on, standing in the shop with a fan of colour swatches and saying, “I am not living inside magnolia. I refuse.”
“What do you think?” Lillian asked, appearing at her shoulder.
Sarah shrugged, but the shrug came out more like a shiver. “Big.”
“It’s not that big,” Lillian said. “Just bigger than the flat. Bigger than a warehouse.”
The last word landed between them.
Sarah had thought about this house from the moment she’d seen the floor plans spread across the kitchen table months ago. Square rooms, real walls, doors that shut properly. She’d traced her finger along the lines and tried not to picture beds in every room, kids tucked into corners, the constant calculation of safety and distance.
Now the rooms were right there, echoing and waiting.
“And your room…” Lillian’s face softened. “You want to see it again or wait until we’ve got the bed in?”
“Now,” Sarah said, surprising herself with how quickly the answer came. “I’ll help with the bed after.”
Upstairs, the landing creaked faintly under her paws.
Her room was at the back, overlooking the small garden where the memorial bed lay dark and still. They’d come to the allotment earlier in the week to lift some of the bulbs, splitting clumps and re‑planting half in the new soil here, a bridge between spaces.
Now the garden looked like a sketch in pencil, lines waiting for colour.
The room itself held only a stripped mattress on the floor and a couple of boxes. The walls were freshly painted, a muted blue that made the white of the skirting boards look sharper.
“We can put the bed along that wall,” Miles said, coming up behind them with a tape measure hanging from his belt. “Desk under the window. Shelves there.”
“No bed under the window?” Sarah asked. Old instincts.
Miles caught the flicker. “We can do either,” he said. “If it feels safer away from the window, we’ll do that. The burglar alarm’s fitted but we can put extra locks on the window if you want.”
She listened for the assumption under his words. There wasn’t one. Just options.
“Desk under the window,” she said. “Bed there.”
He nodded as if she’d chosen something important and started measuring.
Lillian set the laundry basket down on the mattress and pulled out the duvet cover she’d bought. It was soft cotton with a print of abstract shapes that could be flowers or clouds or both, not babyish, not too grown‑up. “You sure you don’t want posters on the walls?” she asked. “We can leave room for them.”
“Later,” Sarah said. “I want to… see it blank first.”
The blankness was its own kind of promise. No damp patches. No cracks. No place where someone else’s temper had left a mark. She helped them carry the bedframe up, piece by piece. Her muscles burned; she welcomed the ache. It was a clean kind of tired, the kind you got from lifting wood and metal instead of smaller bodies and bags of tins.
They argued about the best way to assemble the frame. Miles wanted to follow the instructions precisely. Lillian wanted to trust “common sense” until they nearly pinned a slat in the wrong place. Sarah read the leaflet and translated when they both got stuck.
“See?” she said, pointing. “This bit goes there, then that one slots over.”
“Engineering gene’s strong in this one,” Lillian muttered.
“I learned from you two arguing over flat‑pack bookshelves,” Sarah said.
By early afternoon, the bed was up, mattress in place, duvet thrown over in a rough approximation of neat. The desk was still flat in its box on the floor. One of the boxes labelled CLOTHES sat open, socks already spilling out.
“We’ll do the desk tomorrow,” Miles said, leaning against the door frame. “My shoulders are staging a rebellion.”
“We should feed you before they strike altogether,” Lillian said. “Sandwiches?”
Down in the kitchen, boxes towered like unstable city blocks. The kettle had finally been found and reconnected; it hummed on the counter, a reassuring domestic noise. Sarah made sandwiches with supermarket bread and whatever fillings turned up first: cheese, hummus, sliced carrots. She lined the plates up on the bare worktop, the rhythm of it calming.
“I can do that,” Lillian protested.
“You’re carrying plates,” Sarah said. “Go sit down. It’s my turn.”
They ate sitting on the living room floor, backs against the walls, legs stretched out. The radiator clicked and settled. Cars went past outside, tyres swishing on wet tarmac. Somewhere a baby cried, then was soothed.
“Tomorrow,” Miles said between bites, “we bring over the rest of the books. And the drum kit. And maybe people.”
“People?” Sarah asked warily.
“Soft launch,” Lillian said. “Juno’s desperate to show us her new clarinet. Milla wants to check we’ve done something sensible with the piano. Asim has volunteered to carry anything that weighs more than him, which is most of the furniture.”
The thought of all those bodies in the house made Sarah’s shoulders rise involuntarily. Noise, questions, hands on walls.
Lillian noticed. Of course she did.
“We can set boundaries,” she said. “No bedroom tours unless you say. No dropping by without texting first. No leaving with our mugs.”
Miles nodded. “I’ll put a sign on the fridge. ‘Guests will be politely removed at ten p.m.’”
Sarah snorted. “You’d do it too.”
“Gently,” he said. “With spreadsheets.”
They were halfway through washing up when the doorbell rang.
Sarah froze, hands in the bubbly water. For a moment she was back in the warehouse: pound of boots on concrete, older voices, younger children pressing in behind her. Fight or flight slammed into her chest.
“It’s okay,” Lillian said quietly. “We’re expecting them.”
Were they? Sarah couldn’t remember. Plans blurred: who was coming when, what day the van was booked, when the gas man was due.
Miles wiped his paws on the tea towel and went to answer.
Voices in the hall. Laughter. Juno’s high, excited tone; Asim’s lower rumble; the scrape of something heavy being shuffled through the doorway.
“Housewarming brigade,” Lillian said. “Brace yourself.”
Sarah dried her paws and followed her into the hall.
Juno stood just inside the door, arms wrapped round a potted plant so large it obscured half her face. Its leaves spilled over the sides, shiny and green.
“It’s a monstera,” she said breathlessly. “It’ll make everything look like an album cover.”
“It’ll make everything look like a jungle,” Miles said. “Where did you even—”
“Charity shop,” Asim said from behind her, carrying one end of a second‑hand sofa. “Apparently it ‘didn’t fit their aesthetic’.”
“Our aesthetic is ‘we have somewhere to sit’,” Lillian said. “Get it in the living room before we change our minds.”
The house filled up quickly.
Milla brought cushions and a lasagne that she insisted was “mostly vegetables, don’t panic”. Jonathan turned up later with a camera and a bottle of cheap prosecco he’d been given as a thank‑you by a client and had no intention of drinking himself.
They argued over where to put the sofa, then argued over where to put the rug in relation to the sofa, then argued over where the amp should go so that Sarah would have light for drawing and Juno would have the right acoustics.
Sarah hovered at the edges, heart at the back of her throat, waiting for the moment it tipped from “helpful chaos” into “too much”. Old habits counted exits, calculated lines of sight. New habits tried to overlay those maps with softer ones: who had keys, who would listen if she said stop.
“You okay?” Asim asked quietly at one point, catching her eye over the back of the sofa.
She considered lying. “It’s loud,” she said instead.
“Yeah,” he said. “Want a breather?”
He nodded towards the back door. She followed him out into the small garden. The air was damp and cool. The memorial bed sat along one fence, soil darker where the new bulbs had gone in. The rest of the garden was a rectangle of grass and bare earth, with an elderly washing line pole in the middle.
“I like it,” Asim said. “It looks like potential.”
Sarah shoved her paws into her pockets. “It looks small.”
“You’re used to industrial scale.”
“You make it sound fancy.”
“It is, in its way,” he said. “Not many people could run a place like that as long as you did. You did logistics before any of us knew where the warehouses even were.” She shrugged, uncomfortable. “Hey,” he said gently. “This doesn’t have to be a community centre from day one. It can just be your house. Our meetings can stay at the youth centre. Kids can visit when you say yes, not because there’s nowhere else.”
“They’re going to need somewhere,” she said. “If the council close the shelter in Dalston…”
“Then we fight that,” Asim said. “With petitions and meetings and extremely boring speeches at committee. Not by letting your living room become a dormitory unless you actually want it to.”
She looked at the dark soil of the memorial bed. Somewhere under there, bulbs waited. For light, for warmth, for the right time.
“I don’t know what I want,” she admitted.
“That’s allowed,” he said. “Wanting is a skill. You’re out of practice.”
He bumped her shoulder lightly with his. “Come on,” he said. “If we leave them unsupervised with that sofa any longer, Miles will invent a spreadsheet for cushion placement.”
They went back in.
The evening blurred into snapshots: Lillian and Milla arguing about where to put the lamp; Juno curled on the new sofa with her clarinet across her lap, testing the room’s sound; Jonathan taking candid photos of mugs on windowsills and feet on floorboards.
At some point, Sarah fell asleep on the sofa under a spare blanket, the murmur of voices like distant traffic.
She woke to darkness and the sound of rain on the windows.
The house was quiet now. The guests had gone. The boxes were still there, but somehow fewer. A lamp cast a warm pool of light in the corner of the room. The clock on the mantelpiece — when had they put a clock there? — said eleven.
She lay for a moment, disoriented, then sat up.
“Hey,” Miles’ voice said from the doorway. “You okay?”
He stood there in his T‑shirt and jeans, paws still faintly paint‑stained. His ears were tilted in that way they had when he was trying not to startle anyone.
“Yeah,” she said. Her mouth tasted of sleep. “What time is it?”
“Late enough.” He stepped into the room. “We were going to wake you and suggest bed, but you looked… settled.”
She pulled the blanket tighter around her shoulders. “Where’s Lillian?”
“Upstairs. She crashed about half an hour ago. Paint fumes and people have defeated her.”
Sarah swung her feet to the floor. The wood was cool under her pads.
“Come on,” Miles said. “Let’s get you into your room. First night in your own bed.”
The words made something lurch inside her. Her own bed.
They climbed the stairs in companionable silence. At the top, the landing light cast soft shadows. Her bedroom door was ajar.
Inside, the bed waited, duvet slightly rumpled from where they’d sat earlier. Her bag sat in the corner, contents half‑spilled. The desk was still in pieces. One of the memorial stones from the allotment sat on the windowsill, white letters catching the lamplight: FOR WHO IS STILL COMING HOME.
She stepped inside and stopped.
Her chest tightened, sudden and sharp. The room felt too exposed, too quiet. No breathing from other beds, no rustle of bodies. Just her.
“I can… sleep on the sofa,” she said. The words came out faster than she meant. “It’s fine. I’m used to noise.”
Miles leaned on the door frame, not blocking the exit.
“You can,” he said. “But you don’t have to. We can leave your door open. Ours too. We can put a radio on low if that helps. Whatever makes it easier.”
She swallowed. Her eyes stung suddenly. “What if I…” She couldn’t quite say it. Wake up screaming. Wake up convinced the roof’s gone. Wake up and bolt.
“If you need us,” he said quietly, “you shout. We come. That’s the deal.”
The promise settled heavy and warm.
She nodded. “Okay.”
He stepped back. “I’ll be in the next room doing battle with a box of screws if you need a distraction,” he said. “Sleep well, Sarah.”
He left the door half open.
She got into bed.
The sheets were stiff with newness. The pillow smelled faintly of laundry powder and their flat. She lay on her back and stared at the ceiling, tracing the faint pattern of the plaster. Her ears tracked sounds: the distant rush of cars, the creak of the boiler, Miles humming tunelessly in the next room as he swore under his breath at the desk instructions.
For a while, sleep didn’t come. Her mind flicked between then and now, between concrete and floorboards.
A car went past outside, headlights sweeping across the ceiling. The shadows shifted. Panic surged, bright and sharp. Her body moved before her mind did.
“M—” she started, then stopped herself. Old habits. Old names. Her voice came out louder than she meant, ripping the quiet. “Mum! Dad!”
Silence. Then the thud of feet.
Lillian appeared in the doorway first, hair flattened on one side, T‑shirt askew, glasses forgotten. Miles behind her, one paw still holding a screwdriver. “What? What is it?” Lillian demanded, scanning the room for danger.
Sarah’s heart hammered. The shadows on the ceiling were just shadows again. Her breath came in shaky gasps.
“Sorry,” she blurted. “I… the car lights. It looked like— I thought the roof…”
Words tangled. Shame flooded in hot and fast. She’d shouted. She’d used—
Lillian came to the side of the bed and sat down heavily, mattress dipping. “It’s okay,” she said. “You did the right thing.”
Sarah stared at her. “I woke you up.”
“Aye. That’s what you do when you’re scared.” Lillian reached out, hesitated, then rested a paw lightly on Sarah’s ankle over the duvet. “You shout for your parents.”
The word hung there. Parents. She hadn’t meant to say it. Hadn’t planned it. It had come from somewhere deeper than words.
Miles leaned against the door frame, breathing a little harder from the sprint. His expression softened as what she’d shouted registered. “Lights from cars look different here,” he said. “We can get blackout curtains if that’d help. Or stick some cardboard up temporarily like students.”
“Very glamorous,” Lillian said.
“Student chic,” he said. “All the best people have done it.”
Sarah’s breathing slowed, the humour threading through the fear. The room settled around her again, angles familiar.
“Do you want one of us to sit with you until you drop off?” Lillian asked. “We can take turns reading incredibly boring council reports aloud.”
“Asim would be hurt if he heard you call his work boring,” Miles murmured.
“He can come and perform it himself if he wants.”
Sarah shook her head, a small movement against the pillow. “I think… I think I’m okay now,” she said. “Thanks.”
Lillian squeezed her ankle once more and stood up, joints clicking.
“Right,” she said. “We’re just there.” She nodded towards their room. “You shout if shadows misbehave again, all right?”
“Okay.”
They hovered a moment longer, then retreated, leaving the door open a little wider.
Sarah lay in the half‑dark, heart still beating fast but not out of control. The word she’d shouted replayed in her head, as startling as the headlights.
Mum. Dad.
She hadn’t meant to give it away. It had slipped out like breath.
On the windowsill, the stone caught a glimmer of streetlight.
FOR WHO IS STILL COMING HOME.
She turned onto her side, pulled the duvet up to her chin, and let the house’s new sounds fold round her.
This time, when sleep came, it brought no warehouses with it.


 
## Chapter 30: Planting grief | 3,000 words  

### 7.30.1
The soil was colder than it looked.
Milla pressed her fingers into it and hissed a breath between her teeth. March light lay thin over the allotment plots, turning every metal surface to dull pewter. Somewhere a dog barked; somewhere nearer, a train rattled over points.
“You all right?” Lillian asked.
“Fine,” Milla said automatically. Her fingers cramped. She flexed them, then curled them round the trowel again. “Just forgot gloves.”
Lillian raised one eyebrow. “You didn’t forget. You said you needed to feel it. ‘I need to feel it in my fingers,’ you said.”
Milla made a face. “Don’t quote me at me.”
“You’re the one that talks in poetry when you’re knackered.”
The raised bed in front of them was new wood, pale and raw, corners braced with silver brackets Miles had insisted on. Behind it, the rest of the allotment was a patchwork of other people’s lives: rhubarb crowns forcing through, last year’s bean poles leaning, a row of tiny cabbage seedlings under fleece. The memorial bed sat close to the back fence, where the ground dipped slightly and the earth held moisture.
“He’s gone a bit overboard,” Milla said, nudging one of the corner posts with her boot.
“Miles?” Lillian shrugged. “He’s an engineer. This is romantic for him.”
“Bolts and brackets?”
“Aye. Means it’ll outlast us.”
The thought settled between them: this bed might stand when they were gone, when the bulbs inside had bloomed and died and been divided ten times over. It made the empty soil feel deeper.
Lillian knelt carefully on the foam pad Miles had insisted on, joints clicking. Milla heard the little intake of breath and pretended not to. Lillian set a sack of bulbs between them — daffodils, tulips, bluebells — all pale oniony curves and little tufts of dried root.
“Right,” Lillian said. “Plan?”
Milla swallowed.
She hadn’t slept much. Her last appointment with the therapist had left her crying in the Tube toilets afterwards, furious at herself for the salt on her fur. It had taken three hours to eat dinner the night before, spoonful by spoonful, with Felix’s steady presence on the other end of the phone. Now the ground felt like it was tilting, and not just because of the uneven boards under her knees.
“We plant,” she said. “We don’t… name them all out loud if you don’t want.”
Lillian’s paw stilled on the soil.
“Do you?” she asked.
Milla looked down at her own hands, at the mud already gathering under her claws. “Some of them,” she said. “Not all. Some names are… not helpful.”
Lillian nodded slowly. “Okay. Some, not all.”
They worked in silence at first.
Lillian used the trowel, small neat divots in the soil. Milla used her hands, digging in with her fingers the way she had as a child on her grandparents’ plot, before music schools and conservatoires and practice rooms with no windows. The earth was damp, cool, not yet sticky with spring warmth.
“This row,” Lillian said after a while, “for the ones we’ve already talked about.”
“Plural?” Milla’s mouth was dry.
Lillian’s shoulders moved. “Aye.” She didn’t look up. She placed a bulb in the hole she’d made, pointed end up, and covered it with soil. Her hands were steady. Her voice was not. “For the one that never made it past a line on a stick,” she said. “For the one that did and then didn’t.”
Milla placed her own bulb beside Lillian’s, hands shaking. “For the one I pretended was an accident. For the way I starved myself to make sure it stayed that way.”
Lillian’s head turned sharply. “Milla.”
“You know this,” Milla said. “I told you. I just… not like this.”
The March air felt thin. She dug another little hollow and dropped a bulb in, almost harder than she meant to.
“For every bloody time someone told me my body was the problem and not the world.” Her voice cracked on the last word. She pressed soil over the bulbs with the heel of her hand, hard enough that her wrist protested. Lillian reached across the bed and put her paw over Milla’s, muddy fur and muddy fur.
“For your body,” she said quietly. “And mine. And every student’s I see them slag off in tabloids.”
Milla laughed once, the sound close to a sob. “You always bring it back to work.”
“That wasnae work,” Lillian said. “That was them making you a lesson for other fat girls. ‘Do this or end up like her.’”
Milla shut her eyes. She could still see the headline if she let herself: before and after photos that were really just different dresses, an article that framed her as a warning, the comments under it.
“I know,” she said.
They planted another row.
“This one,” Lillian said, “for the students.”
She named them as she dropped the bulbs in, voice as steady as if she were calling a register. First names only, little snapshots: the boy who’d been moved three times because neighbours complained about his drum kit; the girl who’d stopped turning up to lessons when her benefits got sanctioned; the kid whose attendance record hid a whole story of housing officers and home visits.
Milla added her own. The young violinist who’d disappeared between auditions. The boy whose mother had apologised for his “lack of discipline” when his hands shook from low blood sugar.
“And,” Lillian said at the end of the row, “for the Strays. The ones in the warehouse, the ones we havenae met yet. The ones who never made it to the youth centre.”
They did not say Sarah’s name. She did not belong here. Sarah was for other beds, other futures.
“What about…” Milla began, then stopped. Lillian waited. “What about the lives we thought we were going to have?” Milla asked. “The versions of us that went to conservatoire and came out with perfect CVs and no… no detours. The ones that didn’t end up in food banks or therapy waiting rooms or…” She gestured vaguely, encompassing the allotment, the city, the whole damned decade.
Lillian huffed out a breath. “Ambitious. Planting for ghosts of ourselves now, are we?”
“Why not? They died too.”
Lillian considered, then nodded. “All right. A row for them.”
They dug and planted, naming conservatoire scholarships that never materialised, orchestras they’d thought they might play in, festivals they’d dreamed of headlining. It should have felt self‑indulgent. It didn’t. Every bulb dropped into the earth made something unclench in Milla’s chest.
By the time they reached the back of the bed, the sack was lighter and their knees were sore. Mud streaked Lillian’s jeans and had worked its way into the fur on Milla’s wrists. The air smelled of damp wood and distant fried chicken. “We should mark them,” Milla said.
“The rows?”
“Not with names,” Milla said quickly. “Just… something.”
Lillian rummaged in the canvas bag she’d brought and produced a handful of smooth slate chips and a white marker pen. “Art department perks,” she said. “You want to write?”
Milla took the pen. It felt heavier than it was.
On the first stone she wrote simply: FOR WHAT WAS. On the second: FOR WHO WE LOST. On the third: FOR WHO WE COULD HAVE BEEN. On the last: FOR WHO IS STILL COMING HOME.
She pushed each stone into the soil at the head of a row. The white letters stood out against the damp dark.
“Too much?” she asked.
Lillian shook her head. “Just enough.”
They sat back on their heels.
The bed looked underwhelming. Just a rectangle of turned earth with a few stones. No flowers yet, no colour. Milla felt a flicker of disappointment that surprised her in its childishness. “I keep expecting them to… do something,” she said.
“They will,” Lillian said. “Just not on our timetable.”
Milla snorted. “You sound like my therapist.”
“I’ll take that as a compliment.”
The wind stole some of the warmth from her cheeks. Milla rubbed her hands together to get the feeling back and tried not to think about calories burned. Old habits tugged at the edge of her mind, numbers and bargains and rehearsed apologies. “Can I ask you something?” she said.
“Of course.”
“Does he know?” Milla kept her eyes on the soil. “Miles. About…”
“No,” Lillian said. The answer came quickly, clipped.
Milla looked up. Lillian’s jaw was set. The light caught the little lines at the corners of her eyes.
“Do you want him to?” Milla asked.
Lillian stared at the bed as if answers might sprout from the bulbs on the spot. “Sometimes,” she said. “Sometimes I think if he knew he’d… I dunno. Look at me different. In a good way, maybe. Understand why I—” She broke off, shook her head. “Other times I think if he never knows, it can stay mine. That there’s parts of me that arenae for sharing. That I dinnae have to hand over every bit of blood and grief like proof.”
Milla let the words sit. “You don’t owe him that,” she said finally. “You don’t owe anybody your medical history like it’s a love token.”
Lillian huffed. “Romantic.”
“Says the woman who built an entire band round mutual trauma.”
They both laughed, the sound brief and real.
Milla sobered first. “But you also don’t have to carry it alone forever,” she said. “You’re allowed to put some of it down. Maybe not with him. Maybe with… with me. Or Jonathan. Or that therapist you keep not phoning.”
Lillian made a face. “You and Felix ganging up on me now?”
“He’ll only join if I ask nicely.”
They sat in silence for a minute, watching a pigeon hop along the fence.
“Tell you what,” Lillian said eventually. “We make a deal.”
“Oh?”
“You keep eating. I’ll make the phone call. We both check in with each other about it without pretending it’s fine when it isnae.”
Milla opened her mouth to protest the bluntness, then shut it again. It was a relief, in a way, that Lillian said it plainly. No euphemisms. No “food issues”. Just eating. “Okay,” she said. “Deal.”
They spat into their palms like children and shook on it, mud and all.
“Disgusting,” Milla said, wiping her hand on her jeans.
“Authentic,” Lillian said.
They stood carefully, knees complaining. Lillian pressed a hand to the small of her back. Milla pretended not to notice.
“We’ll need to come back and weed,” Milla said, looking at the bed.
“Aye. Weeding’s half the work.”
At the gate, Milla turned back for one last look.
The bed was still just soil and slate, but it felt… occupied now. Not by ghosts, exactly, but by something that might become colour if given time and water and attention.
“For who is still coming home,” she read under her breath.
In her mind’s eye she saw Sarah, shoulders squared, clutching a sketchbook; saw younger Strays stumbling through Lillian’s front door; saw herself, one day, sitting down to eat without counting out penances in her head.
It was a lot to ask of a few bulbs.
She closed the gate behind them anyway.
 
## Chapter 31: Adoption Hearing

### 7.31.1
The waiting room was beige.
Beige chairs, beige carpet, beige walls with framed prints of landscapes that had never seen rain. The kind of place that wanted to be neutral so badly it had erased all texture.
Sarah sat on the edge of one of the chairs, sketchbook on her knees, and counted the dots in the ceiling tiles.
One, two, three, four.  
If the panel says no, we’ll go back.  
Five, six, seven, eight.  
If the panel says no, I’ll make sure the kids have blankets this time.  
Nine, ten, eleven—
“Tea?” Miles’ voice cut in, quiet at her elbow.
She blinked, brought the room back into focus. He held out a paper cup with a plastic lid, his other paw wrapped round his own cup like it was something to hold on to.
“No, thanks,” she said. Her throat was too tight for hot liquid.
“Water?”
She shook her head.
He nodded and sat down on the chair beside hers, suit trousers pulling slightly over his knees. He’d worn the same dark suit he’d bought for their wedding, but with a different tie. Less colour, more serious. The knot was slightly crooked. Lillian had tried to fix it in the mirror that morning and given up halfway through, laughing at herself.
Lillian sat on Sarah’s other side now, hands wrapped round her own tea. Her dress was the kind of thing you could wear to a staff meeting and a gig and an adoption hearing: dark blue jersey, cardigan with the sleeves pushed up. Her glasses were properly on her nose rather than perched in her hair. She’d put on lipstick and then wiped it off again, leaving just a faint stain. “You all right, love?” she asked.
Sarah nodded. Her hands were cold. She dug them under her thighs to warm them on the backs of her legs.
Across the room, another family waited. Human couple, human boy about ten. He swung his feet, trainers not quite reaching the floor, and whispered something to his dad. The dad smiled and ruffled his hair. The mum sat very straight, handbag clutched tight.
There was a poster on the wall about safeguarding. Another about the council’s fostering programme. A third about “celebrating diversity” with a stock photo of a carefully chosen mix of faces smiling at nothing in particular.
“Stop looking,” Sarah told herself. “Just breathe.” She opened her sketchbook instead.
The page she’d left off on was full of hands. Her own, Lillian’s, Miles’. Different angles: claws on paper, fingers on strings, fur against fur. She’d started it the night after the rally, when her thoughts had been too loud to sleep and the only thing that had made sense was capturing the way Lillian’s paw had closed around the mic stand, knuckles white, and the shape of Miles’ hand on her shoulder afterwards.
Now she added the three of them as they were in the waiting room: Lillian’s tea cup, Miles’ clipboard balanced on his knees, her own fingers worrying the corner of the page.
“They’re running ten minutes late,” Miles said, glancing at the clock. “Typical.”
Lillian huffed. “At least they’re consistent.”
Sarah drew the clock too. It gave her something to hang the minutes on.
The door to the hearing room opened.
“Ms Edgecombe? Mr Ferguson? And Sarah?” A woman in a navy dress and sensible heels stood there, clipboard in hand. Her hair was pinned back in a style that looked neat and tired at the same time.
Sarah’s stomach swooped. She closed the sketchbook carefully and stood up.
“That’s us,” Lillian said, with a quick smile that didn’t quite reach her eyes.
They followed the woman down a short corridor and into a room that looked like somebody had tried to design a courtroom from memory after only seeing them on TV. There was a long table at the far end with three people behind it. A man in a grey suit with a laptop open in front of him; a woman in a patterned blouse with a stack of files; and another woman, older, with glasses low on her nose and a pen poised.
On their side of the room, three chairs and a smaller table. No witness box, no dock. Just chairs. Sarah’s relief at that surprised her.
“Please, have a seat,” the navy‑dress woman said. “I’m Karen, the panel clerk. This is Mr Hughes, Ms Gallagher, and Mrs Long.” She indicated the panel members in turn. “We’re here to consider your application in respect of Sarah.”
“In respect of Sarah,” Sarah thought. Like she was a case. A file. A bullet point on an agenda.
Lillian sat in the middle, Miles on her right, Sarah on her left. The clerk checked names, dates of birth, case numbers. Sarah’s name sounded strange in that room: Sarah Ferguson, said in the same tone as “agenda item four”.
The introductions blurred. Social worker names, report titles. Sarah clung to the floor under her feet, to the feel of the chair fabric under her thighs. “We’ve all had the opportunity to read the reports,” Ms Gallagher was saying, calm and efficient. “But we’d like to hear from you in your own words as well.”
Mr Hughes leaned forward slightly. His tie had tiny blue squares on it. “Perhaps you could start by telling us,” he said to Lillian and Miles, “why adoption, and why now.”
Lillian’s fingers tightened slightly on the edge of the table. Sarah saw the movement more than the expression. “Because she asked,” Lillian said simply. “And because we’ve already chosen her. This is about the law catching up.”
Mr Hughes’ eyebrow rose. “She asked?”
Sarah’s throat closed. Had she? Not in so many words. She’d packed her bag and knocked on their door and stayed. She’d drawn them into her notebooks until the pages had blurred. She’d said “if you want me there” about a hundred times. She hadn’t actually said “adopt me.”
Lillian glanced at her. “Sarah?”
The panel’s eyes shifted in unison. Three sets of human gaze and one from Karen at the side. Sarah wanted to flatten herself into the chair. She swallowed. “I… yeah,” she said. Her voice sounded small in the room. “I asked.”
“In what way?” Mrs Long’s tone was not unfriendly, exactly, but careful. Her pen hovered.
Sarah groped for words. They slipped like soap. “I turned up,” she said finally. “At their flat. With a bag. And I didn’t leave.”
There was a flicker of something at the corner of Ms Gallagher’s mouth. Not a smile, but the ghost of one.
“That’s not quite legally binding,” Mr Hughes said, but his tone had softened.
Miles cleared his throat. “We’d been… visiting her and the other kids at the warehouse for months,” he said. “Bringing food, clothes, that sort of thing. We’d talked about fostering, adoption. We’d started looking at the process. Then things escalated with the older Strays. It stopped being theoretical.”
He didn’t go into detail. They’d all agreed on that. No need to relive the night of the firebreak in a room with beige carpet.
“We brought her home,” Lillian said quietly. “That’s the short version.”
“And since then?” Ms Gallagher asked.
They talked about school. About the art course the youth centre had helped her get onto. About the chaos of cramped bedrooms and the promise of the new house still in planning permission limbo. About curfews and washing machines and the way Sarah did the dishes more reliably than the adults some weeks. “She’s not a project,” Lillian said at one point, voice firmer. “She’s our kid. The paperwork willnae change that. It’ll just mean if something happens to one of us, the other doesnae have to fight twice as hard to keep her.”
Mr Hughes nodded slowly. “And Sarah,” he said, turning back to her, “what does adoption mean to you?”
The word sat huge in the air.
Adoption.
As if she could pin it to one answer.
She thought of the warehouse, of cold concrete and kids tucked into corners, of counting tins of beans. She thought of Lillian’s kitchen, cluttered and warm. Of Miles’ spreadsheets. Of the way they both said her name.
“It means… not having to choose between them and the kids,” she said. “Not being… temporary. Not waiting for someone to say the funding’s run out or I’ve timed out.” Her voice shook on the last word.
“Thank you,” Ms Gallagher said softly.
They asked about anger. About violence. About “aggression”. “You’ve been through a lot, Sarah,” Mr Hughes said. “We have to be confident that you can live in a family home without posing a risk.”
There it was. The word risk, slid into the conversation like it was neutral.
Sarah’s hands went cold again. “To who?” she asked before she could stop herself.
Silence. Karen’s pen paused in its margin notes.
“To any younger children who might visit,” Mr Hughes said carefully. “To Ms Edgecombe and Mr Ferguson.”
“I’ve been looking after younger kids since I was eight,” Sarah said. The anger came up fast, familiar. “I kept them fed. I kept them warm. I got in the way when older ones wanted to hit them. If I was going to be a risk, it’d have happened years ago.”
“Sarah,” Lillian murmured, warning in her tone.
“No, it’s okay,” Ms Gallagher said. “It’s a fair question.”
Mr Hughes sat back. “You have to understand,” he said, “we see a lot of cases where trauma leads to—”
“Are you asking her,” Miles cut in, voice level but steel‑lined, “because of her history, or because she’s anthro?”
The temperature in the room shifted.
“Mr Ferguson—” Mrs Long began.
“Because you haven’t asked us if we’re going to be violent,” Miles went on. “You haven’t asked if I’ll pose a risk. And I have a history of being angry too. I just learned to bury it in spreadsheets and management reports.”
Lillian stared at him. She’d never heard him talk like that in a meeting. At home, sure. In the kitchen at midnight, ranting about tokenism and “diversity champions” who never moved past photo ops. But here, in this beige room?
“I appreciate your concern for fairness,” Mr Hughes said, but there was a flush rising under his collar now. “We have to consider—”
“We’re considering it,” Ms Gallagher said smoothly, cutting across him. “The question is relevant, but so is how we ask it.” She turned back to Sarah. “Have you ever hurt anyone?” she asked. “Not in self‑defence, not to protect someone else, but because you lost control?”
Sarah thought of the warehouse, of fists and snarls and the way her own claws had bitten into her palms. She thought of the one time she’d shoved a younger kid too hard in a panic and had spent the night watching for signs of concussion. “I’ve hit people,” she said. No point lying. “Older kids. When they tried to take food off the little ones. When they… when they tried to do other things. I’ve never… I’ve never hit one of the little ones. Not on purpose. I’ve yelled. Thrown things at walls. I’ve wanted to break faces. But I know what breaking does. I don’t want to be that.” Her throat felt raw. She swallowed.
“We’ve seen no indication of uncontrolled aggression in the months she’s been with us,” Miles said. “Anger, yes. She’s entitled to that. But not violence for its own sake.”
“All right,” Ms Gallagher said. She made a note.
They called witnesses next.
Juno, in her school blazer with a band T‑shirt just visible at the collar, talked about Sarah teaching her younger kids chords between shifts at the youth centre. Asim spoke about the warehouse, about what he’d seen before Miles and Lillian ever went near it.
The panel asked about support networks. About money. About the house. Miles produced folders, printouts, spreadsheets. Lillian talked about leave entitlements and timetable flexibility and the difference between a school that said it was inclusive and one that actually meant it.
Sarah waited for the question about “cultural fit”. It came late, from Mrs Long. “There is still some… concern among certain members of the public,” she said delicately, “around anthro adoption. About stability. About public perceptions. How do you think you’ll handle that?”
“We’ve been handling it since we started dating,” Lillian said dryly. “We’ll keep doing what we’ve been doing. Being boringly functional in public until folk get tired of being outraged that we exist.”
Miles added numbers: hate crime stats, policy statements, guidance about anthro carers that had sat in drawers until someone forced councils to use it.
“You will be under scrutiny,” Mrs Long said. “Are you prepared for that?”
Sarah answered before either adult could. “We already are,” she said. “Every time we walk down the street together, people stare. Every time we sit in a café, there’s at least one person who thinks they’re the first to make a joke about dog hair. The difference is, if you say yes today, when they stare I can tell myself, ‘They’re staring at my parents.’”
The word felt strange and solid on her tongue. Parents. It hung in the beige air for a moment.
Lillian’s hand found her knee under the table and squeezed.
The panel conferred in low voices. Karen collected papers, redistributed them. There was a lot of shuffling. Sarah’s ears picked up snatches of phrases: “attachment”, “resilience”, “support plan”.
“We’ll adjourn briefly to consider our decision,” Ms Gallagher said.
They were ushered back into the waiting room.
The other family was gone. The clock hands had shifted. Sarah had no idea how long they’d been inside. Her tail had gone numb where it pressed against the back of the chair.
“You did brilliantly,” Lillian said.
Sarah shook her head. “I got angry.”
“You told the truth,” Miles said. “They can work with that.”
He stood up and began to pace, counting steps between the vending machine and the window. Lillian sipped cold tea.
Sarah opened her sketchbook again. She drew the panel room: three figures behind a table, a smaller figure in front. It could have been any meeting. Any gate. Any time she’d stood on one side of a desk and waited for someone to decide her future. This time, she drew the line between them differently. Less like a wall, more like a table they were all leaning over.
The door opened.
“They’re ready for you,” Karen said.
Back in the room, the chairs were in the same place. The faces were the same. Sarah’s heart slammed anyway.
Ms Gallagher folded her hands on the table.
“Thank you for your patience,” she said. “We’ve considered the reports and what you’ve told us today. We’re satisfied that you understand Sarah’s history and her needs, that you can meet those needs, and that adoption is in her best interests.”
The words blurred after that. Sarah caught fragments: “order granted”, “Parental Responsibility”, “new birth certificate”.
“To be clear,” Mrs Long said, “from today, legally, Sarah is your daughter.”
Sarah stared at her.
The room felt suddenly too bright. The beige sharpened. “Could you…” Her voice came out hoarse. “Could you say that again?”
Ms Gallagher smiled properly this time, faint lines creasing at the corners of her eyes. “From today,” she said, “you are Sarah Ferguson’s parents. And she is your daughter.”
The word fell like a key turning.
Lillian let out a sound that might have been a laugh or a sob. Miles’ paw closed around Sarah’s hand so tightly her fingers tingled. She didn’t pull away.
Karen passed papers across the table. Miles signed where indicated, Lillian after him. When they pushed a copy towards Sarah, she held the pen for a long moment before writing her name.
Sarah Ferguson.
No warehouse name. No case number. Just that.
Outside, the sky had brightened to a thin, watery blue. On the steps of the civic centre, they paused. “Fancy a curry?” Lillian asked, wiping at her eyes with the back of her paw. “Or chips. I could murder chips.”
“Is that… appropriate?” Miles asked weakly. “Shouldn’t we do something more… ceremonial?”
“Chips can be ceremonial,” Lillian said. “You can light a candle next to the ketchup if you like.”
Sarah heard their bickering with half an ear. Most of her attention was on the piece of paper in her paws and the weight of the new name in her mouth.
“Mum,” she said, trying it out.
Lillian froze.
“Aye?” she said, very carefully.
“I want extra vinegar,” Sarah said. Her voice shook. “On the chips.”

### 7.31.2
Asim’s mother had been cooking since dawn.

You could tell from the smell alone.

When Toby stepped into the house behind Asim, he was hit with layers: onions softened to sweetness, cardamom and cumin blooming in hot oil, something tomato‑rich simmering patiently. Underneath it all was the clean scent of detergent and the faint musk of a home that had been lived in for decades.

Asim had warned him on the walk over.

No touching. No hand‑holding. No looking like a couple.

Just tea.

Toby had nodded like he understood.

He understood enough.

The living room looked exactly like it had the first time Asim had described it to him: the patterned sofa with plastic on the arms, the Qur’an stand by the corner, the framed school photos of nieces and nephews arranged with pride.

Asim’s father sat in his chair with his jaw set like stone.

Asim’s mother moved through the room like she owned the air. “Shoes,” she said, and Toby complied immediately, lining his trainers up neatly by the rack.

“Good,” she said, as if he’d passed a test.

She put a tray down on the coffee table with a soft thump: tea glasses, a plate of dates, little pastries dusted with sugar.

Toby sat where Asim gestured, knees together, hands folded. It took all his effort not to reach for Asim’s paw. He kept his posture polite. Non‑threatening. Like a guest in a house that could, at any moment, decide he didn’t belong.

Asim sat on the edge of the sofa, shoulders tight.

His mother poured tea.

His father didn’t speak.

The silence stretched until it became its own kind of conversation.

Finally Asim’s mother looked at her husband and said, in Somali, “Do not sit there like a dead fish.”

Toby blinked.

Asim’s mouth twitched.

His father’s nostrils flared. “I am being respectful,” he said, in English now, clipped.

“Respectful,” his mother repeated, with the tone of someone tasting something sour. She turned her gaze to Toby again. “Eat,” she commanded.

Toby picked up a pastry. “Thank you,” he said.

She nodded, satisfied.

Asim’s father’s gaze flicked to Toby’s hands. To the careful way he held the pastry. To the fact that he didn’t sprawl. Didn’t take up space like he owned it.

“So,” Asim’s father said finally, voice edged, “you live with my son.”

Toby’s pulse kicked.

Asim spoke before Toby could. “Aye,” he said. “I do.”

His mother cut in, sharp. “He asked Toby, not you,” she said.

Asim blinked.

Toby swallowed. “Yes,” he said, meeting the older man’s gaze as steadily as he could. “I live with Asim.”

“Why?” Asim’s father asked.

The question was not curious.

It was an accusation wrapped in grammar.

Toby felt Asim’s body go rigid beside him.

His mother’s eyes narrowed. “Do not start,” she warned her husband.

Asim’s father ignored her. “You are a human man,” he said. “And he is… what he is.”

Asim’s jaw clenched.

Toby kept his voice low. “He’s Asim,” he said.

That was, apparently, the wrong answer.

Asim’s father scoffed. “He is a Muslim,” he said. “He is my son. He has duties. You cannot just take him.”

Asim’s mother made a sound like a knife sliding into a cutting board.

“Take him,” she repeated.

She turned on her husband fully now.

“You talk like our son is a goat someone stole from the market,” she said, voice rising. “No one took him. He left because you pushed. You stood in our kitchen and told him to choose between God and truth as if you were God.”

Asim’s father’s face darkened. “I told him to repent,” he said.

“You told him to lie,” she snapped.

The room went still.

Toby sat perfectly upright, heart hammering, trying to make himself smaller without looking like he was ashamed.

Asim stared at his mother like he was watching an earthquake.

She leaned forward, tea glass in hand, finger pointed at her husband.

“You drove three hours to listen to him speak about Strays,” she said. “You sat in a hall and let everyone see you have a son, and you were proud, even though you tried to hide it. And then you came home and pretended you had no son again.”

Asim’s throat tightened.

His father’s mouth opened.

His mother cut him off.

“No,” she said. “You listen. You do not get to call yourself a father if you only want the version of your child that makes you comfortable.”

She turned her gaze to Toby.

The anger in her face softened into something sharp and tired.

“Toby,” she said. “My son is stubborn. He is loud. He thinks he is always right.”

Asim muttered, “Hooyo—”

“He is,” she said, without looking at him.

Toby’s mouth twitched. “I’ve noticed,” he said.

Asim let out a wet, surprised laugh.

His mother nodded once, approving of the honesty.

“If you hurt him,” she said, “I will find you. I will not need Google. I will simply follow the trail of your guilt.”

Toby’s throat went tight. “I won’t,” he said.

She stared at him for a beat, then nodded again.

Then, as if deciding something internally, she reached across the table and patted Toby’s hand—quick, firm, the same gesture she’d given Asim.

It wasn’t affection.

It was acceptance.

Asim’s father stared at the gesture as if it were a betrayal.

His mother looked at him and said, in English, flat and final: “If you want to keep your son, you will learn how to sit in a room with his life without trying to burn it down.”

No one spoke for a moment.

Then Asim, voice rough, said, “Tea’s good, Hooyo.”

His mother sniffed. “Of course it is.”

Toby took a sip.

His hands were shaking.

But he was still in the room.
 
## Chapter 32: Performance and Nightmares

### 7.32.1
By the end of the second set, Lillian’s shirt clung damp to her back and her throat tasted of metal and smoke.
‘The Basement’ pulsed in front of her: bodies close, air thick, lights catching on fur and skin and the occasional raised can of lager. The room smelled of sweat and spilt beer and something sharper underneath — adrenaline, maybe. Or hope.
“You all right?” Miles asked in her ear as she stepped back from the mic.
She nodded, rolling her shoulders. “Grand.”
He eyed her. “On a scale of one to Scottish…?”
She snorted. “Hush. You’ve got a count‑in to think about.”
He huffed a laugh, then turned back to his kit. The stage was barely big enough for all of them. Juno had already managed to whack her headstock on a low beam once; there was a dent in the plaster to prove it. Asim hovered near the spare mic, scarf loose, notes tucked into his back pocket.
“We’ve got two more for you,” Lillian told the room, leaning into the mic. “Then you can all go home and file complaints with the council about the noise.”
There was laughter, a few cheers.
“This next one’s… personal,” she said. The word felt inadequate. “It’s called ‘Thunder’s Daughter’.”
A murmur rippled through the room. Some of the regulars had heard it before in half‑finished form. Tonight was the first time she’d committed to it in a full set, title and all.
She glanced to the side.
Sarah stood in the wings, sketchbook open, pencil already poised. The angle of her shoulders told Lillian the girl was watching more than she was drawing. Juno stood a little behind her, bass strap over her shoulder, eyes fixed on Lillian with the intensity of someone watching a storm come in.
Lillian wrapped her paw around the mic stand and took a breath.
She thought of her father, of the way he’d say “that lass is thunder” when she got going, pride and worry entwined. Of the last time she’d heard his voice on the phone, rasped thin by illness. Of Edinburgh rain on tenement stone, of the first time she’d realised she could be both too much and not enough at the same time.
She thought of the warehouse. Of the bathroom. Of the garden bed where bulbs slept under dark soil.
Then she sang.
The first verse came low, almost conversational. Stories of nights when lightning lit up her childhood bedroom, of counting seconds between flash and rumble. Of learning that storms passed, and that sometimes you were the storm.
She didn’t look at Miles when she sang that part. She wasn’t ready to see what might be on his face.
What she did see, in a flick of vision between phrases, was Sarah’s expression: ears forward, eyes bright, pencil forgotten. Recognition, not of specifics but of weather. Of what it meant to live in a body that other people thought they could read like a map and still miss all the important bits.
The bridge pulled the song out of her and into something bigger. Juno’s backing vocals braided round her, soft and fierce. Miles kept the heartbeat steady underneath.
On the last chorus, the crowd picked it up. Not all of them; it wasn’t that kind of song yet. But enough that the words came back at her from the dark: thunder’s daughter, flood and shelter.
She let the final note hang until her lungs burned, then dropped her chin and stepped back.
For a second there was silence.
Then the room erupted.
Applause, whistles, the scrape of chairs as people stood, the kind of roar that didn’t feel like they were applauding her personally so much as the fact that someone had said something out loud they’d been carrying in their own chests.
Lillian’s knees wobbled. She gripped the mic stand harder.
Juno caught her eye from the wings. The younger girl’s face was wet, whether with sweat or tears, Lillian couldn’t tell. Juno mouthed something — thank you, maybe, or sorry, or both. Lillian gave her a small nod. Later. They’d talk later.
She blinked the room back into focus.
“Right,” she said into the mic, voice rough. “Enough feelings. One more for you and then they’ll kick us out.”
The laughter that answered was softer this time, the room still vibrating.
Miles counted them in.
The opening riff of For the Things We Believe In was muscle memory now. Her fingers found the shapes without thinking. The lyrics sat in the back of her throat like a familiar prayer.
Tonight, the song landed differently.
Maybe it was the months behind it: the meetings, the marches, the late‑night logistics. Maybe it was the sight of Sarah in the wings, not crammed against a warehouse wall but on a stage edge, sketchbook open. Maybe it was the knowledge that when they went home, it would be to a house with more rooms than they’d ever had before, with a bed that was indisputably Sarah’s.
She sang of food banks and lesson plans, of council cuts and stubborn joy. Of kids in cold places and adults who refused to look away. The words had started life as scribbles on the back of a rehearsal schedule. Now they belonged to more people than she could count.
Asim stepped up during the middle eight, taking the mic with an ease that made her half smile even as she focused on her harmonies.
“Some people think crisis is a storm,” he said over a beat that had dropped to almost nothing. “It comes, it goes, we mop up. But crisis is what happens when the roof was never built properly in the first place. When the funding was never there. When certain kids were never written into the plans.”
The crowd murmured. Some nodded. Some wiped at their faces.
“We’re not here to be inspiring tragedies,” he went on. “We’re here to build better roofs. Better plans. Boring, sturdy, everyday structures that mean kids don’t end up in warehouses in the first place. That’s what we believe in. That’s what this song is for.”
He stepped back. Miles brought the drums up again. Lillian came back in on the chorus.
The last line always made her want to laugh and cry at once. Miles had insisted on keeping it, saying there was something right about the image of patched‑together structures sewn from whatever they had to hand.
She thought of the house on Leconfield Road, of paint on fur and friends carrying sofas up too‑narrow stairs. Of Sarah’s shout in the night, the word that had torn out of her as easily as breath.
Mum. Dad.
Her voice hitched for a second. She swallowed it down and let the crowd carry the line.
At the very end, as the last chord rang out and the room roared, she saw it: a flash of something on Sarah’s face that wasn’t just awe or pride or that tight, wary hope she wore so often. It was… ease, maybe. The way her shoulders dropped, just a fraction. The way her paw moved over the page with less force.
Later, when the amps were off and the lights had come up and they were packing cables into crates, Lillian slipped outside into the alley for air.
The night smelled of damp brick and cigarette smoke. The posters from the rally months ago had long since peeled, but someone had spray‑painted a new slogan on the wall: STRAYS ARE FAMILY.
She leaned against the cool brick, chest still heaving slightly, and tilted her head back.
The sky over London never went fully dark. Light from shops and flats and streetlamps bounced off low clouds, painting everything a soft orange‑grey. Somewhere high up, a plane blinked.
Footsteps behind her.
“You ran away,” Sarah said.
Lillian smiled without turning. “Aye. Briefly. Don’t tell the fans.”
Sarah came to stand next to her, shoulder to shoulder, not quite touching.
“They were asking for you,” she said. “Well. Some of them. Juno’s handling the rest.”
“She can have all the autograph requests,” Lillian said. “My paw’s cramping.”
They stood in silence a moment.
“That song,” Sarah said finally. “The thunder one.”
“Mmm?”
“It’s about you.”
“A wee bit.”
Sarah’s ears flicked. “It made sense,” she said. “The bit about… being the storm and the shelter. I… I get that.”
“Aye,” Lillian said softly. “I thought you might.”
They let that sit.
“You scared me a bit,” Sarah admitted after a moment. “On that note you held. I thought you were going to fall over.”
“You and Miles both,” Lillian said. “If I’d gone, you could’ve caught me between you.”
“I’m not that strong.”
“You’re stronger than you think,” Lillian said. 
The alley hummed faintly with the bass still bleeding through the walls.
“Do you… ever regret it?” Sarah asked suddenly. “Not having your own kid. Like. From you.”
The question landed without warning, but not without preparation. Lillian had heard versions of it in the tilt of people’s heads, in the way some colleagues looked at her when she mentioned Sarah.
“Sometimes,” she said. No point lying. “There’s a version of me that exists in some parallel Edinburgh where I raised some wee badger that has my eyebrows and my temper. I think about her sometimes. Wonder if she’s happy.”
She watched Sarah’s face as she said it. No flinch yet.
“But then I look at my life,” she went on. “At you. At Miles. At the band. At the garden. At nights like this. And I think… this is the one I’ve got. This is the one I built. It’s no’ second best. It’s just different.”
Sarah’s shoulders rose and fell. “You’re allowed to miss the other one.”
“Aye,” Lillian said. “Same way you’re allowed to miss the version of your life where you never had to be in charge at eight. We can grieve what we didnae get and still be glad about what we have.”
Sarah nodded slowly.
“I drew you,” she blurted then, switching tracks with the abruptness of someone who’d nearly lost her nerve. “During the thunder song. D’you want to see?”
“Of course.”
She handed over the sketchbook.
The drawing was rough — done in the half‑dark, fast — but the lines were sure. Lillian at the mic, head tipped back, mouth open mid‑note. The curve of her belly under her shirt, the slope of her shoulders, the way her paw wrapped round the stand. Behind her, shadowy but solid, the band. At the edge of the page, barely there, a smaller figure holding a sketchbook.
“That you?” Lillian asked, tapping the margin.
Sarah shrugged. “Maybe.”
“I like it,” Lillian said. “You’ve made me look… sturdy.”
“You are.”
“Tell that to my knees.”
Sarah’s mouth twitched. “Your knees did fine.”
“My knees are demanding a bath and a large mug of tea as we speak.”
“They can have it when we get home,” Sarah said. “Mum.”
The word slipped out as easily as it had that night in the new house, but this time she didn’t snatch it back.
Lillian’s breath caught. Then she smiled, slow and wide. “Aye, love,” she said. “Let’s go home.”
They stepped back into the venue.

### 7.32.2
Sarah woke up choking.

For a half second she didn’t know where she was. The dark was too soft. The air too warm. There was no drip of water into a bucket, no distant scuffle of older boys, no corrugated roof rattling when buses went past.

That was what made the panic hit harder.

Her body had learned danger by sound.

Silence meant you’d missed something.

She sat bolt upright, duvet tangled around her legs.

In the corner of the room, her rucksack sat where she always put it.

Her chest heaved.

The dream still clung to her like cobweb.

Warehouse.
Connor.
The side door coming off its hinges.
Bun on the floor, blood spreading, and Sarah’s paws slipping, slipping.

In the dream she’d tried to call 999 and her fingers had turned into claws that couldn’t press buttons.

She blinked hard.

The room remained.

Blank wall.
Curtains.
The faint glow of a streetlight through the fabric.

Her throat tightened.

She didn’t want to shout.

She didn’t want to be a problem.

And yet the fear didn’t care what she wanted.

Her mouth opened.

“Mum—”

The word came out cracked.

Footsteps hit the landing almost immediately.

The door opened.

Lillian stood there first, hair a mess, glasses crooked, a cardigan dragged on over a T‑shirt. Miles behind her in pyjama bottoms, one paw already reaching as if he could catch the panic by touch.

“What is it?” Lillian demanded, scanning the room, scanning Sarah.

Sarah’s breathing went too fast.

“I’m sorry,” she blurted. “I had— I thought—”

“Nightmare,” Miles said quietly, not a question.

Sarah nodded, shame burning hot.

Lillian’s shoulders dropped. “Aye,” she said. “Right. Okay.”

She crossed the room and sat on the edge of the bed, mattress dipping under her weight. She didn’t touch Sarah straight away. She just existed in the same space, steady and solid.

Miles leaned against the doorframe, leaving the exit clear.

“Tell me what you saw,” Lillian said, voice low.

Sarah swallowed. The images were sharp. Too sharp. “The warehouse,” she managed. “Connor came in. Bun was bleeding again. I couldn’t— I couldn’t press the buttons.”

Lillian nodded slowly. “Your brain is replaying it,” she said. “Like a record that’s stuck. That’s normal after… after that kind of thing.”

Sarah let out a shaky laugh. “Normal,” she repeated, like it was a joke.

Miles pushed off the doorframe and stepped closer. “Can I?” he asked.

Sarah hesitated.

Then nodded.

Miles sat on the floor beside the bed, back against it, close enough that his shoulder brushed Sarah’s shin through the duvet.

“We can do breathing,” he said, awkward as if the words were borrowed. “Or tea. Or we can just sit. Whatever helps.”

Sarah’s hands were shaking.

Lillian reached out then and rested her paw on Sarah’s ankle over the duvet—light, anchoring.

“You’re safe,” she said, not as reassurance, but as fact. “The doors are locked. The roof’s solid. Connor isnae here. We are.”

Sarah’s throat closed.

She nodded hard.

The panic didn’t vanish.

But it stopped escalating.

Minutes passed.

Her breathing slowed.

At some point, Lillian’s voice softened into something almost conversational. “Do you want me to tell you a boring story?” she asked.

Sarah blinked. “What kind of boring story?”

“About the time Miles tried to fix the boiler in our flat and nearly flooded the kitchen,” Lillian said.

Miles groaned. “That wasn’t flooding. That was… controlled water redistribution.”

Sarah’s mouth twitched.

Lillian started anyway, voice dry, and Miles muttered corrections under his breath. The story was stupid and domestic and utterly irrelevant to warehouses and blood.

Which made it perfect.

Sarah lay back down, still tense, but no longer drowning.

“We can stay,” Lillian said quietly. “Till you drop off. Or till morning. Whatever you need.”

Sarah stared at the ceiling.

The old reflex rose—don’t ask, don’t take.

She swallowed it.

“Till morning,” she whispered.

Lillian’s paw squeezed her ankle once.

Miles’s shoulder pressed steady against the bed.

“Alright,” Miles said softly. “Till morning.”

### 7.32.2
Asim was halfway through chopping coriander when the news alert came through.

He hadn’t turned the radio on; he’d learned, after the first month of living in London, that the city did its best work at creeping into you sideways. Toby’s phone lay on the counter by the fruit bowl, screen lighting up against the dim kitchen tiles: red banner, white text, the kind of headline that made your stomach decide it had bones.

WALSALL — EXPLOSION — SUSPECTED DEVICE.

Asim froze with the knife hovering over the chopping board, the smell of green and sharp rising up under his nose.

From the bathroom, water ran.

Toby was singing under his breath—some nonsense song he’d made up about shampoo, the tune deliberately ugly.

Asim stared at the words until they stopped being words and started being a place.

Walsall wasn’t Birmingham, not exactly. But it was close enough that his mind automatically drew a circle around it and put everyone he loved inside.

Hooyo.

Hodan.

Yusuf.

His dad.

The hall where he’d once played football as a boy.

The corner shop where the owner gave him extra sweets when he helped carry crates.

He wiped his paw on a tea towel that didn’t need wiping, then picked up the phone.

There was a missed call.

ABBO.

Asim’s throat tightened. His dad didn’t ring like this. His dad sent the occasional clipped text through his mother—Your auntie says salaam. Eid is next month. You should come.

A call meant the world had shifted.

He pressed the green button before he could overthink it.

“Abbo?” he said.

Silence on the line for a second, and then his father’s breathing, too loud, too close.

“Asim,” his father said, voice rougher than usual.

Asim’s chest loosened and tightened in the same breath. “I’m here,” he said. “I’m fine. What—are you alright? Hooyo? Hodan and Yusuf?”

His father made a sound that was not quite a sigh, not quite a prayer. “We are alive,” he said. “Alhamdulillah.”

“As-salaamu alaykum,” Asim said, the greeting coming out on instinct now, like a hand finding a railing.

“Wa alaykum as-salaam,” his father replied.

They held the line between them for a beat, filled only with the low hum of Toby’s hair dryer through the bathroom door and the faint London traffic outside.

“I saw the news,” Asim said.

“Yes,” his father said. “That is why I am calling.”

“You don’t have to explain that,” Asim muttered, but there was no heat in it.

His father’s voice tightened. “It is… close,” he said.

“I know,” Asim replied. He pushed the knife further away from him as if it were dangerous now. “Is it near you?”

“No,” his father said. “Not near our street. But close enough that everyone starts calling everyone. Your auntie called your mother, your mother called Hodan, Hodan called me, and then I… I called you.”

Asim swallowed.

The fact of it landed heavy.

Not a reprimand.

Not a negotiation.

Just: I called you.

“I’m alright,” Asim said again, because his father’s fear deserved something simple. “I’m in Holloway. I’m at home.”

There was a pause. “Home,” his father repeated, like he was trying the word in his mouth.

Asim felt his own eyes sting with sudden, ridiculous anger. “Aye,” he said. “Home. With Toby. We were making dinner.”

His father didn’t rise to it. He sounded tired, older. “Tell me what you heard,” he said.

Asim glanced at the headline again. It had already updated, already turned human suffering into a list of details. “They don’t know much yet,” he said. “Explosion, suspected device, police—”

“Not the news,” his father interrupted, voice firm. “Tell me what you heard in London.”

Asim blinked. “In London?”

“You are in London,” his father said, as if Asim had forgotten. “When a bomb goes off in Walsall, people talk in Birmingham, yes. But London… London talks louder. People look at our faces and think they know what we believe. They look at you—anthro, Muslim, young—and they think… I do not like you being there when everyone is scared.”

Asim’s throat tightened.

This was his father’s love: fear shaped into warnings.

“I’m careful,” Asim said.

“You were careful when you were twelve and you still came home with your lip split because someone wanted to prove a point,” his father snapped.

Asim went very still.

In the bathroom, the shower turned off.

Toby’s voice drifted through the door. “Asim? You okay?”

Asim didn’t answer.

On the line, his father breathed out. “I should not shout,” he said, quieter. “I am sorry. I just… I saw the alert and my heart did something stupid. I thought, if something happens and I have not heard your voice—”

Asim closed his eyes.

For a moment, he saw his father driving, knuckles white on the steering wheel, coming to the community meeting because his wife had shouted at him for an hour and because somewhere under all that pride there was a man who couldn’t stand the idea of his son being a headline.

“I’m okay,” Asim said, softer. “I promise. And you’re right—people are weird. But I’m not alone here. Toby’s here. Lillian and Miles. The band. People check on each other.”

There was another pause. “How is your mother?” his father asked.

Asim’s mouth twisted. “You’re in the same house,” he said.

“Yes,” his father said patiently, as if Asim were the one being difficult. “But she does not tell me everything. She tells me what she thinks will stop me worrying.”

Asim let out a small, shaky laugh. “She learned that from you.”

His father made a disapproving noise, but it didn’t have teeth in it.

“She’s fine,” Asim said. “She texts me. She sends me pictures of rice like I’m going to forget what food looks like.”

“And your sister?” his father asked.

“Hodan’s still loud,” Asim said, warmth flickering through him. “Still tells everyone what they should be doing. Yusuf’s… Yusuf’s Yusuf. Quiet. But he asked about you last week.”

His father went silent.

Asim imagined him, in their living room, staring at the carpet, taking in a piece of softness and not knowing what to do with it.

“Abbo,” Asim said, carefully, “do you want me to come up?”

The question surprised even him.

It was instinct—pack instinct, born of crisis.

His father’s breath caught. “I was going to ask,” he admitted.

Asim’s chest tightened.

“To Birmingham?” he said.

“Yes,” his father said. “Just for a weekend. Or a week. I don’t know. Just… to be together. To not be alone in our heads. Your mother would like it. She misses you.”

Asim leaned back against the counter. The tile was cool through his T-shirt.

He thought of Toby, towel-wrapped, walking out of the bathroom with damp fur and his eyebrows doing that concerned shape.

He thought of the band’s next gig, of Lillian’s rehearsals, of Miles’s endless lists.

He thought of the warehouse kids—of Sarah’s shoulders always tight, of Faroukh checking his phone like it was a leash.

He thought of Birmingham, and the smell of home, and the ache of being loved by people who wanted him edited.

“I can’t disappear,” Asim said quietly. “Not right now. There’s… things here.”

His father’s voice tightened. “Because of the Strays,” he said.

“Yes,” Asim replied.

“And because of him,” his father said, and even through the phone Asim could hear him swallowing the name.

Asim’s ears warmed. “Aye,” he said. “Because of Toby as well.”

Silence.

Then his father said, very quietly, “Are you happy?”

Asim blinked.

The question destabilised him more than any argument.

It didn’t demand repentance. It didn’t ask for proof.

It asked for truth.

Asim looked at the chopping board with its half-diced coriander, at the pot on the stove waiting for him to put it together, at the small, ordinary kitchen that smelled of cumin and garlic and lemon.

He heard Toby moving, the soft thump of bare paws on the hallway floor.

“Yes,” Asim said. The word came out simple. “I am. Not… not always calm. But happy. I’m building something.”

His father exhaled, long and weary. “Good,” he said.

Asim swallowed. “If you want me to come up soon,” he said, “we can plan it. Not as an emergency. As a… choice.”

His father made a sound like agreement and grief tied together. “Your mother will be pleased,” he said.

“And you?” Asim asked.

His father hesitated. “I will be… less afraid,” he admitted.

Asim closed his eyes.

On the other side of the bathroom door, Toby knocked once, gentle. “Hey,” Toby called softly. “You want tea?”

Asim opened his eyes and stared at the door as if it were another kind of threshold.

“I’ve got to go,” he said into the phone.

His father’s voice tightened again, not with anger this time. “Asim,” he said.

“Aye?”

“Be careful,” his father said.

Asim’s mouth twisted into something like a smile. “Always,” he said.

The line clicked off.

Asim stood still for a moment with the dead phone in his paw.

Then he put it down gently on the counter as if it might shatter.

When Toby opened the door, towel round his waist, fur damp and fluffed, he took one look at Asim’s face and didn’t ask for details.

He just came closer and bumped their shoulders together—solid, careful contact.

“Phone call?” Toby asked.

“Aye,” Asim said, voice rough.

“Tough one?”

Asim breathed out. “Not even tough,” he said. “Just… my dad being my dad.”

Toby nodded, then reached past him to turn the kettle on. The click was loud in the small kitchen.

“Sit,” Toby said, like a directive.

Asim obeyed.

The coriander waited on the board.

Dinner would still happen.

The world, apparently, would too.


## 7.32.3

Felix woke up to the sound of Milla breathing.

It wasn’t dramatic.

It was just there.

In the grey light of early morning, her flat looked softer—less like a showroom of control, more like a place where a person lived. A jumper draped over the chair. A mug in the sink. A pile of sheet music slumped on the coffee table like it had finally given up pretending to be tidy.

Felix lay still for a moment, listening.

He’d slept on the sofa with a blanket over him because he’d offered to go home and she’d said, very quietly, “Stay.”

He got up, careful not to make the floorboards complain, and padded into the kitchenette.

He opened cupboards until he found what he needed.

Eggs.
Bread.
Butter.

He made scrambled eggs the way his mum did: too much butter, a bit of salt, slow heat.

The smell filled the flat.

He heard movement behind him.

Milla appeared in the doorway in an oversized T‑shirt, hair loose, eyes sleepy. No makeup. No armour.

She blinked at him. “What are you doing?”

“Cooking,” Felix said.

Milla frowned. “In my kitchen.”

“Yeah,” Felix said. “Illegal, I know.”

She stared at the pan.

Her jaw worked.

Felix waited for the fight.

Instead Milla’s shoulders sagged like she’d been holding them up with string. “It smells good,” she admitted, as if it cost her.

Felix’s throat tightened. “Sit,” he said, nodding at the table.

Milla sat.

He put a plate in front of her.

Eggs.
Toast.

Nothing fancy.

Milla stared at it.

Felix sat opposite her and started eating immediately, deliberately casual. Normal. Just breakfast.

Milla picked up her fork.

She took a bite.

And then another.

No flinch.

No apology.

She ate like she was hungry.

Felix’s chest ached.

He kept his face neutral.

After a minute, Milla looked up, eyes sharp even with sleep still in them. “Don’t make it a thing,” she warned.

Felix shrugged. “It’s eggs,” he said. “I’m not writing a poem.”

Milla’s mouth twitched.

She took another bite.

For a few minutes, her brain didn’t seem to be punishing her for it.

Felix watched her hands instead—steady, competent, real.

He thought, with a quiet, stubborn hope that scared him, that maybe this was what recovery looked like at the start.

Not a triumphant speech.

Just breakfast.
 
## Chapter 33: The Wedding 

### 7.33.1
Sarah woke to the sound of the shower running and the faint, muffled swearing of a badger trying to shave her legs in a too‑small bathroom.
She lay still for a moment, staring at the cracks in the ceiling paint of the flat’s tiny second bedroom. Morning light seeped round the edges of the thin curtains. Somewhere downstairs a car alarm chirped, then stopped. Her heart thudded in her chest, steady and disbelieving.
Wedding day, she thought. Their wedding day.
Her first instinct was to get up and check the kids.
There were no kids.
Her brain took a few seconds to catch up with the fact that there were no warehouse corners to patrol, no sleeping bodies to count. Just the pile of clothes on the chair, the half‑unpacked bag by the door, and the knowledge that today two adults she cared about were going to stand in front of paperwork and people and say something official about what they already were.
She slid out of bed, feet hitting cold floorboards, and padded to the door.
In the hall, steam fogged the air outside the bathroom. Lillian’s voice drifted through the door, half muttered, half sung, a run of notes that sounded like she was trying to warm up her vocal cords and curse at the same time.
“You all right?” Sarah asked through the crack.
“Aye,” Lillian called back. “Just discovering my left knee is no’ a fan of bending this much.”
“Do you want help?”
There was a beat of silence, then a laugh. “No, you’re grand. I dinnae think either of us is ready for that level of bonding.”
Sarah smiled despite herself. “Fair.”
In the kitchen, Miles stood in his T‑shirt and pyjama bottoms, staring at a list on the table as if it might rearrange itself into something manageable. His fur stuck up in tufts where he’d clearly slept on it wrong. A row of mugs marched along the counter, each at a different stage of being made.
“Morning,” he said when he spotted her. “Tea? Coffee? Hot water and anxiety?”
“Tea,” she said. “And toast, if the toaster still works.”
He gestured to the bread on the counter, already half sliced. “One appliance I can rely on.”
She put slices in the toaster and watched him move round the kitchen with the ballast of someone holding a day together with lists and kettle steam. “What’s left?” she asked, nodding at the paper.
He ran a paw down the columns. “Transport: taxis booked. Food: sorted. Registry office: paid. Rings: hidden. Witnesses: wrangled. Clothes: in progress. Nerves: off the scale.”
“You’ll be fine,” she said.
“I always imagined if I ever did this I’d be the calm one,” he said. “Turns out, not so much.”
She buttered toast, clumsy with the unfamiliar normality of it. “You still get to do logistics,” she said. “Just with more flowers.”
He made a face. “Don’t remind me about the flowers.”
Later, when the shower noise stopped and the bathroom door opened, steam billowed out like a stage effect. Lillian stepped into the hall wrapped in a towel, glasses fogged, hair plastered to her skull.
“The hot water is on a timer,” she announced. “And it hates us.”
“I’ll reset it,” Miles said automatically.
“You’ll be late,” she said.
“I’ll be efficient.”
Sarah watched them argue the toss about who got the last of the heat, the dance of two people who’d lived around each other long enough to make a ritual out of small inconveniences. It made something in her chest ache in a way that had nothing to do with jealousy and everything to do with wanting.
“You go first,” Miles said finally. “I’ll take whatever’s left.”
“There’ll be nowt left,” Lillian said.
“Then I’ll have a Scottish shower,” he said. “Cold wake‑up. Builds character.”
“You’ve enough character,” she said. But she passed him in the narrow hall and touched his shoulder briefly in thanks.
Sarah went back to her room to get dressed.
The dress Lillian had helped her choose hung on the back of the door: simple, dark green, with a skirt that let her move and pockets deep enough to hide her hands in.
