They’d spent an afternoon trawling charity shops to find it, Lillian insisting they could do better than anything on the high street that assumed teenagers either wanted glitter or nothing but black. “You look like yourself,” Lillian had said when she’d tried it on. “That’s the point.”
She pulled it over her head now and smoothed the fabric down over her fur. It felt strange to wear something new that was hers and not a donation, not a hand‑me‑down, not something she’d bartered for. Strange, and good.
When she emerged, hair brushed as flat as it would go, she found Lillian in the bedroom, half dressed and half panicking.
The dress lay on the bed, a simple knee‑length thing in deep red with a wrap front and sleeves that actually covered her upper arms. Lillian stood in her bra and knickers, staring at it as if it had offended her.
“I can’t zip it,” she said when she saw Sarah in the doorway. “And if I do, there’ll be no breathing and probably some sort of international incident when I burst out of it in front of your social worker.”
Sarah leaned against the door frame. “It fit in the shop.”
“That was five days ago and I’m incredibly suggestible to gravity.”
“Turn round,” Sarah said.
Lillian did, muttering under her breath in a mix of English and Scots that Sarah had come to recognise as her default when she was both embarrassed and amused.
Sarah picked up the dress and held it out. “Arms in.”
Lillian obeyed. The fabric slid over her shoulders, hugged her belly. When it came to the zip, there was a moment where it caught.
“See?” Lillian said. “The universe has opinions.”
“Hold still,” Sarah said.
She eased the zip up slowly, careful not to catch fur in the teeth. The dress closed. It was snug, yes, but not strained. It sat on Lillian’s body like it belonged there, curves and all.
“There,” Sarah said. “Told you.”
Lillian stared at herself in the mirror.
For a moment Sarah saw what she saw: a badger woman in her thirties with thick upper arms and a soft stomach, glasses slightly askew, hair refusing to be tamed. Then the image flickered and she saw something else layered over it — the glossy magazine covers that had mocked bodies like this, the headlines that had used Lillian as a cautionary tale.
She stepped between Lillian and the mirror, blocking the line of sight. “You look good,” she said, more forcefully than she’d intended.
Lillian blinked. “Aye?”
“Yeah,” Sarah said. “Like… you. But… more. I dunno. Fancy.”
Lillian’s mouth twitched. “Poet.”
“Shut up,” Sarah said, but her lips curled too.
“Will you… stay near?” Lillian asked, quieter. “At the registry. I know you’re not— you don’t have to come into all the photos and fuss. But I’d… like to see your face.”
Sarah nodded. “I’ll be there.”
In the kitchen, Miles had achieved the near‑impossible: a tie that was straight and a shirt that had only minimal tea stains. He adjusted his cuffs, glanced at the clock for the fourth time in ten minutes, and tried not to look like he was counting breaths.
“You look very respectable,” Sarah said.

“I resent that,” he said. “I was aiming for mildly disreputable in a charming way.”

Lillian entered and whatever reply he’d had ready vanished. His ears went up. His mouth did something unguarded.

“Wow,” he said, which was not eloquent but was honest.

Lillian rolled her eyes. “It’s a dress, Miles. You’ve seen one before.”

“Not that one,” he said. “Not like that.”

Colour rose under her fur. Sarah looked away, suddenly aware she was witnessing something private and fragile.

They caught a taxi to the registry office because Lillian had declared she was not arriving on a bus on her wedding day “like I’m going to the dentist.” The driver barely glanced at them in the rear‑view mirror, used to ferrying people to this particular building for marriages, civil partnerships, citizenship ceremonies, and the occasional unfortunate parking appeal.

Outside the registry office, friends and family gathered in clumps: Juno and Toby sharing a packet of mints; Asim adjusting his tie; Milla in a dress that looked like it had taken three weeks of therapy to choose. Jonathan stood a little apart with his camera, already snapping candid shots: Miles’ paw on the small of Lillian’s back as they stepped out of the taxi, Sarah’s hand tightening on the strap of her bag.

The ceremony room itself was smaller than Sarah had expected. Rows of chairs, a table at the front with a book on it, a vase of slightly wilting flowers. On one wall, a framed black‑and‑white photograph of the building from the fifties, couples in hats and gloves outside.

She took a seat near the aisle, not too close to the front, close enough to see.

The registrar read the legal bits in a clear, well‑practised voice. There were phrases that sounded like they’d been written by someone who liked words, and others that sounded like they’d been written by committee. Lillian and Miles repeated the important lines, tripping slightly over “lawful impediment” and grinning through it.

When it was time for their own words, Miles went first.

He didn’t have a piece of paper. Trust Miles to have written and memorised a speech without telling anyone.

“Lillian,” he said, looking at her and not at the room. “We’ve been doing the work of this for a long time. Bills and buses and lesson plans and rehearsals and spreadsheets and all. So today is… catching up, I suppose. But I wanted to say this out loud anyway.”

Sarah felt the room lean in.

“I promise to do the boring work,” he went on. “The stuff that doesn’t make songs. The forms and the phone calls and the meetings. I promise to build structures that hold, as far as I can. I can’t fix the roof of the whole world, but I can make sure ours doesn’t leak. And when it does, I’ll be up there with a bucket and a plan.”

There was a ripple of laughter. Lillian’s eyes shone behind her glasses.

“I promise to listen,” he said. “Even when you’re not saying something out loud. I promise to try not to fix things you don’t want fixed yet. And I promise to keep making space for music and kids and chaos, even when my spreadsheets don’t like it.”

He cleared his throat. “I love you,” he added, simply.

When it was Lillian’s turn, she did have a piece of paper, folded and refolded until the edges had gone soft.

“Miles,” she said, voice thick. “You know I’m not great at… vows. My da raised me on ‘dinnae promise what you cannae keep’, and the world’s given me enough examples of folk saying the right words and doing the wrong thing.”

She glanced down at the paper, then back up.

“So I’m going to promise this,” she said. “I’ll tell you the truth, as much as I can bear it, even when it’s messy. I’ll try no’ to hide behind sarcasm and lesson plans when I’m scared. I won’t always manage it. But I’ll keep trying.”

Sarah thought of secrets Lillian hadn’t told him, the ones she’d glimpsed in half‑finished sentences and quiet trips to the bathroom. The promise sounded like a bridge being built, shaky and important.

“I’ll keep fighting for our kids,” Lillian went on. “The ones in classrooms, the ones in warehouses, the one sitting there pretending she’s just here for the free biscuits.”

A ripple of laughter broke the tension; Sarah sank lower in her seat, heat flooding her ears.

“I’ll keep singing,” Lillian said. “Even when I’m tired. Even when the papers make a spectacle of it. Because my voice is… it’s ours now. Mine and yours and theirs. And I’ll use it.”

She swallowed. “I love you,” she said, the words like a chord she’d practised until it came clean.

They exchanged rings — simple bands, cool metal on fur. Jonathan’s camera shutter clicked softly in the background. The registrar said the words about being husband and wife, about witnesses and signatures and legalities.

When the registrar invited them to kiss, Lillian laughed, a little startled burst of sound, then reached up and pulled Miles down to her in a kiss that was almost clumsy with relief.

The room applauded. Someone whooped. Sarah found herself clapping too, hands stinging.

Afterwards, in the small courtyard outside, people mingled with plastic cups of cheap prosecco and orange juice. The sun had deigned to make an appearance, though it hovered low, the light thin but kind.

Juno pressed a small bouquet into Lillian’s paws. “We made it from corner‑shop flowers,” she said. “But we did colour theory, so it’s proper.”

Lillian laughed and kissed the top of her head. “It’s perfect.”

Asim hugged Miles hard enough to make his glasses skew. Milla dabbed at her eyes and pretended she wasn’t. Toby handed out Tupperware boxes of sandwiches like a one‑man catering service.

Sarah stood by the wall, cup in hand, watching.

She was not used to celebrations that didn’t end with someone vomiting behind a bin or throwing a punch. The air here held a different tension: ordinary social awkwardness, the balancing act of small talk and genuine feeling.

“How are you doing?” Jonathan asked, appearing at her elbow with the uncanny ability of someone who’d spent years reading rooms for portraits.

She shrugged. “It’s a lot.”

“Good lot or bad lot?”

“Both.”

He nodded, as if that made perfect sense. “Can I steal you for a photo?” he asked. “You don’t have to smile. I just want you in one frame with them.”

She hesitated.

Lillian caught her eye from across the courtyard. Raised her brows in a silent question. You okay?

Sarah nodded. “One photo,” she said to Jonathan. “No… no cheesy poses.”

“Perish the thought,” he said.

He lined them up by the brick wall: Miles in the middle, Lillian tucked under his arm, Sarah on Lillian’s other side. He adjusted their shoulders slightly, not fussing, just aware of angles.

“Look at me,” he said.

Sarah did.

“Now look at each other,” he said.

They did.

The shutter clicked.

In that fraction of a second, Sarah felt something slide into place. Not a sudden, miraculous feeling of belonging. That would have been too much. Just a small easing, a recognition of the fact that, for this moment at least, nobody was about to tell her to move out of frame.

Later, after the modest reception in the back room of a pub that Lillian had chosen because it did decent chips and had a piano in the corner, after Toby’s terrible speech and Juno’s song and Asim’s stern warning to the bar staff about topping up glasses without asking, they went home.

Home. The word had started to mean the flat, cramped and full of instruments. Soon it would mean the house on Leconfield Road. For today, it meant wherever the three of them were together.

In the quiet that followed the door closing behind them, Lillian kicked off her shoes with a groan.

“My feet are going to sue,” she said.

“I told you to wear the flats,” Miles said.

“It’s my wedding day. I’m allowed to make bad decisions about footwear.”

Sarah hovered with her bag, sudden uncertainty prickling. “Do you want me to… go? Give you space?”

The question hung there. She’d asked versions of it all her life: do you want me here, do you want me gone.

Lillian’s head snapped round. “Absolutely not,” she said. “You’re family. We’re ordering chips and watching terrible telly in our wedding outfits until the first person falls asleep. That’s the law.”

Miles nodded. “It’s in the small print on the certificate,” he said. “You can check.”

Relief flooded Sarah so fast it made her dizzy.

They ended up on the sofa: Lillian with her dress hitched up enough to free her knees, Miles with his tie loosened, Sarah curled at the far end with her sketchbook balanced on her knees.

On the coffee table, three plates of chips steamed in the lamplight, vinegar sharp in the air. The television played a drama none of them were really watching. On the wall above, a calendar marked ordinary things: school terms, gig dates, the date of the next community meeting.

Sarah drew them as they were: shoes abandoned in a heap, Lillian’s head tipped back on the cushion, Miles’ hand resting lightly on her knee, their rings catching the light. Her own legs tucked under her, the slight lean of her body towards them that she only noticed when she saw it on the page.

At some point, Lillian’s paw reached blindly across the cushions and found her ankle, fingers curling loosely round it as if to say I know you’re there. Sarah let herself be held.

Much later, when the chips were gone and the credits rolled and Miles snored softly with his glasses askew, Lillian turned her head and looked at Sarah.

“Thank you for coming,” she said. “For… being there. It made it… I dinnae have the words. Less like a performance. More like… the thing itself.”

Sarah shrugged, throat tight. “I live here,” she said. “It would’ve been weird not to.”

Lillian smiled. “Aye,” she said. “You do.”

Outside, the city moved on: buses and sirens and strangers with their own lists of appointments and ceremonies and bad days. Inside the small flat, three people sat in the afterglow of a day that had shifted something quiet and important.

Years later, when Sarah thought back to the wedding, she wouldn’t remember every word of the vows or the exact shape of the registrar’s desk. She’d remember the feel of Lillian’s dress under her claws as she zipped it up. The taste of vinegar on chips. The sound of Miles’ voice when he said “our kids” in his speech. The weight of a paw on her ankle anchoring her to the sofa.

The details of the day would blur at the edges. What stayed sharp was the sense of a line drawn — not between before and after in a dramatic way, but between maybe and yes.

They had chosen each other. The paperwork had caught up.
For the first time, Sarah let herself imagine a future that included next wedding anniversaries, next gigs, next ordinary Tuesdays with school runs and shopping lists and someone to text if the bus was late.
She closed her sketchbook on the last drawing of the night and leaned her head back, listening to the steady breathing of the two people who had just promised, in front of witnesses and forms and badly arranged flowers, to keep doing the work of loving.
It wasn’t a fairy tale.
It was better: something they could live in.

 
## Chapter 34: Christmas (Epilogue) | 3,500 words  

December made London smell like burnt sugar and damp wool.

Miles walked through Camden Market with his collar turned up against the cold, bags cutting into his paw, and tried to pretend he enjoyed Christmas shopping.

“You do,” Sarah said, reading his face.

“I enjoy efficient shopping,” Miles corrected. “This is… chaos shopping.”

Sarah snorted. She’d grown a bit since spring—shoulders broader, cheeks less hollow. There was still a waryness to her, but it sat differently now, like a habit rather than an emergency.

They were buying stocking fillers.

Not for Lillian—she’d already banned anyone from buying her scented candles “because I smell like a badger, deal with it.”

For the youth centre kids.

For Lila.

For two little ones who’d been placed in foster homes but still showed up on Sundays for dinner and refused to stop calling Sarah “boss.”

Miles held up a pair of gloves. “These?”

Sarah felt the lining with her fingertips. “Too scratchy,” she decided.

“You’re fussy,” Miles said, but he put them back.

They moved on.

There were lights strung over the stalls, pretending warmth. Somewhere a speaker played a tinny version of “Fairytale of New York.” Someone spilled mulled wine and swore.

Miles found himself watching Sarah’s body language the way he used to watch the warehouse: scanning, always, for threat.

But now she also scanned for… delight.

She stopped at a stall selling cheap prints and lingered over one of a fox in charcoal.

Miles didn’t comment.

He simply noted it.

Because buying a child a future sometimes looked like buying them the right kind of paper and letting them decide what to put on it.

## Chapter 34 (Beat 2)

They took a shortcut through the alley behind the chicken shop because Miles didn’t trust crowds.

The air back there smelled of grease and piss and cold stone.

Sarah walked a half step behind him out of old habit.

Then she stopped so hard Miles nearly bumped into her.

“What?” he asked.

Sarah didn’t answer.

She stared down the alley.

At first Miles saw only a bundle of blankets.

Then the bundle shifted.

A face emerged—gaunt, fur patchy with grime, eyes too bright.

Connor.

Miles’s chest tightened.

Connor’s ears flattened as he recognised them.

He tried to stand.

His legs didn’t cooperate.

“Christ,” Miles muttered.

Sarah’s hands had gone still at her sides.

Connor’s mouth twisted into something that might once have been a smirk. “Look at you,” he rasped. “Proper family outing.”

Sarah took a step forward.

Miles’s hand shot out instinctively, stopping her.

Sarah didn’t look back. “It’s fine,” she said, voice flat.

Miles didn’t believe her.

Connor coughed, wet and deep.

The sound echoed off the bricks.

Sarah looked at him for a long moment.

Then she reached into her pocket and pulled out a scrap of paper.

Miles realised with a jolt that she’d been carrying it prepared.

She knelt a careful distance away and held it out.

Connor stared.

“What’s that?” he asked.

“Asim’s number,” Sarah said.

Connor blinked.

Sarah’s mouth tightened. “He runs community meetings,” she added, as if that explained anything. “He knows people who can get you a bed. A shelter. Someone who’ll talk to you like you’re human.”

Connor let out a laugh that turned into a cough.

“You think I want charity?” he spat.

Sarah’s eyes stayed on his. “I don’t care what you want,” she said. “I care what happens next.”

Connor’s gaze flicked to Miles, then back.

For a second, something old and raw showed in his face.

Then it vanished.

“Fuck off,” he muttered.

Sarah didn’t move.

She set the paper on the ground beside him, weighted with a coin from her pocket.

“It’s there,” she said. “If you change your mind.”

Miles’s throat tightened.

Sarah stood.

She didn’t look triumphant.

She looked tired.

They walked away.

Behind them, Connor didn’t call out.

## Chapter 34 (Beat 3)

At home, the house smelled of onions and roast chicken and Lillian’s cheap vanilla candle that she claimed she hated and kept lighting anyway.

Sarah came in first, boots kicked off by the door, shopping bags thumping onto the hall floor.

“You’re late,” Lillian called from the kitchen. “I was about to send a search party. Juno volunteered. I refused on the basis that she’d get distracted by a shiny leaf and forget you existed.”

Sarah’s mouth twitched.

Miles hung up his coat. “We got stuck in a crowd,” he called back.

“Aye, and I got stuck with a turkey that refuses to cook evenly,” Lillian replied. “Life’s full of injustice.”

The kitchen was warm.

The table was set.

Not perfectly.

Just… set.

There was a jug of gravy, a pile of roast potatoes, carrots that were slightly burnt at the edges because Lillian liked them that way.

Sarah stood in the doorway for a moment, letting the domestic noise wash over her.

Miles bumped her shoulder gently. “Go on,” he murmured. “Before she eats all the crispy bits.”

Sarah went in.

## Chapter 34 (Beat 4 + closing)

After dinner, when the plates were stacked and the dishwasher whirred like a small, loyal engine, Sarah went upstairs.

In her room, the window rattled faintly with wind.

Her desk was messy now—proper messy. Sketchbooks, pencils, a half‑finished sculpture assignment, letters from social workers in neat piles that no longer made her stomach drop.

She opened the wardrobe.

On the top shelf, she’d been hiding a box.

Not hiding like a secret.

Hiding like a squirrel.

Inside were small presents.

Cheap things.

Useful things.

Warm socks.
Toiletries.
Little sketch pads.
Chocolate coins.

She’d labelled them in her head.

Lila.

Faroukh.

Eli.

The others whose faces still lived in her memory like pencil marks.

She sat on the floor and arranged them into a neater pile.

Downstairs she could hear Miles and Lillian talking, low and familiar, a rhythm she’d come to trust.

Sarah held a packet of socks in her hands and thought about the year.

The warehouse.

The first time Miles showed up with crates.

The first time Lillian called her love.

The night Bun bled and the sirens came.

The day a panel in a beige room said daughter.

The first time she’d shouted Mum and Dad in the dark.

And today.

Connor in an alley, broken down to bones and breath.

She’d expected, once, that survival would feel like victory.

It didn’t.

It felt like work.

It felt like washing up.

It felt like remembering to eat.

It felt like buying gloves that weren’t scratchy and putting someone else’s number on a piece of paper because you couldn’t stand the idea of anyone being entirely unreachable.

Ordinary life, she realised, wasn’t the absence of danger.

It was the presence of people.

She closed the box gently.

Downstairs, Lillian called, “Sarah! Tea!”

Sarah wiped her hands on her jeans and stood up.

“Coming,” she called back.

She went down the stairs into the warm, lit house.

Outside, London carried on.

Inside, they did too.
